Rabbinic Authority I
סמכות רבנית כרך א
Rabbinic Authority: The Vision and the Reality, Urim Publications. Jerusalem, 2013
https://www.nli.org.il/he/books/NNL_ALEPH997009861531405171/NLI

Rabbinic Authority I

Foreword

In referring to the highest level of truth finding in a judicial proceeding, our Sages use of the phrase, “<i>Ha-dan din emet la-amito</i>.” What is being conveyed here is that knowledge of the law is not sufficient to render a verdict of truth. In order for the <i>dayan</i> to arrive at the correct verdict, the precise facts of the case must be discovered as well.
A beth din panel is not only thoroughly versed in the relevant hala­khot, but is also well-equipped by virtue of educational background and professional work experience to grasp the reality on the ground. This entails a relentless commitment to relevant fact finding, taking nothing at face value, and understanding the implications of the facts for the commercial behavior of the parties.
A second characteristic of the halakhic-judicial process dealing with monetary matters consists of the documentation that must accompany the verdict that the panel hands over to the litigants at the conclusion of the proceedings. The claims of the plaintiff, as well as the respondent’s rebuttals and counterclaims, are meticulously recorded. Next, a thorough discussion of the issues from a halakhic perspective is presented. Finally, the verdict is rendered, and this verdict is demonstrated to have flowed from the halakhic discussion.
R. Dr. A. Yehuda Warburg has assumed a vital role in implementing this vision of a “double level” of truth in the Beth Din of America and on other panels. In the present work, <i>Rabbinic Authority: The Vision and the Reality</i>, R. Warburg presents a number of his judicial rulings. What stands out in these decisions is the halakhic framework, legal perspective, and “reasoned opinions” R. Warburg sets up to support his decisions.
For a number of decades, I have been involved on an ad hoc basis as a <i>dayan</i>, including serving on judicial panels for the Beth Din of America<i>.</i> In this capacity, I have often crossed paths with R. Warburg and served together with him on the same judicial panel. In each panel we served on together, R. Warburg’s outstanding Torah scholarship was always in evidence, scholarship motivated by a perfectionist’s drive to achieve new vistas in advancing the “double dimension” of truth that stands as the ideal for the halakhic-judicial process.
R. Dr. Aaron Levine, <i>z”l</i>
19 Adar 1, 5771
February 23, 2011

Preface

The present volume is intended as an introduction to a subject unfamiliar to many – rabbinic authority as reflected in our halakhic sources. Its subtitle – “The Vision and the Reality” – indicates the two perspectives from which we will approach this topic.
In discussing the halakhic “vision” of rabbinic authority, we will address a number of questions. How does Halakhah (Jewish law) envision rabbinic authority? What are the credentials for being a rabbinic authority? What is the difference between a rabbi who is “an authority” and one who is “in-authority”? What is the status of a congregational rabbi? How does a rabbi arrive at a halakhic decision? Is a rabbi bound to follow the opinions of his predecessors, or may he choose to render his own independent opinion? Under what conditions can he choose to issue his own judgment?
The author of <i>Sefer Ha-Arukh</i>, the first halakhic lexicon, defines Halakhah concisely:
<span class="poetry indentAll"><small>Something that travels from the beginning of time until the end, or the path that the Jewish community walks.</small></span>
Halakhah embodies the traditions transmitted throughout the generations; it is the path that the Jewish People have chosen to follow. But how wide is that path?
John Stuart Mill, the father of modern liberalism, famously claimed:
<span class="poetry indentAll"><small>The only purpose for which power can be exercised over any member of a civilized community, against his will, is to prevent harm to others.<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote"><i>On Liberty</i>, (NY: 1956) chap. 1, para. 9.</i></small></span>
Does Halakhah endorse such a perspective, which would lead “socially harmless” matters to the individual conscience, even when it comes to business affairs?
This question leads us to many others. Are directors and employees in the corporate world halakhically and/or legally accountable for all their decisions? Should a halakhic arbiter or a civil court defer to a decision rendered by an employee or director, or should we scrutinize the propriety of their decision? To answer these questions, we will examine how two different legal systems – Halakhah and American law – approach the application of the business judgment rule in the profit and not-for-profit corporate world.
The final component of my discussion of the “vision” of rabbinic authority is an attempt to solve a particularly vexing problem faced by the contemporary Jewish court in some cases of divorce – the problem of one spouse refusing to execute a <i>get</i>, a bill of Jewish divorce. Coercing a recalcitrant spouse to grant a <i>get</i> results in a <i>get me’usah</i>, a forced <i>get</i>, which is invalid. In this volume, I present a viable halakhic solution to this problem in an expanded version of an article that originally appeared in the <i>Jewish Law Annual</i> (vol. 18), entitled, “Recovery for Infliction of Emotional Distress: Toward Relief for the <i>Agunah</i>.” Our contribution is an attempt to invoke the halakhot of <i>nezikin</i> (monetary damages for injury caused) to reduce the problem of “chained spouses” in our community.
The “reality” of rabbinic authority presented in this volume deals with one type of that authority – the Jewish court, or <i>beit din</i>. As members of our covenant-faith community, we are obligated to resolve our differences in a <i>beit din</i> rather than resorting to litigation in civil court.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Midrash Tanhuma</i>, <i>Mishpatim</i>, <i>piska</i> 6; <i>Gittin</i> 88b; <i>SA</i>, HM 26:1–3.</i> The parties who appear before a <i>beit din</i> sign a <i>shtar borerut</i> (arbitration agreement) that empowers the panel of <i>dayanim</i> to resolve the matter in contention according to the norms of Halakhah.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Rema</i>, <i>Hoshen Mishpat</i> (HM) 12:7; <i>Sma</i>, HM 12:18.</i> Assuming the <i>beit din</i> process and its decision comply with the rules of secular arbitration law, the judgment will be enforceable in a competent civil jurisdiction in the United States.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">See Uniform Arbitration Act, sec. 1. A <i>dayan</i> is responsible not only to render a judgment in accordance with Halakhah, but equally to ensure that the decision will be enforceable in civil court; see <i>Hiddushei ha-Ritva</i>, <i>Mo’ed Katan</i> 14b; <i>Teshuvot ha-Rashba</i> 1:18; <i>Perush ha-Gra</i>, <i>Mishlei</i> 31:9; <i>Teshuvot Hatam Sofer</i>, HM 177.</i>
Interestingly, as central as the <i>beit din</i> system is in vision and reality, it is an institution whose workings and contributions to the “path of Halakhah” are largely unknown to most segments of the Jewish community. One of the primary reasons for unfamiliarity with the <i>beit din</i> can be traced back to a Mishnaic ruling:
<span class="poetry indentAll"><small>Upon finishing their deliberations, they would bring the litigants back to the courtroom, and the senior member of the panel would state: Mr. X, you are acquitted, and Mr. X, you are liable.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sanhedrin</i> 3:7.</i></small></span>
A review of relevant passages in the Talmud and rulings throughout the ages and until today demonstrates that Jewish courts generally took this Mishnaic ruling to heart, limiting their decisions to identifying the innocent and responsible parties, but not offering much in the way of reasoning.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Bava Metzia</i> 84b; <i>Shavuot</i> 30b; <i>Sanhedrin</i> 6b; <i>Shulhan Aruch</i> (SA), HM 19:1.</i> However, prior to a <i>beit din</i>’s <i>psak din</i> (final decision), a litigant may request that the panel provide a reasoned opinion if he is concerned that one or more of the judges are ignorant of a relevant <i>halakhah</i> or that they are biased in some way.<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Tur</i>, HM 14; <i>SA</i> and <i>Rema</i>, HM 14:1, 4; <i>Sma</i>, HM 14:25; <i>SA</i>, HM 12:2; <i>Teshuvot Sha’ar Yehoshua</i> 1–2. Cf. <i>Teshuvot Hatam Sofer</i>, HM 12.</i> According to some authorities, this is true not only of a standing <i>beit din</i> that requires litigants to appear before them regarding a contentious matter, but also when the <i>beit din</i> is an ad hoc panel, known as <i>zabla</i> (“<i>zeh borer lo ehad</i>” – “one chooses one”)<i>.</i><sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">In the absence of a standing <i>beit din</i> or when one or more of the litigants do not want to appear in front of a standing <i>beit din</i>, each party may choose one <i>dayan</i>, and the two <i>dayanim</i> (arbitrators) choose a third; see <i>SA</i>, HM 13:1–2.</i>
<i>Maharah Or Zarua</i> and <i>Havot Ya’ir</i> are of the opinion that each and every <i>dayan</i> should provide the grounds for his decision.<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Maharah Or Zarua</i> 13; <i>Teshuvot Havot Ya’ir</i> 165.</i> In our day especially, many litigants question a <i>dayan</i>’s credibility, halakhic expertise, and/or business acumen; therefore, if at all possible, a reasoned opinion should be forthcoming.<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">R. Tzvi Yehuda ben Ya’akov, “The Obligation of Giving a Reasoned <i>Psak Din</i>” [Hebrew], 19 <i>Tehumin</i> (5759), 223, 234.</i>
Due to the fact that halakhic court procedure does not mandate the issuance of a reasoned opinion, however, contemporary <i>dayanim</i> have generally refrained from submitting reasoned judgments. Nevertheless, certain <i>dayanim</i> who sit on the Beth Din of America in New York City and others who serve in the Israeli <i>beit din</i> networks under the aegis of Israel’s Chief Rabbinate and Eretz Hemdah-Gazit have done so.
During the last thirteen years, I have served as a <i>dayan</i> on the Beth Din of America and appeared on various <i>zabla</i> panels as well as a single arbitrator for the Hassidic, Modern Orthodox, Sephardic, and <i>yeshiva</i> communities in the New York-New Jersey metropolitan area. In numerous instances, I have rendered reasoned opinions in commercial matters and domestic relations. Among other matters, these decisions touch on issues of employment termination and severance awards, partnership dissolution, the validity of <i>minhag ha-soherim</i> (commercial practice), civil law, contractual agreements and preliminary agreements, the interpretation of contracts, the principle of indemnity in insurance law, recovery of economic loss of funds, consequential damages, lease, construction, and loan agreements, self-dealing in non-profit organizations, and copyright infringement. In addressing family matters, I have dealt with the grounds for issuing a divorce judgment, dividing up marital assets upon divorce, spousal support, child support and placement, filial obligations to one’s parents, and <i>yerushah</i> (inheritance) issues, such as guidelines for drafting a halakhic will and the validity of a civil will and a trust agreement.
The cases chosen for this volume cover the range of subjects characteristic of all modern legal systems – <i>dinei mamonot</i> (civil matters), public and administrative law, family law, and philosophy of law. Given that Halakhah is a religious legal system, the impact of <i>issur ve-heter</i> (ritual law) upon the monetary issues under investigation is equally dealt with in our judgments.
In this volume, I have included ten presentations inspired by reasoned opinions handed down as a member of a <i>beit din</i> panel. Two of these, dealing with drafting a halakhic will and labor relations, are expanded versions of my decisions that have recently appeared in the journal <i>Hakirah</i> (vols. 10 and 12). In each presentation, I offer a rendition of the facts of the case, followed by the claims of the <i>Tove’a</i> (plaintiff), the reply of the <i>Nitva</i> (defendant), and any counterclaims. Subsequently, there is a discussion of the halakhic issues emerging from the parties’ respective claims and counterclaims, followed by a decision rendered by the <i>beit din</i> panel. To preserve the confidentiality of the parties involved in these cases, all names have been changed, and some facts have been changed and/or deleted.
It is my hope that this collection will educate our community to the parameters and scope of rabbinic authority in general and the institution of the <i>beit din</i> in particular.
My appreciation to the publisher of Urim Publications, Tzvi Mauer. I offer my best wishes that the passion and sense of mission he brings to his calling to disseminate Torah scholarship will continue to be crowned with great success.
My thanks is given to Michal Alatin and Batsheva Pomerantz of Urim Publications for their expertise and technical skill in preparing my book for publication.
A. Yehuda (Ronnie) Warburg
20 Tevet 5773
January 2, 2013

Part I; Rabbinic Authority; The Vision

Chapter 1; Towards Defining the Concept of Rabbinic Authority; A Contemporary Analysis

<big><strong>Towards Defining the Concept of Rabbinic Authority: A Contemporary Analysis</strong></big>
Mary Ann Glendon, a law professor at Harvard University and an astute observer of the interaction of law and social mores, observes:
<span class="poetry indentAll"><small>The American rights dialect is distinguished by not only what we say and how we say it, but also what we leave unsaid. Each day’s newspapers, radio broadcasts and television programs attest to our tendency to speak of whatever is most important to us in terms of rights, and to our predilection for overstating the absoluteness of the rights we claim. Our habitual silences concerning responsibilities are more apt to remain unnoticed.<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">Mary Ann Glendon, <i>Rights Talk: The Impoverishment of Political Discourse</i> (NY: 1991), 76.</i></small></span>
Whereas American society places an emphasis on rugged individualism and individual rights,<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">See Ronald Dworkin, <i>Taking Rights Seriously</i> (Boston: 1977); Joel Feinberg, <i>Harm to Others: The Moral Limits of the Criminal Law</i> (NY: 1984); Will Kymlicka, <i>Liberalism, Community and Culture</i> (NY: 1989). America’s right-based system traces its ideological origins to the classical liberal utilitarian morality of John Stuart Mill and the possessive individualism of Thomas Hobbes. See Alexis de Tocqueville, <i>Democracy in America</i> (NY: 1945), vol. 1, 254; Mill, <i>On Liberty</i> (NY: 1956), 99–100; Mill, <i>Utilitarianism</i> (NY: 1910), 55; C. B. Macpherson, <i>The Political Theory of Possessive Individualism: Hobbes to Locke</i> (England: 1962), Part II; and H. Marks Roelof, <i>Ideology and Myth in American Politics</i> (Boston: 1976), 51, 56–60, 67–69.</i> from a halakhic perspective, the mission of man – and equally of the Jew – focuses upon duty, compliance with a divine norm.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">For man in general, the mission centers around the fulfillment of seven “Noahide” commandments, while for the Jew there is an obligation to comply with 613 commandments. For further discussion regarding the halakhic system as a duty-based system, see my “May One Destroy a Neighbor’s Property In Order to Save One’s Life: A Case Study in a Conflict of Duties and Its Contemporary Implications for the Incorporation of Jewish Law into the Israeli Legal System,” in Michael Shmidman (ed.), <i>Turim: Studies in Jewish History and Literature Presented to Dr. Bernard Lander</i> (NY: 2007), 331.</i> Thus, <i>R. Dr. Joseph Soloveitchik</i> writes:
<span class="poetry indentAll"><small>Natural man, moving straight forwards, comes suddenly to a stop, turns around, and casts as an outsider, a contemplative gaze upon his environment … He discovers an awesome and mysterious domain of things and events which is independent of and disobedient to him, an objective order limiting the exercise of his power … In the wake of this discovery, he discovers himself. Once self-discovery is accomplished, and a … I-awareness of an existence which is limited and opposed by a non-I outside emerges, something new is born – namely the divine norm … “And the Lord God commanded the man” … Man attains his unique identity when, after having been enlightened by God that he is not only a committed but also a free person, endowed with power to implement his commitment, he grasps the incommensurability of what he is and what he is destined to be.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">Joseph B. Soloveitchik, “Confrontation,” 6 <i>Tradition</i> (1964), 5, 11.</i></small></span>
Thus, in contrast to the habitual silence in American society regarding legislating morality except to avoid the infliction of harm, a committed Jew must develop a more expansive notion of obligation vis-à-vis God and his fellow-man based upon fulfillment of divine commandments.
Furthermore, being a liberal-democratic society, legal authority in American life has been seen as appropriate for the public square, but limited in the private realm. An American inquires of a lawyer about his rights and their infringement. A committed Jew, by contrast, must seek to understand his obligations, which extend well beyond “negative rights,” his right of protection against individual and governmental intrusion.
How does one discern one’s halakhic obligations? In the words of the Torah itself, “What does the Lord thy God ask of you?”<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Devarim</i> 10:12.</i> Most committed Jews, even well-educated ones, will not be able to answer this question on their own. Rather, we are told, “Establish a rabbi for yourself and avoid doubt.”<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Avot</i> 1:15 and <i>Rambam</i> ad loc. Cf. <i>Avot</i> 1:6 and <i>Rambam</i> ad loc. The latter <i>mishnah</i> addresses the need to choose a rabbi for the purposes of study, rather than as a spiritual guide.</i> Who is the rabbi in question, and what are the parameters of his authority? In this essay, we will focus on the varying approaches in which the halakhic tradition, in particular post-Talmudic sources, has grappled with these questions.
This essay seeks to map and depict the main functions and normative meanings that encompass the concept of rabbinic authority. Other than a handful of dogmatic and historical descriptions, there have been very few attempts to focus upon the post-Talmudic sources that address this topic.<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">For the most comprehensive and systematic compilation of rabbinic sources regarding our topic, see Hanina Ben-Menahem, Neil Hecht, and Shai Wosner (eds.), <i>Controversy and Dialogue in the Halakhic Sources</i> [Hebrew] (Boston and Jerusalem: 1991,1993), vol. 1, 243–354, 401–58; vol. 2, 753–954. For our study, this compilation has been mined assiduously.<br>Although the scope of authority encompasses Halakhah, <i>minhag</i>, and matters of faith and belief (<i>emunot ve-de’ot</i>), our study will focus solely on Halakhah. Moreover, this is a phenomenological sketch based upon post-Talmudic sources that does not purport to illustrate a particular codifier’s or respondent’s methodology. Finally, certain subjects, such as the option of an individual to choose between conflicting views, the right of an authority to resolve matters for himself and his family or to legislate, act stricter in private than his public posture, and practice his own rejected approach, are beyond the scope of this essay.</i> Although some of the sources that will be analyzed have historical implications, we are concerned in developing a conceptual framework based upon the sources that have been assigned normative value by the halakhic system.
<b>1. The Parameters of “<i>Lo Tasur</i>”</b>
The Torah states that a member of the Jewish community who has a doubt regarding a point of Halakhah is obligated to seek halakhic counsel and abide by the ruling of the court:
<span class="poetry indentAll"><small>If there is a matter too hard from you in judgment, between blood and blood, between ruling and ruling … subjects of controversy within your gates; then you shall arise and go up to the place that God will choose. And you shall come to the … judge who shall be in those days, and inquire, and they shall render the words of the judgment. And you shall follow according to the sentence which they declare to you from that place which God shall choose and you shall observe to do according to what they instruct you. According to the law which they shall instruct you and according to the judgment which they shall tell you, shall you do. You shall not deviate (<i>lo tasur</i>) from the word they shall tell you, neither to the right nor to the left.<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Devarim</i> 17:8–13. This translation is culled (with certain modifications) from Aharon Cohen, “The Parameters of Rabbinic Authority: A Study of Three Sources,” 27 <i>Tradition</i> (Summer, 1993), 100–1.</i></small></span>
The Talmud explains that the “place that God will choose” refers to the Great Sanhedrin, which met in the <i>lishkat ha-gazit</i> on the Temple Mount in Jerusalem.<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Tosefta Hagigah</i> 2:9; <i>Sanhedrin</i> 88a.</i> Accordingly, the commandment of “<i>lo tasur</i>,” “you shall not deviate from the word they shall tell you,” is limited to the judgments and interpretations of Halakhah as rendered by the <i>Sanhedrin ha-Gadol</i>. Many decisors – among them <i>Rambam</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sefer ha-Mitzvot</i>, positive mitzvah 174, negative mitzvah 312; <i>Mishnah Torah</i>, <i>Hilkhot Mamrim</i> 1:1. However, according to <i>Rambam</i> and others, in situations where there are <i>batei din</i> or rabbinic assemblies, there is an obligation to follow their respective rulings based on the principle of “follow the majority.” See his Introduction to <i>Mishnah Torah</i>; <i>Teshuvot Hikrei Lev</i>, YD 84 (p. 126, column 3).</i> <i>Ramban</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ramban,</i> Commentary on the Torah, <i>Devarim</i> 17:11.</i> <i>Rivash</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot ha-Rivash</i> 261.</i> <i>R. Yehuda ha-Levi</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Kuzari</i> 3:41.</i> and <i>Ran</i><sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Derashot ha-Ran</i>, <i>derush</i> 12.</i> – thus conclude that the prohibition does not apply to decisions handed down by lower courts or by independent rabbinic authorities.<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">It is possible that these <i>Rishonim</i> would argue that compliance is mandated due to the general mitzvah of “<i>li-shmo’a li-divrei hakhamim</i>.” See <i>Yevamot</i> 20a; Talmud Yerushalmi <i>Sukkah</i> 3:4; <i>Sha’arei Yosher</i>, <i>sha’ar</i> 1, <i>perek</i> 7; <i>Kiryat Sefer</i>, <i>Hilkhot Mamrim</i> 1. Consequently, compliance to our arbiters includes abiding by <i>mitzvot</i>, <i>takanoth</i> (rabbinic legislation), <i>gezerot</i> (rabbinic decrees), and <i>minhagim</i> (customs). See <i>Rambam</i>, supra n. 10; <i>Sefer ha-Hinukh</i>, mitzvah 496; <i>Hiddushei ha-Ran, Sanhedrin</i> 89a; <i>Kuntres Divrei Soferim</i> 1:32 in the name of Rabbi Chaim Soloveitchik; <i>R. Ya’akov Anatoly</i>, <i>Melamed ha-Talmidim</i>, <i>Mishpatim</i> (Mekizei Nirdamim edition), 71b–72b.<br>Cf. <i>Peri Megadim</i>, Pesicha Ko’lelet, helek aleph (40); R. Yosef Engel, <i>Tzi’yunim le-Torah</i>, Kelal 10 who contend that “<i>lo tasur</i>” is applicable to <i>gezerot</i>, rabbinic legislation which extends or adds prohibitions beyond the preexisting Halakhah.</i>
The <i>Sefer ha-Hinukh</i>, however, suggests otherwise:
<span class="poetry indentAll"><small>As for the obligation given to us to obey the words of our ancient Sages, our great authorities in the wisdom of the Torah, and our judges in our generation, it is in effect everywhere, at every time.<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sefer ha-Hinukh</i>, mitzvot 495–496. R. Eliyahu Dessler claims that antecedents to this approach can be found in <i>Sifrei</i>, <i>Devarim</i> 17:154 (Finkelstein edition) and <i>Rashi</i>, <i>Devarim</i> 17:11. See his <i>Mikhtav me-Eliyahu</i> 1:75–77.</i></small></span>
Similarly, <i>Rashba</i> writes:
<span class="poetry indentAll"><small>We must listen to one of the <i>Rishonim</i> [early rabbinic authorities], provided that he is a scholar that one can rely upon … We must listen to the scholars of every era, as it is written: “And to the judge that will be in those days,” even if they tell you that right is left …<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot ha-Rashba</i> 2:322. For an antecedent to this approach, see Yerushalmi <i>Horayot</i> 1:1. Cf. the variant version, “even when it appears to you that they are saying that right is left and left is right, you must obey them;” <i>Sifrei</i>, <i>Devarim</i>, <i>Shofetim</i>, sec. 154; <i>Midrash Tannaim</i>, 102–103; <i>Yalkut Shimoni</i>, <i>Shofetim</i> 911. See also <i>Teshuvot ha-Rashba</i> 5:246; <i>Teshuvot Tashbetz</i> 2:68; <i>Teshuvot Heikhal Yitzhak</i>, EH 1:1, 5; <i>Teshuvot Yaskil Avdi</i> 6:96.</i></small></span>
According to <i>Sefer ha-Hinukh</i> and <i>Rashba</i>, the positive commandment of “adhering to what they teach and rule” and the prohibition of “<i>lo tasur</i>” apply to all rabbinic leaders in every generation, not only to the <i>Sanhedrin ha-Gadol</i>.
<i>Ran</i> concurs with the opinion that there is an obligation to adhere to the halakhic decisions of the arbiter in every community and in every generation, but he invokes a different source for the requirement:
<span class="poetry indentAll"><small>How do we know that we are obligated to comply with the decisions of the scholars in every generation? The answer is that the Biblical verse states, “Follow the majority.” This is a general warning to follow Torah judgments [based upon the consensus of the majority of scholars].<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Derashot ha-Ran</i>, supra n. 14.</i></small></span>
The common denominator of both approaches is the requirement for an individual to choose a rabbinic authority and adhere to his rulings. While some authorities maintain that there is an obligation that mandates compliance with decisions of a rabbinic authority in every generation, others maintain that there is no religious duty to comply with a decisor’s ruling unless he was a member of the <i>Sanhedrin ha-Gadol</i>. According to the latter view, once an authority has been accepted, his decisions become binding, but a violation thereof does not entail contravention of a biblical imperative.
Translating this controversy into the conceptual scheme of authority elucidated by Richard T. De George is quite instructive.<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">R. Dr. Aharon Lichtenstein argues cogently that there is merit to such comparison: “The structure and substance of law … fall within the purview of general as well as Torah thought. Knowledge of how such questions, legal and/or philosophic, have been treated in different traditions can frequently enhance our understanding of Torah positions, as regards either broad outlines or specific detail.” See his “Torah and General Culture: Confluence and Conflict,” in J. J. Schacter (ed.), <i>Judaism and Encounters with Other Cultures</i> (NY: 1997), 220, 230.</i> George distinguishes between two types of authority – imperative/executive and epistemic. Whereas an imperative authority has the right or power to act and expect compliance to his orders, an epistemic authority is an authority in a field of knowledge, but he does not possess any right to command:
<span class="poetry indentAll"><small>A person is an epistemic authority if he is considered an authority by another or by others with respect to some field or area of knowledge. Thus, X is a … epistemic authority if there is some Y who considers X an authority for Y in some realm … For Y to consider X a … epistemic authority for Y means that at least under certain conditions and at least to some extent, Y is willing to believe what X says in R …<br>Imperative authority involves the right or the power of some bearer … to command someone who is subject to authority to act or to forbear from acting in certain ways.<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">Richard T. De George, <i>The Nature and Limits of Authority</i> (Kansas: 1985), 27, 63.</i></small></span>
Essentially, an imperative authority is “in-authority,” while an epistemic authority is simply “an-authority.” The halakhic positions regarding the nature of rabbinic authority can be described accordingly.
The view that limits the purview of “<i>lo tasur</i>” to the judgments of the <i>Sanhedrin ha-Gadol</i> views all other rabbinic authorities as examples of epistemic authority. It is only upon the questioner’s acceptance of the authority’s spiritual hegemony that his status as “an-authority” is transformed into one of “in-authority.”<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">R. S. Peters, “<i>Authority,</i>” 32 <i>Proceedings of the Aristotelian Society</i>, Supplementary, 86–89.</i> Thus, <i>R. Moshe Soloveitchik</i> maintains that a decisor who does not have the status of being ordained (<i>semikhah</i>) is
<span class="poetry indentAll"><small>essentially a reference guide, providing reliable information about what the tradition and its sources, properly understood and interpreted, state; but it is they, rather than he, that bind authoritatively.<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">Aharon Lichtenstein, <i>Leaves of Faith: The World of Jewish Learning (</i>NY: 2004), 293.</i></small></span>
Those who either extend the purview of “<i>lo tasur</i>” or invoke other obligations to accept rabbinic decisions throughout the generations view these rulings as an exercise of imperative authority. Upon acceptance of their authority, their rulings become binding upon the inquirer.<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">Accordingly, abiding by the rulings of the late R. Elyashiv, <i>z”l</i>, accepted by many in the Ashkenazic community as the greatest Torah scholar of the 21st century, can be based upon varying rationales. Either one is bound to his rulings as one who is “in-authority” due to “<i>lo tasur</i>” or due to his status as being “one’s rabbi,” even though one has not formally learnt from him. See <i>Sefer ha-Hinukh</i>, supra n. 16; <i>Tosafot</i>, <i>Berakhot</i> 31b, s.v. <i>moreh</i>; <i>Teshuvot Terumat ha-Deshen</i> 238. Alternatively, his decisions are followed based upon one’s formal acceptance of his rulings, similar to a <i>mara de-atra</i> and no different from any other rabbinic authority or his rulings must be complied with by the entire Jewish community, no different than following the rulings of the <i>Tanna’im</i> and <i>Amora’im</i>. See <i>Kuntres Divrei Soferim</i> 2:4; A. Sherman, “The Authority of <i>Gedolei ha-Dor</i> in Matters of Personal Status and Conversion” [Hebrew], 30 <i>Tehumin</i> (5770), 163. S. Daichovsky, “<i>Da’at Torah</i>” [Hebrew], 30 <i>Tehumin</i> (5770),174, 182; Avraham Schindler, “Rabbinical Authority to Change Synagogue Practice to be More Stringent” [Hebrew], 12 <i>Shurat ha-Din</i> (5767),119, 125; Shlomo Shapiro, “Rabbinical Authority to Change Synagogue Practice” [Hebrew], ibid.,152; Ya’akov Epstein, “The Authority of the Scholars of the Generation via-a-vis the Community” [Hebrew], 31<i>Tehumin</i> (5771), 206.<br>R. Z. Goldberg argues that after the appearance of <i>Shulhan Arukh</i> and <i>Rema</i>, who are our <i>mara de-atra</i>s, there is no decisor who wields authority over the community unless he is one’s personal or community rabbi. Hence, there is no duty to comply with contemporary authority’s rulings unless the decisions are grounded in <i>Shulhan Arukh</i> and/or <i>Rema</i>. See Z. Goldberg, <i>Darkhei ha-Psak</i> [Hebrew] (Givat Shmuel: 5764), 34. Cf. <i>Hazon Ish</i>, YD 150:5.</i>
<b>2. The <i>Mara De-Atra</i></b>
<i>Rambam</i> describes the extent of rabbinic authority in the following fashion:
<span class="poetry indentAll"><small>If a court established in any country after the time of the Talmud made decrees and ordinances or introduced customs for those residing in its particular country or for residents of other countries, its enactments did not gain the acceptance of all Israel because of the remoteness of the Jewish settlements and the difficulties of travel. And as the court of any particular country consisted of individuals [whose authority was not universally recognized], while the Supreme Court of seventy-one members had, several years before the compilation of the Talmud, ceased to exist, no compulsion is exercised on those living in one country to observe the customs of another country; nor is any court directed to issue a decree that has been issued by another court in the same country. So too, if one of the <i>Ge’onim</i> taught that a certain way of judgment was correct, and it became clear to a court at a later date that this was not in accordance with the view of the Gemara, the earlier authority is not necessarily followed, but the view is adopted which seems more reasonable, whether it be that of an earlier or of a later authority. The foregoing observations refer to rules, decrees, ordinances, and customs that originated after the Talmud had been compiled. But whatever is already mentioned in the Babylonian Talmud is binding on all Israel.<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Mishnah Torah</i>, Introduction. Translation culled (with certain modifications) from Isadore Twersky, <i>Introduction to the Code of Maimonides (Mishnah Torah)</i> (New Haven: 1980), 128–9.</i></small></span>
Although <i>Rambam</i> writes specifically about the judicial independence of the Jewish court, <i>R. Joseph Karo</i> notes that his conclusions equally apply to all authorities who are interpreters and arbiters of Halakhah – both the courts and independent arbiters have accepted to be subservient to the rulings found in the Mishnah and Talmud.<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Kesef Mishnah</i>, <i>Hilkhot Mamrim</i> 2:1. Pursuant to the <i>Kesef Mishnah</i>’s logic, had scholars refrained from making such a determination, the Talmudic and post-Talmudic sages could have overruled their predecessors. See Z. H. Chajes, <i>Kol Sifrei Maharatz Hayut</i> (Jerusalem: 5718), 109. Whereas <i>Rambam</i> maintains that the authority of the Talmud is due to the assent of the Jewish community and that it was part and parcel of an unbroken tradition dating back to Moshe, R. Chajes and R. Elhanan Wasserman argue that the finality is attributed to a decision by all or the majority of scholars, whose status is akin to the <i>Sanhedrin ha-Gadol</i>. Others, such as <i>Hazon Ish</i>, contend that the authority of the Talmud is a result of “the decline of the generations” (see text below). Rejecting this historically contingent explanation, R. Hayyim Soloveitchik contends that there is actually no prohibition for Talmudic and post-Talmudic scholars to overrule their predecessors, but they refrain from doing so in practice. For an overview of these approaches and others, see Shlomo Havlin, “On ‘Literary Sealing’ as the Foundation for the Division of Halakhah into Epochs” [Hebrew], in <i>Mehkarim be-Safrut ha-Talmudit</i> (Israel: 1983), 148, 169–83.<br>Acceptance of the Talmud’s rulings was not limited to courts and arbiters, but equally affirmed by the Jewish community. See <i>Rambam</i>, supra n. 24; <i>Kesef Mishnah</i>, <i>Hilkhot Mamrim</i> 2:1; <i>Teshuvot Maharam Schick</i>, <i>Yoreh Deah</i> (YD) 115:3; <i>Derashot ha-Ran</i>, <i>derush</i> 12.</i>
The authority of the Talmud Bavli stems from its acceptance by the Jewish community, as its Sages comprised the total or the majority of the scholars of Israel, and the chain of tradition from them to Moses was never broken. <i>Rambam</i> is quick to note that embarking upon “the sea of the Talmud” and delving into its difficult formulations requires education and skills. Consequently, it is no wonder that after its compilation, divergent interpretations of this corpus were to be found among the <i>dayanim</i> and decisors throughout the various communities.
Decentralization of the Jewish community coupled with an acute need to resolve halakhic indeterminacies were factors in the emergence and proliferation of rabbinic authorities who made decisions for particular Jewish communities. Such rabbinic personalities were referred to as the “<i>mara de-atra</i>,” literally, the “master of the place.” Upon acceptance of his authority, his rulings are authoritatively binding upon the members of his community. Briefly sketching this role, Professor Aharon Kirschenbaum observes:
<span class="poetry indentAll"><small>Traces of such rabbinic authority … for the inhabitants of a limited geographic area may be found among the <i>Tanna’im</i>. Thus, although his colleagues limited the suspension of Sabbath restrictions to the circumcision itself, “in the place of R. Eliezer they used to cut wood [on the Sabbath] to make charcoal in order to forge an iron instrument.” R. Eliezer held that all necessary requisites for the circumcision … superseded the Sabbath prohibition. Hence, even the charcoal necessary for the forging of the circumcision knife was permitted to be prepared. Although R. Eliezer was overruled by the other <i>Tanna’im</i>, his position was accepted as valid “in his place.”<br>Similarly, R. Jose the Galilean’s opinion that the cooking of the flesh of fowl in milk was totally permitted was rejected by his colleagues … Nevertheless, the Talmud states that, “in the place of R. Jose the Galilean, they used to eat fowl’s flesh cooked in milk” …<br>On the basis of these Tannaitic precedents, the <i>Amora’im</i> ruled that the opinion of the <i>mara de-atra</i> was binding on the <i>atra</i> even where it was in opposition to the normative Halakhah. For example, there is a well-known dispute in the matter of <i>muktzah</i>, wherein R. Simeon rejected the rules of <i>muktzah</i> as we know them. Although R. Hamnuna agreed with R. Simeon, he excommunicated a certain disciple who gave a practical decision in accordance with R. Simeon’s position. He did so because the disciple had made that decision in an area that was within the jurisdiction of Rav. Since Rav opposed R. Simeon’s position, the disciple should have acted accordingly. Thus, the Talmud defends the excommunication declared by R. Hamnuna even though the latter agreed with the substance of the disciple’s decision. The respect due Rav, the <i>mara de-atra</i>, was overriding. <sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">“<i>Mara De-Atra:</i> A Brief Sketch,” 27 <i>Tradition</i> (Summer 1993), 35–36.</i></small></span>
Even if a <i>mara de-atra</i> follows a minority view – even if he permits something that according to others is biblically prohibited – his ruling must be followed by the community.<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Shabbat</i> 130b; <i>Teshuvot ha-Rashba</i> 1:253; <i>Teshuvot ha</i>-<i>Ran</i> 48; <i>Teshuvot Ginat Veradim</i>, EH, <i>kelal</i> 2:9.</i>
At first glance, this seems to conflict with the most basic principle of halakhic decision-making, “<i>aharei rabbim le-hatot</i>,” the rule to follow the majority.<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sanhedrin</i> 3:6; <i>Rashi</i>, <i>Sanhedrin</i> 29a.</i> However, as various decisors note, the application of that rule is limited to resolving issues within the confines of a <i>moshav beit din</i>, judicial proceedings.<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Get Pashut</i>, <i>Kelalim</i>, <i>kelal</i> 1, 5; <i>Teshuvot Maharlbah</i> 147; <i>Beit Yosef</i>, HM 13 (end) in the name of <i>Rashba</i>; <i>Teshuvot Hikrei Lev</i>, OH 95, YD 82; <i>Teshuvot Torat Emet</i> 207.</i> Since a <i>mara de-atra</i>’s ruling is a resolution of a controversy among decisors, outside of a <i>moshav beit din</i>, the command to follow the majority view is inapplicable, and his position, although reflecting a minority view, becomes binding upon the members of the community, whether it reflects a stringent or lenient opinion.<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Be’er Eliyahu</i>, HM 25:22; <i>Kuntres Divrei Soferim</i> 5:18; <i>Teshuvot Yabia Omer</i>, EH 3:19; <i>Hazon Ish</i>, YD 150:1; <i>Teshuvot Heikhal Yitzhak</i>, EH 1:17(4). The <i>mara de-atra</i>’s ruling is binding even though the matter would have been resolved according to the majority opinion had the <i>mara de-atra</i> sat in a <i>moshav beit din</i>, and his community is obligated to comply with his view even though it is a minority opinion. Moreover, his ruling must be followed regardless of whether there exists a more stringent or lenient opinion. See <i>Teshuvot Maharashdam</i>, EH 15; <i>Teshuvot Ginat Veradim</i>, OH 3; <i>Pri Hadash</i>, OH 496, <i>Minhagei Issur</i> 11; <i>Teshuvot Ein Mishpat</i>, YD 9; <i>Hazon Ish</i>, YD 150:1. However, if the community accepted as their <i>mara de-atra</i> someone who is no longer alive (see text below), a scholar who advocates a stricter position may be followed. See <i>Teshuvot ha-Rashba</i> 1:253; <i>Bah</i>, HM 25; <i>Teshuvot Semikhah le-Hayyim</i>, YD 5. Although a community must follow a <i>mara de-atra</i>’s teachings in ritual matters, in monetary matters that are subject to arbitration, one may deviate from the <i>mara de-atra</i>’s rulings. See <i>Teshuvot Semikhah le-Hayyim</i>, EH 9; <i>Teshuvot Hikikei Lev</i>, YD 1:43. Cf. <i>Teshuvot ha</i>-<i>Ridbaz</i> 1:405, 2:638.</i>
Did this model of rabbinic authority persist beyond the Talmudic period? <i>R. Joseph b. David ibn Lev</i> (<i>Maharival</i>), a sixteenth century <i>posek</i> (decisor) from Salonika, writes:
<span class="poetry indentAll"><small>It was only during those days [the Mishnaic and Talmudic periods], when in every city there was a rabbi who would instruct them … that in every city they were obligated to honor their teachers. However, in these times, in all these matters our decisors and our rabbinic scholars from whose waters we drink are our rabbis …<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Maharival</i> 1:73. According to <i>Mishpetei Shmuel</i> (Conforto), HM 2, <i>Maharival</i> allows for the appointment of a <i>mara de-atra</i> for his expertise solely in defining halakhic controversies, rather than issuing a leniency in biblical matters. See also <i>Teshuvot Mishpetei Shmuel</i> (Kali) 67; <i>Teshuvot Hemdah Genusah</i>, <i>Kuntres ha-Pesikah ha-Hilkhatit</i>, 47–51.</i></small></span>
Whereas the <i>mara de-atra</i> was once the binding authority in all matters of Halakhah, we are now privileged to have many rabbis, and we must thus refrain from adopting one binding authority. Should there be a difference of opinion amongst the rabbis, the typical decision-making rules, such as following the majority, must facilitate the resolution of the matter. A community is authorized to appoint a <i>mara de-atra</i> only to resolve monetary issues, regarding which one may make stipulations contrary to what is written in the Torah.<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ketuvot</i> 83b–84a; <i>Bava Metzia</i> 54a–b.</i>
<i>Maharival</i>’s position reflects a minority view, however; the role of the <i>mara de-atra</i> persisted well after the compilation of the Talmud.<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">Among those rejecting <i>Maharival</i>’s approach are <i>Pri Hadash</i>, OH 496:11, and <i>Hikrei Lev</i>, <i>Orah Hayim</i> (OH) 496:96. <i>Hikrei Lev</i> points out that regarding personal status matters (<i>ishut</i>), one cannot follow one decisor; <i>Pri Hadash</i> argues that only “some of the great scholars of the generation” can be empowered with such authority.</i> R. Dr. Yitzhak Z. Kahana lists numerous communities throughout medieval and modern Jewish history who chose to follow the rulings of various authorities, the <i>mara de-atra</i>s of these communities.<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">Yitzhak Z. Kahane, “The Polemics Regarding Determination of the Halakhah According to <i>Rambam</i>” [Hebrew], <i>Mehkarim be-Safrut ha-Teshuvot</i> (Jerusalem: 5733), 8–88.</i> As in Mishnaic and Talmudic times, communal acceptance was the grounds for legitimating the binding authority of the <i>mara de-atra</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Mahari Weil</i> 151; <i>Teshuvot ha-Ran</i> 49 and 62; <i>Teshuvot Tashbetz</i> 3:210; ibid., <i>Hut ha-Meshulash</i>, <i>ha-Tur ha-Shlishi</i>, 35; <i>Teshuvot ha-Rivash</i> 105; <i>Teshuvot ha-Ridbaz</i> 1:192, 547; <i>Teshuvot Maharam Alshakar</i> 16, 49; <i>Teshuvot Avkat Rokhel</i> 32; <i>Teshuvot Maharashdam</i>, YD 90; <i>Teshuvot ha-Radakh</i>, <i>bayit</i> 18, <i>hadarim</i> 8–13; <i>Teshuvot Hikekei Lev</i>, YD 43; <i>Teshuvot Hikrei Lev,</i> YD 49; <i>Teshuvot ha-Ranah</i> 109; <i>Teshuvot Perah Mateh Aharon</i> 2:81; <i>Teshuvot Divrei Malkiel</i> 4:82; <i>Teshuvot Avnei Nezer</i>, YD 312:37; <i>Piskei Din Rabbaniyim</i> (hereafter: <i>PDR</i>) 10:38; 11:97. In certain instances, other scholars were involved in his communal appointment. See <i>Teshuvot Mahari Bruna</i> 221. Cf. <i>Arukh ha-Shulhan</i>, YD 245:29, who argues that during the period of <i>Rivash</i>, <i>Tashbetz</i>, and others, rabbinic appointment was <i>de facto</i> – that is, by dint of his teaching, the scholar assumed the position.<br>Relying upon an inference from <i>Arukh ha-Shulhan</i>, YD 242:57, R. Aharon Lichtenstein points out that a community has no obligation to elect a <i>mara de-atra</i>. See Aharon Lichtenstein, <i>“</i>The Israel Chief Rabbinate: A Current Halakhic Perspective,” 26 <i>Tradition</i> (Summer 1992), 26–27, n.9.<br>For a general overview, see Menachem Elon, <i>Jewish Law</i> (Philadelphia: 1993), vol. 2, 678–779.</i>
Thus, a communal enactment evidently promulgated in fourteenth century Spain addressing a dispute between a husband and wife states:
<span class="poetry indentAll"><small>The community in which she lives … agreed and enacted that in all their laws and legal controversies, they will follow the books of <i>Rambam</i>, of blessed memory, in regard to everything that is written in them concerning matters of ritual law, monetary matters, marriage … and divorce.<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot ha-Ran</i> 48.</i></small></span>
The communal enactment obligated members of the community to follow <i>Rambam</i>’s rulings in certain designated areas of Halakhah. Being selective regarding which matters are under the purview of a <i>mara de-atra</i> is thus permissible.<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">In a teacher-disciple relationship, one may choose a rabbi to answer questions in certain designated area(s) of Halakhah; see <i>Shakh</i>, YD 242:12. Logically, this conclusion should equally apply to choosing a <i>mara de-atra</i>. Numerous authorities approved of this practice regarding a <i>mara de-atra</i>’s sphere of jurisdiction; see Kahane, supra n. 34, 18, 20, and 41.</i>
Five hundred years later, <i>R. Samson Raphael Hirsch</i> responded in uncompromising terms to a “guest rabbi” who directed R. Hirsch’s congregation to rebel against their <i>mara de-atra</i>’s teachings:
<span class="poetry indentAll"><small>The members of the Jewish community, who accepted a scholar as a rabbi and spiritual guide, have obligated themselves to heed his instructions regarding all communal matters dealing with the individual as well as regarding the community, in particular, given that this has been established in the communal enactments that he is the exclusive decisor to resolve communal matters …<br>But his honor, who is a foreigner to our city, did not refrain from interfering with my communal matters, having been appointed to be the rabbi and chief justice [<i>av beit din</i>], and he refused to abstain from persuading members of my community to rebel against my ruling, telling them that my ruling is not a ruling, and he did not refrain from publicizing his view in a newspaper …<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Shemesh Marpeh</i> 52.</i></small></span>
Clearly, for hundreds of years, various communities were guided by their respective <i>mara de-atra</i>s.
In effect, community consensus regarding the appointment of a <i>mara de-atra</i> resulted in a spatial division of authority. In many instances, there could be various communities in the same locale practicing divergent halakhic behaviors. At first glance, this diffusion of authority among two communities in the same town seems to violate the injunction of “<i>lo titgodedu</i>,” which the Sages interpreted to refer to a prohibition against creating factions in the same community.<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">Based on <i>Devarim</i> 14:1. The verse literally means, “You shall not gash yourselves.” The Talmud <i>Yerushalmi Pesachim</i> 4:1, interpreted it, “<i>lo ta’asu agudot agudot</i>,” “do not create divergent groups.”</i> It is possible that just as two different courts in the same locale may issue contradictory decisions resulting in divergent behaviors in the same place,<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ramban</i> and <i>Ran</i>, <i>Hullin</i> 43b; and <i>Ritva</i>, <i>Yevamot</i> 14a. However, according to <i>Shakh</i>, this is contingent upon the fact that the two <i>batei din</i> are well known; see <i>Shakh</i>, YD 242 (end); <i>Kitzur Issur ve-heter</i> 10. Second, if the two <i>batei din</i> are unequal in scholarly stature, the proscription applies; see <i>Shakh</i>, ibid., in the name of <i>Rashba</i> and <i>Sefer ha-Hinukh</i>. Cf. <i>Teshuvot Maharashdam</i>, YD 153. Moreover, the proscription applies to a community, rather than to an individual who deviates from the common practice. See <i>Hiddushei ha-Ritva</i>, <i>Yevamot</i> 13b, s.v. <i>ta shma</i>; <i>Tosafot Rid</i>, ad loc., s.v. <i>tenan hatam</i>.</i> two communities in the same town are entitled to abide by different <i>mara de-atra</i>s who may issue different rulings;<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Maharshdam</i>, ibid.; <i>Teshuvot Re’em</i> 53.</i> neither <i>mara de-atra</i> has jurisdiction over the adherents of the other.<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Maharashdam</i>, HM 12.</i> Alternatively, it is possible that the prohibition against factionalism refers to teaching others to follow one’s practices when one authority advocates one position and the other demurs. Since two communities living side by side are simply practicing rather than exhorting others to follow their behavior, the prohibition is inapplicable.<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Beit Yosef</i>, <i>Bedek ha-Bayit</i>, YD 242 in the name of R. Yeruham; <i>Teshuvot Avkat Rokhel</i> 32; <i>Teshuvot ha</i>-<i>Mabit</i> 21. Generally speaking, regarding enactments adopted by a community, each community within a town was viewed as a “<i>kahal</i>,” a halakhic community that was not promoting factionalism. Whether the prohibition against promoting factionalism applies to diverse practices, <i>minhagim</i>, of communities within a town is subject to much debate and beyond the scope of our study.</i>
The institutional legitimatization of halakhic pluralism poses great challenges in the modern State of Israel. Given the differing traditions of the Ashkenazic and Sephardic communities, how is halakhic practice to be structured for the citizenry? Ought the goal be to establish uniformity of practice or to promote communal diversity? Is either goal attainable at present? While some have advocated the need to maintain halakhic diversity, others argue that the <i>mara de-atra</i>(s) of the Land of Israel ought to be <i>Rambam</i> and/or <i>R. Karo</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">For a discussion of these varying approaches, see Ratzon Arusi, “Conflict of Laws and The Ethnic Factor in Halakhic Determination in Israel” [Hebrew] (unpublished doctoral dissertation; Tel Aviv, 1987), 250–348. For the application of these views to the area of Halakhic matrimonial law, an ongoing topic of public and legal discourse in contemporary Israel, see Elimelech Westreich, “The Preservation of Marital Status of the Jewish Woman in Israel: Confrontation Between Legal Traditions of Different Ethnic Backgrounds” [Hebrew], 7 <i>Pelillim</i> (5759), 273.</i>
What is the scope of the <i>mara de-atra</i>’s authority? Which members of the community create it? Is every member of the community duty bound to ask their questions of the <i>mara de-atra</i>? Is an individual who joins the community subsequent to the communal acceptance of the <i>mara de-atra</i> obligated to ask him his questions and abide by his judgments? Some argue that given that the election of the <i>mara de-atra</i> was executed via a communal enactment, the norms of <i>takanot ha-kahal</i>, communal enactments, are applicable.<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot ha</i>-<i>Rivash</i> 105; <i>Teshuvot ha-Ran</i> 62.</i> Because such enactments are passed upon majority rule of its members or its representatives, even the minority must comply,<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Asheri</i> 6:5; <i>Teshuvot ha-Rosh</i> 6:105, 107; <i>Teshuvot Re’em</i> 53; <i>SA</i>, YD 228:34; <i>Rema</i>, HM 163:1. One may contend that in this matter, as in others, the community may permit individuals to exclude themselves; see <i>Teshuvot ha</i>-<i>Rivash</i> 249.</i> and the legislation binds even individuals who have yet to permanently reside in the locale.<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot ha</i>-<i>Rivash</i> 399. Therefore, just as a community may impose penal measures upon its members, a <i>mara de-atra</i> possesses such authority; see <i>Arukh ha-Shulhan</i>, HM 2:2; <i>Teshuvot Da’at Kohen</i> 193.</i> The <i>mara de-atra</i> is “in-authority” for all these individuals.<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">See supra text accompanying nn. 20–21.</i>
Alternatively, the <i>mara de-atra</i> is granted authority only over those who accept him upon themselves. <i>R. Shaul Yisraeli</i> observed in 1966:
<span class="poetry indentAll"><small>The majority of the public in Israel has accepted the Chief Rabbinate as its rabbi … It follows that the Chief Rabbinate enjoys the status of “the local master” throughout Israel and its local rabbis act only as its agents.<sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Amud ha-Yemini</i>, 51. Given that an individual cannot exempt himself from being subservient to a communal enactment (see <i>Teshuvot ha-Rashba</i> 1:769, <i>SA</i>, YD 228:34, and many others), R. Yisraeli’s position seems problematic. However, the position may be tenable based upon the following two grounds: First, if the community is not insistent that a member comply with its legislation, a dissenter may refrain from complying; see <i>Teshuvot ha-Rivash</i> 249 in the name of <i>Rashba</i>. Second, given that the minority members of the community and out of town residents (under certain circumstances) must still adhere to the <i>mara de-atra</i>’s rulings of a public nature (see text below), outward unity as reflected in public acceptance of the <i>mara de-atra</i> would still exist. Alternatively, in our contemporary context, due to the breakdown of communal structures and various other factors (see text below), the role of the <i>mara de-atra</i>’s authority is limited <i>de facto</i> to certain public matters; consequently, every member, even the minority, must accept his authority for these matters. However, regarding personal matters, members of the community and/or synagogue may turn to other rabbinic authorities for their spiritual guidance. Hence, the issue of a minority’s unwillingness to accept a particular <i>mara de-atra</i> will not arise.<br>Although one might suggest that the permissibility of the minority’s noncompliance is grounded in the opinions that mandate unanimity for passage of a communal enactment, there are authorities who argue that this position is inapplicable in cases in which the custom is to follow majority rule or the community or its officials is empowered to always resolve matters based upon majority rule. See <i>Teshuvot ha-Rema</i> 2 and 73; <i>Teshuvot Hatam Sofer</i>, HM 116; <i>Knesset ha-Gedolah</i>, HM 231:29, 77.<br>For antecedents of the view that Israel is to be construed as a “community,” see <i>Horayot</i> 3a; <i>Rambam</i>, <i>Perush ha-Mishnayot</i>, <i>Bekhorot</i> 4:3; <i>Teshuvot Avnei Nezer</i>, OH 314; R. Yitzhak Herzog, <i>Constitution and Law in a Jewish State According to the Halakhah</i> (Hebrew) (Jerusalem: 5749), vol. 2, 58; <i>Teshuvot Yaskil Avdi</i>, vol. 6, HM 8, 28; R. Mordekhai Eliyahu and R. Avraham Shapiro, “The Status of Civil Legislation and Halakhah” [Hebrew], 3 <i>Tehumin</i> (5742), 242, 249.</i></small></span>
<i>R. Yisraeli</i> concludes that segments of the Israeli populace who have not accepted this authority are free to follow their own decisors. If an individual chooses to accept the <i>mara de-atra</i>’s authority, he is bound by his teachings; should he decline to do so, he may choose another decisor.
Accordingly, those who disagreed with the election of the <i>mara de-atra</i> or moved to the locale only after his installment may freely choose to follow a different recognized authority. However, the dissenting individual is no different than any visitor who arrives in the town, who must refrain from any public behavior that contradicts specific rulings of the <i>mara de-atra</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote">The Talmud thus relates that upon R. Avahu’s arrival “in the place of R. Yehoshua b. Levi,” he would carry candles on Shabbat in accordance with his own view, but when he was in the place of R. Yohanan, he refrained from carrying them; see <i>Yevamot</i> 14a. See also <i>Eruvin</i> 94a.</i>
According to both schools of thought, the binding nature of a <i>mara de-atra</i>’s rulings is based upon communal acceptance, either actualized through the adoption of a communal enactment or the execution of a “<i>ketav rabbanut</i>,” a rabbinic contract.<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote">Ya’akov Katz, “On the History of the Rabbinate at the End of the Middle Ages” [Hebrew], in <i>Binyamin DeVries Memorial Volume</i> (Tel Aviv: 1968), 281–94; Reuvain Bonfil, <i>The Rabbinate in Renaissance Italy</i> [Hebrew] (Jerusalem: 5739), 67–122, 238–44; Meir Benayahu, <i>Marbitz Torah</i> [Hebrew] (Jerusalem: 5713); Mordekhai Breuer, <i>The Ashkenazic Rabbinate in the Middle Ages</i> [Hebrew] (Jerusalem: 5736). In fact, in certain communities, a <i>mara de-atra</i> was mandated by the community to follow certain decisors, such as <i>Shulhan Arukh</i> and <i>Rema</i>. See Chaim Tchernowitz, <i>Toledot ha-Poskim</i> [Hebrew] 1:8. The selection of a <i>mara de-atra</i> by one community does not preclude the right of another scholar to be elected as a <i>mara de-atra</i> by another community in the same town; see <i>Rema</i>, HM 245: 22. The issue of <i>hasagat gevul</i>, economic competition, is beyond the scope of this essay.</i> Consequently, an individual who agrees and subsequently reneges on his commitment is viewed as someone who makes an oath not to be bound by a communal enactment, a meaningless oath that is therefore in vain and in violation of a biblical commandment.<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote"><i>SA</i>, YD 228:33. For the difficulties with this position, see <i>Bi’ur ha</i>-<i>Gra</i> ad loc., 94; <i>Teshuvot Terumat ha</i>-<i>Deshen</i> 281. Just as an individual who asks a question of a scholar is bound to abide by his answer (even if it is <i>le-humrah</i>, stringent), a town which accepts a <i>mara de-atra</i> is obligated to adhere to all of his teachings; see <i>Teshuvot Tashbetz</i> 3:210.</i> Moreover, according to some authorities, he has violated the biblical prohibition of “<i>lo tasur</i>.”<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote">Binyamin Lipkin, “The Legislative Authority of the Public” (Hebrew), 20 <i>Torah she-Be’al Peh</i> (5739): 108,126-7. Cf. <i>Teshuvot Hikrei Lev</i>, YD 49.</i>
In short, “an-authority” is transformed by the community into a <i>mara de-atra</i> who is “in-authority.” His rulings are therefore biblically binding in all public matters, such as marriage, divorce, liturgical practice, <i>kashrut</i> standards, and <i>eruvin</i>, as well as personal matters, such as monetary obligations, interest, theft, and Shabbat and Yom Tov regulations.
Furthermore, a <i>mara de-atra</i>’s decision that is grounded in a minority view has been characterized as “akin to being of biblical status” and “like <i>Halakhah le-Moshe mi-Sinai</i>,” traditions from Sinai regarding which there is unanimity.<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Tashbetz</i>, <i>Hut ha-Meshulash</i>, <i>Tur Shilishi</i>, 35; <i>Teshuvot ha</i>-<i>Ridbaz</i> 1:229; <i>Teshuvot Maharif</i> 59.</i>
Given the stature assigned to the <i>mara de-atra</i>, as we will demonstrate in the latter portion of our presentation, he must exhibit piety (<i>yirat Shamayim</i>), possess a proficiency in Talmudic and post-Talmudic scholarship, and have honed skills in analogical reasoning and rendering decisions.<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote">Pursuant to various Talmudic dicta, the right to instruct requires that the individual be both learned (“<i>gamir</i>”), capable of cogent reasoning (“<i>savir</i>”), and God-fearing. These are requirements for an arbiter in ritual matters as well as in judging monetary affairs. See <i>Ketuvot</i> 103a; <i>Horayot</i> 2b; <i>Beit ha-Behirah</i>, <i>Horayot</i> 2b; <i>Teshuvot Divrei Malkiel</i> 4:82; Bonfil, supra n.51, 236. According to <i>Rema</i>, YD 242:30, along with these skills, the rabbi must be capable of rendering a <i>psak</i>, rather than simply engage in the pilpulistic reasoning of halakhic study.(The identical point is made earlier by <i>Hakhmei Ashkenaz</i> of the fifteenth century; see <i>Teshuvot Mahari Weil</i> 164; <i>Terumat ha-Deshen</i> 16; <i>Teshuvot Maharil ha-Hadashot</i> 134; <i>Teshuvot Mahari Bruna</i> 92.)<br>Whether a son inheriting his father’s rabbinical post must be a scholar or “somewhat of a scholar” is subject to debate. See <i>Rema</i>, YD 245:22; <i>Arukh ha-Shulhan</i>, YD 245:29; <i>Teshuvot Avnei Nezer</i>, YD 312:45; <i>Mishnah Torah</i>, <i>Hilkhot Kelei ha-Mikdash</i> 4:20; <i>Teshuvot Divrei Malkiel</i> 4:82.<br>In <i>Rema</i>’s words, a scholar “whose trade is his study of Torah” is defined as one who knows Talmudic argumentation (“the give and take of the Talmud”), understands most of the topics discussed in the Talmud, its commentaries, and Geonic opinions, and whose sole profession is Torah study; see <i>Rema</i>, YD 233:2. This definition of scholar has no bearing on a <i>mara de-atra</i>’s credentials if he holds a second job.<br>Although <i>Arukh ha-Shulhan</i>, YD 242:29, 56, argues that the relationship between an individual and his <i>mara de-atra</i> is equivalent to a teacher-student relationship (<i>rabbo muvhak-talmid</i>), many disagree. See <i>Teshuvot Maharam Mintz</i> 98;<i>Teshuvot Maharashdam</i>, YD 220; <i>Teshuvot Hikrei Lev</i>, OH 95; <i>Hiddushei Hatam Sofer</i>, <i>Ketuvot</i> 6a; R. Shmuel D. Munk (attributed author)<i>, Kuntres She’eilot u-Teshuvot be-Ein Hazon</i> (Haifa, 5736), 71.</i> Although a <i>mara de-atra</i> has great authority over his constituents, it is not unlimited. If it is clear that the <i>mara de-atra</i> is in error – if he made a “<i>ta’ut be-devar mishnah</i>” (a mistake in black-letter Halakhah),<sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Eruvin</i> 63a, 94a; <i>Hiddushei ha-Ritva</i>, <i>Eruvin</i> 94a; <i>Amud ha</i>-<i>Yemini</i> 6:10.</i> or permitted the forbidden,<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Beit ha-Behirah</i>, <i>Eruvin</i> 94a; <i>Teshuvot Maharam Mintz</i> 98.</i> one is not obligated to follow his directive. <i>R. Zerahiah ha-Levi</i> (<i>Ba’al ha-Ma’or</i>) states that if the arbiter awarded an item to one party in a dispute and it was later revealed that the decision was in error, the decision is reversible and the other party must be reimbursed for his loss:
<span class="poetry indentAll"><small>Since it was an error in a matter of a <i>mishnah</i>, he [the litigant] should have been aware of the error and should not have relied upon the arbiter and executed the decision. Rather, he ought to have inquired and uncovered the error. This is as clear as “a clear <i>mishnah.</i>” Hence, the party was negligent.<sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ba’al ha-Ma’or</i>, <i>Sanhedrin</i> 12b. See also <i>Hiddushei Ba’al Seredei Esh</i>, 22.</i></small></span>
In other words, even if one receives a <i>psak</i> from one’s rabbi, it is the individual’s responsibility to understand the reasons for the ruling. If he fails to do so and therefore follows an incorrect ruling, the halakhic system labels him a “<i>poshei’a</i>,” a negligent person. “<i>Emunat hakhamim</i>,” faith in the wise, does not entail, at least in this context, blind obedience.
<b>3. A Deceased <i>Mara De-Atra</i></b>
In an often-cited and widely accepted <i>teshuvah</i> (responsum), <i>R. Shlomo ben Aderet</i> (<i>Rashba</i>) succinctly describes the role of the <i>mara de-atra</i>:
<span class="poetry indentAll"><small>If there is one rabbi in their location who has taught them, they should follow his opinion … The same is true of those who have customarily acted according to one of the great authorities, as in a place in which they invariably follow the rulings of <i>R. Alfasi</i> or <i>Rambam</i>, for they have adopted these great decisors as their own rabbi. <sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot ha-Rashba</i> 1:253. For a list of the sources that cite this landmark <i>teshuvah</i>, see Shlomo Havlin, “R. Yehiel Ashkenazi and His Teshuvot<i>”</i> (Hebrew), 7 <i>Shalem</i> (5762), 71, 93, n. 16. The significance of this <i>teshuva</i> is underscored by the fact that numerous <i>Aharonim</i>, such as <i>Pri Hadash</i> (OH 496), <i>Massa Melekh</i> (<i>Dinei ha-Minhagot</i>, <i>hakirah</i> 13), and <i>Teshuvot Hikrei Lev</i> (OH 296), offer painstaking analysis to resolve the seeming contradiction between this <i>teshuvah</i> and another one of <i>Rashba</i> (1:190).</i></small></span>
According to <i>Rashba</i>, the <i>mara de-atra</i> of a community may already be deceased, as <i>Rif</i> and <i>Rambam</i> were when he penned this <i>teshuvah</i>. A community may either choose to accept the jurisdiction of a contemporary authority or rely on an earlier <i>mara de-atra</i> for rendering decisions. Even though these rabbinic authorities may never have lived in this place and are no longer living, the community practices the custom (“<i>keivan she-nahagu</i>”) to abide by their decisions.<sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote">See the numerous <i>teshuvot</i> cited by Kahane, supra n. 34.</i>
Concurring with <i>Rashba</i>’s position, <i>R. Nissim ben Yosef Mizrahi</i> (eighteenth century Turkey) states:
<span class="poetry indentAll"><small>Even though <i>Alfasi</i> and <i>Rambam</i> were not the <i>mara de-atra</i> in that place, since in all actions the community follows their words, <i>Alfasi</i> and <i>Rambam</i> are considered their rabbis and <i>mara de-atra</i>s, even though their net was not spread over the place.<sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Admat Kodesh</i> 1:10.</i></small></span>
Obviously, a community’s choice to accept a <i>mara de-atra</i> who is no longer living presents formidable challenges. Since many members of the community are likely neither knowledgeable nor conversant regarding their <i>mara de-atra</i>’s rulings, a scholar would have to be elected to transmit those teachings. Thus, in one of the dozens of communities that have accepted <i>Rambam</i>’s authority in halakhic matters, the scholar would require broad knowledge of <i>Rambam</i>’s <i>Mishnah Torah</i>, commentary on the Mishnah, and responsa. In order to fully comprehend <i>Rambam</i>’s rulings, the scholar would also of necessity need to be able to unearth <i>Rambam</i>’s Talmudic and Geonic sources. Moreover, since the community would be posed from time to time with questions that were never addressed by <i>Rambam</i>, the scholar would also need the erudition to deliver <i>pesikah</i> that would either optimally “follow in <i>Rambam</i>’s footsteps” or be rooted in the teachings of others. Operating within the context of abiding by the judgments of a deceased rabbi, of course, is no small feat.
What is the justification for relying on an authority who is no longer alive to serve as a community’s <i>mara de-atra</i>? In the Tannaitic and Talmudic periods, dispute resolution demanded the expertise of a <i>rav</i> (rabbi), and his disciples were proscribed from issuing decisions in his presence. In post-Talmudic times, however, the norms governing the teacher-student relationship were abrogated. As <i>R. Moshe of Evreux</i>, a twelfth century Tosafist, explains:
<span class="poetry indentAll"><small>From the time the Jewish people was exiled from its land, when our Temple was destroyed … the law that reverence for a teacher must be like reverence for God no longer applies, and all the rules regarding the relationship between disciple and master are abrogated. This is because the books, the treatises, and the commentaries are our masters, and everything depends on intelligence and logic.<sup class="footnote-marker">62</sup><i class="footnote">Aharon ha-Kohen of Lunel, <i>Orhot Hayyim</i>, <i>Hilkhot Talmud Torah</i>, ch. 21. While most halakhic authorities and Jewish legal historians contend that this is the earliest source for this notion, some historians argue that precedents to this concept are to be found in the earlier writings of Ri and Rash mi-Shantz. See Ephraim Urbach, <i>Ba’alei ha-Tosafot</i> [Hebrew] (Jerusalem: 5740), 251; Ephraim Kanarfogel, “Rabbinic Authority and the Right to Open an Academy in Medieval Ashkenaz,” 12 <i>Michael</i> (1991), 233; idem., “Progress and Tradition in Medieval Ashkenaz,” 14 <i>Jewish History</i> (2000), 287.<br>Accepting this concept as a technique of decision-making has led various authorities to claim that the Talmudic requirement requiring an individual to be forty years old prior to rendering a decision no longer applies. See <i>Lehem Mishnah</i>, <i>Hilkhot Talmud Torah</i> 5:1–4; <i>Teshuvot Avkat Rokhel</i> 200; <i>Teshuvot Ridbaz</i> 5:2147; <i>Teshuvot Maharashdam</i>, HM 1. Cf. <i>Teshuvot Maharik</i>, <i>shoresh</i> 369; <i>Rema</i>, YD 242:31. Even during the periods when such a requirement existed, the scope of the requirement was limited; see <i>Teshuvot ha</i>-<i>Mabit</i> 280. Others, such as <i>Sefer ha</i>-<i>Ittur</i> 1; <i>Berurin</i> (2b); <i>Teshuvot Shevut Yaakov</i> 1:140; and <i>Teshuvot ha</i>-<i>Ridbaz</i> 5:2147, contend that this age requirement is limited to <i>hora’ah</i>, ritual matters.<br>According to R. Ovadiah Yosef, “the books … are our masters” mandates that all books must be examined prior to rendering a <i>psak.</i> Accordingly, if certain decisors arrive at a <i>psak</i> by looking only at certain books, such as the Talmud and <i>Rishonim</i>, the age requirement should remain operative. See <i>Teshuvot Yabia Omer</i>, vol. 4, HM 1:6. Accordingly, <i>Noda be-Yehuda</i>, who explicitly states that his <i>pesakim</i> are based upon the Talmud and early codifiers and who refrains from delving into contemporary <i>teshuvot</i> (responsa) and recent predecessors’ writings, would be proscribed from rendering decisions prior to the age of forty. See <i>Teshuvot Noda be-Yehuda</i>, vol. 1, YD 55, vol. 2, YD 86, and vol. 1, OH 38. In fact, R. Yosef claims that all early Ashkenazic rabbinic decisors who failed to study the teachings of the <i>Aharonim</i> prior to handing down a <i>psak</i> were prohibited from serving as decisors prior to the age of forty.</i></small></span>
While this change led to attenuated or nonexistent personal ties between teachers and students, reliance upon “the books rather than scribes” (“<i>mi-pi seforim ve-lo mi-pi soferim</i>”) expanded the sources for rabbinic decision-making. Many communities chose to abide by the decisions of rabbinic arbiters of yesteryear, such as <i>Rif</i> and <i>Rambam</i> – “<i>mi-pi seforim</i>.”<sup class="footnote-marker">63</sup><i class="footnote">See Kahane, supra n. 34.</i>
Despite the fact that <i>Rashba</i> accorded the status of <i>mara de-atra</i> to authorities who are no longer alive, he notes:
<span class="poetry indentAll"><small>If there is one rabbi who has taught them, they should follow his opinion … If there be a scholar qualified to rule on Halakhah who adduces a proof to prohibit what they [<i>Rif</i> and <i>Rambam</i>] have permitted, one should prohibit, since they are not truly like their own rabbis. For if they do not abide by the opinion of the rabbi in their own locale, this would be disrespectful.<sup class="footnote-marker">64</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot ha-Rashba</i> 1:253.</i></small></span>
<i>Rashba</i> (and others) introduce a caveat that the rule of halakhic subservience only applies to a <i>mara de-atra</i> out of concern for his honor and in deference to his rulings. Since these concerns are inapplicable to a decisor who is no longer living, if a scholar marshals proofs to prohibit a matter against the <i>mara de-atra</i>’s opinion, the scholar’s judgment may become binding. Upon the demise of a <i>mara de-atra</i>, it is permissible for another <i>posek</i> to overturn his rulings.<sup class="footnote-marker">65</sup><i class="footnote">Numerous legists have offered the rationale for compliance to a <i>mara de-atra</i>’s rulings as an expression of honor; see <i>Teshuvot ha-Rashba</i>, ibid.; <i>Rashi</i>, <i>Shabbat</i> 46a; <i>Teshuvot ha-Rivash</i> 256; <i>Teshuvot Maharam Mintz</i> 98; <i>Teshuvot ha-Radakh</i>, <i>bayit</i> 12, <i>heder</i> 3; <i>Teshuvot Maharashdam</i>, YD 90, 149; <i>Teshuvot Muharash ha-Levi</i>, YD 10; <i>Teshuvot Hikrei Lev</i>, OH 95; <i>Pri Hadash</i>, <i>Yom Tov</i> 496, <i>Dinei Minhag</i> 2; <i>Teshuvot Mateh Yosef</i>, vol. 2, YD 2; <i>Teshuvot Hatam Sofer</i>, OH 2, HM 100; <i>Arukh ha-Shulhan</i>, YD 242:57; <i>Kovetz Shiurim</i>, <i>Bava Batra</i> 273. However, this rationale is specifically applicable to a <i>mara de-atra</i>, rather than every <i>moreh hora’ah</i>.<br><i>Berakhot</i> 63b states that concerning <i>issur ve’heter</i>, ritual matters, “if a scholar prohibited something, his colleague has no authority to permit it after it already has been forbidden.” Most legists understand this to mean that once something has been declared forbidden, it can no longer be declared permissible. See <i>Avodah Zarah</i> 7a; <i>Perush ha-Ra’avad</i>, <i>Avodah Zarah</i> 7a; <i>Hiddushei ha-Rashba</i>, ad loc.; <i>Teshuvot Tashbetz</i> 1:66. Cf. the views of <i>Rashi</i>, <i>Niddah</i> 20b, and <i>Hiddushei ha-Ran</i>, <i>Avodah Zarah</i> 2a (<i>Rif</i> pagination), who claim that “<i>kevod ha-Rav</i>,” deference and respect, precludes the second decisor from overruling the first decisor. (<i>Ran</i> notes that he lacks the status to counter the majority position, which disagrees with this view. See <i>Teshuvot ha-Ridbaz</i> 1:362). For other approaches which bar overruling a previous decisor – either to preclude the conception that there are two systems of Halakhah or because the questioner has accepted the first decisor’s decision – see <i>Ran</i>, <i>Avodah Zarah</i> 7a; <i>Teshuvot ha-Rivash</i> 379.<br>Based upon this debate, <i>Shiltei ha-Gibborim</i>, <i>Avoda Zarah</i> ad loc., argues that if the original <i>posek</i> issued a permissive ruling, according to Ra’avad et al., a second decisor may forbid the matter; according to <i>Rashi</i> et al., the second decisor must refrain from issuing such a ruling. A cursory glance at the works of <i>Poskim</i> will attest to the fact that legists frequently overrule and forbid what colleagues have previously permitted, thus corroborating the majority position. Moreover, numerous authorities have sanctioned overruling of colleagues; see <i>Tosafot</i>, <i>Avodah Zarah</i>, ad loc.; <i>Rema</i>, YD 242:31; <i>Shakh</i>, YD 242:58; <i>Teshuvot Tzemach Tzedek</i> (Lubavitch), YD 197; <i>Teshuvot Maharit</i> 86. This conclusion extends even to being stringent against the Halakhah; see <i>Teshuvot ha-Rashba</i> 1:112; <i>Teshuvot Tashbetz</i> 3:188. Finally, some have argued that the rule “if a scholar prohibited something, his colleague has no authority to permit it after it already has been forbidden” is inapplicable today. See <i>Arukh ha-Shulhan</i> YD 242:63, <i>Teshuvot ha-Rama me-Pano</i> 108; <i>Teshuvot Maharsham</i> 9:79. Cf. <i>Rema</i>, YD 242:34; <i>Teshuvot ha-Ridbaz</i> 1:362; <i>Teshuvot Meishiv Davar</i>, YD 9.<br>This conclusion, however, is limited to a non-institutional rabbinic authority. A scholar must refrain from deciding in a more stringent fashion than a living <i>mara de-atra</i>; see <i>Teshuvot ha-Rashba</i> 1:253. Hence, the fact that a <i>mara de-atra</i>’s ruling is final is a unique <i>halakhah</i> regarding deference to his rulings and inapplicable to every <i>moreh horo’ah</i>. While a scholar’s issuance of a more stringent opinions – except in matters of <i>ishut</i>, personal status – is not generally perceived as an affront to his lenient predecessor (“<i>la’az al ha-rishonim</i>”), one must refrain from deciding in a stringent manner contrary to a <i>mara de-atra</i>’s lenient opinion. See <i>Teshuvot Mayyim Amukim</i> 3:12; <i>Teshuvot Hikrei Lev</i>, OH 95; Eliav Shochetman, “The Concern for Reflecting Unfavorably upon Earlier Decisors as a Factor in Halakhic Decision-Making” [Hebrew], 18–19 <i>Bar Ilan Yearbook</i> (5741), 170.<br>Alternatively, others have advanced the notion that overruling a <i>mara de-atra</i>’s judgments displays improper manners, “<i>lav orcha de-ara</i>,” rather than a formal violation of deference.
