See <i>Rashi</i>, <i>Berakhot</i> 63a and <i>Hullin</i> 53a; <i>Teshuvot Binyamin Ze’ev</i> 239.</i>
A case in point is the nuanced approach of a number of 16–17th century Egyptian-Israeli authorities to the rulings of <i>Rambam</i>. <i>R. David ibn Zimra</i> (<i>Ridbaz</i>), for example, accepted <i>Rambam</i> as the <i>mara de-atra</i> of Egypt;<sup class="footnote-marker">66</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot ha-Ridbaz</i> 1:335; 4:1048, 1165; 6:2181.</i> nevertheless, in certain instances, <i>Ridbaz</i>’s autonomous judgment prevails.<sup class="footnote-marker">67</sup><i class="footnote">Ibid<i>.</i>, 1:121, 432, 433, 518; 3:1011.</i> Similarly, <i>R. Ya’akov Castro</i> (<i>Maharikash</i>) and <i>R. Avraham ha-Levi</i> (<i>Ginat Veradim</i>) exhibit a gravitational pull to <i>Rambam</i> as the <i>mara de-atra</i> of Egypt, yet remain innovative in many of their own rulings.<sup class="footnote-marker">68</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Oholei Yaakov</i> 13, 78; <i>Erekh Lehem</i>, OH 388; <i>Teshuvot Darkhei Noam</i>, HM 15; <i>Teshuvot Ginat Veradim</i>, OH 3:26, YD 3:6; <i>Even Ha-Ezer</i> (EH) 2:7, HM 3:29.</i> As we will see, this navigation between the poles of deference and autonomy is characteristic of halakhic methodology in general and to the decisor’s relationship with a deceased <i>mara de-atra</i> in particular.
To buttress his conclusion, <i>Rashba</i> invokes the Talmud’s account of <i>Rabbah bar Hanna</i>’s practice when he visited Bavel (Babylonia). While there, he ate fat that the Sages of the Land of Israel had declared permissible, but which had been prohibited by the Sages of Bavel. <i>R. Ashi</i> explained that since <i>Rabbah</i> had intention to return to the Land of Israel, he was permitted to consume this fat; one must follow the practice of the place where he permanently resides, whether that practice is lenient or strict.<sup class="footnote-marker">69</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Pesahim</i> 51a. See <i>SA</i>, OH 574 and YD 214:2; <i>Shakh</i>, YD 214:8; <i>Magen Avraham</i>, OH 468:9. In general, if there is a conflict between the <i>minhag ha-makom</i> and family practice, the <i>minhag ha-makom</i> supersedes the family <i>minhag</i> and becomes binding upon the resident; see <i>Teshuvot Havot Ya’ir</i> 127. However, if a <i>mara de-atra</i> follows his own <i>minhag</i> and some of the residents practice their own custom, these families may continue to follow their own <i>minhag</i>; see <i>Teshuvot Ezrat Kohen</i> 103.</i> Since the “<i>minhag ha-makom</i>,” the local custom, in the Land of Israel was to follow the lenient view, <i>Rabbah</i> was compelled to follow it as well. Had he decided to permanently relocate to Bavel, he would then have been exempt from all of the communal enactments of his former locale, unless these enactments also existed in his new residence.
<i>Rashba</i> concludes that just as <i>minhag ha-makom</i> determines proper practice in cases of <i>issur ve-heter</i> (ritual Halakhah) in cases in which there is a difference of opinion, it is similarly the deciding factor in dealing with communities guided by a <i>mara de-atra</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">70</sup><i class="footnote">These norms applying to communal enactments are analogous to the norms applying to divergent customs; see <i>SA</i>, YD 214:2. Moreover, one must continue to abide by his <i>mara de-atra</i>’s rulings even if he is temporarily residing elsewhere; see <i>Teshuvot Maharam Alshakar</i> 49; <i>Teshuvot Mateh Yosef</i>, YD 1. If the communal enactment is accompanied by the sanction of <i>herem</i> (excommunication), the individual must comply with the <i>mara de-atra</i>’s judgments even when permanently residing in another community; see <i>SA,</i> YD 228:29 in the name of <i>Radakh</i>; <i>Teshuvot Radakh</i>, <i>bayit</i> 12, <i>heder</i> 3; <i>Teshuvot Maharashdam</i>, YD 149.<br>For antecedents for this position, see <i>Teshuvot ha-Ran</i> 48. However, others point out that <i>Ran</i>‘s ruling deals solely with enactments emanating from the <i>Beit din ha</i>-<i>Gadol</i>, the supreme court; see <i>Teshuvot Hikrei Lev</i>, HM 111; <i>Massa Melekh</i> 11a; <i>Pri Hadash</i> 496:13. On the role of the <i>herem</i> in communal legislation, see Gerald Blidstein, “Individual and Community in the Middle Ages” in D. Elazar (ed.), <i>Kinship and Consent</i> (Washington D.C., 1983), 217, 227–33.</i> Just as <i>Rabbah</i> heeded his <i>mara de-atra</i>’s ruling, members of any community are obligated to adhere to their <i>mara de-atra</i>’s decisions, even in the face of contrary ruling by a qualified authority. If, however, the community appoints an authority that is no longer alive as its <i>mara de-atra</i>, one can no longer speak of “his place.” A qualified authority is thus entitled to overrule the <i>mara de-atra</i>’s view.
If a duly elected <i>mara de-atra</i> subsequently dies, <i>R. Dovid Gershon</i> (seventeenth century Egypt) concludes that his rulings are still binding upon the community, even if other recognized authorities disagree with his view.<sup class="footnote-marker">71</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Ginat Veradim</i>, EH, <i>kelal</i> 2:9. However, a reading of <i>Teshuvot ha-Rashba</i> 1:1190 indicates otherwise.</i> In effect, the notion of <i>minhag ha-makom</i> remains operative even after the <i>mara de-atra</i> is no longer alive. Other authorities, such as <i>R. Yeshayahu de-Trani</i> (<i>Shiltei ha-Gibborim</i>) and <i>R. Yosef ibn Ezra</i> (<i>Massa Melekh</i>), concur with this conclusion.<sup class="footnote-marker">72</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Shiltei ha-Gibborim</i>, <i>Avodah Zarah</i> 2a (Rif pagination); <i>Massah Melekh</i>, <i>Ne’ilat She’arim be-Minhagei Issur</i>, <i>hakirah</i> 13.</i>
Others, however, including <i>R. Yoel Sirkes</i> (<i>Bah</i>), <i>R. Hezekiyah De Silva</i> (<i>Peri Hadash</i>), and <i>R. Yosef ben Moshe ha-Levi</i> (<i>Mateh Yosef</i>), argue that a <i>mara de-atra</i>’s decisions must be followed by members of a community residing within his geographical location only during his lifetime. Should the community desire to continue to follow the <i>mara de-atra</i>’s teachings after his death, the community would have to reaffirm him as their <i>mara de-atra</i>. Otherwise, the rulings of a new <i>mara de-atra</i> may become binding upon the community or those of a recognized decisor may become binding upon individuals. <sup class="footnote-marker">73</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Bah</i>, HM 25; <i>Pri Hadash</i>, OH, <i>Minhagei Issur</i> 2; <i>Teshuvot Mateh Yosef</i>, vol. 2, YD 2.</i>
This latter position affirms the critical role that the community plays in the establishment of obligatory halakhic compliance with the rulings of the <i>mara de-atra</i>. But what is the nature of that authority? Do the <i>mara de-atra</i>’s rulings govern the community members of a particular locale or the locale itself? For example, if a community was disbanded due to war or expulsion and Jews repopulate the area years later, are they bound to the rulings of the previous <i>mara de-atra</i> based upon <i>minhag ha-makom</i>, even if the <i>mara de-atra</i>’s community no longer exists? Do we insist that <i>kavod ha-rav</i> necessitates that we defer to the <i>mara de-atra</i>’s view, even in the absence of his constituency, or is <i>minhag ha-makom</i> solely a function of the presence of the <i>kahal</i>, the community?
Both <i>Shiltei ha-Gibborim</i> and <i>R. Avraham Gombiner</i> (<i>Magen Avraham</i>) address this question, but they fail to resolve it.<sup class="footnote-marker">74</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Shiltei ha-Gibborim</i>, supra n. 72; <i>Magen Avraham</i>, OH 493:6.</i> <i>Hok Ya’akov</i>, however, concludes that if “new faces” have arrived in a locale bereft of a community, they are free to choose their own rabbinic authority.<sup class="footnote-marker">75</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Hok Ya’akov</i>, OH 468:9. Similarly, since the <i>minhag</i> has disappeared, there is no obligation upon new residents to follow the locale’s <i>minhag</i>; see <i>Bi’ur Halakhah</i>, OH 468:4. However, if at the inception of this local <i>minhag</i> it was stipulated that this <i>minhag</i> applies for future generations, the <i>minhag</i> remains binding even for new residents entering the devastated community; see <i>Teshuvot Maharam Alshakar</i> 49.</i> Accordingly, <i>Hazon Ish</i> rules that since the original community headed by <i>R. Yosef Karo</i> no longer exists in Israel, residents of contemporary Israel need not abide by <i>R. Karo</i>’s rulings.<sup class="footnote-marker">76</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Hazon Ish</i>, <i>Zera’im</i>, <i>Shevi’it</i> 23:5. Cf. <i>Teshuvot Yabia Omer</i>, vol. 3, EH 12; <i>Teshuvot Yehaveh Da’at</i> 1:12, 4:36.</i> If a community relocates to an inhabited place and maintains its independence, it retains the practices of its former place rather than adopting the <i>minhag ha-makom</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">77</sup><i class="footnote">This conclusion is contingent upon the fact that the newcomers remained separate from the host community, in which case each <i>kahal</i> is accorded the status of “a city” in and of itself. See <i>Pri Hadash</i>, OH 496:10; <i>Massa Melekh</i>, <i>Torat ha-Minhagot</i>, <i>hakirah</i> 11; <i>Teshuvot Ginat Veradim</i>, YD, <i>kelal</i> 5:3.</i> By extension, if a <i>kahal</i> relocates to a locale that has lost its Jewish populace, it may retain its own practices.
What happens if two communities with different traditions simultaneously populate a locale or a community joins an already established community with a different tradition? Addressing Eretz Yisrael and Iberian communities which were simultaneously established in the Ottoman Empire, <i>R. Karo</i> allowed each one to retain its tradition.<sup class="footnote-marker">78</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Avkat Rokhel</i> 32.</i> In contrast, <i>R. Karo</i> argues that a community that abides by <i>Rambam</i>’s rulings retains halakhic hegemony in the face of the arrival of a community following Ashkenazic teachings, as long as it is in the majority. Even if the new community eventually numerically outnumbers the older established community, the latter’s traditions are to be followed by both communities.<sup class="footnote-marker">79</sup><i class="footnote">Ibid., 212.</i> In other words, <i>minhag ha-makom</i> is defined by the original residents. Others, however, such as <i>Maharival</i>, <i>Maharashdam</i>, <i>Ginat Veradim</i>, and <i>Massa Melekh</i>, disagree, arguing that as long as the new arrivals comprise a “<i>kahal</i>,” an independent community, they retain their practices.<sup class="footnote-marker">80</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Maharival</i> 3:10; <i>Teshuvot Maharashdam</i>, YD 40; <i>Ginat Veradim</i>, supra n. 77; <i>Masseh Melech</i>, supra n. 77.</i>
<b>4. The <i>Mara De-Atra</i> and the Modern Jewish Community</b>
This entire discussion is premised upon the sociological existence of a <i>kahal</i>. With the advent of modernization, secularization, the widespread bureaucratization of authority, and social mobility, the traditional communities, the “<i>atra</i>s” (the places), have been weakened significantly. These social and political forces, combined with easy access to other arbiters of national and international standing via telephone, fax, and e-mail, undermine and undercut the traditional role of a <i>mara de-atra</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">81</sup><i class="footnote">Menachem Friedman, “The Changing Role of the Community Rabbinate,” 25 <i>The Jerusalem Quarterly</i> (Fall 1982), 79–99; idem., “Halachic Rabbinic Authority in the Modern Open Society,” in J. Werthheimer (ed.), <i>Jewish Religious Leadership: Image and Reality</i> (NY: 2004), 757–70. Whether the rise of Hasidism and the Lithuanian <i>yeshiva</i> in the 19th century impacted upon traditional rabbinic leadership, as argued by Friedman, I leave as an open question for further study.</i> Commenting upon this sociological change, Professor Menachem Friedman observes:
<span class="poetry indentAll"><small>In the past, I coined the term “voluntary community” to distinguish between the traditional Jewish community that existed in Europe, at least until the middle of the eighteenth century, and the Jewish community that developed via the processes of modernization and secularization, which was paralleled by the disintegration of the traditional community framework. While the former was defined primarily by geographic bounds and included all the Jews who resided within these bounds … the voluntary community, to the extent that such a term can be used in this context, was organized on the basis of the willingness of the individual to be affiliated with it and to assume the norms and values that define the identity of the community … The development of the voluntary community became possible from the moment that the leadership of the Jewish community no longer had the power to enforce the observance of the traditional Jewish way of life on the members of the community.<sup class="footnote-marker">82</sup><i class="footnote">Friedman, “Halachic Rabbinic Authority,” supra n. 81, 765–6.</i></small></span>
Despite the countervailing forces of modernity, an authority who possesses the requisite scholarly credentials can function institutionally in the United States as a <i>mara de-atra</i> within certain contexts. This is the case, for example, in the context of the rebbe of a Hasidic community or a <i>Rosh Yeshiva</i> within the walls of his <i>yeshiva</i>. However, for all practical purposes, the presence of a <i>mara de-atra</i> as a <i>kahal</i> authority has vanished from most Jewish communities. Thus, R. Feinstein observes that neither Manhattan nor Brooklyn possess a <i>mara de-atra</i> who is the authority for the entire populace.<sup class="footnote-marker">83</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Iggerot Moshe</i>, OH 1:159. In a later <i>teshuvah</i>, he points out that communities retaining their own customs do not exist; see ibid., OH 4:33. R. Rafael Bloom similarly mentions that there is no <i>mara de-atra</i> functioning in the United States; see <i>Teshuvot Birkhot Shamayim</i>, EH 47:3.</i> Similarly, in Israel, residents of Bnei Brak are not necessarily bound to follow the teachings of the late <i>Hazon Ish</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">84</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Iggerot Moshe</i>, YD 3:88. See also Z. Goldberg, supra n. 23, 33–34. Cf. R. Shlomo Shapiro, supra n. 23, 149, n. 1, who argues that synagogue membership entails accepting the rabbi as one’s <i>mara de-atra</i>.</i>
Despite the absence of a communal framework within many Jewish enclaves, in many instances, the synagogue in a particular locale may be deemed halakhically as a <i>kahal</i> if it operates as an autonomous institution.<sup class="footnote-marker">85</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Maharashdam</i>, YD 153; <i>Bi’ur Halakhah</i>, OH 468. For others who subscribe to or reject this position, see R. Shalom Schwadron, <i>Mishpat Shalom</i>, HM 231:32.</i> For these purposes, the synagogue’s autonomy is defined by having its own rabbi, conducting daily prayer services with its own liturgical practices, and maintaining governance by its lay membership. If the synagogue is construed as a <i>kahal</i>, the elected rabbi, contingent upon possessing the requisite scholarship, is to be viewed as the <i>mara de-atra</i> of the synagogue.
The synagogue’s <i>mara de-atra</i> will either be “an-authority” or “in-authority” to the same degree that any communal <i>mara de-atra</i> is. Synagogue acceptance of the <i>mara de-atra</i> is either actualized through the adoption of a <i>takanat ha-kahal</i>, synagogue bylaws,<sup class="footnote-marker">86</sup><i class="footnote">Although the halakhic norms governing the construction, use, and sale of a synagogue are predicated upon the authoritative <i>takanot ha-kahal</i> promulgated by the duly elected and representative members of a particular town (<i>SA</i>, OH 153), we equally extend this capacity of passage of <i>takanot ha-kahal</i> to the general membership and/or board of a synagogue.</i> and/or the execution of a <i>ketav rabbanut</i>, a rabbinic contract. Members of the synagogue are then obligated to abide by his rulings either by dint of the membership’s election or by voluntarily accepting his authority<i>.</i> Should a member decline to accept him as his <i>mara de-atra</i>, the member (or a non-member attending the synagogue) must refrain from any public behavior in the synagogue that undercuts specific rulings issued by the <i>mara de-atra</i>. As <i>R. Hayyim D. ha-Levi</i> elucidates:
<span class="poetry indentAll"><small>Although there is an obligation to comply with … the local rabbi’s rulings … surely if one does not accept his rulings … since he knows that other scholars differ with him, it is clear that he is not labeled a sinner. However, he is prohibited from acting in variance with the rabbi and his congregation in any public fashion, since such behavior would be an infraction of “<i>lo titgodedu</i>,” which seeks to prevent controversy and promote peace in the community.<sup class="footnote-marker">87</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Mayyim Hayyim</i> 1:71. For further discussion, see supra text accompanying n. 50; <i>Teshuvot Radakh</i>, <i>bayit</i> 22, <i>heder</i> 8; <i>Teshuvot Moharash ha-Levi</i>, YD 10; <i>Iggerot Moshe</i>, YD 2:99; <i>Teshuvot Seredei Esh</i> 2:12. According to many, the role of a <i>mara de-atra</i> today is limited to matters of a public nature. Thus, R. Herschel Schachter has stated that although he places his <i>tallit</i> over his head during certain portions of the prayer service, he does not do so if he is praying in a synagogue in which the rabbi has not adopted this practice. Additionally, a person who enters a synagogue to pray accepts the practices of that synagogue due to the need to follow <i>minhag ha-makom</i>; see <i>Teshuvot Hatam Sofer</i>, HM, <i>Hashmatot</i>, 5:185.</i></small></span>
<i>Any rulings which entails public behavior in the synagogue, such as synagogue liturgy, kashrut standards in the synagogue, and legal decisions affecting synagogue practice, apply to all synagogue members, even those who follow another rabbi’s teachings. Indeed, it is not uncommon in many of our communities to find members of one synagogue who choose to pose their halakhic questions to the rabbi of another synagogue or a yeshiva. As long as when you pray at a particular synagogue you communicate public recognition and validation of the synagogue rabbi and his rulings relating to the synagogue rather than practicing in the synagogue and/or voicing the rulings of your rabbi, such behavior appears to be entirely legitimate. To state it differently, though a particular congregant has chosen a rabbi for halakhic counsel (who is “in authority”) to address his personal needs, nevertheless, when attending the synagogue he must abide by all the rulings and practices of the congregational who is “in authority” in the synagogue.</i>
As we saw above regarding a communal <i>mara de-atra</i>, every synagogue member, regardless of whether he has selected the synagogue rabbi as “one’s rabbi” or not is obligated to refrain from complying with a <i>mara de-atra</i>’s ruling if he has erred “<i>be-devar Mishnah</i>,” in a matter of black-letter Halakhah or in permitting the forbidden.<sup class="footnote-marker">88</sup><i class="footnote">See supra text accompanying nn. 56–58.</i>
<b>5. <i>Mara de-Atra</i> of an Ideological Community</b>
In the foregoing discussion, we discussed the authority of living and deceased <i>mara de-atra</i>s, the extent of that authority, and the applicability to the modern day Jewish community. The common feature of the <i>mara de-atra</i>s that we have discussed is that they are chosen by a community in a particular geographical location. Can the notion of community transcend geographical boundaries? Can ideological and principled commitment create an ideological community, which in turn chooses its <i>mara de-atra</i>?
<i>R. Aharon Lichtenstein</i> responds to this question:
<span class="poetry indentAll"><small>The definition of the relevant community, however, is murky. The <i>gemara</i> speaks of locale, but it seems strange that geography should be the sole determinant … Would only residents of twelfth-century Egypt be entitled to rely upon the Rambam’s minority <i>kulot</i>? … It seems far more likely that other factors – ethnic identity or, above all … spiritual and ideological fealty – should carry no less weight … Physical proximity is obviously not intended here. What is envisioned is, evidently, a principled and consistent attachment … [S]piritual commitment rather than geographic continuity is the determining factor. A Sephardic congregation in Warsaw could be still bound by the rulings of the <i>Rishon le-Tzion</i>. Would a Gerer <i>hasid</i> cease to be part of the <i>Beis Yisrael</i>’s community just because he had moved to Paris?<sup class="footnote-marker">89</sup><i class="footnote">Aharon Lichtenstein, <i>Leaves of Faith: The World of Jewish Learning</i> (NY: 2004), vol. 2, 289.</i></small></span>
Upon the basis of this presentation, which is grounded in his interpretation of <i>Rashba</i>’s teachings,<sup class="footnote-marker">90</sup><i class="footnote">See text accompanying n. 56. A more explicit recognition of a <i>mara de-atra</i> advancing a macro halakhic tradition and ideology, such as <i>Beit Hillel</i> and <i>Beit Shammai</i>, may be found in the writings of <i>Rashba</i>’s teacher; see <i>Hiddushei ha-Ramban</i>, <i>Hullin</i> 43b.</i> <i>R. Lichtenstein</i> concludes:
<span class="poetry indentAll"><small>The implications for a contemporary Orthodox Jew’s legitimating of his response to modernity are self-evident. Were there no genuine <i>gadol</i> who had subscribed to the core halakhic positions of what is roughly denominated as Modern Orthodoxy, ordinary rabbis and laymen would be hard-put to cling to them … No objective observer of the American Torah scene – or the international scene, for that matter – can fail to acknowledge the Rav’s [R. Joseph B. Soloveitchik] position as one of the <i>gedolim</i> of this century, and his advocacy of the critical values of Modern Orthodoxy … On the central issues … he has been steadfast and consistent: on the interrelated questions of general culture, confronting the world and Religious Zionism … There can also be no question of his right to champion his positions. That right is relevant not only to the Rav personally, but to any declared member of his ideological community. Those who identify with his worldview and halakhic orientation can rightfully regard their similar views as legitimized by his authority – with the proviso, of course, that they generally submit to that authority.<sup class="footnote-marker">91</sup><i class="footnote">Supra n. 89, 289–90.</i></small></span>
Whereas, as we have seen, the members of community in a particular locale formally choose their <i>mara de-atra</i>, in this context, the ideological community residing in various places, without any formal mechanism of election, adopts their adherence to one <i>posek</i>’s rulings. This entails compliance with his positions regarding such macro issues as the legitimacy of secular education, the significance of the State of Israel, and relations with fellow Jews and non-Jews, but at the same time, it entails subservience to his rulings in all realms of Halakhah, such as <i>kashrut</i>, Shabbat, family relations, business matters, and the like. One cannot simply decide to follow a <i>mara de-atra</i>’s teachings related to his <i>weltanschauung</i> and abstain from compliance to his rulings dealing with daily halakhic concern.<sup class="footnote-marker">92</sup><i class="footnote">Whether he is “an-authority,” and his decisions accordingly become binding due to individual choice, or he is “in-authority,” and his decisions are thus binding upon individuals and noncompliance entails a violation of <i>lo tasur</i>, is subject to debate; see supra text accompanying nn. 8–18. Alternatively, individual choice is irrelevant and a conclave like the Sanhedrin is empowered to issue decisions for the entire Jewish community. See infra n. 93.</i> This notion of an ideological community being bound by a <i>mara de-atra</i> equally extends to following the teachings of an enclave of scholars, such as the <i>Moetzes Gedolei Hatorah</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">93</sup><i class="footnote">For precedents for viewing a collective body as a <i>mara de-atra</i>, see <i>Hullin</i> 43b; <i>Hiddushei ha-Ramban</i>, <i>Hullin</i> 43b. For discussion regarding the status of rabbinic groups convened after the cessation of the Sanhedrin, see <i>Teshuvot Ya’avetz</i> 153; Menachem Mendel Landau, <i>Sefer Meikitz Nirdamim</i> (Petrokov, 1904), 44, 65; <i>Kol Kitvei Maharatz Chayot</i>, 103; Elhanan Wasserman, <i>Kovetz Shiurim</i>, <i>Kuntres Divrei Soferim</i> 2:3; R. Shmuel Munk (attributed author), <i>Kuntres She’eilot u-Teshuvot be-Ein Hazon</i>, Introduction and no. 9.</i> Whether the guidelines that address the credentials and parameters of the <i>mara de-atra</i> in a particular geographical location apply to a <i>mara de-atra</i> of an ideological community are beyond the scope of this presentation.
<b>6. The <i>Psak</i> of a <i>Mara de-Atra</i>: Subservience to One’s Predecessors or Autonomous Judgment?</b>
Until this point, we have focused upon rabbinic authorities who are institutionally based, either within the context of the community, a <i>yeshiva</i>, or a synagogue. Whether these arbiters either live in physical proximity to their adherents, live elsewhere, or are no longer living, their adherents have a principled and committed attachment to their teachings. What remains to be addressed are the dynamics involved in a <i>mara de-atra</i>’s or a non-institutional rabbinic authority’s issuance of a <i>psak din</i> (decision). How does any <i>mara de-atra</i> – or, for that matter, any <i>posek</i> (arbiter) resolve a matter in question? Given the dissemination of <i>sifrei hiddushim</i> (novellae), <i>sifrei psak</i> (restatements), and <i>teshuvot</i> (responsa), ought an individual arbitrarily choose one ruling over another?
Expounding upon the dictum, “Designate yourself a teacher and remove doubt,”<sup class="footnote-marker">94</sup><i class="footnote">Supra n. 6.</i> <i>Yosef ibn Nahmias</i> cites his teacher, <i>R. Asher ben Yehiel</i> (<i>Rosh</i>):
<span class="poetry indentAll"><small>If he has a [legal] tradition from his master, he should act accordingly, and if he does not, he should choose one of the restatements from among [those written by] the great authors, one that he admires, and act in accordance with his decision.<sup class="footnote-marker">95</sup><i class="footnote">Commentary of Yosef ibn Nahmias on <i>Avot</i> 7a, quoted by Judah Galinsky, “Ashkenazim in Sefarad: The Rosh and the Tur on the Codification of Jewish Law,” 16 <i>The Jewish Law Annual</i> (2006), 3, 14–15.</i></small></span>
According to <i>Rosh</i>, in the presence of conflicting views regarding a point of Halakhah, one should either opt to abide by one’s teacher or, assuming one is learned and erudite, follow a specific restatement (such as the <i>Mishnah Torah</i>, <i>Tur</i>, or <i>Shulhan Arukh</i>). <i>Rosh</i> notes, however, regarding <i>Rambam</i>’s <i>Mishnah Torah</i> that, “One should refrain from relying upon his reading in this book to assess and render decisions unless one finds proof in the Talmud.”<sup class="footnote-marker">96</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot ha-Rosh</i> 31:9.</i> If a <i>sefer psak</i> characteristically lacks corroborating sources, one should preferably rely on one’s autonomous judgment rather than simply invoke the ruling found in the restatement. If one has the ability, he can overrule his predecessor by unearthing his sources and revealing his latent processes of reasoning.
However, <i>Rosh</i> introduces two qualifications regarding the right of a scholar to overrule an earlier opinion by adducing proofs for his own view:
<span class="poetry indentAll"><small>In cases where two scholars of stature argue regarding a halakhic determination, let not an arbiter say, “I will render a decision as I see fit.” If he acted in this manner, this is a false judgment – unless he was a scholar of stature, learned, and capable of reasoning and is able to resolve the matter according to one of the opinions based on clear and cogent proofs …<br>And I say that it is surely true that someone who erred regarding the rulings of the <i>Ge’onim</i> because he had not heard of their view, but when he was informed of the Geonic decision it appeared correct to him, he has erred in black-letter Halakhah (<i>ta’ut be-devar Mishnah</i>). And the same is true not only of Geonic rulings, but also of the scholars of every generation … Should their words not appear agreeable to him and he cites proofs for his position which are acceptable to his generation, then “Yiftah in his generation is like Samuel in his.”<sup class="footnote-marker">97</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Piskei ha-Rosh</i>, <i>Sanhedrin</i> 4:6. For this explanation of his position, see <i>Kovetz Shiurim</i>, <i>Bava Batra</i> 272. For a similar approach, see <i>Iggerot Moshe</i>, OH 1, Introduction; YD 1:101, 3:88. There are times when R. Feinstein would rely upon the stature of the authority as a grounds for a decision; see <i>Iggerot Moshe</i>, OH 1:39, 5:13, 24.</i></small></span>
Halakhah limits the finality of a judgment (<i>res judicata</i>) by distinguishing an error committed by an arbiter regarding fundamental law, such as a matter explicit and unanimously accepted in the Mishnah, Tosefta, or Talmud (<i>ta’ut be-devar Mishnah</i>), in which case the decision is reversible, and error in resolving a disputed point of law (<i>ta’ut be-shikul ha-da’at</i>), in which case the decision is final. <i>Rosh</i> argues that the opinions of the <i>Ge’onim</i> and <i>Rishonim</i> are accorded the status of <i>devar Mishnah</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">98</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sanhedrin</i> 33a; <i>Mishnah Torah</i>, <i>Hilkhot Sanhedrin</i> 6:1; <i>Beit ha-Behirah</i>, <i>Sanhedrin</i> 33a. For an extensive discussion of <i>Rishonim</i> regarding this subject, see my “The Finality of a Judicial Decision in Jewish Law” [Hebrew] (unpublished doctoral dissertation; Hebrew University Faculty of Law, 1984).</i> Accordingly, if an arbiter ignores their rulings in favor of another view, it is construed as an error in <i>devar Mishnah</i>. Furthermore, the stature of these writings obligates a halakhist to be aware of their existence; failing to take them into account at the time of the decision renders the <i>psak</i> null and void if it contradicts his predecessors. Nevertheless, <i>Rosh</i> argues that a scholar who is aware of these early opinions and yet adduces clear and compelling proofs (<i>berurot u-nekhonot</i>) is entitled to overrule these opinions, provided that these proofs are agreeable (<i>mekubalot</i>) to his own generation.
Following in the footsteps of <i>Rosh</i>, <i>R. Moshe Isserles</i> (<i>Rema</i>) states:
<span class="poetry indentAll"><small>If it seems to the scholar and his contemporaries, based upon compelling proofs, that the Halakhah is at variance with what is mentioned among the decisors, one may disagree with them, since it is not mentioned in the Talmud …<sup class="footnote-marker">99</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Rema</i>, HM 25:1. See also, <i>Rema</i>, YD 242:3, <i>Hazon Ish</i>, YD 150, <i>Hazon Ish</i>, HM, Likkutim 1.</i></small></span>
According to <i>Rema</i>, overruling one’s predecessor(s) is contingent upon advancing “compelling proofs” which are acceptable to the arbiter and his contemporaries. The “compelling proofs” in this context may either be proofs based upon a halakhic tradition of arbiters’ rulings or convincing proofs without halakhic precedent.<sup class="footnote-marker">100</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Rav Betzalel Ashkenazi</i> 1 adopts the latter interpretation.</i> Whether the unearthing of sources for scrutiny and potential dissent is limited to the Talmud, <i>Ge’onim</i>, and <i>Rishonim</i> or encompasses the teachings of <i>Aharonim</i> as well is subject to much debate.<sup class="footnote-marker">101</sup><i class="footnote">Based upon the Talmudic discussion of the finality of judgment and judicial malpractice (see text accompanying n. 98), divergent conclusions have been drawn. See <i>Mishnah Torah</i>, supra n. 98; <i>Teshuvot ha-Rambam</i> (Blau edition) 2:310; <i>Piskei ha-Rosh</i>, <i>Sanhedrin</i> 4:6; <i>Tur</i>, HM 25; <i>Ba’al ha-Ma’or</i>, <i>Sanhedrin</i> 12a; <i>SA</i>, HM 25:1 and commentaries ad loc.</i>
Relying upon the view of <i>R. Yisrael Isserlin</i> (<i>Terumat ha-Deshen</i>),<sup class="footnote-marker">102</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Terumat ha-Deshen</i> 2:241. In an earlier <i>teshuva</i> (233), a distinction is made between “compositions which are widespread” and “compositions which are unknown.”</i> <i>Rema</i> adds the following caveat:
<span class="poetry indentAll"><small>However, one cannot be lenient regarding strictures found in compositions (<i>hibburim</i>) which have spread throughout the majority of Israel, unless one has a tradition from one’s teachers that we do not practice this particular stricture.<sup class="footnote-marker">103</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Rema</i>, HM 25:1. The phrase “books which have been spread throughout the majority of Israel” brings to mind the Talmudic comment, “We are accustomed to daily decisions being handed down in this manner;” see <i>Ketuvot</i> 68b, 95b; <i>Bava Batra</i> 163b. Precedent for <i>Rema</i>’s position regarding the significance of the dissemination of the books can be found in <i>Ran</i>, <i>Avodah Zarah</i> 36a (<i>Rif</i> pagination). See <i>Bi’ur ha-Gra</i>, HM 25:7.</i></small></span>
<i>Rema</i>’s reference to “books” and <i>Rosh</i>’s focus upon “early authorities” is an implicit adoption of the thirteenth century Ashkenazic tradition that “[halakhic] compositions … are the teachers of men.”<sup class="footnote-marker">104</sup><i class="footnote">See supra text accompanying nn. 62–63.</i>
In contrast, <i>Maharam Alsheikh</i>, <i>Maharam ibn Haviv</i>, and <i>Shakh</i> argue that even if the individual has the requisite credentials, if there is a significant disparity of knowledge between the predecessor and himself, it is improper for him to overrule his predecessor.<sup class="footnote-marker">105</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Maharam Alsheikh</i> 39; <i>Get Pashut</i>, <i>Kelalim</i>, <i>Kelal</i> 3; <i>Shakh</i>, HM 25:21.</i>
<i>R. J. David Bleich</i> invokes the significance of considering normative Halakhah as the grounds for one’s <i>psak</i> prior to issuing an autonomous judgment:
<span class="poetry indentAll"><small>Leniencies and permissive rulings exist in abundance. The point is to seek neither the stringent nor the lenient, but the view that is most authoritative. Moreover, there usually is a view which has been accepted in practice by the majority of <i>Poskim</i> as the accepted standard. Thereupon, such a ruling becomes normative and deviation cannot be considered other than by virtue of compelling reasons.<sup class="footnote-marker">106</sup><i class="footnote">J. David Bleich, <i>Contemporary Halakhic Problems</i> (NY: 1995), vol. 4, Introduction, 13–14.</i></small></span>
Adopting “our teachers are the books” as a basis for decision-making poses a formidable challenge for every arbiter. Whether one is a recognized authority or a <i>mara de-atra</i>, the <i>posek</i> has a daunting task. Prior to rendering a decision, the arbiter must acknowledge his own finitude in terms of the level of his own halakhic knowledge compared to his contemporaries and predecessors, as well as recognize the importance of rendering a <i>psak</i> pursuant to normative Halakhah regarding the matter he is addressing. Should compelling reasons dictate the need to deviate from normative Halakhah, utilizing the canons of decision-making, he may render an autonomous judgment. To arrive at a <i>psak</i>, the <i>posek</i> must delve into commentaries on the Talmud and responsa literature, which generally reveal their reasoning processes, as well as the restatements and rabbinic court judgments, which are frequently devoid of proofs and explicit reasoning.<sup class="footnote-marker">107</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sanhedrin</i> 3:7; <i>Sanhedrin</i> 30a, 31b; <i>SA</i>, HM 22:2.</i>
It is worth noting that we have thus far referred to <i>sifrei psak</i> as “restatements,” rather than the more conventional translation, “codes.” A code is characterized by a sense of certitude and finality. Frequently, its rules are in canonical form; they have been given a particular authoritative formulation. We have avoided this translation because a <i>sefer psak</i> is actually merely a restatement of a particular area of Halakhah that remains subject to future debate and interpretation.<sup class="footnote-marker">108</sup><i class="footnote">M. Elon, supra n. 35, vol. 2, 1144–8.</i> Thus, Professor Isadore Twersky writes:
<span class="poetry indentAll"><small>Any student who follows the course of rabbinic literature from the Geonic works of the eighth century through the <i>Mishnah Torah</i> and <i>Turim</i> and on down to the <i>Shulhan Arukh</i> cannot ignore this see-saw tendency … No sooner is the need for codification met than a wave of non-codifactory work rises. A restatement, even in the eyes of its admirers, required vigilant explanation and judicious application.<sup class="footnote-marker">109</sup><i class="footnote">Isadore Twersky, <i>Studies in Jewish Law and Philosophy</i> (NY: 1982), 138.</i></small></span>
Although <i>sifrei psak</i> are characterized by their comprehensiveness, systematization, abstractness, and <i>ko’ah ha-hiddush</i> (creativity), their rulings did not nullify existing opinions within the halakhic system, opinions which are also authoritative or potentially may become authoritative in the future.
Moreover, the legist’s creative-interpretative role remains attempting to clarify ambiguities and gaps in the restatement’s rulings. In fact, according to <i>Maharal</i>, even if a scholar with credential errs in interpreting a passage in the Talmud, it is preferable to follow one’s interpretation rather than abide by a restatement “like a blind traveler on a highway.”<sup class="footnote-marker">110</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Netivot Olam</i>, <i>Netiv ha-Torah</i>, ch. 15. Indeed, we find him “even disagreeing with the greatest of the <i>Rishonim</i>.” See <i>Teshuvot Noda be-Yehuda</i>, YD 2:63.</i> On the other hand, <i>Maharshal</i> frowns upon relying upon independent judgment without being aware of “the books.”<sup class="footnote-marker">111</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Yam Shel Shlomo</i>, <i>Bava Kama</i> and <i>Hullin</i>, Introduction.</i>
<i>R. Yehoshua ben Yosef</i> (<i>Pnei Yehoshua</i>), <i>R. Ya’akov Lorberbaum</i> (<i>Netivot ha-Mishpat</i>), <i>R. Menachem Krochmal</i> (<i>Tzemach Tzedek</i>), and others contend that the explicit rulings of the <i>Shulhan Arukh</i> and/or <i>Rema</i> have the status of “<i>devar Mishnah</i>.” Hence, any dissenting judgments handed down after these rulings are null and void.<sup class="footnote-marker">112</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Pnei Yehoshua</i>, vol. 2, EH 47; <i>Netivot ha-Mishpat</i>, <i>Kelalei Tefisah</i>, HM 25:20; <i>Teshuvot Tzemach Tzedek</i> 9. Cf. numerous Sephardic legists who contend that <i>Shulhan Arukh</i>’s rulings have the status of “<i>shikul ha-da’at.</i>” See Ben-Menahem, Hecht, and Wosner, supra n. 7, 952–3; <i>Hazon Ish,</i> HM, Likkutim 1; <i>Hazon Ish</i>, YD 150; <i>Teshuvot Havazelet ha-Sharon</i>, EH 28.</i> In fact, in accordance with <i>Tumim</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">113</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Kitzur Takfo Kohen</i>, 48(4).</i> should an opinion fail to be cited in <i>Beit Yosef</i>, <i>Shulhan Arukh</i> or <i>Rema</i>, we should not factor such a view in rendering a <i>psak</i>. However, numerous commentators on <i>Shulhan Arukh</i> view it as akin to any other <i>sefer psak</i> whose rulings can be overruled. Regardless of the source of the <i>Shulhan Arukh</i>’s authority,<sup class="footnote-marker">114</sup><i class="footnote">For the differing approaches to the significance of the rabbinic affirmation of <i>Shulhan Arukh</i>, see Yaakov Hayyim Sofer, “Regarding Maran, Who Received the Approbation of 200 Rabbis” [Hebrew], <i>Zekhor le-Avraham</i> (Holon, 5754–55), 27.</i> its decisions were subject to much debate and disagreement.<sup class="footnote-marker">115</sup><i class="footnote">A cursory glance of <i>Shulhan Arukh</i>’s commentaries certainly corroborates such a conclusion.</i>
The absence of finality regarding a majority opinion and the <i>Shulhan Arukh</i>’s rulings is reflected in the policy of “<i>ko’ah de-hetera adif</i>” (the power of rendering a lenient ruling is preferable). While a lenient view may have been overruled, it can be taken into account under certain conditions in arriving at a <i>psak</i>.
<i>Rashba</i> establishes the following guidelines concerning the implementation of this rule:
<span class="poetry indentAll"><small>It is normative Halakhah that we rule according to the superior opinion in wisdom and in number, and even in an emergency situation; we do not rely upon the opinion that is inferior in number and in wisdom. A similar rule applies in a controversy between the individual and majority view, unless it is an emergency-situation, when there is a danger of financial loss or the like.<sup class="footnote-marker">116</sup><i class="footnote">See supra n. 59.</i></small></span>
In cases of disputes between arbiters of equal scholarly stature, we may pronounce, in Talmudic parlance, “Rabbi X is worthy to be relied upon in a dire need” (one may rely upon a minority lenient opinion in times of emergency).<sup class="footnote-marker">117</sup><i class="footnote">For definitional guidance, see <i>Or Zarua</i>, <i>Sukkah</i> 2:306; <i>Teshuvot Tashbetz</i> 1:75; <i>Teshuvot Binyan Olam</i>, OH 14. Regarding <i>agunah</i> matters, see <i>SA</i>, EH 127:2. To rely upon this principle, there must be a consensus that indeed this is a state of emergency; see <i>Teshuvot Rabbi Akiva Eiger</i> 205; <i>Teshuvot Emunat Shmuel</i> 24.</i>
The right of an arbiter to exercise discretion, relying upon an individual opinion in cases of emergency and significant financial loss, even when the majority rules against that view, is firmly rooted in Talmudic precedent.<sup class="footnote-marker">118</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Berakhot</i> 9a; <i>Shabbat</i> 45a; <i>Gittin</i> 18b; <i>Niddah</i> 6a, 9b.</i> <i>Radakh</i> and <i>Hazon Ish</i><sup class="footnote-marker">119</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Radakh</i>, <i>bayit</i> 3, <i>heder</i> 1; <i>Hazon Ish</i>, YD 160:3–4.</i> argue that that the presence of a majority view is to be construed as “<i>itmar hilkhisa</i>” – the <i>halakhah</i> has been decided and one therefore cannot impart legitimacy to an individual opinion even in extenuating circumstances. It appears that in their view, “abiding by the majority” in halakhic controversy is akin to following the majority in a <i>beit din</i> proceeding. However, <i>Rashbatz</i>, <i>Rashbash</i>, <i>Bah</i>, <i>Rema</i>, <i>Shakh</i>, <i>Taz</i>, and <i>Noda be-Yehuda</i> disagree.<sup class="footnote-marker">120</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Hiddushei u-Piskei ha-Rashbatz</i>, <i>Niddah</i> 9b; <i>Teshuvot Rashbash</i> 513; <i>Bah</i>, YD, <i>Kuntres Aharon</i>; <i>Rema</i>, HM 25:2; <i>Shakh</i>, YD 242; <i>Taz</i>, YD 293:3; <i>Teshuvot Noda be-Yehuda</i>, vol. 1, YD 21, vol. 2, EH 9. Cf. <i>Teshuvot Binyan Olam</i>, YD 50, who argues that this rule may be only invoked in cases enumerated in the Talmud. Although limited to ritual matters (see <i>Teshuvot Yakhin u-Boaz</i> 2:33), some contend that this rule applies even to biblical prohibitions; see <i>Bah</i>, ibid.; <i>Taz</i>, ibid. Cf. <i>Teshuvot Yakhin u-Boaz</i> 2:8; <i>Shakh</i>, ibid.; <i>Nekudat ha-Kesef</i>, YD 293. According to <i>Rashba</i> and <i>Bah</i>, this principle is operative only in the absence of a decisive ruling in either direction and the views are divided equally. However, one may adhere to the lenient opinion provided that the authority is of greater erudition than the advocate of the stricter view. Should the authority of the strict view be of greater prominence, one must abide by his view. Finally, one cannot rely upon an individual lenient opinion in case of pressing need without accompanying financial loss. However, <i>be-diavad</i> (ex post facto), if the rendering of the <i>psak</i> affects the arbiter’s honor, we construe the situation as akin to “financial need” and give credence to the position. See <i>Teshuvot Rabbi Akiva Eiger</i> 1:122.<br>Others argue that in cases in which a matter has been resolved based upon the rules of halakhic decision-making, such as “<i>halakhah ke-Shemuel be-mamona</i>” or “<i>mishnat Rabbi Yossi kav ve-naki</i>,” then one may rely on a minority view in a situation of a pressing need. See <i>Teshuvot Hakham Tzvi</i> 11.</i> Whereas a majority view is determinative following a judicial proceeding, the various authorities debating a halakhic issue generally function as discrete individuals adducing evidence for their respective positions, rather than as a group sitting together arriving at a reasoned judgment. Since halakhic controversy transcends geographic location and time period, one can clearly conclude that “<i>lo itmar hilkhisa</i>” – there is no definitive ruling, and one can therefore rely on an individual opinion in a time of emergency and significant financial loss. The fact that one can ascertain the position of the majority is irrelevant;<sup class="footnote-marker">121</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Rashbatz</i>, supra n. 120; <i>Rashbash</i>, supra n. 120; R. Avraham Kook, <i>Shabbat ha-Aretz</i>, Introduction, ch. 10.</i> the fact that the majority never deliberated face to face with the minority is determinative.<sup class="footnote-marker">122</sup><i class="footnote">Some contend that even if the decision was resolved in favor of the majority, as long as the controversy has not been determined on its merits, one can rely upon the individual opinion. Others claim that if the Talmud explicitly rejects the minority opinion, one can never rely upon it.</i>
The legitimacy of recourse to an individual opinion under these conditions equally becomes a source of debate regarding the <i>Shulhan Arukh</i>. Are its rulings to be characterized as “<i>itmar hilkhisa</i>” or not? Whereas <i>Hazon Ish</i>, pursuant to his earlier cited position, and <i>R. Naftali Tzvi Berlin</i> argue that its decisions are akin to a majority view and one may therefore not rely on a different minority opinion even in extenuating circumstances,<sup class="footnote-marker">123</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Hazon Ish</i>, supra n. 118; <i>Teshuvot Meishiv Davar</i> 4:80.</i> <i>R. Tzvi ben Ya’akov Ashkenazi</i> (<i>Hakham Tzvi</i>) argues that the <i>Shulhan Arukh</i>’s rulings are categorized as “<i>de-lo itmar hilkhisa</i>,” similar to any other <i>sefer psak</i> that is subject to debate and overruling.<sup class="footnote-marker">124</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Hakham Tzvi</i> 100. For the underpinnings of this position, see the Sephardic sources mentioned supra n. 112.</i> However, an arbiter cannot rule in accordance with a minority opinion of scholarly eminence – even if some authorities agree to the position and even in a situation of financial need and emergency – if a definitive strict ruling has crystallized in rulings of the authorities and the ruling has become widespread throughout the majority of Jewish communities, unless there is a tradition that it is not the practice to abide by a strict opinion.<sup class="footnote-marker">125</sup><i class="footnote">See text accompanying n. 117. Cf. <i>Teshuvot Binyan Olam</i>, YD 50.</i>
The requisite need to engage in interpretation of our restatements equally applies to published decisions of Jewish courts. Because there is no general obligation to provide a reasoned judgment in a <i>beit din</i> proceeding,<sup class="footnote-marker">126</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sanhedrin</i> 3:7; <i>Sanhedrin</i> 31b.</i> most decisions are rendered without accompanying rationales. Here again, it becomes the arbiter’s responsibility to delve into these rulings in order to uncover the grounds for the ruling, rather than simply accepting them at face value, lest one erroneously misinterpret them. In sum, “our teachers are our books” is actually a formidable challenge to an authority who accepts the awesome responsibility for rendering judgment.
The absence of finality of a <i>psak</i> is also true of <i>she’eilot u-teshuvot</i> (responsa) literature, in which the facts of the case under discussion are usually clear and the decision is accompanied by reasoned corroboration. When the son of <i>R. Akiva Eiger</i>, a renowned eighteenth century legist, asked his father for permission to publish his responsa under the name “<i>Pesakim u-Ketavim</i>,” <i>R. Eiger</i> responds:
<span class="poetry indentAll"><small>And regarding my dear son’s request, if it is favorable in my eyes to describe these <i>pesakim</i> and these writings … The name “<i>pesakim</i>” is improper, because it seems that this is the final decision for me and one cannot disagree with it … It is difficult for me to hear that upon the appearance of halakhic composition some authorities abide by its rulings. Such an approach is improper, since they are responsible to investigate the matter thoroughly; if, after proper examination, they find that the author’s teachings are correct, then they should follow him. But to believe whatever is found in the written word … is not from the ways of wisdom.<sup class="footnote-marker">127</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Rabbi Akiva Eiger</i>, p. 1. Regarding R. Eiger’s stature, see R. C. Berlin, <i>Meromei Sadeh</i>, Introduction; <i>Teshuvot Rabbi Eliezer</i>, Introduction.</i></small></span>
Thus, <i>R. Eiger</i>, who some view as having the stature almost equal to that of a <i>Rishon</i> (early authority), preconditioned any acceptance of a ruling upon exercising one’s autonomous judgment.
<b>7. The Role of <i>Hekesh</i> (Analogical Reasoning) in the Rendering of a <i>Psak</i></b>
The ability to analyze and potentially overrule one’s predecessor, whether he be the author of a <i>teshuvah</i> or a <i>sefer psak</i>, requires that the arbiter (including a <i>mara de-atra or a dayan</i>) possess <i>yirat Shamayim</i> (religious piety) and scholarship. In his decision-making, he must weigh the significance of contrary precedent opinion and exercise logic as well. Clearly, many questions posed to an arbiter can be answered by simply opening <i>sifrei psak</i> and <i>teshuvot</i>. In order to address new questions, however, being conversant with these sources does not suffice.
Borrowing Talmudic terminology, <i>Rosh</i> argues that an arbiter must engage in a special type of logic, “<i>medameh milta le-milta</i>,” applying analogical inferences for the purpose of discerning the similarities and differences between cases in order to address new situations.<sup class="footnote-marker">128</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot ha-Rosh</i>, <i>kelal</i> 55:9 and 78:3. There is an ongoing debate about which types of reasoning are permissible in <i>pesikah</i>. See, for example, <i>Or Zarua</i> 2:33; <i>Teshuvot Mahari Weil</i> 164; <i>Teshuvot Terumat ha-Deshen</i> 16; <i>Teshuvot Mahari Bruna</i> 92; <i>Teshuvot ha-Rashba</i> 2:322; <i>She’alat David</i>, <i>Kuntres ha-Hiddushin</i>, 73.</i> Implicitly relying upon Talmudic precedent, <i>R. Avraham</i> the son of <i>Rambam</i> expounds upon the nature of this type of logic:
<span class="poetry indentAll"><small>An arbiter who in his decisions follows only what is written and explicitly stated is weak … In every decision that he is considering, he should analogize his case to something that is similar to it and derive branches from those roots … They [Talmudic precedents] were preserved for a reason … in order that the wise man, hearing them frequently, will develop his ability to weigh the matters with discretion and render decisions properly.</small></span>
The vitality and effervescence of the decision-making process is maintained when the arbiter seeks to resolve a problem by searching for examples that may serve as fruitful sources for comparison. In comparing these precedents to the case at hand, the arbiter analyzes the similarities and differences between them, deriving a tentative analogy that posits a logical relationship between the examples and the problem. Finally, the arbiter confirms the rule by inquiring whether it meets the guidelines for invoking an analogy in this particular situation.<sup class="footnote-marker">129</sup><i class="footnote">For other examples, see my “A Parent’s Decision to Withhold Medical Treatment from Children – A Case Study in Competing Analogies,” 17 <i>Dinei Israel</i> (1993–94), 35; “The Investment Advisor: Liabilities and Halachic Identity,” 58 <i>Journal of Halacha and Contemporary Society</i> (2009),107; “Self-Dealing in the Not-for-Profit Board Room: An Inquiry into a Trustee’s Multifaceted Halakhic Identity,” 43 <i>Tradition</i> (Spring 2010), 7 and “The Multi-Faceted Halakhic Identity of a Jewish Investment Broker,” 43 <i>Tradition</i> (Fall 2010), 51.</i> In effect, the method of analogy, unlike ordinary open-ended reasoning, starts with the prior decisions of one’s predecessors, rather the arbiter’s own reasoning and intuition.
The practice of analogical reasoning reflects the ongoing dialectic between deference to early authorities and creative innovation. The spur to originality and creativeness, <i>ko’ah ha</i>-<i>hiddush</i>, is tempered by the commitment to thoroughly investigate a wealth of sources and the collaborative effort of numerous arbiters over time who may have addressed similar and/or identical problems. The weightiness of precedent opinion facilitates avoidance of flawed and misplaced reasoning. The engagement in “<i>medameh milta le-milta</i>” is thus concomitantly a stimulant and a depressant – a spur to creativity, but equally a motive for restraint.
As the Talmud informs us:
<span class="poetry indentAll"><small>Once a person has asked and was informed that a <i>halakhah</i> was to be taken as a guide for practical decisions, he may continue to give practical decisions accordingly, provided he draws no comparisons. But surely, in the entire domain of the Torah comparisons are made! <i>R. Ashi</i> said: It is this that was meant: Provided one draws no comparisons in ritual questions related to <i>treifot</i> [diseased animals which, though ritually slaughtered, are forbidden to be eaten] …<sup class="footnote-marker">130</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Bava Batra</i> 130b.</i></small></span>
The Talmud argues that the application of “<i>medameh milta le-milta</i>” is embedded and integrated within the entire corpus of Halakhah, allowing the arbiter to analogize from one branch to another. Upon further reflection, pursuant to <i>R. Ashi’</i>s posture, extreme care must be given prior to comparing one case to another, lest one succumb to a mistaken analogy.<sup class="footnote-marker">131</sup><i class="footnote">This conclusion is reiterated in <i>Gittin</i> 19a and 37a.</i> According to <i>R. Yair Bachrach</i> (<i>Havot Ya’ir</i>), a mistaken analogy is in fact subsumed in the category of a “<i>ta’ut be-devar Mishnah</i>,” an error in black-letter Halakhah, which means that such a decision is null and void.<sup class="footnote-marker">132</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Havot Ya’ir</i> 45.</i>
While the Talmud employs this type of dialectic in the contexts of both <i>issura</i> (ritual law) and <i>mamona</i> (monetary matters),<sup class="footnote-marker">133</sup><i class="footnote">Moshe Silberg, <i>Talmudic Law and the Modern State</i> (NY: 1973), 65–66, cited by Elon, supra n. 35, 111.</i> the Talmud itself exhorts us not to draw an analogy between the two; “no analogy in a manner involving ritual law may be established from monetary matter,”<sup class="footnote-marker">134</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Berakhot</i> 19b.</i> and conversely, “no analogy in a monetary matter may be established from a matter concerning ritual law.”<sup class="footnote-marker">135</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ketuvot</i> 46b.</i>
These statements leave us with many questions. Are there any situations in which one can derive a norm in monetary matters from a norm in ritual matters or vice versa? Is it possible to draw an analogy to ritual matters from a norm in a matter comprised of both ritual and monetary aspects, or an analogy to monetary matters from a norm in a matter comprised of both ritual and monetary characteristics? Can one draw an analogy between two commandments, two rules of Shabbat, two <i>minhagim</i> (customs), or two legal presumptions? Can we compare legal fictions from different spheres or permit a legal fiction based upon the use of analogy? Is it possible to draw an analogical inference when there is an explicit proof in the Talmud but later teachings lead to a contrary conclusion? Can one analogize from the portion of legal thinking that has no bearing on the conclusion (<i>obiter dictum</i>), or must such reasoning be limited to the reasoning essential to the conclusion (<i>ratio decidendi</i>)? In monetary matters, may one rely upon a minority lenient opinion in emergency circumstances, as is the case in ritual matters (“Rabbi X is worthy to be relied upon in a dire need”)? Can one analogize from one piece of legislation to another, from <i>hilkhot mikveh</i> to <i>hilkhot Sukkot</i>?
These questions and many others pose significant challenges to the arbiter as he begins the process of reasoning by analogy. Given the penchant for analogical reasoning and that this reasoning is viewed as infinitely malleable, it is not surprising that the Talmud mandates that prior to invoking an analogy, the arbiter must seek advice from a colleague who is of greater scholarly stature than himself regarding the propriety of this reasoning.<sup class="footnote-marker">136</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Yevamot</i> 109b. Some argue that consultation with “the books” rather than a great scholar fulfills this obligation. See <i>Teshuvot Yabia Omer</i>, OH 1, Introduction, p. 10. Although the Talmud does not mandate consultation with another scholar for matters that do not involve the exercise of this type of reasoning, some authorities nevertheless require it; see <i>Teshuvot Maharshal</i> 35; <i>Teshuvot ha</i>-<i>Mabit</i> 3:93.</i> The classical restatements, including <i>Mishnah Torah</i>, <i>Tur</i>, and <i>Shulhan Arukh</i>, reiterate this need for consultation.<sup class="footnote-marker">137</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Mishnah Torah</i>, <i>Hilkhot Sanhedrin</i> 20:8, <i>Tur</i>, HM 10; <i>Beit Yosef</i>, HM 10:3; <i>SA</i> 10:2. This requirement equally applies to a <i>mara de-atra</i>; see <i>Birkei Yosef</i>, HM 10:3.</i> Their positions have been understood as mandating this requirement even if the arbiter is free of doubt regarding how to resolve the case. In fact, according to certain authorities, even if he has consulted a greater scholar, he is obligated to confer with him again regarding a similar case, lest he assume that the second case is analogous to the first.<sup class="footnote-marker">138</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Rashi</i>, <i>Yevamot</i> 109b; <i>Bah</i>, HM 10:3; <i>Perishah</i>, HM, ad loc. Cf. <i>Nimmukei Yosef</i>, <i>Bava Batra</i> 58b (<i>Rif</i> pagination); <i>Ketzot ha-Hoshen</i>, HM 14:1. One of the opinions cited by the <i>Hukkot ha-Dayanim</i>, HM 3:3 extends the logic of this position by arguing that if the arbiter is personally confident in the cogency of his analogical reasoning, he may refrain from conferring with a greater scholar; see <i>Teshuvot Mishpetei Uziel</i>, HM 1:3.</i>
Analogical reasoning coupled with the requirement of consultation with one’s colleagues counteracts the errors of open-ended judgment. Reliance serves as a reason for the arbiter to align his own decisions to prior decisions of other arbiters.
Over-reliance on earlier opinions bereft of rationale equally has its pitfalls. As the Talmud Yerushalmi observes, “All agree that we do not learn the law from a previous ruling.”<sup class="footnote-marker">139</sup><i class="footnote">Yerushalmi <i>Hagigah</i> 1:8.</i> One of the commentators notes there:
<span class="poetry indentAll"><small>If one scholar ruled on an issue of Halakhah, one should refrain from determining the <i>halakhah</i> accordingly, since one might be wrong regarding the scholar’s reasons for that decision, since there are many who err in their studies.<sup class="footnote-marker">140</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Korban ha-Eidah</i>, ibid.</i></small></span>
Thus, conforming to past decisions that are devoid of reasoned judgments must be eschewed.
Even reasoned judgments of predecessors cannot guarantee proper decision-making. As <i>R. Yeshaya ben Yisrael Basan</i>, an eighteenth century Italian scholar, elucidates:
<span class="poetry indentAll"><small>All these arbiters who stick like glue (<i>to’ke’ah be-devar halakhah</i>) to a particular <i>teshuvah</i> without examining the roots of the manner are not arbiters at all. <i>Rivash</i> of Barcelona … in replying to someone who wanted to rely upon one of his rulings … answered that it should not be used as a precedent because he might have followed <i>Rashba</i> … since Barcelona was under his jurisdiction. And he may have … had his own reasons for deciding in this manner … Not everyone has the privilege of learning from responsa … since frequently their points are statements which do not form a necessary part of the scholar’s ruling.<sup class="footnote-marker">141</sup><i class="footnote">Quoted in <i>Pahad Yitzhak</i>, part 1, p. 325; translation culled from Zorach Warhaftig, “Precedent in Jewish Law,” in H. Ben-Menahem and N. Hecht (eds.), <i>Authority, Process, and Method</i> (London: 1998), 1, 22–23. This position has its Talmudic antecedent in <i>Bava Batra</i> 130b, which addresses the potential pitfalls of drawing erroneous conclusions from actual cases.</i></small></span>
Hence, reliance on precedent should be weighed properly in the course of analogical reasoning.
<b>8. The Prerequisite of “<i>Yirat Shamayim</i>”</b>
The value of reliance is inextricably connected with the arbiter’s concern for <i>yirat hora’ah</i>, the fear of rendering a decision. This fear is engendered in turn by the <i>posek</i>’s <i>yirat Shamayim</i>, his awareness that the halakhic system is reflective of the will of God. The opinions examined during his deliberations are characterized as <i>divrei Elokim hayyim</i>, the words of a living God. Accordingly, “any arbiter who fails to adjudicate a truthful judgment causes the departure of the divine presence from Israel.”<sup class="footnote-marker">142</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sanhedrin</i> 7a. As <i>Maharam Schick</i> observes (<i>Teshuvot Maharam Schick</i>, YD 331), arbiters commit errors; this is “part of the human condition.” They therefore choose to consult with others in order to share any ensuing punishment due to an erroneous judgment. See <i>Sanhedrin</i> 7a and <i>Rashi</i> ad loc.; <i>Teshuvot Pesakim ve-Ketavim</i> 130; <i>Teshuvot Maharil</i> 127; <i>Teshuvot Maharam Mintz</i> 21.<br>It is unsurprising that many decisors recommended that cases being resolved in a <i>beit din</i> setting should be based upon discretion or <i>pesharah</i> (compromise), rather than <i>din Torah</i> (the formal rules of Halakhah), lest punishment ensue due to errors in their decision-making. See <i>Sefer Mitzvot Gadol</i>, positive commandment 107; <i>Sefer Mitzvot Katan</i>, mitzvah 227; <i>Hagahot Maimoniyot</i>, <i>Sanhedrin</i> 24:2; <i>Tur</i>, HM 12; <i>Bah</i>, HM 12:6; <i>SA</i>, HM 12:20. For the precedent for resolving matters based upon <i>pesharah</i> or discretion, see Tosefta <i>Sanhedrin</i> 5:1; Yerushalmi <i>Sanhedrin</i> 1:1; <i>Sanhedrin</i> 5a.</i>
As a pious individual, an arbiter who engages in arbitrary or capricious judgment must always fear that punishment will ensue, either in this world or the world to come. Nonetheless, the Talmud observes:
<span class="poetry indentAll"><small>An arbiter might exclaim: What do I need all this anguish [assuming the personal responsibility of rendering a decision]? To him the Bible responds: “He [God] is with you in giving judgment.” An arbiter should be guided by his own understanding.<sup class="footnote-marker">143</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sanhedrin</i> 6b.</i></small></span>
<i>Rashi</i> explains that as long as the legist utilizes his knowledge and understanding, he need not fear punishment.<sup class="footnote-marker">144</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Rashi</i>, <i>Sanhedrin</i> 6b.</i> <i>Yirat hora’ah</i> should not lead to an abdication of responsibility. Indeed, the Talmud writes that if an individual who has the credentials to adjudicate refuses to render a <i>psak</i>, the consequence is “<i>va-atzumin kol harugeha</i>,” “many are her slain.”<sup class="footnote-marker">145</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Avodah Zarah</i> 19b.</i>
An arbiter who engages in interpretation, analogy, and logical inference while weighing the import of tradition and precedent is walking a tightrope, but as long as his deliberation is marked by deep-seated humility in resolving <i>devar Hashem</i>, he will not be punished. This is succinctly articulated by <i>R. Aryeh Leib Heller</i> in an oft-cited opinion:
<span class="poetry indentAll"><small>Man is fearful lest he err in anything he may state regarding the Torah, and human reason … pursues the truth and in particular the Torah’s intent … But the Torah was not given to the ministering angels; God gave the Torah to man, who possesses human reason … God said: It is my desire that “truth should emerge from the earth” … and truth will be what the scholars agreed upon, as per their human reason …<sup class="footnote-marker">146</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ketzot ha-Hoshen</i>, Introduction.</i></small></span>
This requisite sense of humility is engendered by the simple fact that an arbiter is functioning within a religious legal system, but it is also stimulated by the mentality of “the decline of the generations.” The sense that later scholars are inferior to their predecessors is epitomized in various Talmudic aphorisms:
<span class="poetry indentAll"><small>If the earlier sages were sons of angels, we are but sons of men, and if they were sons of men, we are like asses … Better was the fingernail of the earlier generation than the entire later generation.<sup class="footnote-marker">147</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Shabbat</i> 112b; <i>Eruvin</i> 53a.</i></small></span>
This notion of decline in scholarship informed the thinking of numerous decisors during post-Talmudic times. Extraneous factors, such as social instability and the prevalence of disease (such as during the period of the Black Plague, 1348–1350), coupled with this sense of modesty, led arbiters to describe their historical situation as a generation “enshrouded by the darkness of the intellect and error.” They describe themselves as “dwarfs sitting on the shoulders of giants,” “orphans of orphans,” “like a monkey vis-à-vis a human being,” and “placing our heads under the soles of the feet of our early ones.”<sup class="footnote-marker">148</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Urim ve-Tumim</i>, HM 25, <i>Tumin</i>, Introduction; <i>Teshuvot ha-Rid</i> 62; <i>Shibbolei ha-Leket</i>, Introduction, p.18; <i>Teshuvot Maharik, shoresh</i> 169; <i>Yam Shel Shlomo</i>, <i>Bava Kama</i> 2:5; <i>Get Pashut</i>, <i>Kelalim</i>, <i>Kelal</i> 5; <i>Hazon Ish</i>, <i>Kovetz Iggerot</i>, vol. 1, <i>mikhtav</i> 32; <i>Teshuvot Maharashdam</i>, EH 127; <i>Shem ha-Gedolim</i>, <i>Ma’arkhet Gedolim</i>, <i>Ma’arkhet</i> 40:162; <i>Teshuvot Hayyim Sha’al</i> 2:38.</i>
A case in point is the fifteenth century Ashkenazic teachings of <i>Maharil</i>, <i>Mahari Weil</i>, <i>Terumat ha-Deshen</i>, <i>Mahari Bruna</i>, and <i>Mahari Mintz</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">149</sup><i class="footnote">Yedidya Dinari, <i>The Rabbis of Germany and Austria at the Close of the Middle Ages</i> [Hebrew] (Tel Aviv: 5744), 17–55.</i> These authorities approached early authorities with humility and self-effacement; despite the absence of any general normative obligation to consult with rabbinic colleagues, their advice was enlisted prior to rendering a decision on numerous occasions. This reliance upon precedent and insistence on the need for consultation are further indicated by fifteenth century Ashkenazic rulings that demonstrate subservience to earlier opinions marked by their stringency, <i>humrah</i>, leading one Jewish historian to conclude:
<span class="poetry indentAll"><small>Whenever a doubt existed about the law in a special case and some authority of the past was found who had decided according to the more austere view, no examination was performed, but this strict decision was adopted.<sup class="footnote-marker">150</sup><i class="footnote">H. Zimmels, <i>Ashkenazim and Sephardim</i> (London: 1958), 204, cited by Dinari, ibid., 93.</i></small></span>
This position, however, has been found to be untenable. In fact, a review of other rulings of these <i>Poskim</i> demonstrates that they engaged in logic and inference, setting parameters for exercising this methodology and hammering out rulings on the anvil of actual study. More significantly, numerous positions are built upon a leniency, and not necessarily one rooted in precedent opinion. Thus, this period of Jewish legal history is to be characterized as a period leaning toward neither leniency nor strictness.<sup class="footnote-marker">151</sup><i class="footnote">Dinari, supra n.149, 93–118.</i> One must view the individuality and uniqueness of each case under the prism of critical scrutiny rather than subsume a decisor or group of decisors under any self-inclusive category of being either strict or lenient.
In sum, the arbiter navigates between the poles of precedent reflected in Talmudic and post-Talmudic writings and creative innovation grounded in ordinary reasoning and/or analogical inference, which is tempered by <i>yirat hora’ah</i>. This conception of the decision-making process serves as the rationale for the accepted rule “<i>hilkheta ke-vatra’ei</i>” – in a dispute between earlier and later authorities, the <i>halakhah</i> is in accordance with the later authorities – a technique utilized by many arbiters. <i>Rosh</i>, who programmatically set forth the parameters for the process, explains:
<span class="poetry indentAll"><small>We consider the views of those later in time to have greater authority, since they were aware of the reasoning of the earlier authorities as well as their own, and they reached their decision on the basis of choice among all views and after fully deliberating in order to get to the heart of the matter. <sup class="footnote-marker">152</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Piskei ha-Rosh</i>, <i>Sanhedrin</i> 4:6 and <i>Teshuvot ha-Rosh</i> 55:9. For a more detailed presentation of the rule, see <i>Teshuvot Maharik, shoresh</i> 94, which is authoritatively cited by <i>Rema</i>, HM 25:2, and <i>Shakh</i>, YD 242; <i>Kitzur Hanhagot Issur ve-Heter</i>, 8. Earlier references may be found in <i>Shabbat</i> 61a; <i>Seder Tana’im ve-Amora’im</i>, 25, 50–55; <i>Sefer ha-Eshkol</i>, <i>Hilkhot Sefer Torah</i> 14; <i>Rif</i>, <i>Eruvin</i> 35b (<i>Rif</i> pagination). Cf. <i>Teshuvot Maharam Alsheikh</i> 39, 53, 54, who argues that this rule was applicable only to sages during Talmudic times.</i></small></span>
Conversely, if the later <i>Poskim</i> reached their contrary position without being aware of their predecessor’s view, the arbiter may not rely upon the later authorities.<sup class="footnote-marker">153</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Piskei ha-Rosh</i>, ibid.; <i>Teshuvot Maharik, shoresh</i> 94.</i> Similarly, if an opinion is unearthed which was unknown to the later authorities and we can assume that upon examination the later authorities would have subscribed to this view, the arbiter may follow this emerging opinion.<sup class="footnote-marker">154</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Rema</i>, HM 25:2; <i>Teshuvot ha-Ridbaz</i> 3:994; <i>Teshuvot Maharam Alsheikh</i> 39; <i>Shakh</i>, supra n. 152; <i>Teshuvot Avnei Nezer</i>, OH 540; <i>Teshuvot Yosef Ometz</i> 73; <i>Teshuvot Hatam Sofer</i>, EH 1:119. In fact, some <i>Aharonim</i> implemented this conclusion as grounds for overruling <i>Shulhan Arukh</i> and <i>Rema</i>; see <i>Shakh</i>, HM 46:117; <i>Taz</i>, YD 53:11; <i>Teshuvot Hayyim Sha’al</i> 1:56; <i>Mishnah Berurah</i>, OH 446:4, <i>Bi’ur Halakhah</i>. Cf. <i>Urim ve-Tumim</i>, <i>Kitzur Tokfo Kohen</i> 123–124; <i>Urim</i>, HM 25:22; <i>Teshuvot Hatam Sofer</i>, EH 3:102; <i>Teshuvot Divrei Hayyim</i>, vol. 2, YD 105; <i>Teshuvot Kapei Aharon</i>, vol. 1, EH 13. See <i>Teshuvot Avnei Nezer</i>, OH 1, 4:22, who contends that this conclusion is inapplicable to the rulings of <i>Shulhan Arukh</i> and <i>Rema</i>. The divergence of opinion regarding whether <i>hilkheta ke-vatra’ei</i> allows one to overrule the <i>Shulhan Arukh</i> is conceptually similar to the aforementioned controversy regarding the acceptability of relying upon a minority opinion contrary to <i>Shulhan Arukh</i> in extenuating circumstances. See supra, text accompanying nn. 123–124.</i>
How should an arbiter weigh the value of the rule “<i>hilkheta ke-vatra’ei</i>” against a judgment of a deceased <i>mara de-atra</i>? On the one hand, the institution of the <i>mara de-atra</i> demands compliance with his rulings. On the other hand, abiding by “later authorities” entails the possibility that one’s decision may be contrary to the teaching of one’s predecessors, including a <i>mara de-atra</i>’s judgments. Accordingly, it is no surprise that in certain instances, an arbiter will invoke “the place of his master,” while in others he abides by “the later authorities.”<sup class="footnote-marker">155</sup><i class="footnote">For example, see <i>Teshuvot ha-Ridbaz</i> 1:518 and 2:604.</i>
In situations in which an individual is unwilling to follow a certain <i>mara de-atra</i> or there is no individual authority who has the requisite credentials and personal acumen, it is incumbent to identify an arbiter who can resolve the matter in doubt according to three basic decision-making rules.<sup class="footnote-marker">156</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Hikrei Lev</i>, OH 96; <i>Urim ve-Tumim</i>, <i>Kitzur Tokfo Kohen</i> 123–124; <i>Teshuvot Maharif</i> 59; <i>Hazon Ish</i>, YD 150:1. Given the impossibility of determining who constitutes the majority opinion by number, “<i>rov minyan</i>,” R. Ya’akov Emden and Hazon Ish conclude that <i>pesikah</i> based on “the majority opinion” must be accompanied by strong proofs or by logic. See <i>Teshuvot She’alat Yavetz</i> 1:157 and <i>Hazon Ish</i>, op. cit. For further discussion of this problem within the context of <i>Shulhan Arukh</i>’s decision to abide the majority rule, see <i>Urim ve-Tumim</i>, op. cit., and <i>Teshuvot Beit Ephraim</i>, OH 26–27. However, outside the confines of a <i>beit din</i>, if an individual possesses the proper credentials, he should not rely on the principle of majority rule. See <i>Hazon Ish</i>, <i>Zera’im</i>, <i>Kela’im</i> 1:1. Whether a majority is defined by scholarship rather than numbers would be determinant; see <i>Hiddushei ha-Ramban</i>, <i>Sanhedrin</i> 32a; <i>Ran</i>, <i>Yoma</i> 4a (<i>Rif</i> pagination); <i>Teshuvot Maharashdam</i>, OH 37.</i>
First, one must adhere to the majority opinion among authorities. Just as this rule applies within the confines of a formal pronouncement of <i>beit din</i>, which is predicated upon deliberation among arbiters, it equally applies in disputes between decisors, <i>mahloket ha-poskim</i>, which characteristically lacks discussion between individuals.<sup class="footnote-marker">157</sup><i class="footnote">Others argue that the majority rule is inapplicable to disputes between rabbinic authorities. See <i>Get Pashut</i>, <i>Kelal</i> 5 and supra nn. 29–30. Although <i>Hazon Ish</i>, <i>Zera’im</i>, <i>Kelayim</i>, sec. 1 and YD 150:8 endorses this position, he argues elsewhere that the presence of a majority view is to be construed as “<i>itmar hilkhisa</i>” – that is, the <i>halakhah</i> has been decided, and one therefore cannot impart legitimacy to a minority opinion even in extenuating circumstances. See supra nn. 118–119 and cf. n. 120.</i> Given the absence of debate, the majority may rely upon a minority view which is strict unless the customary practice or a corroboratory proof supports the lenient position or if it is a qualitative majority.<sup class="footnote-marker">158</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Get Pashut</i>, <i>Kelal</i> 5 (end). This position is an outgrowth of his opinion that majority rule is inapplicable to rabbinic differences of opinion; see supra n. 156. Whereas in the context of the <i>Beit Din ha-Gadol</i>, the <i>Sanhedrin</i>, we follow the numerical majority of judges, after its lapse, we follow the majority in rabbinic scholarship, such as the authors of the classical restatements and erudite scholars. See <i>Sefer ha-Hinukh</i>, mitzvah 78; <i>Hiddushei ha-Ramban</i>, <i>Sanhedrin</i> 32a.</i> Majority rule applies even if the reasons offered by the various authorities in the majority differ from one another.<sup class="footnote-marker">159</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Rema</i>, HM 25:2. For qualifications of this rule, see <i>Shakh</i>, HM 25:19; <i>Teshuvot Noda be-Yehuda</i>, vol. 2, HM 3; <i>Teshuvot Sho’el u-Meshiv</i>, <i>Mahadura</i> 4, 3:84; <i>Teshuvot Maharasham</i> 5:51.</i> The significance of the rule is reflected in the presumption that if a decisor’s opinion is unclear, we assume that he identifies with the majority.<sup class="footnote-marker">160</sup><i class="footnote">See Ya’akov Sofer, “Regarding the Parameters of the <i>Shulhan Arukh</i>’s Rulings” [Hebrew], in <i>Zekhor le-Avraham</i> (Holon: 5762), 67, 82.</i>
If there is no definitive ruling and the controversy has an even number of authorities affirming each position, then one follows the scholars who possess a greater stature or have more adherents.<sup class="footnote-marker">161</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Avodah Zarah</i> 7a; <i>Yad Malakhi</i>, <i>Kelal</i> 189; <i>Get Pashut</i>, <i>Kelal</i> 2, s.v. <i>u’n’man</i>.</i> In cases in which neither of these two rules are applicable, one follows the rule of “<i>safek de-oraita le-humrah ve-safek de-rabbanan le-kulah</i>” – in a matter of doubt regarding a biblical prohibition, the stricter opinion prevails, whereas in a matter of doubt concerning a prohibition of rabbinic status, the lenient opinion prevails.<sup class="footnote-marker">162</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Avodah Zarah</i>, ibid.
