The legal decision would be dictated solely by the application of deductive logic to these <i>a priori</i> legal rules and principles, without regard to policy … Policy, societal conditions and context would be overtly ignored.<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">Aharon Schreiber, “Positivism, Policy, Morality and Discretion in Jewish law,” 19 <i>Dine Israel</i> (5757/5758), 5, 9.</i></small></span>
In fact, however, as we will show, the decisors sought to address the issue of recovery for emotional stress by moving beyond the formal rules of damages and invoking the arbiter’s discretionary capacity, and specifically the capacity to exercise “emergency powers.”
Compensation is paid to an injured party for five elements of dam­age: <i>nezek</i> (loss of capacity to work), <i>tza’ar</i> (pain), <i>ripui</i> (“healing,” medical expenses), <i>shevet</i> (loss of income during convalescence), and <i>boshet</i> (shame and embarrassment) — as assessed by the arbiter. Imposition of these payments is conditional on the injury’s having been caused by a physical act.
Prior to the mid-fourth century CE, arbiters who had received ordination (<i>semikhah</i>, “the laying on of hands”) were authorized to resolve both ritual and civil matters, including damages by individuals. After the lapse of the ordination process, non-ordained arbiters, namely, community leaders and lay arbiters (<i>beit din shel hedyotot</i>), were granted authority to resolve cases involving common injuries that caused the injured party a financial loss. However, the compensation for such bodily injuries that could be imposed by the said arbiters was limited to two elements – loss of income and medical expenses.<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote"><i>SA</i>, HM 1:1–2; <i>Bah</i>, HM 1; <i>Shakh</i>, HM 1:7. Cf. <i>Piskei ha-Rosh</i>, <i>Bava Kama</i> 8:2.</i> By law, then, claims of liability due to shame and embarrassment (<i>boshet</i>), for example, though arising from bodily damage ensuing from a physical act,<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote"><i>SA</i>, EH 83:1. In cases of assault in which no injury resulted, there may be liability for <i>boshet</i>, but there is no legal liability in the absence of a physical act of assault; see <i>Bava Kama</i> 8:6; <i>Rema</i>, HM 420:43.</i> could not be submitted to a non-ordained arbiter for adjudication, since there was no loss of income.
Although certain matters are excluded from the jurisdiction of non-ordained arbiters, nevertheless, pursuant to normative Halakhah, ordained and non-ordained arbiters are empowered to exercise exi­gency jurisdiction both in matters of civil damages, such as <i>boshet</i>, and in matters involving capital punishment,<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot ha-Rashba</i> 1:612; 3: 109; <i>SA</i>, HM 2:1; <i>Rema</i>, HM 2:1. Numerous responsa that take this position are cited in Aharon Schreiber, <i>Jewish Law and Decision-Making: A Study through Time</i> (Philadelphia: 1979). However, cf. <i>Nimmukei Yosef</i> on <i>Rif</i>, <i>Bava Kama</i> 96b, <i>Sanhedrin</i> 52a; <i>Hiddushei ha-Ran</i>, <i>Sanhedrin</i> 46a; <i>Urim ve-Tumim</i>, HM 2:2, which maintain that given the demise of ordination, the court’s jurisdiction is limited to imposing non-capital punishment.</i> under the Talmudic rubrics of “<i>beit din makin ve-onshin she-lo min ha-din</i>” (a court may mete out punishment not prescribed by the Torah),<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Yevamot</i> 90b; <i>Sanhedrin</i> 46a.</i> “<i>le-migdar milta</i>” (protective measures),<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Yevamot</i> 90b.</i> and “<i>ha-sha’ah tzerikhah le-kakh</i>” (the times demand it).<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">Ibid. The phrase “<i>hora’at sha’ah</i>” (temporary measure) is often invoked in such a situation; see Menachem Elon, <i>Jewish Law: History</i>, <i>Sources</i>, <i>Principles</i> (Philadelphia: 1994), 533–6; H. Ben-Menahem, <i>Judicial Deviation in Talmudic Law</i> (Chur, Switzerland: 1991), 173–9.</i> The arbiters’ punitive powers are not limited to criminal matters, then, but encompass civil matters as well. To promote broader policy goals, arbiters are authorized, in certain circumstances, to deviate from the letter of the law and impose actual and/or punitive damages.<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">There is ongoing debate among contemporary scholars as to whether this extra-halakhic capacity is legislative or judicial in nature. My view is that the latter approach is the correct one; see H. Ben-Menahem, ibid., 146–8. Cf. Menachem Elon, ibid., 515.</i>
<span class="poetry indentAll"><small>Some of the basic policy goals in Halakhah as expressed in broad classifications at a high level of abstraction, are: to bring the spirituality and holiness to God down to earth and, through operative principles, to incul­cate them into mundane matters and conduct in the finite world; to sanc­tify God’s name … to preserve life and the law … to act for the benefit of individuals and society, by not harming them and making life pleasant; to preserve modesty and morality … to improve society by correcting inequities … to maintain public order …<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">See Schreiber, supra n. 23, 10. See also J. David Bleich, <i>Contemporary Halakhic Problems</i> (NY: 1995), vol. 4, xv–xix.</i></small></span>
Granting the arbiter discretion allows his decisions to be shaped by the aforementioned goals. In effect, the halakhic system encompasses exigency law, a juristic technique for effectively resolving problems for which the strict law has no efficient solution, so that the covenantal faith community’s basic objectives can be realized.
But what constitutes an “exigency situation?” Three approaches to its definition can be found in the writings of the Later Authorities:
<span class="poetry indentAll"><small>Falk,<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sma</i>, <i>SA</i>, HM 2:2.</i> in his commentary on Karo, attempts to delineate express guide­lines. He states if all the people are not dissolute as to certain matters, an individual may not be punished under this authority unless he is a habit­ual wrongdoer … Shakh<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Shakh</i>, <i>SA</i>, HM 2:2.</i> views this analysis as an appropriate restate­ment of the principles as derived from the Talmud and as codified by the <i>Tur</i>. Falk’s approach is cited with approval by … Netivot,<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Netivot ha-Mishpat</i>, <i>SA</i>, HM 2:1.</i> and Beer Hagola.<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Be’er ha-Golah</i>, SA, HM 2.</i><br>Shaar Ephraim<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Sha’ar Ephraim</i> 72.</i> … held that only if many people in the community are engaging in this type of conduct may an individual be punished.<br>This opinion … is in conflict with the holding of Falk and Maharam.<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Maharam Lublin</i> 138. See also <i>Kenneset ha-Gedolah</i>, HM 2:2.</i> For Falk holds that even where the majority of the people are not dissolute, if the individual is dissolute he may be punished by the court invoking its extrajudicial power. And Maharam holds that even if the community is not dissolute or the individual is not dissolute, if a court feels that failure to punish this person may cause other people to feel that they might act in a similar manner with impunity, the court may impose extrajudicial sanc­tions on the individual. In short, as enunciated by Maharam, extrajudicial authority may be invoked by a court in any situation when it feels that sanctions are necessary to deter potential misconduct. Shaar Ephraim, by contrast, would limit the court’s authority to situations where the com­munity has, in fact, engaged in such misconduct.<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">Emanuel Quint and Neil Hecht, <i>Jewish Jurisprudence: Its Sources and Modern Applications</i> (Chur, Switzerland: 1980), 174–5.</i></small></span>
On all three views – namely, the views of <i>R. Yehoshua Falk</i>, <i>Sha’ar Ephraim</i> (R. Ya’akov Katz, 1616–1678), and the <i>Maharam of Lublin</i> – if the individual is a habitual wrongdoer with regard to a certain practice, or has frequently committed a transgression in public,<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Shevut Ya’akov</i> 1:136.</i> and many mem­bers of the community, by engaging in this misconduct, have shown themselves to be dissolute (<i>parutz ba-aveirot</i>),<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">This term is used in <i>Tur</i>, HM 2 and <i>SA</i>, HM 2:1. According to R. Ya’akov Katz, if the community is not dissolute, a court may not exercise such sanctions against a habitual wrongdoer; see <i>Teshuvot Sha’ar Ephraim</i> 72 (43b).</i> an emergency exists if the individual will continue violating the halakhic norm unless he is pun­ished. Under these circumstances, a rabbinical court may exercise extrajudicial authority (<i>le-migdar milta</i>) as a deterrent, to forestall the danger that others will emulate the wrongdoer’s behavior if it goes unpunished.
Who is authorized to impose such extrajudicial sanctions? What types of non-ordained arbiters possess such authority? On the view of <i>Rosh</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Piskei ha-Rosh</i>, <i>Bava Kama</i> 9:5. See also <i>Beit ha-Behirah</i>, <i>Sanhedrin</i> 52b; <i>Bnei Shmuel</i>, HM 2:2.</i> <i>R. Yosef Karo</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote"><i>SA</i>, HM 2.</i> and <i>Maharshal</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Yam Shel Shlomo</i>, <i>Bava Kama</i> 9:7. According to <i>Maharshal</i>, in such matters, the scholar serves as a member of the community court.</i> such authority may be exercised by the greatest scholar of the generation. <i>R. Yoel Sirkes</i> (<i>Bah</i>), in the name of <i>R. Yeruham</i>, holds that the greatest scholar of the generation may impose these extrajudicial sanctions even if he has not been accepted by the community.<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Bah</i>, HM 2.</i> <i>Meiri</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Beit ha-Behirah</i>, <i>Bava Kama</i> 94b.</i> <i>R. Karo</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Beit Yosef</i>, HM 2; <i>SA</i>, HM 2:1. See also <i>Bnei Shmuel</i>, HM 2:2.</i> <i>R. Moshe Isserles</i> (<i>Rema</i>),<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Rema</i>, HM 2:1.</i> <i>R. Mordekhai Jaffe</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Levush Ir Shushan</i>, HM 2.</i> <i>R. Yehoshua Falk</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sma</i>, HM 2:9.</i> <i>R. Ya’akov Lorberbaum of Lissa</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Netivot ha-Mishpat</i>, HM 2:5.</i> and <i>R. Yehiel Michel Epstein</i><sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Arukh ha-Shulhan</i>, HM 2:2. See also <i>Teshuvot Tashbetz</i> 1:161; <i>Teshuvot Binyamin Ze’ev</i> 132, 303. On a present-day proposal to utilize the Knesset as a communal institution to nullify marriages, see Berachyahu Lifshitz, “Have the Rabbis Neglected Marriage Matters?” [Hebrew], <i>Kerem B’Yavneh Jubilee Volume</i> (2004), 314. Although generally appointed by the community at large or by communal representatives, such as “the seven elders of the city,” such panels may be appointed by the majority of the rabbis in a given town; see <i>Teshuvot lggerot Moshe</i>, HM 1:13; E. Schlesinger, “The Hala­khic Validity of the State-Recognized Rabbinical Courts” [Hebrew], 45 <i>Torah she-Be’al Peh</i> (2005): 165, 171 (R. Elyashiv’s opinion).</i> claim that exigency rulings can also be handed down by communal leaders, generally laymen, who have been appointed arbiters by their com­munity (<i>beit din shel hedyotot</i>).
Given that the law authorizes such exigency rulings, some contend that laymen are empowered to impose severe penalties. Indeed, during the thirteenth and fourteenth centuries in the Spanish provinces of Catalonia, Majorca and Valencia, Jewish communities had an institution known as the “board of inquiry into transgressions” (<i>berurei aveirot</i>)<i>.</i> Authorized by the king of Spain, these lay courts would contract com­munal loans, sell communal property, render decisions regarding financial matters (such as taxation and wills) and personal status claims, and in certain communities even had the power to administer oaths to litigants and witnesses and to excommunicate offenders. More­over, as numerous <i>teshuvot</i> by <i>Rashba</i>, <i>R. Yitzhak ben Sheshet</i>, and others attest, the <i>berurei aveirot</i>, as custodians of religious rectitude and order, were authorized to mete out criminal and monetary sanctions for ritual and moral offenses that were eroding the communal fabric. As grounding for the institution of <i>berurei aveirot</i>, the halakhic author­ities invoked the classic Talmudic sources that provided for exigency authority in these matters.<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot ha-Rashba</i> 3:385, 393; 4:311; <i>Teshuvot ha-Meyuhasot le-Ramban</i> 279; <i>Teshuvot ha-Rivash</i> 265; <i>Teshuvot ha-Ran</i> 41.</i> Indeed, no rabbinical discussion of the legitimacy of criminal sanctions and fines for civil injuries so levied was deemed necessary.<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot ha-Rashba</i> 1:1187; 3:318.</i> Following in this tradition, <i>R. Tzemah ben Shalom Duran</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Yakhin u-Bo’az</i> 1:126. See also <i>Teshuvot Rashbash</i> 211.</i> <i>R. Yehoshua Falk</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Prisha</i>, HM 2.</i> <i>Halakhah Pesukah</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Halakhah Pesukah</i>, HM 2:1, n. 13. See also <i>Mishpatim le-Yisrael</i>, 42–43, 59–60.</i> <i>R. Z. Goldberg</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote">Hanina Ben-Menahem, “Non-Legislative Punishment” [Hebrew], <i>Mishpetei Eretz – Jurist</i>, <i>Jurisdiction</i>, <i>Jurisprudence</i> [Hebrew], (Ofra, Israel: 2002), 152, 159, n. 17.</i> and others<sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Beit ha-Behirah</i>, <i>Bava Kama</i> 94b; <i>Teshuvot ha-Rosh</i>, rule 100:9; <i>Mordekhai</i>, <i>Gittin</i> 384; <i>Teshuvot Terumat ha-Deshen</i> 307; <i>Teshuvot ha-Ridbaz</i> 3:480; <i>Nahalat Shiva</i> 66; <i>Divrei Ge’onim</i> 34:4. Though none of these sources explicitly endorse the said position, their argumentation and use of the term “court” supports this conclusion. For an explicit endorsement, see <i>Kenneset ha-Gedolah</i>, HM 2:19. A contemporary Israeli arbiter recently reached the same conclusion; see S. Daichovsky, “Monetary Enforcement Meas­ures against Parties Who Refuse to Grant a <i>Get</i>” [Hebrew], 26 <i>Tehumin</i> (5766), 173, 177.</i> recognize the authority of lay communal arbiters to impose these types of sanctions in emergency situations.
Whether a contemporary rabbinical court has the extrajudicial authority to impose a criminal or monetary sanction, such as punitive damages, on a habitual wrongdoer who is part of a dissolute com­munity where many engage in the same improper behavior will depend on whether a non-ordained panel of arbiters has the jurisdic­tion to wield this type of authority. The authority of a non-ordained panel of arbiters is predicated on both parties’ willingness to submit their claims to this body. If one party refuses to accept the authority of a communal rabbinical court, the signing of an arbitration agreement (<i>shtar borerut</i>), which requires both parties to submit to the panel’s authority, provides a way to resolve a claim by imposing damages.<sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote">There may be courts that subscribe to the view that extra-judicial powers are reserved solely for the greatest scholar of the generation or for com­munal officers who have been appointed as arbiters, yet nevertheless allow for tort recovery on the basis of the signing of an arbitration agreement (<i>shtar borerut</i>). See infra 176–177.</i>
<span class="poetry indentAll"><small><b>III.</b></small></span>
Let us now revisit our hypothetical case of a husband’s denying his wife conjugal relations and thereby causing her mental anguish. Assuming a contemporary rabbinical court is empowered to have recourse to extrajudicial authority in exigency situations, it must be decided if the circumstances in question indeed constitute an exigency situation. Does a husband’s refusal to engage in sexual relations consti­tute wrongdoing of the sort that creates an exigency situation that may entitle the victim to damages? Consider the following Talmudic dictum:
<span class="poetry indentAll"><small>R. Eliezer ben Ya’akov said: A man must not marry a woman if it is his inten­tion to divorce her, as it is said, “Devise not evil against your neighbor, seeing he lives securely by you” (Proverbs 3:29).<sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Yevamot</i> 37b; <i>Gittin</i> 90a.</i></small></span>
As the <i>Sefer ha-Hinukh</i> explains, in such a situation, the wife is con­sidered a married woman, yet the husband views her as a divorcee.<sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sefer ha-Hinukh</i>, mitzvah 455.</i> Entering into a marital agreement requires both spouses to have the same mental intent vis-à-vis the marriage, viz., the intention to remain married to each other. A marriage that is factually dead, that is, in which the spouses do not engage in conjugal relations, yet the husband either remains under the same roof, moves out of the marital home while remaining formally married for the sake of the children (or other reasons), or refuses to grant a <i>get</i>, is deemed a violation of the law by <i>Rambam</i>, <i>Tur</i> and <i>Shulhan Arukh.</i><sup class="footnote-marker">62</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Mishnah Torah</i>, <i>Hilkhot Issurei Bi’ah</i> 21:28; <i>Hilkhot Gerushin</i> 10:21; <i>Tur</i>, EH 119; <i>SA</i>, EH 119:1–2; <i>Helkat Mehokek</i>, EH 119:1; <i>Taz</i>, EH 119:2.</i> <i>R. David ben Shmuel ha-Levi</i> interprets the aforementioned Talmudic dictum as follows: “Even though it is permissible to ‘devise evil,’ that is, to divorce one’s wife, in accordance with the law … he who desires to do so should do so [soon], and not delay the divorce. Therefore the verse states, ‘he lives securely with you’ – this is the essence of the prohibition.”<sup class="footnote-marker">63</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Taz</i>, ibid.</i>
That is, a husband’s unwillingness to have sexual relations with his wife, whether due to a personal vendetta, sheer hatred of his wife, or a desire to gain financial concessions with respect to a divorce he is contemplating, involves a violation of the injunction “devise not evil against your neighbor, seeing he lives securely by you.”<sup class="footnote-marker">64</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Mishnah Torah</i>, <i>Hilkhot Gerushin</i> 10:21; <i>Tur</i>, EH 119; <i>SA</i>, EH 119:1. See also <i>Beit Shmuel</i>, <i>SA</i>, 119:1; <i>Pri Hadash</i>, EH 119:2. But cf<i>. Helkat Mehokek</i>, n. 62 above, and <i>Mishnah la-Melekh</i>, <i>Hilkhot Gerushin</i> 10:21, who question this position.</i> The psycho­logical consequences of violation of this norm, namely, <i>tza’ar</i> (mental anguish) are recognized by the Halakhah. Denying one’s wife conjugal relations during an ongoing marriage in which no divorce is impending or she is unaware of any contemplation of divorce on her husband’s part prevents her from engaging in sexual relations with someone else, as she remains a married woman. The mental anguish involved in this situation to some extent parallels that facing the <i>agunah</i>, the “chained” woman who is prevented from remarrying because of her husband’s refusal to grant a divorce.<sup class="footnote-marker">65</sup><i class="footnote">On defining <i>tza’ar</i> as emotional anguish, see infra n. 72. Causing such anguish also constitutes violation of the prohibition against causing pain to living creatures (<i>tza’ar ba’alei hayyim</i>), which applies to human beings as well as to animals. See <i>Bava Metzia</i> 32b; <i>Teshuvot ha-Rashba</i> 1: 252, 256, 257; <i>Drishah</i>, HM 272:15; <i>Sma</i>, HM 272:13; <i>Teshuvot be-Tzel ha-Hokhmah</i> 4:125; <i>Teshuvot Divrei Hakhamim</i>, YD 1.<br>In addition to the psychological consequences of a husband’s unwillingness to have relations with his wife, the court may declare him a rebel (<i>mored</i>), a possible consequence of which is that his wife may be granted an increase in the amount due her from her <i>ketuba</i>; see <i>SA</i>, EH 77:1; <i>Rema</i>, ad loc.</i>
Among a husband’s monetary obligations and rights vis-à-vis his wife are the obligations to provide maintenance and pay for ransom and burial expenses; he has rights to articles his wife finds and to be her sole heir.<sup class="footnote-marker">66</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Mishnah Torah</i>, <i>Hilkhot Nisu’in</i> 12:2–3.</i> Another halakhic consequence of marriage is the establish­ment of spousal property relations. One of the rules governing these relations is that “in financial matters (<i>mamon</i>), one’s stipulation is valid.” That is, in monetary matters such as spousal support, one may make stipulations contrary to biblical law.<sup class="footnote-marker">67</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Kiddushin</i> 19b.</i> However, where no such private stipulations have been agreed to by the couple, the biblical rules are default rules that govern the marital relationship. Do private arrangements extend beyond the realm of monetary affairs into the sphere of personal matters? Can a husband stipulate that he is under no duty to have intercourse with his wife? If the husband stipulates that he is under no obligation to engage in conjugal relations, such a stipulation is invalid.<sup class="footnote-marker">68</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Tur</i> and <i>Beit Yosef</i>, EH 88; <i>SA</i>, EH 38:5; <i>Rema</i>, ad loc. Cf. Yerushalmi <i>Bava Metzia</i> 7:7; <i>Tosefta Kifshuta</i>, <i>Kiddushin</i> 3:7, 947–48.</i> Expounding the biblical verse, “her conjugal rights he shall not diminish,”<sup class="footnote-marker">69</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Shemot</i> 21:10. According to some decisors, this injunction is contravened even if the husband, by refraining from relations, unintentionally causes his wife pain; see <i>Shita Mekubetzet</i>, <i>Ketuvot</i> 48a, s.v. <i>ha-omer</i> (in the name of <i>Ritva</i>); <i>Teshuvot Maharam Alsheikh</i> 50. But cf. <i>Mishnah Torah</i>, <i>Hilkhot Nisu’in</i> 14:7; <i>Sefer ha-Mitzvot</i>, mitzvah 262; <i>SA</i>, EH 76:11; <i>Teshuvot ha-Mabit</i> 3:131. On the differences between the view expressed in the <i>Mishnah Torah</i> and that of the <i>Shulhan Arukh</i>, see <i>Adat Ma’oz</i>, EH 2:76:11.</i> and the concept of <i>shi’abud</i> (servitude) in the realm of conjugal relations,<sup class="footnote-marker">70</sup><i class="footnote">In contradistinction to a husband’s duty to engage in intercourse, a wife is bound to him (<i>meshu’abedet</i>), but just as a wife becomes bound to her hus­band rather than acquiring title to his body, so the husband acquires servi­tude to her body. See <i>Yevamot</i> 65b; <i>Nedarim</i> 81b; <i>Ketuvot</i> 71b and <i>Rashi</i> ad loc., s.v. <i>ha-madir; Hiddushei Rabbi Avraham mi-Montpellier</i>, <i>Nedarim</i> 15b and <i>Rashi</i> ad loc., s.v. <i>de-amar Rav Huna; Hiddushei ha-Rashba</i>, <i>Gittin</i> 75a and <i>Nedarim</i> 15b; <i>Teshuvot Mahaneh Hayyim</i>, vol. 2, EH 44; <i>SA</i>, EH 154:6; <i>PDR</i> 1:5. On the distinction between servitude and title vis-à-vis marriage, see J. David Bleich, <i>“Kiddushei Ta’ut:</i> Annulment as a Solution to the <i>Agunah</i> Problem,” 33 <i>Tradition</i> (1998), 90, 116. Implementation of a <i>kinyan</i> to establish matrimonial ties articulates the parties’ willingness to assume the duties and rights associated with marriage and by no means constitutes evidence that the husband has acquired title to his wife; see <i>Birkat Kohen</i>, 101–123; <i>Torah Temimah</i>, <i>Vayikra</i> 22:11.</i> the <i>Netziv</i>, <i>R. Naftali Tzvi Yehuda Berlin</i>, writes:
<span class="poetry indentAll"><small>Reason tells us that man is so duty bound. It is, as we all know, for this purpose that a bride enters into marriage … Hence, if he denies her sex­ual relations, she is deprived of her right. Sexual fulfillment is primarily the husband’s duty and the wife’s entitlement. Sexual relations are the essence of marriage and depriving a woman of this right creates “<i>tza’ar gufa</i>,” that is, emotional pain.<sup class="footnote-marker">71</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Birkat ha-Netziv</i>, <i>Mekhilta de-Rabbi Yishmael</i>, <i>Mishpatim</i> 3 (Horowitz-Rabin edition), 258–9. See also <i>Shita Mekubetzet</i>, <i>Ketuvot</i> 63a. In fact, should a husband initially fail to address her desire for conjugal relations and then dutifully engage in relations, his behavior is construed as a fulfillment of a religious obligation by way of a transgression (<i>mitzvah ha-ba’ah ba-aveirah</i>), namely, transgression of the precept “her conjugal rights he shall not diminish” (<i>Shemot</i> 21:10); see <i>Shita Mekubetzet</i>, <i>Ketuvot</i> 62b, s.v. <i>ve-katvu.</i> Moreover, the withholding of conjugal rights is construed as theft; see <i>Teshuvot Noda be-Yehuda</i>, <i>Mahdura Kama</i>, OH 35; <i>Teshuvot Minhat Hayyim</i>, vol. 1, EH 18. See also <i>Yevamot</i> 118a; <i>Rashi</i>, <i>Kiddushin</i> 19b; <i>Ran</i>, <i>Kiddushin</i> 19b; <i>Rashbam</i>, <i>Bava Batra</i> 126b; <i>Taz</i>, EH 38:7; <i>Beit Shmuel</i>, EH 76:7 and <i>Be’er Heitev</i>, ad loc.; <i>Levush</i>, EH 38:5; <i>Arukh ha-Shulhan</i>, EH 38:12.<br>Neverthe­less, a wife has no duty to engage in conjugal relations and may consent to forgo her conjugal rights, provided her husband’s obligation to procreate has been fulfilled; see <i>Tur</i>, EH 76:6; <i>SA</i>, EH 76:11; <i>Birkei Yosef</i>, EH 1:2. She is, however, entitled to change her mind and reinstate her right to conjugal relations; see <i>Lehem Mishnah</i>, <i>Hilkhot Nisu’in</i> 15:1; <i>Teshuvot Mahari Weil</i> 1. Cf. <i>Teshuvot Ranah</i> 2:44.<br>Many decisors have ruled that a husband’s failure to engage in sexual relations involves contravention of a biblical prohibition (“her food, her clothing, and her conjugal rights he shall not diminish” [<i>Shemot</i> 21:10]); see <i>Mishnah Torah</i>, <i>Hilkhot Nisu’in</i> 12:2; <i>Tur</i>, EH 69; <i>Beit Yosef</i>, ad loc. Accord­ing to R. Shimon Shkop, the status of the violation – biblical or rabbinic – determines whether abstinence from conjugal relations is deemed to create severe emotional distress for the wife. R. Shkop’s contention has been explained as follows: “All biblical prohibitions are proscribed because higher wisdom recognizes that this is as bad for Israel as poison, and the fact is that the prohibition and the warning exist because the matter is bad … And rabbinic prohibitions are the exact opposite. The matter is good … and the proof is that given the Torah did not proscribe it, it isn’t bad – but rather, the Torah mandated that we listen to the rabbis” (<i>Rabbi Hayyim Shmuelevitz Memorial Volume</i> [Hebrew] [Jerusalem: 1979], 285). See also <i>Sha’arei Yosher</i>, <i>sha’ar</i> 1, ch. 10. Cf. <i>Sefer Yerei’im</i> 191 and <i>Smag</i>, negative pre­cept 81, which maintain that failure to fulfill this duty violates a Rabbinic prohibition. On the view espoused by R. Shkop, such a violation does not cause the said harm to the wife.</i></small></span>
Withholding sexual intercourse (<i>onah</i>) causes emotional scarring and may have repercussions for the future of the marriage. This aware­ness of emotional hurt is underscored in other contexts. For example, the Talmud debates whether, given the tension and mental anguish that arises from spousal conflict, the execution of a divorce is a boon for the wife, or whether, notwithstanding marital tensions, the wife none­theless prefers the gratification of her bodily desires (<i>niha de-gufa</i>) to divorce.<sup class="footnote-marker">72</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Yevamot</i> 118b. The initiation of conjugal relations by the husband is for the wife’s pleasure (<i>simhat ishto</i>), while a husband’s pleasure is a beneficial consequence of imparting pleasure to his wife; see <i>Devarim</i> 24:5 and <i>Rashi</i> ad loc.; <i>Pesahim</i> 72b; <i>Ra’avad</i>, <i>Ba’alei ha-Nefesh</i>, <i>Sha’ar ha-Kedushah</i>; <i>Smak</i>, positive commandment 285. This need to instill joy applies to a pregnant and/or barren wife as well. As R. Moshe Feinstein notes, “Conjugal relations do not depend on the possibility of conception, but are part of a husband’s obligation toward his wife, to give her pleasure and not cause her pain;” see <i>Iggerot Moshe</i>, EH 1:102. See also <i>Maggid Mishnah</i>, <i>Hilkhot Nisu’in</i> 15:1. Regarding whether a husband is obligated to sleep in a <i>Succah</i> on <i>Succos</i> without his spouse and the implications of this issue for <i>simhat ishto</i>, see <i>Taz</i>, OH 639:9; <i>Bikurei Ya’akov</i>(Ettlinger), <i>Aruch la-Ner</i> Succah(end), OH 639:18.<br>The halakhic literature suggests that the proscribed pain is not only physical pain, but rather emotional anguish as well. The subtext to the duty to engage in sexual relations is reflected in the requirement that a husband refrain from sexual relations with his wife when intoxicated, angry at her, or intending to divorce her; see <i>Yevamot</i> 37b; <i>SA</i>, OH 240:3, 10. Similarly, the notion of bringing joy to one’s wife entails recognition of the wife’s right to emotional integrity and equanimity. Hence, the with­holding of sexual relations, unless requested by the wife, is deemed to cause mental anguish, and serves, according to some decisors, as potential grounds for compelling a divorce. In <i>Rambam</i>’s formulation, it is grounds for the claim, “My husband is repulsive to me (<i>ma’is alai</i>);” see <i>Hilkhot Nisu’in</i> 14:8. For others who endorse <i>Rambam</i>’s position, see Yuval Sinai, “Coercion of a <i>Get</i> as a Solution for the Problem of Agunah,” 20 <i>Jewish Law Association Studies</i> (2010), 246, which records a substantial number of heretofore unknown decisors who subscribe to <i>Rambam</i>’s view. See R. Avraham Gatinieu’s review of the <i>Poskim</i> concerning this matter and his conclusion that most <i>Rishonim</i> endorse <i>Rambam</i>’s view. See <i>Teshuvot Tzel ha-Kesef</i> 13–14.<br>Although some decisors do not endorse this position as grounds for compelling a <i>get</i>, my proposal to recognize claims for <i>tza’ar boshet</i> in cases of spousal refusal of conjugal relations (see below at nn. 109–118) may have the indirect effect of encouraging recalci­trant husbands to grant a <i>get.</i></i> The Talmudic conclusion is, “<i>tav le-meitav tan du mi-le-meitav armelu”</i> (It is better for two to dwell together than to dwell alone). The underlying idea here is that a woman has a compelling desire for mar­riage, such that she will even accept a degree of routine quarreling and marital strife. The assumption is that given that a woman’s need for sexual relations through the husband’s fulfillment of the “conjugal rights” precept is an existential fact and not conditioned by contingent social or cultural factors, a woman prefers to remain married.<sup class="footnote-marker">73</sup><i class="footnote">This understanding of the <i>“tav le-meitav tan du”</i> dictum was developed in a talk by R. Joseph Soloveitchik, “Surrendering to the Almighty,” summar­ized in <i>Light</i> (17 Kislev, 5736 [1976]), 11–15, 18. Cf. <i>Iggerot Moshe</i>, vol. 4, EH 83. This is, however, a halakhic presumption, and not applicable to every marriage; see Bleich, supra n. 70, 102–8. Whether the “<i>tav le-meitav tan du</i>” presumption assumes the need to engage in sexual relations or can refer to a woman’s desire for companionship and security merits further analysis. On the notion of mental torment (<i>inui nefesh</i>) as denoting, in certain contexts, abstention from conjugal relations, see <i>Bereishit</i> 31:50; <i>Vayikra</i> 16:29; <i>Yoma</i> 76a, 77b.</i> Hence, a husband’s refusal to engage in sexual relations undermines his wife’s ongoing emotional stability.<sup class="footnote-marker">74</sup><i class="footnote">Note that the emotional harm ensuing from a wife’s refusal to engage in conjugal relations is likewise deemed infliction of emotional distress on the husband, and is likewise a violation of Halakhah; see <i>Teshuvot Ri Migash</i> 186; <i>Mishnah Torah</i>, <i>Hilkhot Nisu’in</i> 14:9, 11–12; <i>Hiddushei ha-Rashba</i>, <i>Ketuvot</i> 73a, s.v. <i>ve-ha-amar rav</i>; <i>Hatam Sofer</i> on <i>Taz</i> OH 639:9.</i>
However, a wife may seek to opt out of a deteriorating relationship when the spouses do not engage in conjugal relations though residing under the same roof, or while de facto separated from each other. The husband’s withholding of a <i>get</i> under these conditions results in an untenable situation:
<span class="poetry indentAll"><small>The daughters of Avraham remain grass widows with living husbands. They are left starving, thirsty, and destitute. And we should be apprehen­sive lest they become involved in objectionable conduct … Moreover, these women are young and nubile [and will not be able to wait indefinitely].<sup class="footnote-marker">75</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ginat Veradim</i>, EH 3:20. Although this description relates to a case in which the husband disappeared, it also applies to the present-day <i>agunah</i> whose husband refuses to grant her a divorce.</i></small></span>
The Rabbis likened the situation of such a woman to that of a moribund individual in imminent danger of death (<i>goses</i>)<i>.</i><sup class="footnote-marker">76</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Rashi</i>, <i>Yevamot</i> 122a. Clearly, the analogy to someone about to die (<i>goses</i>) is metaphorical. Although the withholding of sexual relations generally does not lead to death, both the wife and the <i>goses</i> will likely experience emotional distress as a result of their respective conditions. In both cases, there is objective pain that is not self-induced; see <i>Rashi</i>, <i>Sukkah</i> 25a, s.v. <i>tirda de-reshut.</i></i> While withholding a <i>get</i> does not constitute murder per se, in the eyes of <i>R. Yosef Henkin</i>, the prohibition against it is a stricture ancillary to the prohibition against murder (<i>avizrayhu de-retziha</i>)<i>.</i><sup class="footnote-marker">77</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Kitvei ha-Gaon Rav Yosef Henkin</i>, vol. 1, 115. R. Henkin describes a recalcitrant husband as a thief. As Prof. Berachyahu Lifshitz observes (in a personal com­munication to this author), given that the husband is obligated to have relations due to his <i>shi’abud</i> vis-à-vis his wife (see supra nn. 70–72), R. Henkin’s characteriza­tion is apt. On emotional abuse as grounds for divorce in rabbinical writings and contemporary Israeli rabbinical court judgments, see Shochetman, supra n. 1; 54/168, Supreme Rabbinical Court (SRC) (Jerusalem), Nov. 17, 1994; 1-2-016788168 SRC, May 10, 2001.</i>
Moreover, the husband may compound the injury by dismissing the harm, arguing that one who suffers emotional pain has only herself to blame, and that emotional distress is a transient psycho­logical and/or culturally conditioned behavioral pattern rather than an existential fact.
Given the wife’s emotional distress, we are dealing with a husband who, in keeping his spouse from engaging in sexual relations, is a habitual wrongdoer who will continue to violate the law unless he is punished. If many in the community are dissolute (<i>parutz be-aveirot</i>), in that they engage in similar misconduct, or are at risk of doing so, an exigency situation can be said to exist.<sup class="footnote-marker">78</sup><i class="footnote">See supra n. 40.</i> Under these circumstances, can a rabbinical court today exercise extrajudicial authority to forestall the danger that, should the behavior of the husband in question go unpunished, others will emulate the offender? Let us first analyze the emotional distress being perpetrated by the husband.
Above, we noted that according to the strict law (<i>dinei nezikin min ha-din</i>), there are five aspects of personal injury for which compensation is paid: 1. <i>nezek</i> (loss of capacity to work due to the permanent loss of a limb); 2. <i>tza’ar</i> (pain resulting from physical contact); 3. <i>ripui</i> (healing, viz., medical expenses); 4. <i>shevet</i> (loss of income during convalescence); 5. <i>boshet</i> (shame and embarrassment).<sup class="footnote-marker">79</sup><i class="footnote">See above n. 24; <i>Bava Kama</i> 91a; <i>SA</i>, HM 420:3.</i>
Under which category of damage does infliction of emotional dis­tress fall? Though, according to the strict law of torts, the term <i>tza’ar</i> is associated with physiological pain rather than emotional distress, in the context of exigency jurisdiction, it can be taken to have its ordinary sense, that is, to encompass emotional distress.<sup class="footnote-marker">80</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Bava Kama</i> 85a; <i>Piskei ha-Rosh</i>, <i>Bava Kama</i> 8:1; <i>Tur</i>, HM 420:17 and <i>Beit Yosef</i> ad loc.; <i>SA</i>, HM 420:16. The position of R. Ya’akov Blau on this point is unclear; see <i>Pithei Hoshen</i>, vol. 6, 313, n. 32.</i> Alternatively, mental anguish could be categorized as <i>boshet.</i> In the psychological and philosophical literature, shame is intimately connected to the victim’s sense of self and the presence of those who confirm one’s self-esteem.<sup class="footnote-marker">81</sup><i class="footnote">John Rawls, “Self-Respect, Excellence, and Shame,” in R. Dillon (ed.), <i>Dignity</i>, <i>Character and Self Respect</i> (NY: 1995), 128; Gabriele Taylor, <i>Pride</i>, <i>Shame</i>, <i>and Guilt</i> (NY: 1985), 64.</i> The Halakhah defines shame as the consequence of an injury generated by physical contact<sup class="footnote-marker">82</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Bava Kama</i> 91a. Cf. Yerushalmi <i>Bava Kama</i> 8:6, which provides for recovery for humiliation absent physical impact only if the victim is a great scholar.</i> witnessed by a third party.<sup class="footnote-marker">83</sup><i class="footnote"><i>SA</i>, HM 420:7; <i>Rema</i>, HM 420:8; <i>Teshuvot Rivevot Ephraim</i> 6: 453; <i>Teshuvot Az Nidberu</i> 8: 63. But cf. <i>Tosafot</i>, <i>Ketuvot</i> 65b, s.v. <i>be-zman</i>, which argues that the presence of a third party who witnesses the injury is not required.</i> On this view, shame is not simply the humiliated party’s subjective feeling, which was generated by physical contact (such as a slap, spitting, and so on) between the parties, unrelated to any third party’s awareness of the act. Rather, shame is the feeling of humiliation accompanied by the knowledge that others are aware of the event causing the victim’s discomfiture, and the resulting diminution of his sense of worth. The Talmud asks whether, if the victim is unaware of the humiliation (as for instance, if, while asleep, someone disrobed him, and he subsequently died in his sleep, unaware of the incident), other parties who have been indirectly humiliated, such as family members of the directly humbled party, can sue for <i>boshet</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">84</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Bava Kama</i> 86b.</i> Can the heirs sue for family humiliation? If liability depends on third party awareness, there should be no liability in such a case; if liability is grounded in the victim’s feelings of shame, then given his unawareness, the injurer should be exempt. The Talmud’s view is that though family members were indirectly humiliated, a <i>boshet</i> claim arises only if the victim himself experiences shame, and in the case of the disrobed sleeper, the injurer is exempt.<sup class="footnote-marker">85</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Rosh</i>’s ruling, endorsed by <i>Rema</i> and <i>Sema</i>, is that the victim’s heirs cannot sue for <i>boshet</i>; see <i>Piskei ha-Rosh</i>, <i>Bava Kama</i> 8:7; <i>Teshuvot ha-Rivash</i> 27 (cited by <i>Beit Yosef</i>, YD 343:7–8); <i>Rema</i>, HM 420:35; <i>Sema</i>, HM 420:43. Others contend that the Talmud did not take a definitive stance; hence, we must refrain from extracting money from the tortfeasor. See <i>Mishnah Torah</i>, <i>Hilkhot Hovel u-Mazik</i> 3:3; <i>SA</i>, HM 420:35.</i>
But shame has a broader sense than that addressed by the black-letter Halakhah, as it can extend to the humiliated party’s subjective feelings even in the absence of physical contact between the injurer and the victim, or where there are no third party witnesses. By law, some­one expectorating mucus or phlegm that comes in contact with some­one else’s skin is liable to pay for <i>boshet</i>; if, however, the expectoration damages only the other party’s clothing, he is exempt from paying compensation for <i>boshet.</i><sup class="footnote-marker">86</sup><i class="footnote"><i>SA</i> and <i>Rema</i>, HM 420:38. See D. Feldman, <i>The Right and the Good</i> (NY: 2005), 1–5.</i> In the absence of physical impact, the court cannot hold the offender liable. Note that this is in line with the principle that holding someone down while shouting in his ear establishes liability.<sup class="footnote-marker">87</sup><i class="footnote"><i>SA</i>, HM 405:1. Hence, some authorities maintain that the law of damages does not mandate imposition of fines when an engagement is broken; see <i>Ketzot ha-Hoshen</i>, HM 207:7; <i>Teshuvot Rabbi Akiva Eiger</i> (2nd edi.) 75; E. Shochetman, “More About Damages of Breach of Promise to Marry” [Hebrew], 9 <i>Mishpatim</i> (1978), 109, 120. But cf. <i>Beit ha-Behirah</i>, <i>Bava Kama</i> 90b and 91a, where it is argued that there is liability for <i>boshet</i> absent physical impact.</i> Damages for expectoration of mucus that comes in contact with someone’s clothing generates liability, not on the basis of the strict law, but on the basis of exigency law.
Yet breaking an engagement, which likewise does not entail physical contact, may conceivably generate liability for <i>boshet</i>. This has been argued for not by adducing the jilted party’s embarrassment that people are aware of what happened, but rather on the grounds that breaking an engagement leads to a decline in the jilted party’s social status, and reduced prospects for finding a spouse.<sup class="footnote-marker">88</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>PDR</i> 3:205, 210; however, it was left an open question there.</i> A different argu­ment was put forward by <i>R. Yosef Shaul Nathanson</i>:
<span class="poetry indentAll"><small>Shame is that which touches one’s body. Therefore, when it does not touch his body, he is exempt. Yet an engagement has impact on the rational soul and is much worse than physical injury, hence with regard to broken engagements, even in the absence of physical contact, there is liability. And one who verbally embarrasses his friends will not have a share in the world to come, even though he did not physically injury him.<sup class="footnote-marker">89</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Sho’el u-Meishiv</i> (2nd ed.) 4:69. See also <i>Ketzot ha-Hoshen</i>, HM 207:7. But cf. <i>Teshuvot Maharik</i>, root 29. On emotional anguish as a form of <i>boshet</i>, see also <i>Tosafot</i>, <i>Ketuvot</i> 65b, s.v. <i>be-zman</i>; <i>Nedarim</i> 27b, s.v. <i>asmakhta</i>; <i>Kiddushin</i> 8b, s.v. <i>maneh</i>.</i></small></span>
On this view, <i>boshet devarim</i> (verbal embarrassment) is not limited to verbal insults or defamation of character.<sup class="footnote-marker">90</sup><i class="footnote"><i>SA</i>, HM 420:38; <i>Teshuvot ha-Ridbaz</i> 3: 480; <i>Teshuvot Hatam Sofer</i>, HM 181.</i> It also encompasses emo­tional distress unaccompanied by physical contact and unrelated to third party awareness, such as the emotional fallout generated by broken engagements.<sup class="footnote-marker">91</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <i>Teshuvot Beit Yitzhak</i>, EH 1:112, which claims that a broken engagement encompasses not just verbal embarrassment, but a physical act (if, upon breaking the engagement, the party who terminates the relationship becomes engaged to someone else). <i>Ketzot ha-Hoshen</i>, HM 207:7, argues that obligating oneself by executing an engagement document (<i>shtar shiddukhin</i>), generates liability for <i>boshet</i>.</i>
Antecedents for this type of <i>boshet</i> can be found in a Jerusalemite practice from Talmudic times: If someone entrusted preparation of a meal to someone else, who spoiled it, the latter had to compensate the host for the insult to him and to his guests.<sup class="footnote-marker">92</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Bava Batra</i> 93b. The fact that the act in question generates <i>boshet</i> liability according to the laws of heaven (<i>dinei Shamayim</i>), if not according to the laws of man, entails that the Halakhah recognizes embarrassment that does not ensue from physical contact.</i> Liability for the host’s embarrassment ensues from his being publicly embarrassed by the spoiled meal. Liability for guests’ embarrassment is grounded in their mental anguish for the host who wanted to serve a delicious meal; the Talmud identifies these “third-party” feelings as <i>boshet</i> too.
Analogously, the husband’s misconduct may be taken to cause the wife distress that, from the halakhic perspective, gives rise to liability for <i>boshet.</i><sup class="footnote-marker">93</sup><i class="footnote">Assuming that the husband intended to embarrass his wife. Lack of intent would exempt him from liability for <i>boshet</i>; see <i>Bava Kama</i> 27a; <i>SA</i>, HM 421:1, 11; <i>Beit Meir</i>, EH 66:6.</i> Just as a broken engagement by its very nature generates feel­ings of mental anguish, that is, <i>boshet</i>, unrelated to any physical contact or verbal remarks,<sup class="footnote-marker">94</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Beit Meir</i>, EH 7.</i> a husband’s denying his spouse conjugal relations generates emotional stress, that, though there is no accompanying physical contact or verbal insult, should be categorized as <i>boshet.</i>
Given that intentional infliction of mental anguish is akin to <i>boshet devarim</i>, can this claim be resolved by a rabbinical court? While, accord­ing to the strict law, there is no remedy in cases of <i>boshet</i> involving no physical contact, a rabbinical court, whether or not its members are ordained judges, does have the extrajudicial power, “in every locale and at all times,” to benefit the community by imposing criminal sanc­tions and holding the wrongdoer liable for damages for <i>boshet devarim.</i>
At first glance, such a conclusion might seem unfounded. The <i>Shulhan Arukh</i> rules that only ordained arbiters residing in the Land of Israel can assess a specified penalty established by the Torah (<i>kenas katzuv</i>)<sup class="footnote-marker">95</sup><i class="footnote">Halakhah distinguishes between cases in which the offender is liable to pay monetary compensation (<i>mamon</i>), and cases of offenses that incur fines (<i>kenas</i>). Whereas one who confesses to an act commission of which incurs liability for monetary restitution is obligated to pay, this is not so with regard to fines. Here, admitting to an act does not render one liable to payment of the fine; see <i>Bava Kama</i> 64b, 75a. Since a litigant’s con­fession regarding humiliation is admissible in evidence, <i>boshet</i> payments fall into the category of <i>mamon</i>; see <i>Rashi</i>, <i>Ketuvot</i> 42a; <i>Tosafot Rid</i>, <i>Ketuvot</i> 41a; <i>Mishnah Torah</i>, <i>Hilkhot Nedarim</i> 8:3. Nevertheless, in some Tal­mudic and post-Talmudic sources, <i>boshet</i> is arbitrarily designated a fine; see <i>Sma</i>, HM 1:5; <i>Shakh</i>, HM 1:3. Given that non-ordained arbiters may adjudicate only matters that occur frequently and involve a loss of money, injuries such as <i>boshet</i>, if they that do not occur frequently and do not entail a loss of money, are deemed to fall under the category of offenses incurring fines, and as such, cannot be adjudicated; see <i>Teshuvot Rav Natronai Gaon</i> (Brody edition) 329, 363; <i>Otzar ha-Ge’onim</i>, <i>Bava Kama</i> 64–68; <i>Tosafot</i>, <i>Bava Kama</i> 84b; <i>Maggid Mishnah</i>, <i>Hilkhot Hovel u-Mazik</i> 5:6; <i>Beit Yosef</i>, HM 1; Amihai Radzyner, “Foundations of ‘<i>Dine Qena­sot</i>’ in Talmudic Law” (unpublished doctoral dissertation, Bar Ilan University, 2001), 229–36.</i> for personal injuries, and today, all arbiters are non-ordained and hence lack the authority to mete out such penalties.<sup class="footnote-marker">96</sup><i class="footnote"><i>SA</i>, HM 1:1.</i> How, then, can a rabbinical court today award damages for <i>boshet</i>? As we have explained, on the strength of exigency law, even non-ordained arbiters are empowered to mete out penalties known as “fines imposed by the Rabbis” (<i>kenas hakhamim</i>), such as payments for <i>boshet</i>-related injury.<sup class="footnote-marker">97</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Piskei ha-Rosh</i>, <i>Gittin</i> 4:41; <i>Mordekhai</i>, <i>Gittin</i> 384. See Radzyner, n. 95 above, 281–2. In our presentation, we have focused upon the exigency jurisdiction of a contemporary <i>beit din</i>’s authority to address a wife’s claim for tort damages; see <i>SA</i>, HM 2 and supra, text accompanying nn. 26–59 and infra, text accompanying nn. 98–101. A <i>beit din</i> is empowered to render an award for two types of damages: compensatory damages and punitive damages. Compensatory damages represent the financial equivalent of the loss or harm suffered by the victim, to restore the injured victim to the position he was in prior to the <i>nezek</i>. The scope of the compensatory damages includes economic damages, such as <i>nezek</i>, and non-economic damages, such as <i>boshet</i>; see <i>SA</i>, HM 420:15–18, 24. Unlike compensatory damages, punitive damages consist of an assessment of damages above and beyond compensating the victim for the harm suffered. The goal of punitive damages is to punish the <i>mazik</i> for his or her conduct, deter the <i>mazik</i> from repeating his or her improper act, and prevent others from engaging in similar behavior. For a contemporary application of the imposition of punitive damages in a case of physical assault, see File no. 9326-35-1, SRC, Aug. 25, 2008. Regardless of whether the award is compensatory or punitive in nature, the justification of the award is based upon the exigency jurisdiction of a <i>beit din</i>, namely the assessment that the community must deter such improper behavior rather than satisfying a claim due to the fact that the wife is personally entitled to such relief.<br>Should a <i>beit din</i> today deem it necessary to award damages for <i>boshet devarim</i>, it has the option to either award compensatory damages calculating the shame based upon the societal status of the offender and the offended or award punitive damages that would exceed a compensatory damage award. See <i>SA</i>, HM 420:24, 35, 43; <i>Sma</i>, HM 420:25. Since the calculation of the compensatory damages for <i>boshet devarim</i> is subjective, should a <i>beit din</i> determine to render such an award, the panel must be careful to render an assessment that reflects a compensatory damage award, lest one blur the lines between a compensatory damage award and a punitive damage award. Regarding a contemporary <i>beit din</i>’s authority to render an award for <i>boshet devarim</i>, see <i>Teshuvot ha-Rosh</i> 101:1, 8, 9; <i>SA</i>, HM 420:38; <i>Teshuvot ha-Ridbaz</i> 3:480; <i>Teshuvot Hatam Sofer</i>, HM 181.<br>Regardless of whether the damages are compensatory or punitive in nature, the awarding of such damages is viewed as a vehicle to appease the victim and is grounded in the <i>beit din</i>’s exigency jurisdiction. See <i>Tur</i>, HM 1:11; <i>Rema</i>, HM 1:2, 5; <i>SA</i>, HM 420: 38; <i>Teshuvot ha-Mabit</i> 1: 93; <i>Teshuvot ha-Ridbaz</i> 4:1291.</i>
The actual implementation of these powers concerning <i>boshet­</i> related injuries is not limited to Talmudic era rabbinical courts, but has been applied in contemporary times.<sup class="footnote-marker">98</sup><i class="footnote">On rabbinical courts that imposed criminal sanctions on those who insulted and defamed their fellow-Jews, see <i>Teshuvot ha-Rosh</i>, rule 71:1, 8, 9; <i>Teshuvot ha-Rashba ha-Meyuhasot le-Ramban</i> 240; <i>Teshuvot Havot Ya’ir</i> 62; <i>Teshuvot Tumat Yesharim</i> 1:160. On the imposition of <i>boshet devarim</i> fines for broken engagements, see <i>Shakh</i>, HM 207:24, 333:49; <i>Teshuvot Noda be-Yehuda</i>, <i>Mahadura Tanina</i>, YD 146; <i>Teshuvot Avodat ha-Gershuni</i> 74; <i>Teshuvot Givat Pinhas</i> 74; <i>Teshuvot Hatam Sofer</i>, EH 134; <i>Teshuvot Rav Pe’alim</i>, vol. 2, EH 3; <i>Teshuvot Zera Emet</i>, YD 102-103; <i>Teshuvot va-Yomer Yitzhak</i> 1:161; <i>Teshuvot Peri Yitzhak</i> 13; <i>Teshuvot Zekhut Avot</i> 31; <i>Nahalat Shiva</i> 8:6; Shochetman, supra n. 1, 120 n. 49.</i> For example, in 1965, invoking their exigency authority, members of the Supreme Rabbinical Court in Jerusalem, <i>Rabbis Abudi</i>, <i>Elyashiv</i>, and <i>Goldschmidt</i>, marshaled numerous responsa to support the position that damages for <i>boshet</i> can be awarded in the case of a broken engagement.<sup class="footnote-marker">99</sup><i class="footnote"><i>PDR</i> 5:322.</i> In the words of <i>R. Elyashiv</i>, who authored the <i>psak din</i>:
<span class="poetry indentAll"><small>There is a consensus that a rabbinical court is empowered to impose cor­poral punishment or social shunning (<i>nidui</i>) until he assuages the [hurt] feelings, yet this will not be accomplished through <i>boshet</i> payments pursu­ant to the law, but rather, everything is resolved in accordance with the specifics of the matter, the times, and the arbiters’ discretion.<sup class="footnote-marker">100</sup><i class="footnote">Ibid., 327, reprinted in Yosef Shalom Elyashiv, <i>Collected Teshuvot</i> 2:129. Whether R. Elyashiv’s justification for invoking this exigency power rests on the court’s status as a community court (see above at nn. 45–50) or a non-ordained panel of arbiters (see above at nn. 51–58), or is based on the signing of an arbitration agreement (see supra n. 59) is unclear from the ruling in question. But given that in other decisions, R. Elyashiv ascribes to the view that the Israeli rabbinical courts are communal courts, mandating submission to their authority (see <i>PDR</i> 7:225, 227 and E. Shochetman, <i>Civil Procedure in Jewish Law</i> [Hebrew] [Jerusalem: 1988], 169, n. 34), it is clear that he deems the exercise of such authority by community courts fully legitimate. Moreover, given that a community court is a non-ordained panel of arbiters, R. Elyashiv would presumably conclude that any lay panel may exercise such powers. But cf. <i>Teshuvot Divrei Yoel</i>, YD 35:4.</i></small></span>
Adopting <i>R. Elyashiv’</i>s position, subsequent Israeli rabbinical courts have invoked their extra judicial authority and imposed <i>boshet</i> pay­ments for broken engagements.<sup class="footnote-marker">101</sup><i class="footnote"><i>PDR</i> 3:209–10; 6:117, 119.</i>
It remains to identify the specific reason infliction of emotional distress by refusal to engage in conjugal relations serves as grounds for awarding <i>boshet</i> damages. Is it a “<i>le-migdar milta”</i> (a protective measure) or is it due to “the needs of the time?” Is the emotional fallout from spou­sal refusal of conjugal relations like the mental anguish resulting from a broken engagement? On the one hand, this type of distress does not result from any physical injury or impact. Is it, then, like being spat on (where the spittle lands on one’s clothing and does not touch one’s skin), being called derogatory names, or being jilted by a fiancé? On the strength of a court’s authority to invoke its exigency jurisdiction in all these aforementioned situations, the injurer can be fined.<sup class="footnote-marker">102</sup><i class="footnote"><i>SA</i>, HM 420:38.</i> It would seem that this should apply to our scenario as well.
The Talmud and halakhic authorities, however, exhort us to refrain from making rules that impose a fine in one realm on the basis of laws pertaining to a fine in another realm. The halakhic authorities do have recourse to analogical reason­ing to identify similarities and differences between cases (<i>medameh milta le-milta</i>)<sup class="footnote-marker">103</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Bava Batra</i> 130b and supra 53–57.</i> so as to address new situations, but does this allow us to establish a new rule governing a particular fine from a rule pertaining to another fine? For example, the Mishnah gives examples of liability for imperceptible damage.<sup class="footnote-marker">104</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Gittin</i> 5:4.</i> The question then arises whether the laws governing the specified sorts of imperceptible damage can encompass other sorts of imperceptible damage. One school of thought argues that since monetary penalties are involved, the list cannot be added to, as this would constitute establishing a new penalty. Despite putative similarities between two situations, arbiters are not to derive new pen­alties by analogical inference from existing penalties. On this view, though absence of physical contact appears to be a common feature of spitting on someone else’s clothing, defamation, and emotional distress due to spousal refusal to engage in conjugal relations, this putative analogy cannot serve as a basis for expanding the sphere of exigency law to encompass the latter.<sup class="footnote-marker">105</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Yad Malakhi</i> 550–551; <i>Shakh</i>, HM 385:1; <i>Tosfot Yom Tov</i>, <i>Gittin</i> 5:4.</i>
On another opinion, however, the Talmud’s rejection of analogical inferences from one penalty to another is applicable only in the context of black-letter Halakhah. With respect to matters falling under the rubric of <i>le-migdar milta</i>, however, analogical inferences are permissible.<sup class="footnote-marker">106</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Shevut Yaakov</i> 1:145.</i> On this view, if the injury caused by a broken engagement can be subsumed under the category of <i>boshet devarim</i> by analogical inference, so too emotional distress due to spousal refusal to engage in conjugal relations can be classed as falling under <i>boshet devarim.</i> The question, then, is whether breaking an engagement in a manner that humiliates the aggrieved party constitutes <i>boshet devarim.</i> One responsist argues that breaking an engagement should be on a par with all types of <i>boshet</i>, including <i>boshet devarim</i>, and the arbiter should handle it accordingly as a “protective measure.”<sup class="footnote-marker">107</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Avodat ha-Gershuni</i>, supra n. 98.</i> Another contends that it is analogous to the case of spitting on someone’s cloth­ing. If a rabbinical court can invoke its extrajudicial powers to resolve a spitting incident even absent contact with the defendant’s body, surely, on breaking an engagement in a manner that generates emotional scarring of the aggrieved party, liability should ensue.<sup class="footnote-marker">108</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Shoel Umeishiv</i>, supra n. 89.</i>
Having considered this debate, it is my opinion that, given that emotional distress due to spousal refusal to have conjugal relations may have more severe psychological impact than a physical act of injury, it should indeed be handled like <i>boshet devarim</i> and spitting on clothing. The court’s discretion to impose extrajudicial fines can provide monetary relief for this type of emotional anguish.
We saw that a husband’s denying his wife conjugal relations contravenes the biblical directive, “Devise not evil against your neigh­bor, seeing he lives securely by you” (Proverbs 3:29).<sup class="footnote-marker">109</sup><i class="footnote">See supra text accompanying n. 60.</i> A husband’s deny­ing his spouse sexual relations occurs when the spouses reside under the same roof, and either have an ongoing marriage in other respects, or are de facto separated from each other and the marriage is factually “dead,” but the husband is delaying the granting of a divorce. The lack of conjugal relations causes <i>tza’ar</i> (mental anguish) as well as injury to the wife’s sense of self (<i>boshet</i>), the latter not necessarily being a func­tion of the public’s perception of her worth. (As we saw above, by the strict law, damages for <i>boshet</i> are a function of public perception of an individual’s social standing.) Contemporary rabbinical courts, by virtue of their power to exercise exigency authority, can adjudicate claims for such damages.
Moreover, the consequences of violating the prohibition against delaying the granting of a divorce extend beyond the halakhic recogni­tion of <i>tza’ar</i> and <i>boshet</i>, and indeed, beyond the realm of domestic relations. The distress generated by denial of conjugal relations also constitutes a kind of deception:
<span class="poetry indentAll"><small>Just as there is deceitfulness in buying and selling, so there is deceitfulness in verbal exchanges. One should not ask how much something costs if he dos not wish to buy it … If someone is the descendant of converts, he should not admonish, “remember your ancestors’ deeds.”<sup class="footnote-marker">110</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Bava Metzia</i> 4:10.</i></small></span>
Causing unnecessary mental anguish is prohibited in both social and commercial contexts. Inquiring about a price when one has no intention of purchasing the object is termed <i>ona’ah</i> (deception)<i>.</i> Lest the biblical prohibition against deception (Lev. 25:14) be construed as applying only to <i>ona’at mamon</i> (fraudulent business transaction), the Mishnah explicitly states that deception occurs in the realm of conver­sation as well (<i>ona’at devarim</i>)<i>.</i> The Mishnaic examples are edifying: The distress caused by needless verbal insensitivity in conversation is simi­lar to the distress caused to a merchant whose hopes of making a sale are needlessly built up and then dashed.<sup class="footnote-marker">111</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Bava Metzia</i> 58b. See also <i>Sefer Yerei’im</i>, 180; <i>Smag</i>, negative com­mandment 171; <i>Piskei ha-Rosh</i>, <i>Bava Metzia</i> 4:22; <i>Teshuvot Hikrei Lev</i>, YD 4: 80, Y. Sofer, “On <i>Ona’at Devarim”</i> [Hebrew], <i>Mekabtziel</i>, 9, 41, 51. But cf. Tosefta <i>Bava Metzia</i> 3:25; <i>Sifra</i>, <i>Behar</i> 3:2; and <i>Ra’avad</i> on <i>Sifra</i> ad loc., which take the concept of “verbal deception” to apply only to objectionable verbal inquiries.</i> In the halakhic literature, verbal deception has been characterized as the infliction of “dread and fear” and “pain and distress.”<sup class="footnote-marker">112</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Rambam</i>, <i>Sefer ha-Mitzvot</i> 251; <i>Ra’avad</i>, <i>Shita Mekubetzet</i>, <i>Bava Metzia</i> 58b; R. Yonah Gerondi, <i>Sha’arei Teshuvah</i> 3:24; R. Samson Raphael Hirsch, <i>Commentary on the Torah</i>, <i>Vayikra</i> 25:14–17. Cf. J. David Bleich, “<i>Ona’at Devarim</i>,” [Hebrew] 35 <i>Hadarom</i> (1972): 140–1. Others argue that not any insensitivity, but only deceptive speech constitutes <i>ona’at devarim</i>; see <i>Or ha-Hayyim</i>, <i>Vayikra</i> 25:17; <i>She’iltot Rav Ahai Gaon</i>, <i>Behar</i>, <i>She’ilta</i> 113; <i>Sefer ha-Hinukh</i>, mitzvah 337. See also <i>Tur</i>, HM 228; <i>SA</i>, HM 228:3.</i> The individual’s emotional persona must be protected from unjustified verbal assault.
The open-ended nature of the prohibition against <i>ona’at devarim</i> is articulated in <i>Sefer ha-Hinukh:</i>
<span class="poetry indentAll"><small>It is impossible to provide specific details of every type of behavior that causes emotional distress … One who transgresses this prohib­ition and behaves contrary to the directives propounded by the halakhic scholars violates a precept.<sup class="footnote-marker">113</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sefer ha-Hinukh</i>, mitzvah 341 (338).</i></small></span>
Over 700 years later, <i>R. Ya’akov Blau</i> introduces his exam­ination of <i>ona’at devarim</i> by describing the many acts subsumed under this prohibition:
<span class="poetry indentAll"><small>One who creates noise in his home or in the street while the neighbors are sleeping … [and] one who resides on an upper floor and disturbs his neighbor on a lower floor is deemed to be causing pain … If one needs to move or remove beds, it should be done during the day … and during the evening hours, one should walk with light footwear and not run elec­tric saws or washing machines and the like … In this book, he discusses whether students in a study hall or <i>yeshiva</i> in a residential area can learn aloud … There is also discussion about eating food that produces a vile odor that may cause bad breath that is offensive to someone one is conversing with.<sup class="footnote-marker">114</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Pithei Hoshen</i>, vol. 4, 15:3.</i></small></span>
On <i>R. Blau</i>’s view, when one party to a relationship is deliberately inconsiderate of the other, he may be liable for <i>ona’at devarim.</i> In inter­personal relations, such as relations between neighbors, the respective parties’ sense of self-worth is to be secured and enhanced, and care must be taken to avoid giving offense or creating mental anguish. There is a special prohibition against <i>ona’at devarim</i> vis-à-vis one’s wife.<sup class="footnote-marker">115</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Tur</i>, HM 228; <i>SA</i>, HM 228:3.</i> A fortiori, a husband’s denying his wife conjugal relations, a direct assault on her sense of self-worth, is an instance of <i>ona’at devarim.</i>
With regard to <i>ona’at devarim</i>, no act has been committed, but rather, the offense is merely verbal, and hence not justi­ciable according to the strict law.<sup class="footnote-marker">116</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sema</i>, HM 420:49. Articulating words is not deemed an act; see <i>Teshuvot ha-Rashba ha-Meyuhasot le-Ramban</i> 240 regarding the legal import of a blasphemer’s words.</i> It is thus exigency law that empowers a rabbinical court to impose fines for <i>ona’at devarim</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">117</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Beit Yosef</i>, HM 1:12; <i>Darkei Moshe</i>, HM 2:5; <i>Rema</i>, HM 2:1, 420:38.</i> Though our hypothetical scenario falls under the category of <i>boshet</i>, it also constitutes a violation of the prohibition against <i>ona’at devarim</i>. Shaming someone verbally entails “verbal deception.”<sup class="footnote-marker">118</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Rashbatz</i>, <i>Magen Avot</i>, <i>Avot</i> 3:15; <i>Bi’ur ha-Gra</i>, HM 420:49.</i> In effect, a rabbinical court applying exigency law to a case of spousal denial of conjugal relations is at the same time addressing contraventions of the prohib­ition against <i>ona’at devarim.</i>
Given that every decision related to awarding damages for emo­tional scarring can be characterized as a <i>hora’at sha’ah</i> (emergency directive), the rabbinical court must realize that the contemplated rulings are ad hoc determinations arising from exigency situations, and as such call for cool-headedness. As <i>Rashba</i> formulates it:
<span class="poetry indentAll"><small>With patience, deliberation, and consensus, the community will be directed toward [fulfilling the will of] heaven. Every major act and mighty hand requires vigilance and the elimination of anger. The arbiter must worry that his fervent zeal for God will inflame his innards and do away with the correct and proper [judicial] comportment.<sup class="footnote-marker">119</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot ha-Rashba</i> 6:238. See also <i>Teshuvot ha-Rivash</i> 171.</i></small></span>
Applying this to our scenario, the panel must first ascertain whether the husband is indeed a wrongdoer. Let us first consider the question of when a husband is deemed to be refusing to divorce his wife (<i>mesarev get</i>), and at what point his wife is deemed to be an <i>agu­nah.</i> If a married couple have been living in separate rooms in the marital home for three months and appear in front of a rabbinical court for the purpose of executing a divorce, what is the court’s mandate? As long as there are prospects of reconciliation,<sup class="footnote-marker">120</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Ginat Veradim</i>, YD 3:4; <i>PDR</i> 12:199–204.</i> it is the court’s responsi­bility to promote <i>shalom bayit</i> (domestic tranquility)<i>.</i><sup class="footnote-marker">121</sup><i class="footnote"><i>PDR</i> 10:310, 313. In instances in which it is clear that the husband’s claim for <i>shalom bayit is</i> intended only to stretch out the rabbinical court proceed­ings, the court must issue a divorce judgment; see <i>Teshuvot Yaskil Avdi</i>, vol. 6, EH 15; <i>PDR</i> 21:333, 362.</i> But if there is little likelihood that domestic harmony will prevail, a rabbinical court decision to rule with a view to fostering <i>shalom bayit</i> only serves to unduly lengthen the divorce proceedings. Unless the specifics of the marital situation dictate otherwise, the court should set a maximum time of eighteen months to restore marital tranquility.<sup class="footnote-marker">122</sup><i class="footnote">R. Hayyim Palagi, <i>Teshuvot Hayyim ve-Shalom</i> 2:35, 112. Some decisors contend that separation is not akin to divorce, but its purpose is rather to ease spousal tensions and facilitate reconciliation; see <i>Teshuvot u-mi-Tzur Dvash</i>, EH 8; <i>Teshuvot Yaskil Avdi</i>, EH 6: 25, 45; <i>Teshuvot Noda be-Yehuda</i>, EH 3:89; <i>PDR</i> 4:267, 274. Cf. <i>Teshuvot Ta’alumot Lev</i> 3: 20, §4; <i>Teshuvot Seridei Esh</i>, EH 29.<br>In effect, irreconcilable differences between parties and the inability to restore <i>shalom bayit</i> serve as grounds for the parties’ subsequent divorce; see <i>Teshuvot ha-Ridbaz</i> 4:89; <i>Teshuvot Yaskil Avdi</i> 6:96; <i>Teshuvot Yabia Omer</i>, vol. 2, EH 10; R. Henkin, supra n. 77; <i>Iggerot Moshe</i>, YD 4:15; Tel Aviv District Rabbinical Court, 016948564-21-1; Haifa District Rabbinical Court, 051778991-22-1; 050562289-13-1; Netanya District Rab­binical Court, 8885. Whether marital breakdown due to irreconcilable differences culminating in a “dead” marriage allows a court to compel a divorce is subject to debate; see <i>Teshuvot Divrei Malkiel</i> 3:145; <i>PDR</i> 1:161, 162; 6:13; 9:200, 211, 213; 10:168, 173; 9:149, 153; 20:239, 275.</i> If a spouse moves out of the marital home and the couple is continuously separ­ated for the entire eighteen months, or if the spouses are legally separ­ated, the marriage is functionally over, and it becomes the court’s task to persuade the couple to divorce.<sup class="footnote-marker">123</sup><i class="footnote">R. Palagi’s stance, ibid., is intended to persuade rather than coerce the parties to execute a divorce; see <i>PDR</i> 12:198, 206. Cf. <i>PDR</i> 9:200, 211–212; 10:168, 173. Legal separation or de facto separation is a prerequisite for a rabbinical court ruling recommending that the spouses divorce. Living under the same roof in different bedrooms is not deemed separation in this context; see Z. Warhaftig (ed.), <i>Collected Rulings of the Rabbinical High Court of Appeals</i> [Hebrew] (Jerusalem: 1950), 30. Nevertheless, Israeli rabbinical courts have at times recommended separate bedrooms in order to facili­tate reconciliation in the hope of the couple’s eventual return to shared quarters; see <i>PDR</i> 4:267, 274.</i> This is done by issuing a divorce judgment that it is “proper” or “recommended” that “the parties divorce each other.”<sup class="footnote-marker">124</sup><i class="footnote">Although in the period of the <i>Rishonim</i>, such judgments were for­mulated in terms of an “obligation to divorce” (<i>hiyuv le-garesh</i>) rather than “compelling” divorce (<i>kofin le-garesh</i>), Kaplan argues that today, rabbinical courts, in particular in Israel, use even weaker terms, saying that divorce is “recommended” or “a mitzvah.” See Yehiel Kaplan, “Enforcement of Divorce Judgments by Imprisonment: Principles of Jewish Law,” <i>Jewish Law Annual</i> 15 (2004), 57, 77–80, 134–8. A cursory glance at recent cases indicates that courts do in fact use the term “obligation to divorce” (<i>hiyuv le-garesh</i>); see Jerusalem District Rabbinical Court, 1-21-9918 and 2-21­764; Tel Aviv District Rabbinical Court, 038416327-21-1; Petach Tikva District Rabbinical Court, 4927-21-1; Netanya District Rabbinical Court, 1-24-4564; Haifa District Rabbinical Court, 8952-21-1. On the need to avoid the locution “obligation to divorce,” see <i>Teshuvot Yabia Omer</i>, vol. 2, EH 10.</i> Once the judgment has been issued, the parties are to arrange for execution of the divorce (<i>sidur ha-get</i>)<i>.</i> Should the husband refuse to give the <i>get</i> due to a personal vendetta, desire to use the <i>get</i> as a bargaining chip for financial concessions, or sheer hatred of his wife, at that juncture he is deemed a <i>mesarev get</i>. As noted, at this point, there are grounds for providing monetary relief for the wife, who is deprived of conjugal relations, and suffers the attendant emotional distress.
However, prior to addressing and awarding such a claim for infliction of emotional distress, the court must engage in mediation, so that either the victim may be assuaged with words of appeasement or the injurer can voluntarily offer monetary recompense.<sup class="footnote-marker">125</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Rema</i>, HM 1:5.</i> Adducing a Geonic precedent, various halakhic authorities argue that there should be recourse to appeasement “according to the issues at hand, the status of the one who perpetrated the humiliation, and the status of the victim of the humiliation.”<sup class="footnote-marker">126</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Rav Natronai Gaon</i>, <i>supra</i> n. 95, 329; see R. Sherira Gaon, <i>Teshuvot Sha’arei Tzedek</i>, part 4, gate 1, no. 19; <i>Piskei ha-Rosh</i>, <i>Bava Kama</i> 8:3; <i>Perishah</i>, HM 1:12; <i>Levush</i>, HM 1:5. Relying on the Geonic precedents and <i>SA</i>, HM 1:6, there is the option of imposing a ban or social shunning (<i>nidui</i>); after calculation of the approximate damages to be paid, the injurer is released from the ban, regardless of whether he has appeased his wife.</i> Depending on the circumstances of the case and the religious and/or social status of the parties, the court will attempt to mediate an appeasement. Should attempts at mediation be unsuc­cessful, and assuming the standards for invoking exigency Halakhah have been met, the court will proceed to assess the damages caused by the husband’s infliction of emotional distress.
Given that the injuries are imperceptible, how does the court assess these damages? Post-Talmudic sources indicate that the court does so by exercising judicial discretion. Defining this discretion in a negative fashion, some characterized the assessed damages as fines (<i>kenasot</i>) that have no rationale, one decisor even saying, “I am astonished that the sages found any basis for arriving at these assessments.”<sup class="footnote-marker">127</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Otzar ha-Ge’onim</i>, <i>Bava Kama</i> 212, 65; <i>Teshuvot Sha’arei Tzedek</i>, part 4, gate 1, no. 7; <i>Ra’abad</i>, quoted in <i>Shita Mekubetzet</i>, <i>Bava Kama</i> 91b. According to R. Yisrael Isserlein, there is no prescribed formula (<i>middah kavuah</i>) for estimating this type of award; see <i>Teshuvot Terumat ha-Deshen</i> 2:212.</i> Others, among them R. Yosef Elyashiv, describe the court’s discretion in exigency situations in positive terms: “Everything is resolved in accordance with the specifics of the matter, the times, and the arbiters’ discretion.”<sup class="footnote-marker">128</sup><i class="footnote"><i>PDR</i> 5:327, and see above at n. 100. A similar formulation is found in <i>Teshuvot Avodat ha-Gershuni</i> 74; <i>Teshuvot Rav Pealim</i>, vol. 2, EH 3; <i>Kenneset ha-Gedolah</i>, HM 1:35.</i>
I am not, of course, claiming that the court decides on the basis of whim; rather, there is an authoritative decisional standard. <i>Boshet</i> is determined by the principle of proportionality, that is, in accordance with the relative status of the perpetrator and victim of the humiliation. In effect, defendants pay damages commensurate with their wrongs, and plaintiffs recover damages commensurate with their losses. A secular legal system might ask: Ought not the husband to have foreseen the injury that would result from his conduct? Did the wife have an “eggshell personality,” resulting in unforeseeable mental injury? The halakhic system does not ask these questions. The doctrine that a hus­band’s denying his spouse conjugal relations is a violation of the prohibitions against <i>ona’at devarim</i> and <i>boshet</i> enables the court to assume that long term abstinence from conjugal relations engenders anguish in every wife, and does not reflect psychological fragility, but is rooted in the very essence of her humanity. Similarly, the forseeability of the harm ensuing from the husband’s failure to engage in sexual relations need not be proven in order to establish liability.
The <i>boshet</i> generated by spousal denial of conjugal relations cuts across all economic and social strata. It is incumbent upon the husband to be aware of his spouse’s rights and his own marital obligations, and to be aware of the injury that will ensue from failure to fulfill his obligation to engage in conjugal relations (<i>onah</i>) while preventing his wife from severing the marital bond. Failure to be aware of these duties is grounds for liability for his wife’s foreseeable emotional distress.
Although a court is to be guided by the relative religious and/or social status of the parties in assessing <i>tza’ar-boshet</i> payments, such imperceptible injuries cannot be readily quantified and will result in wide variations in monetary awards.<sup class="footnote-marker">129</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Tosafot</i>, <i>Bava Kama</i> 86a, s.v. <i>ke’ilu.</i> This conclusion also applies to com­mon law; see C. McCormick, <i>Handbook on the Law of Damages</i> (St. Paul, MN: 1935), 88, 318–19; S. Atiyah, <i>Accidents</i>, <i>Compensation and the Law</i> (London: 1980), 213.</i> Ought the court implement a policy of minimizing or maximizing these awards? The black-letter Halakhah sets a fixed <i>boshet</i> payment for certain injurious acts that are accompanied by physical contact: “If he spat and the spittle reached him, if he stripped his cloak from him … he gives him four hundred zuz … all in accordance with his dignity [viz., his status].”<sup class="footnote-marker">130</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Bava Kama</i> 8:6.</i>
