The Mishnah notes that an individual is deemed poor if he cannot pay for food and clothing for a year, which at the time came to 200 zuz.<sup class="footnote-marker">131</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Pei’ah</i> 8:8–9; <i>Mishnah Torah</i>, <i>Hilkhot Matnot Aniyim</i> 9:13. Cf. <i>Or Zarua</i>, <i>Hilkhot Tzedakah</i> 14; <i>Tur</i>, YD 253:2. Some argue that the value of the <i>ketubah</i> should be equal to one year’s support; see <i>Sma</i>, HM 88:2; <i>Avnei Me’luim</i> 27:1 (in the name of <i>Rashi</i> and <i>Ritva</i>);<i>Teshuvot Beit Avi</i> 3:137 and R. Daichovsky, <i>PDR</i>, 1-213655, Feb. 22, 2005.</i> In 2008, in New York City, using the Consumer Price Index, the 200 zuz amount would be equivalent to an annual outlay of $55,000 for food and clothing. Hence, the statutory Mishnaic compensatory damages for spitting or stripping off a cloak are $110,000 per occurrence, depending on the relative status of the parties. As understood by Talmud and later authorities, these monetary awards are the maximum sums that can be awarded for the said injurious acts.<sup class="footnote-marker">132</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Bava Kama</i> 91a; <i>SA</i>, HM 420:43.</i> The law adopts a policy of restraint (<i>le-kulah</i>) vis-à-vis compensation for damage claims, including those of <i>boshet</i>, limiting the compensation paid out.<sup class="footnote-marker">133</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Bava Kama</i> 58b; <i>Piskei ha-Rosh</i>, <i>Bava Kama</i> 8:1, <i>Bava Metzia</i> 5:16.</i>
Though the goal of the law <i>stricto sensu</i> is to compensate the victim while keeping the perpetrator’s monetary liability within reasonable limits, exigency law is resorted to for punitive purposes and deter­rence. Applying exigency law in cases where there is a claim of emo­tional distress due to the withholding of sexual relations serves two goals. As we saw, it imposes liability for a type of injury not covered by the strict law, namely, non-physical injuries unaccompanied by direct physical contact. But optimally, the system should not only compen­sate deserving victims, but also deter halakhically reprehensible behavior in the future. Referring to exigency rulings in terms of such rubrics as “protective measures” and “the times requires it,”<sup class="footnote-marker">134</sup><i class="footnote">See supra nn. 26–30.</i> conveys the idea that the system is focusing on an immediate problem, and the imposition of sanctions is punitive. But deterrence is also critical. In the words of <i>R. Natronai Gaon</i>: “One may penalize monetarily at a minimum or at a maximum in order to prevent the increase of perpetrators in Israel. This is the tradition of rabbinic courts, and we learn from them; and this is our judicial practice.”<sup class="footnote-marker">135</sup><i class="footnote">R. Natronai Gaon, n. 95 above. The discretionary nature of the assess­ment, the fact that there is no mention of a policy of limiting awards, is stressed in <i>PDR</i> 3:131, 151; 5:322, 327, as well as in the <i>teshuvot</i> cited in n. 98 above. Some of these sources emphasize that the fine should be calculated on the basis of the status of the perpetrator and the status of the victim.</i>
Given that the damages are not just compensatory, but punitive and deterrent, the amount of the award will be proportionate to the transgression in question. Taking into account the range of possible scenarios, awards for similar injuries may vary considerably from case to case. As the amount of the award is discretionary, wide variations in monetary awards will result. This variation in award is not unique to the halakhic system. In American law, for example, awards for spousally-inflicted emotional distress have ranged from $15,000 to as much as $500,000.<sup class="footnote-marker">136</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Twyman v. Twyman</i>, 855 S.W. 2d 619, 620 (Texas 1993) [trial court award]; <i>Massey v. Massey</i>, 807 S.W. 2d 391, 395 (Tex. Ct. App. 1991), writ denied, 867 S.W. 2d 766 (Tex. 1993); <i>Chiles v. Chiles</i>, 799 S.W. 2d 127 (Tex. App.- Houston [14th Dist.] 1989), writ requested [trial court award].</i>
<span class="poetry indentAll"><small><b>IV.</b></small></span>
We have assumed that a court can hear a claim for infliction of emotional anguish prior to a wife’s receiving her bill of divorce (<i>get</i>)<i>.</i> But would submission of such a claim while her husband was refusing to grant the divorce render the divorce, when ultimately granted, unlawfully coerced (<i>get me’useh</i>)<i>?</i> In our scenario, the wife lived, de facto, apart from her husband during five years of marriage, and subsequently was denied a divorce by her recalcitrant husband for over three years. Can she proceed to submit a claim for emotional distress she suffered during the five years of separation prior to seeking a divorce?
We will address this through the lens of some medieval <i>teshuvot</i> and contemporary rabbinic writings. <i>R. J. David Bleich</i> summarizes some early views and the normative Halakhah on the question of <i>kefiyat get</i> (coerced divorce) in other contexts:
<span class="poetry indentAll"><small>Rashba maintains that any coercion relating to execution of a <i>get</i>, even if self-imposed in the form of a voluntarily assumed penalty for non-execution, renders a <i>get</i> invalid … This view is disputed by R. Simon ben Zemah Duran, <i>Teshuvot ha-Rashbats</i> (<i>Tashbats</i>), II, no. 68, who declares, “One who says, ‘I will give 100 gold pieces to the king if I do not divorce my wife’ may divorce his wife and there is no question in the matter, for since this obligation came of his own accord, he divorces of his own will.”<sup class="footnote-marker">137</sup><i class="footnote">J. David Bleich, “Indirect Coercion in Compelling a <i>Get</i>,” 5 <i>Jewish Law Annual</i> (1985), 66–67. Though according to <i>Rashba</i>, any voluntarily assumed penalty related to the execution of a <i>get</i> constitutes a forced <i>get</i>, some decisors maintain that even a self-imposed penalty unrelated to the execution of a <i>get</i> will render the <i>get</i> invalid; see <i>Teshuvot Betzalel Ashkenazi</i> 15. Cf. <i>Torat Gittin</i>, EH 134:4; <i>Teshuvot Beit Ephraim</i> (2nd ed.), EH 73; <i>Teshuvot Ein Yitzhak</i> 2:33:1; <i>Hazon Ish</i>, EH 99.</i></small></span>
In essence, <i>Rashba</i>’s position is that a <i>get</i> is invalid when executed under duress even if such duress is indirect. Hence, duress compelling a person to fulfill a perfectly binding undertaking to pay compensation for failure to execute a religious divorce invalidates the <i>get</i>, since it is simply an indirect means of securing compliance in executing the <i>get</i>. Those who disagree with <i>Rashba</i> maintain either that self-imposed duress does not constitute duress or that since the enforceable demand is for financial compensation rather than for a <i>get</i>, a <i>get</i> executed under such circumstances is not to be regarded as executed under dur­ess. So long as satisfaction of a lawful claim remains a viable option in order to avoid execution of the <i>get</i>, execution of the <i>get</i> in order to avoid payment of a just debt is regarded as a voluntary act motivated by the self-interest of the husband.<sup class="footnote-marker">138</sup><i class="footnote">J. David Bleich, <i>Contemporary Halakhic Problems</i> (NY: 1989), vol. 3, 337. Obviously, a voluntary monetary obligation assumed by the husband in exchange for granting a <i>get</i> is valid. For example, a few years ago, the Supreme Rabbinical Court in Jerusalem validated a spousal agreement stipulating that in exchange for monetary payment, a husband would grant his wife a <i>get.</i> See 011588860-21-1, SRC, Mar. 6, 2005.</i>
<i>Rema</i> cites both conflicting opinions and rules that a <i>get</i> should be executed in the absence of prior forgiveness of the penalty for non-execution, but adds that if a <i>get</i> has been executed under such circumstances, it is valid even in the absence of prior for­giveness of the penalty, provided that the penalty was assumed voluntarily.<sup class="footnote-marker">139</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Rema</i>, EH 134:5. See Bleich, ibid., 96. Cf. <i>Pithei Teshuvah</i>, EH 134:10.</i>
O<i>nsa de-nafshei</i> (self-imposed duress) is the subject of much debate among the decisors. However, even under the sorts of circumstances in question, the <i>get</i> is valid ex post facto. The question we must address, however, is whether a wife can initiate such a claim prior to receiving her <i>get</i>. To be sure, there are certain types of monetary fines or incarcer­ation that do not raise concerns about <i>get me’useh</i>. As <i>Rivash</i> points out, certain kinds of pressure are not sufficiently coercive to render a divorce invalid, provided the coercive element arises from circumstances that are independent of the divorce.
<span class="poetry indentAll"><small>The case … involved a person cast into debtor’s prison for nonpayment of a debt. His wife’s relatives offered to satisfy the debt on his behalf and thereby obtain his release from prison on the condition that he divorce his wife. <i>Rivash</i> finds no objection to execution of a <i>get</i> under such circum­stances, “for he was not seized in order to [compel] him to divorce [his wife] but on account of his debt; the <i>get</i> is not coerced but [the product] of free will.”<sup class="footnote-marker">140</sup><i class="footnote">Bleich, n. 137 above, 67. This conclusion presumes that the sanctions are themselves legitimate; see <i>Teshuvot Mabit</i> 2: 138; <i>Teshuvot Betzalel Ashkenazi</i> 15; <i>Hazon Ish</i>, EH 99:3; I. Breitowitz, <i>Between Civil and Religious Law: The Plight of the Agunah in American Society</i> (Westport, CN: 1993), 26. Some argue that even if the husband was incarcerated due to a financial obligation not deemed a debt in the eyes of Halakhah or the incarceration was impermissible in the eyes of a Halakhah, the divorce would be proper; see <i>Teshuvot Rivash</i> 127; H. Izirer, “Duress in Monetary, Criminal, and Divorce matters” [Hebrew], 5 <i>Shurat ha-Din</i> (1999), 309–15.</i></small></span>
<i>Rivash</i>’s argument is that the husband was imprisoned for default­ing on a debt rather than as a means of compelling him to execute a divorce. Hence, his release in exchange for executing a divorce does not constitute duress, and the <i>get</i> is not regarded as unlawfully enforced.
<i>Rivash</i> takes the same approach in another <i>teshuvah</i>. A wife attempted to prevent her husband from leaving their locale lest she be denied her right to conjugal relations. Here, the coercion unrelated to execution of the divorce pertains to the husband’s refusal – in effect – to have conjugal relations:
<span class="poetry indentAll"><small>We put him under <i>nidui</i> [a ban], or flog him until he agrees to cohabit with her. If he, of his own [free will], divorces her, in order to save himself from those [measures], it is not an unlawfully enforced <i>get</i>. For the court did not compel him at all to give the <i>get</i>, but rather, to fulfill his conjugal obligation to the best of his ability, just as he is obligated by law to fulfill the [other] commandments.<sup class="footnote-marker">141</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot ha-Rivash</i> 127 (transl. from Kaplan, supra n. 124, 102), accepted by <i>Rema</i>, EH 154:21. Rema extends Rivash’s conclusion, arguing that a rabbinical court can even indirectly coerce a husband and tell him, “either resume conjugal relations or divorce your wife.” Whereas Rivash contends that a court’s power of compul­sion is limited to the restoration of conjugal ties, Rema argues, pursu­ant to his understanding of Rivash’s position, that the court may, in articulating its “coercive” directive, allude to divorce. Note that in the case addressed in the Rivash’s <i>teshuvah</i>, the woman is seeking to remain married, and there is no concern about a coerced divorce. Here, the “coercion by way of choice” (<i>kefiyah be-derekh bereirah</i>) differs from that applic­able in the more typical case, in which the coercive choice offered to the husband would be, “either support your wife or divorce her,” a form of coercion which would not invalidate the subsequent execution of a divorce. See <i>Beit Yosef</i>, EH 134 in the name of <i>Rashba</i>; <i>Beit Meir</i> 117; <i>Pithei Teshuvah</i>, EH 154:4, 8; <i>Iggerot Moshe</i>, EH 1:137; <i>PDR</i> 1:15, 19; 9:1542, 1551.</i></small></span>
Many authorities subscribe to the idea that <i>kefiyah li-devar aher</i> (coercion vis-à-vis another matter) is permissible.<sup class="footnote-marker">142</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot ha-Rivash</i> 232; <i>Teshuvot Tashbetz</i> 1:1; <i>Teshuvot Ranah</i> 1:63. On why <i>kefiyah li-devar aher</i> does not constitute coercion that renders a divorce invalid, see <i>Hazon Ish</i>, EH 99:1; <i>Teshuvot Bigdei Yesha</i>, 36:5; <i>Teshuvot Beit Ephraim</i> (2nd ed.), EH 1:73.</i> However, <i>Rabbis Hayyim Zimbalist</i> and <i>Avraham Azulai</i> added a caveat in a ruling handed down when they sat on the bench of the Tel Aviv District Rabbinical Court:
<span class="poetry indentAll"><small>A young woman had married a man who was serving a four-year prison term in Israel for drug offenses. The wife petitioned for a divorce on the grounds of nonsupport and loss of consortium. The Rabbinical Court of Tel Aviv … directed the husband to execute the <i>get</i> but did not find any grounds … that would mandate the direct imposition of coercive sanctions, such as additional imprisonment or fines. The issue before the court was whether it was … proper to recommend parole or reduction of sentence in exchange for the husband’s executing a <i>get .</i> . . On the one hand, the <i>get</i> is the mechanism by which the prisoner may obtain his freedom, and if a person is told, “Sign the <i>get</i> or stay in jail,” this resembles duress. Yet because the imprisonment was for completely unrelated offenses, the failure to give a <i>get</i> does not, in and of itself, result in direct coercion, but simply removes the … benefits of parole that would otherwise ensue.<sup class="footnote-marker">143</sup><i class="footnote">Breitowitz, supra n. 140, 24.</i></small></span>
Under these circumstances, though the imprisonment was unrelated to the delivery of the <i>get</i>, any recommendation of parole would render the subsequent execution of a <i>get</i> tainted by duress. The linkage of the parole recommendation to compliance with a rabbinical court’s direct­ive to grant a <i>get</i> is regarded by these decisors as coercion.<sup class="footnote-marker">144</sup><i class="footnote">They cite <i>Pithei Teshuvah</i>, YD 134:11, which invokes <i>Torat Gittin</i>, as the source for their ruling. See also Bleich, n. 147 above, 99. Cf. <i>Teshuvot Tashbetz</i> 1:1; <i>Mishnah la-Melekh</i>, <i>Hilkhot Geirushin</i> 2:20; <i>Teshuvot Pnei Moshe</i> 1:26; <i>Pithei Teshuvah</i>, EH 132:6. Cf. <i>Teshuvot ha-Rivash</i> 127; <i>Teshuvot Ranah</i> 1: 63; <i>Teshuvot Pnei Yehoshua</i> 75 and <i>Teshuvot Beit Ephraim</i> (2nd ed.), EH 1: 73, who argue that mention of divorce does not invalidate any subsequent execution of <i>a get</i>.</i>
Others, including <i>Rabbis Daichovsky</i>, <i>Elyashiv</i>, and <i>Rafael</i>, see no objection to executing a divorce under such circumstances, since the husband was imprisoned for failing to repay a debt rather than as a means to compel him to divorce his wife. Since the imprisonment was unrelated to the husband’s recalcitrance in giving a <i>get</i>, a release from prison in exchange for the execution of a <i>get</i> is not, they argue, to be construed as a form of coercion.<sup class="footnote-marker">145</sup><i class="footnote">3763/38, <i>PDR</i> 11:300, 302–307 (R. Daichovsky); <i>PDR</i> 16:271, 272–279 (R. Rafael), 275–276 (R. Elyashiv).</i>
A similar approach is upheld by <i>R. Feinstein</i>. As Professor Breitowitz summarizes:
<span class="poetry indentAll"><small>In a <i>teshuvah</i> dated 5719 (1959), R. Feinstein dealt with the following situation: a husband and wife were civilly divorced and the husband was ordered by a court to pay alimony. Failing to meet those obligations, the husband was imprisoned. The wife then agreed to drop her alimony claim (for arrearages) and procure his release if he would execute a <i>get</i>, which he did. Was this a <i>get me’useh?</i> R. Feinstein ruled that it was not, and such a <i>get</i> could be executed even <i>ab initio.</i><sup class="footnote-marker">146</sup><i class="footnote">Breitowitz, supra n. 140, 135, n. 391. See <i>Iggerot Moshe</i>, EH 1: 37. See also <i>Teshuvot Pnei Yehoshua</i> 2:75.</i></small></span>
Thus <i>R. Feinstein</i> validates a <i>get</i> granted in exchange for escaping a monetary obligation unrelated to the execution of the <i>get</i>, namely, pay­ing maintenance (<i>mezonot</i>).
<i>Rabbis Daichovsky</i>, <i>Elyashiv</i>, <i>Feinstein</i>, and <i>Rafael</i> concur in sub­scribing to the view expressed by <i>R. Moshe of Trani</i>:
<span class="poetry indentAll"><small>A <i>get</i> is only considered unlawfully enforced when [the husband] is coerced with regard to the divorce. But if he is coerced with regard to a different matter, and in order to free himself from that coercion he divorces his wife, [the <i>get</i>] is not regarded as unlawfully enforced.<sup class="footnote-marker">147</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot ha-Mabit</i> 1:22 (translation from Kaplan, supra n. 124, 104–5). Based on this <i>teshuvah</i> as well as <i>Teshuvot ha-Mabit</i> 1:76, this con­clusion applies whether the husband is unwilling to pay the debt or incapable of paying it. See the reading of <i>Mabit</i>’s position advanced in <i>Teshuvot Simhat Kohen</i>, vol. 2, EH 9.<br>On the consistency of these <i>teshuvot</i> by <i>Mabit</i> with other <i>teshuvot</i>, he wrote on the matter (2:138 and 206), see R. Zvi Gartner, <i>Kefiyat Get</i>, 376–77. R. Gartner’s conclusion differs from my own. He contends that <i>Mabit</i>’s true position, expressed in 2:206 and 3:212, is that a husband’s inability or unwillingness to satisfy an independent financial claim does taint any subsequent execution of a divorce; see R. Gartner, ibid., EH 134, 379. Though the <i>teshuvot</i> on which R. Gartner bases his conclusion reflect a minority view and stand in con­tradiction to <i>Mabit</i>’s <i>teshuvot</i> 1:22 and 76, on which I base my own reading of <i>Mabit</i>, and though R. Gartner, in a written com­munication to this author, acknowledges that this minority view is not supported by other <i>Aharonim</i>, some contemporary decisors nonetheless concur with R. Gartner’s understanding of Mabit’s position. See Bleich, supra n. 138, 339; Izirer, n. 140 above, 312; Yosef Goldberg, “Duress Unrelated to Divorce” [Hebrew], 7 <i>Shurat ha-Din</i> (2002): 353; <i>Teshuvot Mayyim Tehorim</i>, EH 15; Uriel Lavi, “Arranging a <i>Get</i> after the Husband has Been Obligated to Pay His Wife Monetary Damages” [Hebrew], 26 <i>Tehumin</i> (2006), 160, 165. In endorsing R. Gartner’s interpretation of <i>Mabit</i>’s view, these decisors argue that we must show deference to the minority view due to the seriousness of the halakhic consequences of an invalid divorce. See J. David Bleich, “Monetary Agreement for the Purpose of Avoidance of <i>Get</i> Recalcitrance” [Hebrew], 41 <i>Or ha-Mizrach</i> (1992), 272–80.<br>In light of the internal contradictions in 2:138 as well as its inconsis­tency with two of <i>Mabit</i>’s other <i>teshuvot</i>, as well as the fact that other <i>Aharonim</i> reject this position or uphold the reading of <i>Mabit</i>’s pos­ition I have endorsed, I see no reason to factor it into decision-making on this matter. Indeed, neither R. Feinstein nor R. Rafael invoke this view. Though <i>argumenta ex silentio</i> of this sort are inconclusive, this silence of major authorities does serve to support the lenient position.</i></small></span>
As long as the sanctions applied are, from the outset, intended to address a breach of an independent claim that is halakhically legitim­ate, and are not simply a means of pressuring the husband to grant the divorce, any subsequent execution of a <i>get</i> will be valid. Just as accord­ing to <i>Rivash</i>, social shunning of one who fails to have conjugal relations with his wife does not create a <i>get me’useh</i>, so too compensating the wife for mental anguish engendered by denial of conjugal relations does not taint any subsequent divorce.
What about a sanction that is ostensibly independent of the <i>get</i>, but employed for purposes of compelling the <i>get?</i> Is the <i>kavanah</i> (intent) to introduce an independent claim at a particular point in time relevant in determining whether a divorce is coerced? How is intent to be ascertained – by noting whether anything is said about divorce when the independent claim is made, by judging the wife’s actions, or by inference from the circumstances? Some argue that even if divorce is merely mentioned, this indicates that the intent of the independent financial claim is to induce the husband to grant the divorce. If divorce is not mentioned, we can assume that the intent of advancing the finan­cial claim is to have it settled, rather than for it to serve as leverage toward procuring a <i>get</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">148</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Iggerot Moshe</i>, EH 1:137, s.v. <i>veto muvan</i>; <i>Teshuvot Ein Yitzhak</i>, EH 2:33:1–6; Gartner, ibid., 379, 386.</i>
Others contend that the focus must be the claim itself. Emotional distress generated by refusal to grant a divorce, financial coercion and blackmail on the part of the husband in the course of the negotiations, and the anguish arising from years of divorce litigation cannot serve as grounds for a monetary award. Claims for compensation for these injuries are related to the <i>get</i> and therefore contravene the requirement that the <i>get</i> be freely given. However, mental anguish due to the absence of conjugal relations is an independent claim, and therefore may be grounds for granting an award. The point in time at which the wife submitted the claim or her verbal remarks linking the claim to receiving a <i>get</i> need not be considered by the court. As we have seen, such a claim may be advanced even where divorce is not contemplated.
In our scenario, a couple had been living separately under the same roof for five years. Though the wife would have liked to engage in conjugal relations with her spouse, her husband willfully refused to have rela­tions. For the sake of their children, the couple remained outwardly married while in actuality living separately. In principle, at any time during this period of separation, the wife could have chosen to submit a claim against her husband for intentional infliction of mental anguish. For various reasons, she chose to refrain from advancing such a claim prior to deciding to be divorced, but the grounds for such a claim did exist throughout the five years of de facto separation. In other words, this claim is inextricably linked to the emotional stress engendered due to the absence of conjugal relations within their mar­riage, and not introduced merely for the sake of pressuring her hus­band to grant the divorce. The fact that subsequent to her years of anguish, the wife is being held hostage by her husband’s recalcitrance, financial coercion, and blackmail ought not impact the legitimacy of her claim, and should not serve to invalidate any subsequent execution of a divorce. Her intent in advancing the claim at this time, three years after the court’s issuance of a divorce directive, is halakhically insignificant.
Others disagree, and invalidate any <i>get</i> executed subsequent to the wife’s recourse to such a claim for damages. They maintain that even though a self-standing claim, such as a claim for payment of a monet­ary debt or the amount specified in the <i>ketuba</i>, is not linked to a request for execution of a divorce, nevertheless, if there is an established pre­sumption (<i>umdena de-mukhah</i>) that these claims were put forward pri­marily to procure a divorce, any subsequent delivery of a <i>get</i> is invalid.<sup class="footnote-marker">149</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Maharashdam</i>, EH 63; <i>Teshuvot Rav Pe’alim</i> 2:3. In a recent decision handed down by the Jerusalem Supreme Rabbinical Court, R. Zion Algerbli states:<br>There is no reason for reluctance to submit to the court a claim for tort damages for <i>boshet</i> and the like due to one spouse’s neglect of the other … even if the claim is procedurally linked to [i.e., submitted along with] a claim for divorce. However, it must be clear that the tort claim was not submitted for the purpose of pressuring the husband to grant a <i>get .</i> . . Only a rabbinical court may determine whether this claim impacts the propriety of the <i>get</i>. (7041-21-1, SRC, Mar. 11, 2008)<br>If, in the eyes of the court, the claim is self-standing and not intended to coerce the granting of a divorce, any subsequent granting of the divorce is proper. On the other hand, <i>Teshuvot Ranah</i> 1:63; <i>Teshuvot Beit Ephraim</i>, EH 1:73; <i>Teshuvot ha-Mabit</i> 1:22; and <i>Teshuvot Pnei Yehoshua</i> 75 all contend that the intent is irrelevant and the subsequent divorce proper.</i> However, our situation is readily distinguishable from situ­ations in which such a presumption is warranted. In our case, even if the ostensibly independent claim for damages for emotional distress was motivated by the desire to procure a divorce, it is a claim, and not an existing obligation. In the other situations, the husband grants the divorce in exchange for the wife’s agreement to release him from a financial duty, in effect saying to him, “Pay up or grant a <i>get</i>.” Our case deals with the advancement of a claim that may or may not result in the awarding of damages.<sup class="footnote-marker">150</sup><i class="footnote">Many decisors contend that a husband who gives a divorce due to a threat is deemed to have been forced to grant the <i>get</i>; see <i>Teshuvot ha-Rashba</i> 2:276; <i>Teshuvot Maharival</i> 2:77; <i>Teshuvot Shem Aryeh</i> 93–94; <i>Teshuvot Avnei Nezer</i>, EH 178:2–3; <i>Pithei Teshuvah</i>, EH 134:15 in the name of the Ridbaz. But these rulings are readily distinguishable from our case, since all deal with situations of “clear and present danger” – that is, these are cases in which a husband is being threatened with harm, imprisonment, or death if he refuses to consent to a divorce, and hence all are indeed instances of unlawfully coerced divorce. By contrast, in our case, the husband is threatened by a monetary claim too remote to rise to the level of compulsion, and especially since the amount of the potential award to the wife is, as we saw, highly variable. As <i>Tashbetz</i> comments, “If he has a remote fear of financial loss and therefore desires to give a <i>get</i>, ought one label this a coerced <i>get</i>?” See <i>Teshuvot Tashbetz</i> 2:69. Indeed, the wife may threaten to submit a claim and never follow through with her threat; see <i>Mordekhai</i>, <i>Gittin</i> 395; <i>Teshuvot Maharik</i> 185; <i>Mikhtav me-Eliyahu</i>, gate 7, no. 18. But cf. <i>Teshuvot ha-Rashba</i> 1:883; <i>Teshuvot ha-Rivash</i> 27 and 32; and <i>Teshuvot Betzalel Ashkenazi</i> 15, which argue that even a mere threat constitutes duress. See also Kaplan, supra n. 124, 98.<br>Obviously, if the rabbinical courts do begin to award damages for this claim, and it becomes a “clear and present danger” akin to the threat of imprisonment, the threat that such a claim will be submitted may render execution of the <i>get</i> coerced. However, even were such an award to become commonplace, the amount of the compensation would not be known in advance, and hence the threat remains remote; see Breitowitz, supra n. 140, 248. Moreover, if a wife threatens to cause her husband financial loss out of spite, and the husband, of his own initiative, decides to give her a <i>get</i>, her threat is not deemed duress, and the <i>get</i> is valid. See <i>Pithei Teshuvah</i>, EH 134:11; <i>Iggerot Moshe</i> 1:137. A fortiori, in our case, a wife’s submission of an independent claim for damages unrelated to the divorce ought not impugn the validity of a subsequent <i>get.</i> Similarly, if there has been no threat to submit such a claim, but the husband grants the divorce because he fears that such a claim will be made if he fail to do so, this is not deemed to be duress; see <i>Teshuvot Bnei Hayai</i>, HM 2:81.</i> Since the consequences of the suit are not spelled out in advance, the putative “coercion” is too remote to rise to the level of duress. Whereas the said authorities invoke an established presumption in the context of an existing independent outstanding debt, which does entail coercion of a <i>get</i>, in our case there is no such existing debt, but only the threat that a claim for damages may be presented to the court.
Moreover, on its face, the advancement of her claim for monetary damages in order to coerce her husband to grant a <i>get</i> is analogous to the case of a husband who receives money in exchange for a divorce, which does not render the resulting divorce a <i>get me’useh.</i> As <i>R. Rafael</i> elucidates:
<span class="poetry indentAll"><small>One is allowed to release from imprisonment someone who is serving time for contempt of the rabbinical court [failing to produce information requested by the court], and condition his release on his giving his wife a <i>get</i>. For his imprisonment is not related to his recalcitrant refusal to grant the <i>get</i>, but is, rather, punishment for contempt of court, and he is redeeming himself by giving the <i>get</i>. And at the time, R. Elyashiv agreed with me.<br>One can promise a prisoner that his term of imprisonment will be reduced by a third in exchange for giving a <i>get .</i> . . and I hear from R. Elyashiv, long may he live, that it is clear that this is not considered a coerced divorce, and it is like [the case of] a wife who purchases her <i>get</i> for a certain sum of money – such a <i>get</i> is entirely acceptable (<i>kosher le-mehadrin</i>).<sup class="footnote-marker">151</sup><i class="footnote"><i>PDR</i> 16:271, 275–276. Whether R. Elyashiv and R. Rafael would agree that a husband’s failure to heed the rabbinical court’s ruling that he should divorce his wife constitutes contempt of the rabbinical court that may be punishable by imprisonment, and whether they would agree to a release in exchange for his granting a <i>get</i>, is an open question. The logic of their positions would seem to imply such a conclusion, but they have not specifically addressed the question of whether failing to heed a court’s divorce judgment constitutes denigration of the court; see infra at nn. 159, 170, and 176.</i></small></span>
In various rabbinical judgments “obligating divorce,” <i>R. Elyashiv</i> permits a husband whose wife does not wish to accept a bill of divorce to appease his recalcitrant wife by offering money in exchange for her accepting the <i>get</i>; the ensuing divorce is not deemed to be unlawfully coerced.<sup class="footnote-marker">152</sup><i class="footnote"><i>PDR</i> 7:111; 8:36; 9:65. R. Avraham Sherman, a student of R. Elyashiv, subscribes to his position; see 2337, SRC, Dec. 27, 2004. See also <i>Teshuvot Tashbetz</i> 1:1; <i>Teshuvot ha-Rosh</i> 35:2; <i>Teshuvot Hemdat Shlomo</i>, EH 80(2); <i>Teshuvot Tzemah Tzedek</i> 262–263; <i>Teshuvot Yabia Omer</i>, vol. 6, EH 10. For a contemporary exposition, see the advisory opinion of R. Shimon Ya’acobi, legal advisor for Israel’s Rabbinical Courts Administration, in Bagatz 2609/05, <i>Plonit v. Supreme Rabbinical Court et al.</i></i> <i>Teshuvot</i> in <i>Tashbetz</i>, <i>Torat Gittin</i>, and <i>Noda be-She’arim</i> (R. Dov Berish Ashkenazi) attest to the not-uncommon phenomenon of wives absconding with their recalcitrant’s husband’s assets and releas­ing them only upon receiving a <i>get</i>. It is hardly, if ever, claimed that these were improper inducements that rendered the divorces coerced.<sup class="footnote-marker">153</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Tashbetz</i> 4:35; <i>Torat Gittin</i> 134:4; <i>Teshuvot Noda be-She’arim</i> 6. See also <i>Pithei Teshuvah</i>, EH 134:11 in the name of <i>Rashbatz</i>. Some authorities argue that if the wife has stolen large sums from her husband and is willing to return the money in exchange for a divorce, the sub­sequent execution of the divorce is indeed coerced; see <i>Teshuvot Tzemah Tzedek</i>, EH 262:3.</i> Financial inducements, whether to appease a recalcitrant husband or a recalcitrant wife, do not contravene the strictures against coerced divorce.
<i>R. Ezra Batzri</i>, a Jerusalem rabbinical court judge, analyzes the dynamics of such a situation as follows:
<span class="poetry indentAll"><small>Should a rabbinical court be aware that the husband is interested in divorcing his wife, his intent to extort monies from her … constitutes coercion by way of unjustified extortion of money from the wife. Essen­tially, [any steps the court takes] do not coerce him to divorce her, since he desires to divorce his wife. He is employing the divorce as a means to achieve things improperly … As long as the husband is not interested in peace and the court is aware that the purpose of delaying the <i>get</i> is not to foster peace with his wife, but on the contrary, to cause pain and take revenge on her or extort from her monies that do not rightfully belong to him – in such a situation, there is no apprehension that the divorce is coerced.<sup class="footnote-marker">154</sup><i class="footnote"><i>PDR</i> 18:71, 81. See also <i>Teshuvot Heikhal Yitzhak</i>, EH 158; <i>Teshuvot Tiferet Tzvi</i>, EH 102; <i>Iggerot Moshe</i>, EH 3:44; Gartner, n. 147 above, 244; <i>Teshuvot Shema Shlomo</i>, vol. 1, EH 15:3; Haifa District Rabbinical Court, 050562289-13-1. The same rationale is given in the context of a wife har­assing her loving husband to grant her a divorce; see <i>Arukh ha-Shulhan</i>, EH 134:22; Haifa District Rabbinical Court, 061391348-21-1.</i></small></span>
In short, financial inducements to procure a divorce do not render the divorce coerced. In effect, a wife’s willingness to waive an award for mental anguish in exchange for her <i>get</i> is no different than forgiving a debt or transferring cash for a delivery of a <i>get</i>. Hence, a wife’s claim for damages for mental anguish due to her husband’s denying her conjugal relations during the years the two were living under the same roof prior to the divorce ought to be permissible.<sup class="footnote-marker">155</sup><i class="footnote">Note that those authorities who argue that any discussion and/or arrangement to waive this claim in exchange for delivery of the <i>get</i> would render execution of the <i>get</i> coerced would insist that there be no discus­sion of the <i>get</i> when the claim is submitted.</i>
Given that such a claim can be put forward, how does the court proceed? Once both the husband and the wife obligate themselves to submit to the court’s resolution of the wife’s claim for mental anguish,<sup class="footnote-marker">156</sup><i class="footnote">The parties’ signing of an arbitration agreement (<i>shtar borerut</i>) gives the court the authority to resolve this matter; see <i>Rema</i>, HM 12:7; <i>Sma</i>, HM 12:18; Yoezer Ariel, “The Halakhic Need for a <i>Shtar Borerut”</i> [Hebrew],14 <i>Tehumin</i> (5754),147, 152; <i>Mishpatekha le-Yaakov</i> 2:405–406; Zvi Lifshitz, “Compensation for Verbal Embarrassment” [Hebrew], 17 <i>Tehumin</i> (5757), 381, 388. Assuming this decision complies with the rules of secular arbi­tration procedure, it would be legally enforceable in a competent civil jurisdiction in the United States; see <i>Uniform Arbitration Act</i>, sec. 1.<br>To address claims for emotional distress, an arbitration agreement that will be halakhically binding on both parties as well as legally enforceable in civil courts should include the following clause:<br>The parties acknowledge that the <i>beit din</i> is authorized to resolve all disputes, including, but not limited to, a spouse’s refusal to engage in conjugal relations, coercion of the other spouse to engage in con­jugal relations, distribution of assets, spousal support, child support, child custody and a spouse’s refusal to give the other spouse a Jewish divorce or receive a Jewish divorce from the other spouse.<br>Signing off on this clause can be construed as a threat that either spouse may in the future assert a claim for mental anguish due to having been denied conjugal relations during the period the divorce was withheld. As we have seen, this type of threat will not impair the legitimacy of the sub­sequent execution of a <i>get</i> in order to stave off this claim or payment of an award for this claim. The divorce is valid provided that any monetary award, such as damages for <i>tza’ar-boshet</i>, is self-standing and independent of the <i>get</i>. See Daichovsky, supra n. 169, 300, and idem., “Monetary Steps of Enforcement Against <i>Mesarvei Get</i>” [Hebrew], 26<i>Tehumin</i> (5766), 173–7.</i> the court proceeds to address the claim on the merits. Upon deliberation, should the court rule in the wife’s favor and hand down an award, it will direct the husband to pay this award. Should the husband refuse to pay the <i>tza’ar-boshet</i> award, the award can be enforced in civil court. On the other hand, should the husband accept the rabbinical court’s decision, he has the option of suggesting to his wife that in exchange for a divorce, she waive her entitlement to the monetary damages awarded her by the court; the resulting <i>get</i> will not be tainted by coercion.<sup class="footnote-marker">157</sup><i class="footnote">See supra at nn.153–155. See also <i>Teshuvot Beit Ephraim</i>, vol. 3, EH 73 (288a-b); <i>Teshuvot Zera Anashim</i>, 36; <i>Teshuvot Tashbetz</i> 1:1; <i>Teshuvot Hemdat Shlomo</i>, EH 80.</i> However, to avoid any concerns that the divorce will be deemed coerced, the monetary award should not be “excessive” and should be tenable given the husband’s financial situation.<sup class="footnote-marker">158</sup><i class="footnote">According to R. Yitzhak Herzog, a <i>get</i> executed in order to escape an exorbi­tant support obligation would run afoul of the strictures of a <i>get me’useh</i>. However, if the stipulated sum is within the husband’s ability to pay, his decision to grant a <i>get</i> is considered voluntary; see <i>Teshuvot Heikhal Yitzhak</i>, EH 1:1, 2. Analogously, as long as the <i>tza’ar-boshet</i> award is nei­ther unreasonable nor exorbitant, it will not render the divorce coerced.</i>
What happens if a husband refuses to proceed to <i>beit din</i> concerning a wife’s claim for mental anguish due to denial of conjugal relations? Upon the court’s determination that the husband’s refusal to comply is unwarranted, the <i>beit din</i> will issue a “contempt of court” order (<i>shtar seruv</i>), with all its attendant consequences,<sup class="footnote-marker">159</sup><i class="footnote">Refusal to comply with a court decision or being declared in contempt of court (<i>mesarev le-din</i>) makes one subject to being placed under a ban (<i>nidui</i>); see <i>SA</i>, HM 11:1; <i>Iggerot Moshe</i>, YD 3: 142:2; <i>PDR</i> 11:168–181; <i>SA</i>, YD 334:43; <i>Teshuvot Maharil Diskin</i>, rulings, 52; <i>Teshuvot Hatam Sofer</i>, HM 177.</i> and a dispensation for the other party to initiate proceedings in civil court (<i>heter arkha’ot</i>)<i>.</i><sup class="footnote-marker">160</sup><i class="footnote"><i>SA</i>, HM 26:2; <i>Arukh ha-Shulhan</i>, HM 26:5; <i>Teshuvot ha-Rema</i> 52; <i>Teshuvot Maharsham</i> 4:105. Others contend that generally or under certain condi­tions, a dispensation is not even required; see <i>Sefer ha-Terumot</i>, <i>Gidulei Terumah</i>, gate 62:3; <i>Tumim</i>, HM 26:7; <i>Kesef ha-Kodshin</i>, <i>SA</i>, HM 26; <i>Teshuvot Tuv Ta’am va-Da’at</i> 3: 261; <i>Teshuvot Maharil Diskin</i>, rul­ings,13. Though some decisors will allow a person to approach a rabbi for a <i>heter arkha’ot</i>, the customary practice today is to request permission from the court; see Asher Weiss, “Permission to Litigate in Civil Court” [Hebrew], 5 <i>Kovetz Darkhei Hora’ah</i> (2006), 99, 102. Whether one requires permission from a panel of three <i>dayanim</i> or it suffices to receive permission from the presiding head of the court (<i>av beit din</i>) or a single <i>dayan</i> appointed by the community is subject to debate; see <i>Teshuvot Maharsham</i> 4:105; <i>Orah Mishpat</i>, HM 26:2; <i>Teshuvot be-Tzel ha-Hokhmah</i> 4:37. Whether one approaches a court or one’s own rabbi, it behooves the authority to hear both sides of the matter prior to issuing any dispensation to litigate in a secular court; see <i>Pithei Teshuvah</i>, HM 67:5; <i>Sefer Meishiv ba-Halakhah</i>, 38–40.</i> Alternatively, prior to the wife’s request to have the <i>beit din</i> address this claim, she may ask a rabbi who has expertise in <i>Even ha-Ezer</i> and <i>Hoshen Mishpat</i> whether she can proceed to civil court without a <i>beit din’s</i> permission, or, if a <i>heter arkha’ot</i> is required, whether she may approach the <i>beit din</i> that dealt with her claim or whether she is allowed to approach another <i>beit din</i> to request permission to have her claim heard in civil court. Note that a <i>heter arkha’ot</i> does not mean that the wife has a <i>carte blanche</i> to advance any and all claims in civil court. Only if the claim is legitimate in the eyes of the Halakhah can it be asserted in civil court.<sup class="footnote-marker">161</sup><i class="footnote">Should a party recover money in civil court that he would not have been awarded in a rabbinical court, it is viewed as stolen and must be returned to the other party; see <i>Teshuvot Tashbetz</i> 2:290; <i>Teshuvot Hut ha-Me­shulash</i> 1:19; <i>SA</i>, HM 28:3; <i>Teshuvot Rabbi Akiva Eiger</i>, HM 26:1. There is an opinion that in cases of <i>boshet</i>, one can recover a civil monetary award which would exceed the amount awarded by a <i>beit din</i>; see <i>Kesef ha-Kodshin</i>, HM 26:2. According to most authorities, this <i>heter arkha’ot</i> is granted whether we are dealing with a claim that is provable (<i>hov barur</i>), e.g., by a creditor’s producing an authenticated document of indebtedness, or whether the claim is dis­puted, e.g., a claim for injuries. See <i>Teshuvot Imrei Bina</i> 27; <i>Yeshuot Yisrael</i> 26:2; <i>Teshuvot Orah Mishpat</i> 26:2. In the opinion of <i>Netivot ha-Mishpat</i>, which argues that a disputed claim cannot be resolved in civil court, a claim for damages for mental anguish may not be resolved there; see <i>Netivot ha-Mishpat</i>, HM 26: <i>Hiddushim</i> (8) and <i>Bi’urim</i> (3). However, I would sug­gest that a claim for damages for mental anguish is to be subsumed under the category of provable claims, given that the abstention from sexual relations is a halakhic fact; see supra n. 73.</i>
On being summoned to the civil proceeding, can the wife assert a claim against her husband for recklessly causing her severe emotional distress in connection with their years of divorce litigation? In fact, there have been a few instances of Jewish wives lodging claims in American courts against their Jewish spouses who wrongfully refused to grant a <i>get</i>, thus inflicting emotional distress.<sup class="footnote-marker">162</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Roth v. Roth</i>, #79-192709-DO (Mich. Cir. Ct. Jan. 23,1980); <i>Perl v. Perl</i>, 126 A.D. 91, 512 N.Y.S. 2d 372, 1987 N.Y. App Div.; <i>Weiss v. Goldfeder</i>, New York Law Journal, Oct. 26, 1990; <i>Golding v. Golding</i>, 176 A.D. 2d 20,581 N.Y.S. 4,1992 N.Y. App Div.; <i>Giahn v. Giahn</i>, New York Law Journal, Apr. 13, 2000, N.Y. Supreme Ct.</i> Are such claims halakhically legitimate or do they risk tainting a subsequently executed <i>get</i>? Addressing a French court’s award of compensation for a hus­band’s failure to execute a Jewish divorce, contemporary decisors, fol­lowing earlier authorities, have construed such an award as a classic example of financial compulsion (<i>ones mamon</i>), viz., indirectly securing compliance in executing a <i>get</i> by exerting financial pressure.<sup class="footnote-marker">163</sup><i class="footnote">In <i>D. v. France</i>, 35 Eur. Comm. H.R.D.R. 199 (1983), the husband had been ordered by a French court to pay his ex-wife 25,000 francs to compensate her for his refusal to deliver a <i>get</i>. See <i>Teshuvot Shevet Ha-Levi</i>, vol. 5, EH 210; <i>Teshuvot Minhat Yitzhak</i> 8:136; 7041-21-1, <i>Plonit v. Ploni</i>, SRC, Mar. 11, 2008. For a survey of civil jurisdictions worldwide that have awarded such compensation, see <i>Bruker v. Marcovitz</i>, 2007 SCC 54 (CanLII).</i>
The qualms expressed regarding the aforementioned claim do not apply to our claim for mental anguish due to denial of conjugal relations during marriage. As I have argued, such a claim is made solely to protect the wife’s emotional integrity. Hence, just as a rabbinical court may deliberate on the merits of such a claim, so too such a claim, submitted to a civil court, may be addressed on the merits.<sup class="footnote-marker">164</sup><i class="footnote">The tort of intentional infliction of emotional distress (IIED), independent of physical harm or impact, has been recognized in all US states; see Robert D. Sack, <i>Sack on Defamation: Libel</i>, <i>Slander and Related Problems</i>, 3rd ed. (NY: 2005), §13.6, pp. 13–45. It appears that no action for damages for precluding a spouse from engaging in sexual relations has ever been brought before an American court. Whether American courts would rec­ognize such emotional harm is an open question. Many jurisdictions are increasingly recognizing IIED tort actions between spouses; see L. Karp, <i>Domestic Torts: Family Violence</i>, <i>Conflict and Sexual Abuse</i> (rev. ed. 2005), vol. 1, 116. New York does not allow such claims; see <i>Xiao Yang Chen v. Fisher</i>, 843 N.E. 2d 723 (N.Y. 2005). Some jurisdictions have concluded that the defendant’s conduct failed to be outrageous enough to allow the plain­tiff’s claim for IIED; see <i>Hassing v. Wortman</i>, 333 N.W. 2d 765 (Neb. 1983); <i>Nagy v. Nagy</i>, 210 Cal. App. 3d 1262 (Cal. 1989); <i>Alexander v. Inman</i>, 825 S.W. 2d 102 (Term. 1991); <i>Ruprecht v. Ruprecht</i>, 599 A. 2d 604 (N.J. 1991); <i>McCul­lob v. Drake</i>, 24 P. 3d 1162 (Wyo. 2001). Other states have held husbands liable for emotional distress, provided there was harassment or assault and battery; see Karp, 118–135. Recently, a court awarded damages for IIED to a wife whose husband refused to grant her a <i>get</i>; see <i>Tessler v. Zadok</i>, #2:08-cv-05695-R-RC (California District Ct., May 11, 2009).<br>Some states have, for public policy reasons, refrained from permitting such suits in general, and in particular those that emerge from marital differences; see Linda Berger, “Lies Between Mommy and Daddy: The Case for Recognizing Spousal Emotional Distress Claims Based on Domestic Deceit that Interferes with Parent-Child Relationships,” 33 <i>Loyola of Los Angeles Law Rev.</i> (2000), 459, 467–70; I. Ellman and S. Sugarman, “Spousal emotional abuse as a tort?” 55 <i>Maryland Law Review</i> (1996), 1269, 1285–89; B. Redman, “Jewish Divorce: What Can Be Done in Secular Courts to Aid the Jewish Woman?” 19 <i>Georgia Law Review</i> (1985), 389, 422–23; R. Orsinger, “Assert­ing Claims for Intentionally or Recklessly causing Severe Emotional Distress in Connection with Divorce,” 25 <i>St. Mary’s Law Journal</i> (1994), 1253, 1293–94. In a written communication, Marc Stern, formerly General Counsel of the American Jewish Congress, observes that given that certain states have a policy of precluding interspousal damage awards, a court may decline to enforce an arbitration award based on public policy grounds; see <i>Aleem v. Aleem</i>, 2008 WL 1945345 (MD 2008).<br>Numerous proposals for and against limiting recovery for emotional distress have been put forward. On the grounds for recognition of the tort, see <i>Restatement</i> (<i>Second</i>) <i>of Torts</i>, sec. 46, comments d, e, f and j (1965). As to the argument that First Amendment problems of entanglement of law and religion preclude the possibility that a civil court will offer relief to a recalci­trant spouse in the context of a Jewish divorce, it has been argued that there is a compelling secular interest to afford such relief; see S. Friedell, “The first amendment and Jewish divorce: A comment on <i>Stern v. Stern</i>,” 18 <i>Journal of Family Law</i> (1979–1980), 525; Redman, 416–25; David Cobin, “Jewish Divorce and the Recalcitrant Husband: Refusal to Give a <i>Get</i> as Inten­tional Infliction of Emotional Distress,” 4 <i>Journal of Law and Religion</i> (1986), 405, 425–29; Michelle Greenberg-Kobrin, “Civil Enforcement of Religious Prenuptial Agreements,” 32 <i>Columbia Journal of Law and Social Problems</i> (1999), 359. On the other hand, Marc Stern contends that entertaining such claims entails entanglement of law and religion on two separate grounds: (a) Awarding such a claim involves addressing the threshold issue of whether the Halakhah mandates the grant­ing or receiving of a <i>get</i>; (b) The granting of such an award amounts to the state’s coercing an individual to comply with a religious act.<br>In Israel, given that the dissolution of Jewish marital ties is effected via execution of a Jewish divorce, the constitutional issue of establishment of religion does not arise. In fact, commencing with 2001, four judges have awarded tort damages against a recalcitrant spouse; see 19480/05, Jerusalem Family Court, Apr. 30, 2006. For additional cases after 2006, see Addendum A.</i> Furthermore, even if it can be demonstrated that the civil court will award more damages than a rabbinical court would, the wife may retain the entire award handed down by a civil court.<sup class="footnote-marker">165</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Kesef ha-Kodshin</i>, HM 26:2. In the view of Rabbis Daichovsky, Elyashiv and Z. Goldberg, prior to accepting an award from a civil court, one must ascertain the appropriateness of the amount of the award. Generally speaking, one must ask his rabbi whether the amount of the award would have been granted in a rabbinical court. If a rabbinical court would have awarded a smaller amount, then only that amount may be accepted; see M. Ralbag, “Litigating in Civil Court with the Rabbinical Court’s Permis­sion,” [Hebrew] 25 <i>Tehumin</i> (5765), 249, 251–52. However, in our case, given that it is permissible to accept a civil court award for an amount larger than may have been awarded in a rabbinical court, there is no requirement that one’s rabbi be consulted about the propriety of accepting the civil award.</i>
<b>V. Concluding Remarks</b>
We have focused on a particular type of contemporary <i>agunah</i>: the wife who seeks damages for mental anguish caused by her husband's refusal to engage in conjugal relations while a divorce is being withheld. This raises a question: given that the primary emotional trauma is due to her husband’s recalcitrance in granting a divorce rather than the denial of conjugal relations, may the wife put forward a claim for damages for this period without rendering a subsequent divorce unlawfully coerced (<i>get me’useh</i>)? More broadly, may a con­temporary <i>agunah</i>, even one who shares a domicile with her husband, but decides to seek a divorce due to irreconcilable differences or spou­sal abuse, and is met by intransigence on the part of her husband, advance a claim for mental anguish incurred during the period of her husband’s recalcitrance? In effect, may an <i>agunah</i> seek claims for emotional stress from her husband for the period of her inability to remarry and engage in conjugal relations with her husband?<sup class="footnote-marker">166</sup><i class="footnote">This claim may be grounds for an award in one of the following ways. First, there is a woman’s compelling desire to be married. Alternatively, an individual who prevents another individual from performing a mitzvah can receive damages <i>min ha-din</i> (black-letter Halakhah) rather than as a <i>kenas</i> (fine). For a discussion of these claims, see the addendum at the end of this chapter.</i> We have argued that as long as the monetary obligation is not linked to the divorce, but independent, and the penalty will remain in place after the <i>get</i> has been granted, execution of the <i>get</i> will be deemed proper. In the words of <i>Torat Gittin</i>:
<span class="poetry indentAll"><small>If he obligated himself with a penalty, should he fail to divorce her by a specific date, and he retracted and refused to divorce her until after the stipulated date, once the stipulated date passes he becomes obligated to pay the penalty even if he divorces her … And the court can mandate the penalty, and it is akin to coercing someone to pay his debt, and if he divorces her in order to free himself from paying the debt, the <i>get</i> is not deemed a forced <i>get</i>, as noted by <i>Rashbatz</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">167</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Torat Gittin</i>, EH 134:4.</i></small></span>
The reasoning is that while monetary pressure is improper if the granting of the <i>get</i> will free the husband of his monetary obligation, if the penalty will have to be paid regardless of the giving of the <i>get</i>, then financial pressure is not the motivating factor in his granting the <i>get</i> and will not taint its execution. The wife may be willing to waive her right to the money in exchange for the <i>get</i>. Such a waiver is proper and no different from what transpires when the court has mandated that a husband pay a debt unrelated to the divorce, such as the <i>ketubah</i> pay­ment, and the couple mutually agree to waive payment of the <i>ketubah</i> in exchange for the <i>get</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">168</sup><i class="footnote">See Bleich, supra n. 147, 272–6.</i>
In light of <i>teshuvot</i> dating back to <i>Rivash</i>, and in light of <i>Torat Gittin</i>’s exposition of the Halakhah, <i>R. Daichovsky</i> argues that a wife may collect <i>tza’ar-boshet</i> damages for the mental anguish caused by her hus­band’s conduct. As these damages relate to the husband’s conduct in the past, even if he decides to grant his wife a divorce, the damages stand and must be paid. And even if the wife waives her right to these damages, this decision will in no way impair the fitness of the <i>get</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">169</sup><i class="footnote">S. Daichovsky, “Rabbinical Courts and Civil Courts: Thoughts on Their Overlapping Boundaries in Family Matters” [Hebrew], 4 <i>Moznei Mishpat</i> (2005), 261, 295–98; idem. 2006, supra n. 156, 173–7. But cf. <i>Shevet ha-Levi</i> and <i>Minhat Yitzhak</i>, supra n. 163, who argue that secular legislation mandating an award for a husband’s recalcitrance in granting a <i>get</i> is improper monetary pressure that would taint subsequent execution of the <i>get</i>. Recent Israeli rabbinical court rulings have refused, on similar grounds, to mandate delivery of a <i>get</i> in the face of such tort claims presented by women in the Israeli civil courts. See Tel Aviv District Rabbinical Court, 031783426, Aug. 4, 2005, 02786214-2, Dec. 6, 2006; Netanya District Rabbinical Court, 054568514, Jan. 29, 2007. These decisions and others can be found in <i>ha-Din ve-ha-Dayan</i> 19 and 27. For the most recent decision, see Netanya Rabbinical Court, 272088/6, Jan. 23, 2011. (In a situation in which it is clear that the husband is voluntarily delivering a <i>get</i> to his wife, the presence of a tort claim for <i>get</i> recalcitrance will not impact the validity of the <i>get</i>. See Tel Aviv Rabbinical Court, 364836/1, Nov. 24, 2009). However, given that the award is based on a free-standing obligation to compensate the wife for <i>tza’ar-boshet</i>, these decisors may well concur with R. Daichovsky’s view. On other potential challenges to R. Daichovsky’s position, see Daichov­sky, op. cit., 299–301.</i> Should a husband refuse to submit to the rabbinical court’s authority, a claim for damages due to <i>tza’ar-boshet</i> may be submitted to a civil court, and given the husband’s failure to heed the rabbinical court’s recom­mendation that he divorce his wife, R. Hadaya suggests that monetary penalties may be imposed on the husband for contempt of court.<sup class="footnote-marker">170</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Yaskil Avdi</i> 6:96. In addition to being in contempt of court (<i>mesarev le-din</i>), the husband is also violating proscriptions against deni­grating a Torah scholar and <i>dayan</i>; see <i>SA</i>, YD 243:6–7, 334:47; HM 27:1–2. In fact, in serving as a <i>dayan</i> in the Israeli rabbinical courts, R. Daichov­sky has, pursuant to Jewish and Israeli law, directed the civil authorities to imprison recalcitrant husbands. See S. Daichovsky, “Divorce Enforce­ment” [Hebrew], 25 <i>Tehumin</i> (5765),132, 138–43. The underlying premise of this position is that imprisonment in contemporary prisons does not render a divorce unlawfully coerced; see <i>Amud ha-Yemini</i> 19 (R. Yitzhak Herzog’s opinion); <i>Mishpetei Shaul</i> 36; <i>Teshuvot Yabia Omer</i>, EH 3:20:34.</i>
What is the purpose of providing an award for emotional distress to this present-day <i>agunah</i>? First, it is an opportunity to bring an action that provides public recognition of the intrinsic value of human dignity by formally acknowledging its violation. By invoking exigency Hala­khah, the rabbinical court is attempting to mitigate the emotional suffer­ing, and particularly the humiliation, of a vulnerable victim who is denied conjugal relations and whose husband’s intransigence pre­vents her from in receiving a divorce and remarrying.
The avoidance of shame and psychological torment is a central principle of other realms of Jewish living.<sup class="footnote-marker">171</sup><i class="footnote">See Alfred Cohen, “The Valance of Pain in Jewish Thought and Practice,” 53 <i>Journal of Halacha and Contemporary Society</i> (2007), 25, 30–35, 47–50.</i> For instance, despite the proscription against hurting and endangering oneself, to avoid shame and relieve mental discomfort, certain types of cosmetic and plastic surgery are permissible.<sup class="footnote-marker">172</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Tosefot</i>, <i>Shabbat</i> 50b, s.v. <i>bishvil; Teshuvot Helkat Ya’akov</i> 3:11; <i>Teshuvot Mishnah Halakhot</i> 2:246–247; <i>Teshuvot Minhat Yitzhak</i> 6:105:2.</i> Protec­tion of a woman’s dignity should also be extended to her emotional persona. The awarding of these damages for mental anguish gives symbolic recognition to the significance of a wife’s bodily and emo­tional integrity. Though monetary damages may not be equivalent to the emotional injury experienced, they can serve as a symbolic means of restoring a sense of personal security and autonomy.
Furthermore, a successful suit can publicly reprimand and eco­nomically penalize a recalcitrant husband who unjustifiably withholds conjugal relations (<i>onah</i>) and/or a <i>get</i>, thereby deterring other members of the community from committing the same offenses. Realizing that there may be a suit filed for this action, a husband may think twice before refusing to engage in sexual relations with his wife or withhold­ing a <i>get</i>. Finally, if the suit is successful, the award will compensate the wife to some degree for the emotional loss caused by the said assaults on her psyche.<sup class="footnote-marker">173</sup><i class="footnote">The primary function of tort damages is to compensate the victim for injuries, restoring him to the position he occupied prior to the tortious act; see <i>Hiddushei Rabbi Hayyim ha-Levi</i>, <i>Hilkhot Toein ve-Nitan</i> 5:2; Yisrael Gustmann, <i>Kuntresei Shiurim</i>, <i>Bava Kama</i>, <i>shiur</i> 6. Com­pensation for medical expenses falls under this rubric; see <i>Maggid Mishnah</i>, <i>Hilkhot Hovel u-Mazik</i> 2:16; <i>SA</i>, HM 420:23. In contrast, <i>tza’ar and boshet</i>, which are non-physical injuries, are compen­sated by imposing a monetary penalty; see <i>Mishnah Torah</i>, <i>Hilkhot Hovel u-Mazik</i> 3:3. Clearly, given that there is no possibility of restoring the wife’s emotional persona to its state prior to the hus­band’s denial of conjugal relations, the only compensation can be a mon­etary penalty.</i>
For all these reasons, prior to endeavoring to resolve a divorce situation, the court should inform the parties that adjudicating spousal claims for emotional distress is within its purview.<sup class="footnote-marker">174</sup><i class="footnote">In other words, the claim or the possibility of a future claim will be incorporated into the arbitration agreement accompanied by a provision empowering the rabbinical court to obligate the parties to deal with a husband’s refusal to engage in conjugal relations; see supra at n.156.</i> Indeed, rabbinical courts should insist that resolving end-of-marriage issues includes addressing spousal claims for mental anguish. Should a party refuse to arbitrate such a claim, a court should decline to address the other issues pertaining to termination of the marriage. Failure to insist on an all-or­ nothing approach simply encourages the already pervasive phenom­enon of shopping around for the “right” rabbinical court – namely, one that serves the husband’s needs rather than addressing all the issues in an honest and forthright fashion. Just as the court should insist on hearing all claims associated with the end of marriage, a woman should insist that all of her claims be heard in court.<sup class="footnote-marker">175</sup><i class="footnote">If the decisional standards have failed to establish that there is “an exigency situation” in order for a <i>beit din</i> to hear claims for <i>tza’ar-boshet</i> (see supra, text accompanying nn. 22–59), an arbitration agreement (as well as a prenuptial agreement; see supra n. 156) may be drafted to entail the undertaking of an obligation (<i>hithayvut</i>) by the parties to advance such claims. In effect, the parties obligate themselves to pay tortuous damages such as <i>tza’ar</i> and <i>boshet</i> based upon a <i>hithayvut</i> rather than the laws of damages. Such a stipulation would not necessitate the need to address the systemic issue of meting out damages in contemporary times. See Avraham Sheinfield, <i>Torts</i> [Hebrew] (Jerusalem: 1991), 362–3 (R. Zalman N. Goldberg’s opinion). For the underpinning of such a view in the <i>Shulhan Arukh</i> and <i>Rema</i>’s rulings, see <i>SA</i>, HM 55:1; 175:40; <i>Rema</i>, HM 386:3; 388:2. For additional sources, see Sheinfeld, ibid., 74–75, 246, 377.<br>In principle, such an obligation may be executed via a mutual verbal commitment of the parties; see <i>Teshuvot Divrei Hayyim</i> 1: 42; <i>Teshuvot Maharash Engel</i> 7:168. In practice, to preempt the possibility of a potential future challenge to the authenticity and veracity of this commitment, this mutual obligation should be recorded into writing. The text of such an arbitration agreement or prenuptial agreement would include the following:<br>The parties acknowledge that the <i>beit din</i> is authorized to resolve all disputes related to a spouse’s refusal to engage in conjugal relations, coercion of the other spouse to engage in conjugal relations against her will, and a spouse’s refusal to grant or receive a Jewish divorce from the other spouse according to their discretion, pursuant to the circumstances, and obligate themselves now (<i>meshabed nafshei mei-achshav</i>) to pay tortuous damages such as <i>nezek</i>, <i>tza’ar</i> and <i>boshet</i>, should they be imposed by <i>beit din</i>.”<br>In effect, the parties obligate themselves to pay tortious damages on the strength of this undertaking (<i>hithayvut</i>), rather their covenantal duty to comply with the laws of damages. See <i>Aruch ha-Shulhan</i>, HM 1:13; <i>Teshuvot Imrei Bina</i>, HM 2; <i>Teshuvot Beit Yehuda</i> 1; <i>Mishpatekha le-Ya’akov</i> 2:32:3; R. Zalman Nehemiah Goldberg, <i>Yeshurun</i> 11 (2002), 602–3; and text accompanying n. 3 in Addendum B below.<br>Cf. <i>Sha’ar ha-Mishpat</i> 1:1; <i>Pithei Teshuvah</i> 1:3; <i>Zeit Ra’anan</i> 2:46:1; <i>Mishkenot Ya’akov</i>, HM 1.</i>
On the other hand, a court’s refusal to hear the claim – and if justi­fied, to award damages – effectively bestows on the injurer a kind of “halakhic entitlement” to cause the injury. The perceived weakness of the rabbinical courts can only lead to increased violation of the law, because others are seen to violate the law with impunity. Given the prevalence of the phenomenon of chained wives in contemporary soci­ety, it is not surprising that twentieth century decisors such as <i>Rabbis Herzog</i> and <i>Hadaya</i> stress the obligation to heed the words of Torah scholars (<i>mitzvah li-shmo’a le-divrei hakhamim</i>) regarding compliance with a court’s issuance of a divorce judgment.<sup class="footnote-marker">176</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Heikhal Yitzhak</i>, EH 1:1:5; <i>Teshuvot Yaskil Avdi</i> 6:96.</i>
These words of admonishment should be applicable to our situ­ation as well. Failure to adjudicate a claim of spousally-inflicted dis­tress (assuming the conditions elaborated on above are obtained) may undermine a community’s trust and confidence in rabbinical authority in general and rabbinical courts in particular.
Hopefully, the following incident recorded in the Talmud could not transpire in our day:
<span class="poetry indentAll"><small>R. Rehumi who was [studying at the school] of Rava at Mahoza, used to return home on the eve of every Day of Atonement.<sup class="footnote-marker">177</sup><i class="footnote">According to <i>Ketuvot</i> 62b, those studying Torah may leave their wives for a two- or three-year maximum. While at home, the frequency man­dated for sexual intercourse was once a week. Today, the minimal fre­quency mandated for conjugal relations is twice a week; see <i>Ketuvot</i> 61b; <i>Iggerot Moshe</i>, EH 3:28.</i> On one occasion, he was so engaged in his studies [that he forgot to return home]. His wife was expecting [him any moment, saying,] “He is coming soon, he is coming soon.” As he did not arrive, she became so depressed that tears began to flow from her eyes. [At that moment,] he was sitting on a roof. The roof collapsed under him, and he fell and died. <sup class="footnote-marker">178</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ketuvot</i> 62b.</i></small></span>
As the <i>Shulhan Arukh</i> admonishes, “One must be mindful of [causing] anguish to one’s wife, for her tears are ever-present.”<sup class="footnote-marker">179</sup><i class="footnote"><i>SA</i>, HM 228:3. Though our presentation focuses upon the right of an <i>agunah</i> to advance a claim for emotional stress against her husband who withholds a <i>get</i>, such a right equally exists for a husband who is unable to receive a <i>get</i> from his wife. However, our discussion focuses on the more frequent situation of the abusive husband.<br></i>

Addendum A

<big><strong>The Propriety of Submitting and Awarding a <i>Nezikin</i> Claim in <i>Beit Din</i> on Behalf of an <i>Agunah</i></strong></big>
<b>I.</b>
On August 17, 2011, Judge Esther Stein of the Rishon Letzion Family Court, an Israeli civil court, awarded compensation in the amount of NIS 680,000 (approximately $182,850) to a Jewish woman whose husband refused to give her a <i>get</i> (a Jewish divorce).<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">1This addendum is adapted from my essay, “The Propriety of Awarding a <i>Nezikin</i> Claim by Beth Din on Behalf of an Agunah,” 45 <i>Tradition</i> (Fall 2012).<br>. File No. 9877/02, Rishon Letzion Family Court.</i>
In her lawsuit, filed with a rabbinical court, the wife argued that she was physically abused and abandoned by her spouse. At times, the husband would hit her and slam her head against the wall and furniture. Additionally, he cursed her and their children. Subsequently, he ceased having relations with her and eventually abandoned the marital home.
A 2003 rabbinical court ruling recommended that a <i>get</i> should be forthcoming; subsequently, a 2004 rabbinical court decision stated that the husband was mandated to give his wife a <i>get</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">A full discussion of the ramifications of the distinction between a <i>beit din</i> coercing, obligating or recommending that a husband deliver a <i>get</i> to his wife, including its impact upon a recognition of the legitimacy of a <i>nezikin</i> claim, is beyond the scope of this presentation. For our purposes, in the absence of a <i>beit din</i> actually obligating a husband to give a <i>get</i>, any tort award in favor of the wife rendered by a civil court will result in a coerced <i>get</i> should a Jewish divorce be arranged. See File No. 000766955-21-1, Jerusalem Regional <i>Beit Ein</i>, 25 Iyar 5764; S. Daichovsky, “Monetary Enforcement Steps Against Recalcitrant Husbands” [Hebrew], 26 <i>Tehumin</i> (5766), 173, 176.</i> Despite these two <i>beit din</i> rulings, the husband refused to give his wife a <i>get</i>. Subsequently, the <i>beit din</i> rescinded these directives due to the husband’s insistence that the division of marital assets be addressed by the <i>beit din</i> prior to the deliverance of the <i>get</i> and due to the wife’s unwillingness to drop her civil damage suit, which she had filed in Rishon Letzion Family Court.
The Family Court held that refusal to divorce constitutes a violation of the values protected by the Israeli “Basic Law: Human Dignity and Freedom,” which includes freedom of choice and the right to dignity and self-fulfillment. Invoking this governing Israeli statute as well as other arguments, the Family Court awarded the wife compensation for the husband’s recalcitrance during a period of eight years in failing to give a <i>get</i> to his wife. This decision follows in the footsteps of various earlier decisions handed down by other Israeli Family Courts, which have awarded civil damages in cases of <i>get</i> recalcitrance (in which either a husband refuses to give or a wife refuses to accept a <i>get</i>).<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">File No. 6743-02, Jerusalem Family Court; File No. 19270-03; File No. 19480/05, Kfar Saba Family Court; File No. 30560-07, Rishon Letzion Family Court; File No. 18561-07, Jerusalem Family Court; File No. 24782-98, Tel Aviv Family Court. For an overview of these cases, see Benjamin Shmueli and Ayelet Blecher-Prigat, “The Interplay between Tort Law and Religious Family Law: The Israeli Case,” 26 <i>Arizona Journal of International and Comparative Law,</i> (2009), 270.</i>
In this context, we will not address the halakhic propriety of the wife’s decision to file a civil claim for <i>nezikin</i> (damages) in civil court. Our focus is rather on how the <i>beit din</i> would have received such a claim for <i>get</i> recalcitrance had it been advanced before them. Essentially, the question is whether awarding monetary damages due to <i>get</i> recalcitrance can serve as a vehicle to coerce the husband to deliver a <i>get</i> to his wife, who would waive her right to the award under those circumstances. If such is the case, is the resulting divorce an instance of a <i>get me’useh</i>, a coerced – and thus invalid – <i>get</i>?
According to numerous authorities, if monetary fines are leveled against a recalcitrant husband, any subsequent execution of a <i>get</i> is unlawful.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot ha-Rashba</i> 4:40; <i>Teshuvot Tashbetz</i> 2:68–69; <i>Meiri, Beth ha-Behirah Gittin</i> 8:1; <i>Rema,</i> EH 134:5; <i>Teshuvot Maharik</i>, <i>shoresh</i> 63; <i>Teshuvot Rav Betzalel Ashkenazi</i> 15; <i>Mishkenot Ya’akov</i> 38; <i>PDR</i> 15:145; 16:260.</i> Consequently, the advancement of a monetary claim to compensate for damages due to a husband’s recalcitrance in delivering a <i>get</i> is without halakhic foundation. Should such an award be forthcoming and the husband decides to give the <i>get</i> in exchange for being released from paying this debt, the subsequent execution of the <i>get</i> constitutes a <i>get me’useh</i>.
In a case similar to the one at hand, a Jewish husband was ordered by a French court to pay his wife 25,000 francs to compensate her for his refusal to deliver a <i>get</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote"><i>D. v. France, 35 Eur. Comm. H. R.D.R.</i> 199 (1983).</i> In responding to the merits of this judgment, <i>R. Yitzhak Weiss</i> and <i>R. Shmuel Wosner</i> ruled that such an award is a classic example of financial compulsion (<i>ones mamon</i>), indirectly procuring a <i>get</i> by exerting financial pressures.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Minhat Yitzhak</i> 8:136; <i>Teshuvot Shevet ha-Levi</i>, vol. 5, EH 210.</i>
It is therefore unsurprising that numerous Israeli rabbinical courts require that any monetary damage claim by the wife must be withdrawn from the <i>beit din</i>’s consideration prior to deliberation regarding the propriety of a husband giving a <i>get</i> and arranging its execution. Furthermore, while a <i>beit din</i> is addressing the matter of a <i>get</i>, it will insist that any monetary damage claims being filed and/or advanced in a civil court proceeding be withdrawn.<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">See the series of Israeli <i>batei din</i> decisions found in <i>Hadin ve-ha-Dayan</i> 19 and 27.</i> In addition to the essential problem that awarding compensation may result in the <i>get</i> being tainted by compulsion, if <i>batei din</i> were to render such awards, the mere advancement of such a claim in <i>beit din</i> might motivate the husband to divorce his wife out of fear of possible financial loss.<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Tashbetz</i> 2:69.</i> Moreover, if the <i>beit din</i> were to impart validity to such an award and permit a wife to submit a claim even after the delivery of a <i>get</i>, a husband might feel coerced to give the <i>get</i> out of fear of a possible impending suit, resulting in a <i>get me’useh</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Rav Betzalel Ashkenazi</i>, supra n. 4; <i>Teshuvot Tzemach Tzedek</i> (Lubavitch), EH 212; <i>Teshuvot Avnei Nezer</i>, EH 178:2–3.</i>
<span class="poetry indentAll"><small><b>II.</b></small></span>
Given the foregoing presentation, is there any monetary claim that can be advanced by an <i>agunah</i> within the context of a <i>beit din</i> that will avoid the strictures of a <i>get me’useh</i>?<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">In our discussion in chap. 3, we focused upon a marriage in which the wife desired to remain married and her husband refused to engage in sexual relations, and we examined a wife’s right to advance claims for her inability to have conjugal relations. Here we are dealing with “a dead marriage” where the wife who requires a <i>get</i> wants to remarry and the husband refuses to grant her a <i>get</i>.</i> Although we have seen that some authorities view any monetary duress as grounds for invalidating a subsequent execution of a <i>get</i>, other <i>Poskim</i> distinguish between different types of monetary and non-monetary coercion. Certain forms of duress will taint the subsequent execution of a <i>get</i>, while others are considered free of compulsion.
<i>Rabbeinu Tam</i> enumerates various social measures that may be imposed upon a husband under certain circumstances in order to compel the issuance of a <i>get</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sefer ha-Yashar</i> 24; <i>Sefer Etz Hayyim</i> 2:198; <i>Teshuvot Binyamin Ze’ev</i> 88; <i>Teshuvot Maharik</i>, <i>shoresh</i> 102.</i> Pursuant to one account of these measures<b>, </b><i>Rema</i> rules:
<span class="poetry indentAll"><small>They may mandate upon all individuals who are members of the community of Israel to refrain from granting him any benefit, nor engage in business with him, nor to circumcise his son, nor to bury him, until he gives her a <i>get</i> …<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Rema,</i> EH 154:21.</i></small></span>
As aptly characterized by <i>R. Yitzhak Herzog</i>:
<span class="poetry indentAll"><small>[These measures] are not so severe that he would divorce his wife if he was in love with her, and if he divorces her, he has not acted under duress.<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Heikhal Yitzhak</i>, EH 1:1; <i>Mordekhai</i>, <i>Gittin</i>, 468–469. Cf. R. Yosef ibn Lev, who claims that “withholding benefit” is to be construed as coercion; see <i>Teshuvot Maharival</i> 2:18, 79, 3:120.</i></small></span>
These restrictions are moderate coercive measures that minimally affect the husband’s free will.
Thus, for example, <i>R. Menachem Mendel Schneerson</i> issued the following ruling regarding a wife who stole 515 silver rubles from her husband and returned 400 rubles so that her husband would give her a <i>get</i>:
<span class="poetry indentAll"><small>Not all monetary duress constitutes duress … 115 rubles in comparison to 515 rubles is not a lot of money, and this is not an instance of full-fledged duress (<i>ones gamur</i>). It is not worse than the <i>harhakot</i> of Rabbeinu Tam, which are not viewed as full-fledged duress.<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Tzemach Tzedek</i>, EH 262.</i></small></span>
If a man is prepared to divorce his wife for 115 rubles, a relatively small amount of money, this is indicative that he is ready to divorce her and that he is utilizing the refusal to grant a <i>get</i> as a bargaining chip to extort her for money or out of spite. In such a situation, any ensuing <i>get</i> would not be tainted by compulsion. This approach was similarly adopted by <i>Netivot ha-Mishpat</i>, and <i>R. Feinstein</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Torat Gittin</i> 143:4; <i>Iggerot Moshe,</i> EH 1:134.</i>
In our case, the civil judge of the Rishon Letzion Family Court awarded the wife approximately $182,850. Would such an award, had it been rendered by a <i>beit din</i>, constitute <i>ones gamur</i>, or if it is only a moderate form of coercion to pressure the husband to give a <i>get</i>? In accordance with <i>R. Schneerson</i>’s approach, the answer to this question is contingent upon the financial situation of the husband. In a case in which such an award would wreak financial havoc in his life, the award would be construed as coercion; in a case in which that amount would not be financially threatening, it would be valid. Coercion is measured proportionally by the <i>beit din</i>, factoring into consideration the husband’s ability to pay.<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">For others who subscribe to this approach, see <i>Teshuvot Beit Ephraim</i>, <i>Mahadura Tanina</i>, EH <i>1:</i>70; <i>Mishkenot Ya’akov</i> 38-41; <i>Teshuvot Heikhal Yitzhak,</i> EH158.<br>Subsequently, in contemporary times, R.
