Amar and R. Probar who serve as <i>dayanim</i> on <i>batei din</i> which are under the aegis of Israel’s Chief Rabbinate, and R. Daichovsky, director of this network of <i>batei din</i> and a former <i>dayan</i> of Jerusalem Supreme Rabbinical Court have adopted this approach. See <i>Teshuvot Shema Shlomo</i>, vol. 1, EH 11; S. Daichovsky, “Rabbinical Courts and Civil Courts: Thoughts on Their Overlapping Boundaries” [Hebrew], 4 <i>Me’oznei Mishpat</i> (2005): 261, 295–8; idem., “Proportionality in Coercing a <i>Get</i>” [Hebrew] 27 <i>Tehumin</i> (5767), 300–2; Nahum Probar, “The Obligation to Give a <i>Get</i>, Return of Gifts, A Justifiable Claim – Regarding a Couple who do not Want to Remain Married,” [Hebrew], <i>Kenas ha-Dayanim</i> (5768), 109, 112.</i> Since we are unaware of the financial ability of the husband in the Israeli case, we cannot determine whether this would have been a legitimate award for a <i>beit din</i> that would not subsequently impact upon the propriety of the <i>get</i>.
Furthermore, as we mentioned in chapter 3, numerous authorities, including <i>Rivash</i>, <i>Tashbetz</i>, <i>Ranah</i>, <i>Mabit</i>, <i>Rema</i>, and <i>R. Feinstein</i>, as well as <i>R. Elyashiv</i>, <i>R. Daichovsky</i>, and <i>R. Rafael</i>, while serving on the Israeli Rabbinical Courts have argued that <i>kefiyah le-davar aher</i>, in which the coercive element emerges from circumstances unrelated to the divorce, does not constitute coercion that invalidates a <i>get</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Tashbetz</i> 1:1; <i>Teshuvot Ranah</i> 1: 63; <i>Rema,</i> EH 154:21; <i>PDR,</i> 11:300, 302–307; 16:271, 272–279, 275–276.</i> “<i>Kefiyah ke-din</i>” (legitimate coercion) avoids the problem of a <i>get me’useh</i>.
It is possible to argue that a <i>nezikin</i> claim can be advanced by the woman unrelated to divorce. As the Talmud Bavli states, “<i>tav lemeitav tan du mi-lemeitav armalu</i>” (it is better for two to live together rather to live alone), or as the Talmud Yerushalmi conveys in more strident terms, “she claims to desire marriage” (<i>tova’at le-hinasei</i>) in order to transcend her personal loneliness.<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">Talmud Bavli, <i>Bava Kama</i> 110b; Talmud Yerushalmi, <i>Ta’anit</i> 4:6; <i>Korban ha-Eidah</i>, ad loc. The parameters of this presumption are beyond the scope of this presentation.</i> A woman has a compelling desire to be married rather than experience a life of spinsterhood. Regardless of whether this compelling desire for marital ties relates to the desire to engage in sexual relations or for the sake of companionship and security,<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">J. David Bleich, “<i>Kiddushei Ta’ut</i>: Annulment as a Solution to the <i>Agunah</i> Problem,” <i>Tradition</i> 33 (1998), 102, 121, n. 18. See also <i>Teshuvot Havot Ya’ir</i> 221.</i> an inability to marry causes emotional scarring that engenders feelings of <i>boshet</i> and <i>tza’ar</i> (emotional stress).<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">For the scope of <i>boshet</i> and <i>tza’ar</i> as well as how these injuries are assessed, see our discussion in chap. 3, text accompanying nn. 80–126.</i> The husband may compound the injury by dismissing the harm, arguing that one who suffers emotional pain has only herself to blame, and that such feelings are a transient psychological and/or culturally conditioned behavior pattern rather than an existential fact is open to challenge. Specifically addressing the <i>hazakah</i> (presumption) of “<i>tav lemeitav tan du mi-lemeitav armalu</i>,” <i>R. Joseph B. Soloveitchik</i> contends:
<span class="poetry indentAll"><small>This has absolutely nothing to do with the social and political status of the woman in antiquity. The <i>hazakah</i> is not based upon sociological factors but on a [verse] in <i>Bereishit</i>, “And thy desire shall be to thy husband” … She suffers incomparably more than the male while in solitude … It is not a psychological fact, it is an existential fact …<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">This understanding of this presumption was developed in a talk delivered by R. Soloveitchik to a convention of the Rabbinical Council of America in Nov. 1975, entitled “Surrendering to the Almighty”; see the summary in <i>Light</i> (17 Kislev 5736 [1976]), 11–15, 18.</i></small></span>
Even if one would argue that <i>boshet</i> and <i>tza’ar</i> feelings are conditioned by psychological or cultural phenomena, Halakhah recognizes that the inability of a woman to marry may serve as grounds for a <i>nezikin</i> claim. The same can be said for a woman who wishes to have children and is emotionally distressed because her spouse’s recalcitrance in delivering a <i>get</i> makes this impossible. Halakhah recognizes the right of an injured party to file such claims. Throughout different periods of Jewish history, from medieval times until to the contemporary period, arbiters have meted out compensatory awards based upon <i>boshet</i> and <i>tza’ar</i> claims for defamation of character and broken wedding engagements.<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot ha-Rosh</i>, <i>Kelal</i> 101:1, 8, 9; <i>Teshuvot ha-Rashba ha-Meyuhasot le-Ramban</i> 240; <i>Teshuvot Tashbetz</i> 3:204; <i>Teshuvot ha-Ridbaz</i> 3:480; <i>Shakh,</i> HM 207:24, 333:49; <i>Teshuvot Noda be-Yehuda</i>, <i>Mahadura Tanina</i>, YD 146; <i>Teshuvot Hatam Sofer</i>, EH 134, HM 181; <i>Teshuvot Avodat ha-Gershuni</i> 74; <i>Teshuvot Zera Emet</i>, YD 102; <i>Teshuvot Rav Pe’alim</i>, vol. 2, EH 3; <i>PDR</i> 3:151.</i> In 1965, invoking their power of <i>le-migdar milta</i> (protective measures, also referred to as <i>hora’at sha’ah</i>), the members of the Supreme Rabbinical Court in Jerusalem, <i>Rabbis Abudi</i>, <i>Elyashiv</i>, and <i>Goldschmidt</i>, marshaled numerous <i>teshuvot</i> to support their position that damages for <i>boshet</i> can be awarded in the case of a broken engagement.<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote"><i>PDR</i> 5:322, 327.</i> Accordingly, if we posit that this is a situation of <i>le-migdar milta</i>, a wife desiring to remarry may advance a <i>nezikin</i> claim against her recalcitrant husband.<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">Implicit in one contemporary approach to this question is that <i>“migdar milta”</i> is inapplicable to the <i>agunah</i> situation. See R. Uriel Lavi, “The Arrangement of a <i>Get</i> after a Husband’s Monetary Compensation Award to his Wife” [Hebrew], 26 <i>Tehumin</i> (5766), 160, 164.</i> To state it differently, if an individual is a habitual wrongdoer with regard to a certain practice and many members of the community by engaging in this misconduct have shown themselves to be dissolute, an emergency situation exists if the individual will continue violating the halakhic norm of being compliant regarding the giving of a <i>get</i> unless he is punished.
Alternatively, if an individual prevents another individual from performing a mitzvah, the individual so precluded is entitled to receive monetary damages.<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Ktav Sofer,</i> HM 26; <i>Sha’arei Teshuvah,</i> OH 482:1; A. Sheinfeld, <i>Nezikin</i>, 306–307, n. 112.<br>This damage claim due to the inability to perform a mitzvah has been applied without explanation to the <i>agunah</i> situation by others. See Yosef Sha’rabi and Yuval Sinai, Halakhic Advisory Opinion, Netanya Academic College, June 23, 2011. The grounds for justifying such a claim is presented here.</i> Thus, if the husband prevents his wife from fulfilling the mitzvah of populating the world by having children, she may seek an award for damages.<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">Although women are exempt from the mitzvah of procreation (see <i>Rambam, Hilkhot Ishut</i> 15:2), some <i>Poskim</i> argue that the mitzvah of populating the world is incumbent upon a woman; see <i>Rashi, Yoma</i> 9b; <i>Tosafot</i>, <i>Shabbat</i> 110b; <i>Teshuvot ha-Ran</i> 32; <i>Birkhei Yosef,</i> EH 1:16; <i>Teshuvot Binyan Tzion</i> 23.</i> Even in the absence of a desire to have children, the act of marriage per se entails the performance of a mitzvah;<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Sefer ha-Hinukh</i>, mitzvah 539 (Chavel edition), following in the footsteps of <i>Rambam.</i></i> should her attempts to engage in this mitzvah be thwarted<i>,</i> there are grounds for submitting a claim for damages.
In sum, a wife may submit a <i>nezikin</i> claim for <i>tza’ar</i>- and/or <i>­boshet</i>-related feelings based upon her right to marry due to her wish to engage in sexual relations and experience companionship or due to being unable to remarry and/or engage in the mitzvah of bringing children into this world. Arguably, each of these claims is unrelated to divorce and therefore halakhically legitimate as <i>kefiyah le-devar aher</i>. Although the submission of a damage claim grounded in the right to marriage clearly assumes that a <i>get</i> must be forthcoming prior to remarrying, we may still focus on the merits of the claim without linking it to a divorce procedure.<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">Others agree with this conclusion. For example, one contemporary posek has suggested that a <i>beit din</i> is empowered to impose monetary penalties upon the recalcitrant husband for the embarrassment and psychological pain engendered due to his intransigence in delivering a <i>get</i> to his wife. In other words, the fact that a <i>get</i> was withheld does not invalidate the right to advance various <i>nezikin</i> claims. We only construe it as financial coercion if the giving of the <i>get</i> will exempt him from the financial burden. However, if he has to pay the financial debt even if he delivers a <i>get</i>, in such a situation the financial pressure is not the cause for giving the <i>get</i>. Subsequently, should his wife waive the debt in exchange for delivery of the <i>get</i>, we are dealing with <i>kefiyah le-devar aher</i> and therefore the halakhic issue of <i>get me’useh</i> does not arise. See Daichovsky, supra n. 2, 176–8. See also <i>Tashbetz</i>, supra n. 17; <i>Torat Gittin</i> 134:4. The same rationale applies in our situation.</i> As long as the wife sincerely desires to remarry<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">This cannot be assumed to always be the case. In many instances, upon receiving her <i>get</i>, an ex-wife will not desire to remarry. In fact, her experiences with her husband may cast doubt on her inability to identify “the right man to marry” or may generate negative feelings to the institution of marriage in general. As a result, marriage and having children no longer remain an option for her.</i> and her claim is not simply a means of pressuring the husband to deliver a <i>get</i>, any subsequent execution of a <i>get</i> will be valid, as her claims are halakhically legitimate.
What is the status of a claim that is ostensibly independent of the <i>get</i>, but employed for purposes of compelling a husband to give a <i>get</i>? Is the <i>kavanah</i> (intent) to initiate such a claim in <i>beit din</i> relevant in determining whether a divorce is coerced? How is intent to be ascertained – by noting if anything is mentioned about delivery of a <i>get</i> when the <i>nezikin</i> claim is submitted, by assessing the wife’s behavior, or by inference from the circumstances?
Many contend that the mere mention of the matter of a <i>get</i> indicates that the claim is in actuality submitted to persuade the husband to become divorced.<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Tashbetz</i>, supra n. 17; <i>Teshuvot Maharashdam</i>, EH 63; <i>Teshuvot Pnei Yehoshua</i> 1:26; <i>Mishnah le-Melekh</i>, <i>Hilkhot Gerushin</i> 2:20; <i>Pithei Teshuvah</i>, EH 134:6.</i> The moment the <i>get</i> is mentioned, it is clear that although he is obligated to pay due to his wife’s inability to remarry, the wife is willing to waive the debt in exchange for receiving her <i>get</i>; if she is not granted the <i>get</i>, she will not waive the debt. Since the withholding of benefit is a form of compulsion,<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Mishkenot Ya’akov</i>, EH 41:7 in the name of R. Hananel; <i>Teshuvot ha-Rif</i> 2; <i>Teshuvot ha-Rashba</i> 4:14.</i> this creates a situation of a <i>get me’useh</i>. However, if the matter of the <i>get</i> is not mentioned in the claim, we can assume that the wife’s intent is to have the <i>beit din</i> address the merits of her plea, rather than for it to serve as leverage to procure her <i>get</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Ein Yitzhak,</i> EH 2:33 (1–6); Tzvi Gartner, <i>Kefiyat Get</i>, 379, 386.</i>
Others argue that withholding of a benefit is not an act of volition,<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Rabbeinu Tam</i>, supra n. 11; <i>Teshuvot ha-Rivash</i> 32; <i>Teshuvot Darkhei No’am</i>, EH 53.</i> and therefore the mention of a <i>get</i> in the <i>nezikin</i> claim will not taint any subsequent delivery of a <i>get</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Ranah,</i> supra n. 17; <i>Teshuvot Beit Efraim</i>, EH 1:73.</i> R. Yosef Goldberg, an expert regarding the grounds for <i>kefiyat get</i>, concludes that withholding benefit should not be construed as coercion. Consequently, the mention of the <i>get</i> will not taint any subsequent deliverance of a <i>get</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">Y. Goldberg, “Withholding Benefit from a Man: Does it Result in a <i>Get Me’useh</i>?” [Hebrew], 8 <i>Shurat ha-Din</i> (5763), 409, 433.</i>
Others contend that even a self-standing claim that is not linked to a request for the execution of a divorce is problematic if there is an <i>umdena de-mukhah</i> (a proven presumption) that this claim was put forward primarily to procure a <i>get</i>, in which case any subsequent delivery of a <i>get</i> would be invalid.<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Maharashdam</i>, supra n. 30; <i>Teshuvot Rav Pe’alim</i>, supra n. 22.</i>
Addressing a <i>nezikin</i> claim related to a husband’s conduct during marriage, <i>R. Tzion Algrabli</i>, in a recent decision handed down by the Jerusalem Supreme Rabbinical Court states:
<span class="poetry indentAll"><small>There is no reason for reluctance to submit to the court a claim for monetary damages for <i>boshet</i> and the like due to one spouse’s neglect of the other … even if the claim is procedurally linked to [i.e., submitted along with] a claim for divorce. However, it must be clear that the <i>nezikin</i> claim was not submitted for the purpose of pressuring the husband to grant a <i>get</i> … Only a rabbinical court may determine whether the claim impacts upon the propriety of the <i>get</i>. <sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">File No. 7041-21-1, Supreme Rabbinical Court, March 11, 2008.</i></small></span>
Yet, there are other <i>Poskim</i> who argue that the wife’s intent in advancing such a plea is irrelevant and any ensuing delivery of a <i>get</i> is proper.<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ranah</i>, supra n. 17; <i>Beit Efraim</i>, supra n. 34; <i>Teshuvot Pnei Yehoshua</i> 75; <i>Teshuvot ha-Mabit</i> 1:22. For an understanding of <i>Mabit</i>’s view based upon an examination of his various rulings regarding <i>get me’useh</i>, see our discussion in chap. 3, n. 147.</i> Claims related to the emotional stress engendered by the husband’s refusal to give a divorce or financial coercion and blackmail on the part of the husband in the course of the negotiations, or claims relating to the anguish resulting from years of divorce litigation, cannot serve as grounds for a monetary award, as these injuries are related to the divorce. In contrast, <i>boshet</i> and <i>tza’ar</i> due to the woman’s desire to be married or the husband’s precluding her from performing the mitzvah of getting married and/or having children is an independent claim that ought to be validated. These rights are self-standing, independent and unrelated to her feelings linked to her existing marriage and the need to dissolve the relationship.
The woman’s <i>nezikin</i> claim proceeds either when both spouses voluntarily appear before the <i>beit din</i> or after the <i>beit din</i> issues a <i>hazmanah</i> (a summons) to the husband at the wife’s request. Once both the husband and wife obligate themselves to the <i>beit din</i>’s jurisdiction<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">The parties’ signing of an arbitration agreement (<i>shtar borerut</i>) gives the <i>beit din</i> authority to resolve this matter. See <i>Rema,</i> HM 12:7; <i>Sma,</i> HM 12:18. Assuming this decision complies with the rules of secular arbitration procedure, it would be legally enforceable in a competent civil jurisdiction in the United States; see Uniform Arbitration Act, sec. 1.<br><i>Should the husband fail to agree to submit to a beit din’s jurisdiction, then the wife should optimally receive permission from the beit din to litigate the nezikin claim in civil court or alternatively receive permission from a rabbinic authority who has expertise in Even ha-Ezer and Hoshen Mishpat to proceed to file a claim in secular court. See SA, HM 26:2; Teshuvot Maharil Diskin 13; Teshuvot Shevet ha-Levi 4:183. Cf. Teshuvot Mishnah Halakhot, 4th ed., HM 202. For the receptivity of such a claim in civil court, see our discussion in chapter 3.</i><br><i>Here again, a monetary claim advanced in civil court would equally have to based upon the parameters outlined in our presentation rather than a claim based upon get recalcitrance. Should someone receive permission to commence a civil suit, it is extremely important that the individual consult with a rabbinic authority who has expertise in Even ha-Ezer and Hoshen Mishpat and possesses legal and jurisprudential education in order to receive competent advice in preparing a claim statement and submitting expert testimony in civil court that will avoid the strictures of a get me’useh.</i></i> to address the grounds for the divorce, the wife’s claim for either a non-excessive monetary award for <i>get</i> recalcitrance, mental anguish due to violation of her right to marry and/or have children, or her inability to perform the mitzvah of marriage and/or having children will be heard. Upon deliberation, should the <i>beit din</i> rule in the wife’s favor,<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">Whether a <i>beit din</i>’s willingness to render such an award requires a <i>beit din</i> to coerce, obligate, or only recommend that the parties dissolve their marriage is beyond the scope of this presentation.</i> it will direct the husband to pay damages.<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">We leave as an open question whether a monetary award given based upon a wife’s right to marriage and/or having children should be limited lest an exorbitant amount be viewed as financial coercion resulting in a <i>get me’useh</i>.</i> Should the husband refuse to remit compensation, the award can be enforced in civil court.
On the other hand, should the husband accept the rabbinical court’s decision, he has the option of suggesting to his spouse that in exchange for giving a <i>get</i>, she waive her entitlement to the monetary damages; the resulting <i>get</i> will not be tainted by coercion. Under such circumstances of a private exchange transaction, such financial inducements, whether to appease a recalcitrant husband<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Maharshakh</i> 1:67, 2:129; <i>Teshuvot Hemdat Shlomo,</i> EH 80; <i>Teshuvot Tzemach Tzedek,</i> EH 262–263; <i>Teshuvot Avnei Nezer,</i> EH 167; <i>Hazon Ish,</i> EH 99:2; <i>Iggerot Moshe</i>, EH 3:44.</i> or a recalcitrant wife who refuses to accept a <i>get</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot ha-Rosh</i>, <i>Kelal</i> 35:2; <i>Teshuvot Tashbetz</i> 4:35; <i>Torat Gittin</i> 143:4; <i>Teshuvot Noda be-She’arim</i> 6; <i>Teshuvot Heikhal Yitzhak,</i> EH 1:9; <i>Teshuvot Yabia Omer</i>, vol. 6, EH 10.<br>In a series of rabbinical court decisions, R. Elyashiv endorsed this position in cases in which the rabbinical judgment obligated the husband to give his wife a <i>get</i>. See <i>PDR</i> 7:111; 8:36; 9:65.</i> do not contravene the stricture against a coerced divorce in situations of “a dead marriage” in which the couple wishes to divorce.
Following in the footsteps of <i>Rivash</i>, <i>Mabit</i>, <i>R. Feinstein</i> and others, <i>R. Rafael</i> observes:
<span class="poetry indentAll"><small>One is allowed to release from imprisonment someone who is serving time for contempt of the rabbinical court [failing to produce information requested by the court], and condition his release on his giving his wife a <i>get</i>. For his imprisonment is unrelated to his refusal to grant the <i>get</i>, but is rather punishment for contempt of court, and he is redeeming himself by giving the <i>get</i>. And at the time R. Elyashiv agreed with me.<br>One can promise a prisoner that his term of imprisonment will be reduced by a third in exchange for giving a <i>get</i> … and I hear from R. Elyashiv, long may he live, that it is clear that this is not considered a coerced divorce, and it is like [the case of] a wife who purchases her <i>get</i> for a certain sum of money. Such a <i>get</i> is entirely acceptable (<i>kasher le-mehadrin</i>).<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote"><i>PDR</i> 16:271, 275–276.</i></small></span>
What happens if the exchange is conducted by the husband and wife privately, not in the presence of a <i>beit din</i>? If the wife threatened to file a claim in <i>beit din</i> and subsequently the husband agreed to grant a <i>get</i> in exchange for his wife’s willingness to refrain from submitting such a claim, would the agreement run afoul of the strictures of a <i>get me’useh</i>? There are indeed <i>Poskim</i> who contend that a husband who gives a <i>get</i> due to a threat is deemed to have been forced to grant the <i>get</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot ha-Rashba</i> 2:276; <i>Teshuvot Maharival</i> 2:77; <i>Avnei Nezer</i>, supra n. 9; <i>Teshuvot Shem Aryeh</i> 93–94.</i>
However, these rulings are readily distinguishable from our case. These <i>Poskim</i> are addressing a situation of “a clear and present danger” – that is, instances in which a husband is threatened with harm, imprisonment, or death if he refuses to consent to a divorce. They are thus indeed instances of unlawfully coerced divorce. By contrast, in our scenario, the husband is threatened by a monetary claim too remote to rise to the level of compulsion, especially since a <i>beit din</i> may not agree that they are empowered to award such <i>nezikin</i> damages<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">See our earlier discussion, chap. 3, at n. 59.</i> and the amount of the award is not known in advance even should such a decision be rendered. As <i>Tashbetz</i> asks rhetorically:
<span class="poetry indentAll"><small>If he has a remote fear of financial loss and therefore desires to give a <i>get</i>, ought one label this a coerced <i>get</i>?<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Tashbetz</i>, supra n.8.</i></small></span>
Indeed, the wife may threaten to submit a claim and never follow through with her threat.<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Mordekhai</i>, <i>Gittin</i> 395; <i>Teshuvot Maharik</i>, <i>shoresh</i> 185. Cf. <i>Teshuvot ha-Rashba</i> 1:883; <i>Teshuvot Hadashot ha-Rivash</i> 27 and 32; <i>Teshuvot Rav Betzalel Ashkenazi,</i> supra n.4; <i>Avnei Nezer,</i> supra n.9, who argue that even a mere threat constitutes duress.</i> Consequently, a private exchange should not taint any subsequent delivery of a <i>get</i>.
Obviously, if our <i>batei din</i> begin to award such damages and it indeed becomes “a clear and present danger” akin to the threat of imprisonment, the threat that such a claim will be submitted may render execution of the <i>get</i> coerced. However, even were such an award to become commonplace, the amount of the award would not be known in advance, and hence the threat remains remote.<sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote">Irving Breitowitz, <i>Between Civil and Religious Law: The Plight of the Agunah in American Society</i> (Westport, 1993), 248.</i>
The foregoing presentation affords the basis of allowing an <i>agunah</i> to advance certain tort claims in the <i>beit din</i>, albeit noting in our discussion those who challenge the propriety of such claims. In the event that a <i>posek</i> and/or <i>beit din</i> decides to render such awards, it is clear that in their mind(s) the awarding of such monies will not create a situation of a <i>get me’useh</i>. Consequently, should other <i>poskim</i> reject such an approach, any subsequent <i>get</i> rendered by a beth din after an award is made ought to be recognized by all segments of our community. In other words, though a <i>get me’useh</i> administered by a <i>Jew</i> is <i>pasul</i>, i.e., invalid,<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Gittin</i> 88b</i> nonetheless, <i>be-diavad</i> (ex post facto) the execution of a <i>get</i> under duress is kosher.<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Mishnah Torah</i>, <i>Hilkhot Gerushin</i> 2:7,20; <i>Hiddushei ha-Ran</i>, <i>Bava Batra</i> 48a; <i>Teshuvot Hatam Sofer</i>, EH 2:174; <i>Teshuvot Ma’aseh Hiya</i> (Rofe), 24; <i>Hazon Ish</i> EH 99:1; T. Gartner, <i>Kefiyah be-Get</i>, 26, 139–141, 232–233.</i> Hence, <i>Poskim</i> who reject the right of a recalcitrant spouse to advance a tort claim, <i>be-diavad</i> a subsequent execution of a <i>get</i> ought to be recognized.
Deciding between the competing arguments relating to the propriety of the different types of <i>nezikin</i> claims for an <i>agunah</i> will be the sole prerogative of the posek. The relative strength of each argument applicable to a case will hopefully be tested within the framework and constraints of future <i>piskei din</i> [halakhic decisions].

Addendum B

In our discussion, we laid the foundation for utilizing a <i>dayan</i>’s exigency authority to address <i>nezikin</i> claims. In the absence of a <i>dayan</i>’s employment of “<i>migdar milta</i>” to resolve these matters, there are three additional avenues that may empower a <i>dayan</i> to address such claims:
(1) Generally speaking, there is a requirement to maintain a <i>beit din</i> composed of <i>mumhim</i> (<i>dayanim</i> who have received ordination handed down from Moshe Rabbeinu). Such <i>dayanim</i> are empowered to award <i>kenasot</i> (penalties) including but not limited to <i>nezikin</i>. In contemporary times in the absence of <i>mumhim</i>, the power of <i>beit din</i> stems in part from the parties’ willingness to accept their authority, and the awarding of <i>kenasot</i> is therefore permissible.<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Shita Mekubezet</i>, <i>Bava Kama</i> 89b, s.v. <i>ve-gedolei ha-mehabrim</i>; <i>Ketzot ha-Hoshen</i> 3:1; <i>Tumim</i> 1:1.</i> In effect, through the acceptance of their authority via the execution of a <i>kinyan</i> (a symbolic act of undertaking the duty to abide by the <i>psak</i> of the <i>beit din</i>), the parties agree to obligate themselves to remit such monies should an award be rendered. This obligation is effective even though the parties do not explicitly state in the <i>shtar borerut</i> (arbitration agreement) that the <i>dayanim</i> may issue a decision regarding a <i>nezikin</i> claim(s) that may entail a <i>kenas</i>. Moreover, since the parties empower the <i>beit din</i> to resolve their matters in accordance with <i>peshara</i>, the panel may resolve matters dealing with the <i>halakhot</i> of <i>kenasot</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot ha-Mabit</i> 1:93; <i>SA</i>, HM 12:2; <i>Teshuvot Beit Yehuda</i> (Landau), HM 1, cited authoritatively by <i>Pithei Teshuvah</i>, HM 1:3; <i>Mishpatekha le-Ya’akov</i> 2:32; Ya’akov Ariel, <i>Dinei Borerut</i>, 187; File No. 70029-1, Eretz Hemdah-Gazit Rabbinical Court, Oct. 31, 2011.</i>
(2) Usually the couple signs a <i>shtar borerut</i> (an arbitration agreement accompanied by the execution of a <i>kinyan</i>) that authorizes the <i>beit din</i> halakhically and legally to issue a ruling. The <i>shtar</i> should specifically authorize the panel to resolve claims relating to the five types of personal injury as well as <i>grama</i>, as we have described. Although contemporary arbiters are generally precluded from addressing these <i>nezikin</i> matters, should the parties obligate themselves to accept a <i>beit din</i>’s decision regarding these claims, such a <i>shtar</i> is valid. In other words, the authority to issue a judgment is grounded in the parties’ willingness to fulfill their respective obligations – as in the case of any contractual agreement – rather than based upon the judicial capacity of a <i>dayan</i> to render a <i>psak</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">Z. Goldberg, 11<i>Yeshurun</i> (2002), 602–3. This conclusion was endorsed earlier by a follower of the <i>Hatam Sofer</i>. See <i>Teshuvot Imrei Binah</i>, HM 2.</i> Alternatively, such acceptance is no different than the imparting of validity to the litigants’ decision to accept a halakhically invalid individual (such as a relative) as an arbiter.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Arukh ha-Shulhan</i>, HM 1:13; <i>Beit Yehuda</i>, supra n. 52. For recent applications of this view, see File No. 9326351, Jerusalem Regional Rabbinical Court, Aug. 11, 2007 (R. Eliazrov’s opinion); File No. 9326351 (Appeal Decision), Supreme Rabbinical Court, Aug. 25, 2008 (R. Sherman’s opinion).<br>Cf. <i>Sha’ar ha-Mishpat</i>, HM 1:1, 2:1, who casts doubt upon the validity of such a <i>shtar</i> with regard to punitive damages. However, regarding non-punitive damages such as <i>nezek</i>, <i>rippui</i> and <i>shevet</i>, <i>Sha’ar ha-Mishpat</i> will validate such a provision in a <i>shtar</i>.</i>
(3) Even in the absence of a provision in the <i>shtar</i> which allows a panel to render a decision in matters relating to personal injury or in the absence of conditions that would render the situation as “<i>le-migdar milta</i>,” in accordance with a <i>takanah</i> (legislation) enacted by the <i>Geonim</i> and “the minhag of <i>batei din</i>” (the practice of rabbinical courts), a panel is empowered to employ its discretion in determining the amount of the monetary award, and they will inform the batterer that this amount will appease the victim. This amount will be final even if the figure arrived at by the panel is unacceptable to the victim. As elucidated by the <i>Poskim</i>, the grounds for this award are to deter others in the community from emulating the batterer’s conduct.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Otzar ha-Geonim</i> (ed. B. Lewin), <i>Ketuvot</i>, <i>Teshuvot</i>, 477; <i>Teshuvot ha-Geonim</i>, <i>Shaarei Tzedek</i>, vol. 4, <i>sha’ar</i> 1, 19 in the name of R. Sherira Gaon; <i>Piskei ha-Rosh</i>, <i>Bava Kama</i> 8:2–3; <i>Tur</i>, HM 1:11; <i>SA</i>, HM 1:5, 420:38, <i>Rema</i>, HM 1:2; <i>Teshuvot ha-Rema</i> (A. Siev ed.) 88 (379–80); <i>Teshuvot ha-Mabit</i> 1:93; <i>Teshuvot ha-Ridbaz</i> 4:1291; <i>PDR</i> 5:322; <i>Piskei Din Yerushalayim</i>, <i>Dinei Mamonot ve</i>-<i>Berurei Yahadut</i>, vol. 3, 205; Supreme Rabbinical Court, supra n. 55; Eretz Hemdah-Gazit, supra n. 53.</i>

Part II; Rabbinic Authority; The Reality

Chapter 4; Decisions in Even haEzer

a) Division of Marital Assets upon Divorce

<b><i>A. Division of Marital Assets upon Divorce</i></b><br><b><i>Rachel Levy v. Reuben Levy</i></b>
On June 1, 2010, the above parties signed an arbitration agreement empowering this panel to resolve this matter according to applicable portions of the <i>Shulhan Arukh</i> and <i>Poskim</i>. A divorcing couple, Reuben Levy and Rachel Levy, residents of Los Angeles, California, submitted to this <i>beit din</i> their differences of opinion and disputes in reference to a family business which was unincorporated. Having given said matters due consideration and having heard all parties testify as to the facts of said disputes, this panel does decide and agree as follows:
<b><i>Tova’at</i>’s Claims</b>
The <i>Tova’at</i> admits that she did not share equal decision-making powers in the day-to-day operations of the business, and on various occasions expressed opposition to the management policies of the company. In cases of disagreement between them, Rachel was compelled to accede to her husband’s decision. Moreover, there were periods when she was dedicated primarily to serving as a homemaker and raising their children and was completely uninvolved in the business.
Nevertheless, Rachel argues that she invested $50,000 into the business venture from monies received from a family inheritance and is entitled to a fifty percent share of the value of the family business partnership. Furthermore, given that her husband managed the business, the accrued debts of the business should be her husband’s responsibility. Moreover, although Rachel signed off on various commercial transactions, including but not limited to loan commitments, given her non-involvement in the day-to-day decisionmaking of the business, she should be exempt from any responsibility to pay-off these loans.
<b><i>Nitva</i>’s Counterclaims</b>
Reuben admits that his wife was the bookkeeper, as well as the individual who dealt with the suppliers while he engaged in the trade. In the minds of both parties, this was a family business.
Given that the majority of the required capital to start the business which totaled $300,000 was contributed from Reuben’s family inheritance, the value of the business should be divided up proportionate to the couple’s respective investment. Finally, given his wife’s co-signature on the various loan agreements, the <i>Nitva</i> claims that responsibility of the debts incurred by the business should be shared by both parties equally.
<b>Discussion</b>
The <i>beit din</i> must address two questions. First, is Rachel entitled to an equal share in the value of the business? Second, is she responsible to pay a portion of the debt incurred by the enterprise?<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">The implicit assumption here is that we are dealing with a commercial enterprise, such as a general partnership, in which there exists personal responsibility for debts.</i>
<b>1. Two Types of <i>Isha Noseit ve-Notenet Betokh ha-Bayit</i></b>
We will begin by addressing the issue of the wife’s responsibility for debt incurred in the operation of the family business in the context of the notion of “<i>isha noseit ve-notenet betokh ha-bayit</i>,” “a wife engaging in business in the home.” The Mishnah in <i>Shavuot</i> states:
<span class="poetry indentAll"><small>And these take an oath, although there is no definite claim: partners, tenants, administrators, the wife who manages the affairs of the home …<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Shavuot</i> 7:8.</i></small></span>
Even though the claimant does not advance a definite claim of fraud against them, but only suspects this group of individuals, the Mishnah states that they must take an oath to refute the charge. Some authorities explain the woman described in this Mishnah is engaged in a business,<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Tiferet Yisrael</i>, ibid.; <i>Rashi, Shavuot</i> 48b<i>,</i> s.v. <i>ve-ha-isha ha-noseit</i>. In other words, the woman is running a family business rather than merely having “the run of the household.” See <i>Shavuot</i> 7:8; <i>Shavuot</i> 45a.</i> and in her commercial dealings she is acting as her husband’s agent.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sefer Ha-Raban</i> 115; <i>Maharshal</i>, <i>Yam Shel Shlomo, Bava Kama</i> 8:29; <i>Teshuvot ha-Mabit</i> 190; <i>Shakh,</i> HM 96:9; <i>Teshuvot Shoel u-Meshiv</i> (3rd ed.) 2:18, <i>Arukh ha-Shulhan</i>, HM 96:8.</i>
Under such an arrangement, a merchant would extend credit and sell to a married woman, relying on the knowledge that should she refuse to pay, Halakhah would charge these purchases to the husband. Under such circumstances, the halakhic discussion revolves upon the extent that the husband is liable for his wife’s commercial dealings, included but not limited to loan transactions.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Rema,</i> EH 86:2 and 96:6, and <i>Teshuvot Maharik, shoresh</i> 189, who reject a husband’s liability for his wife’s debts which were incurred due to her active involvement in commercial trade. Cf. <i>Shakh</i>, HM 62:8.</i> Adopting such an approach that “the wife who manages the household” is the husband’s agent may lead one to the conclusion that she is authorized only in executing small businesss deals and/or should her husband protest that he had never authorized her to act in his name, then she is not deemed an agent.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot ha-Rosh</i> 13:1; <i>Teshuvot Maharik</i>, <i>shoresh</i> 189; <i>Maharshal</i>, supra n. 4.</i>
However, there may be situations where a wife may singlehandedly operate her husband’s affairs. <i>R. Ovadiah Hadaya</i>, a twentieth century Israeli <i>dayan</i>, addresses a situation in which a woman supported a Torah scholar with large sums of money and the scholar subsequently discovered that the disbursement of these funds was done without the permission of the woman’s husband. Here, we are dealing with a different type of “<i>isha noseit ve-notenet betokh ha-bayit</i>.” Describing the wife as an owner rather than merely managing the husband’s property, <i>R. Hadaya</i> writes:<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Yaskil Avdi</i>, vol. 5, YD 36.</i>
<span class="poetry indentAll"><small>It is clear from his actions that he allows her to be involved in trade and use the assets just as a person would use his own property. He brought the items to the store while she was the manager whom all the customers viewed as the owner. Therefore, his actions demonstrate that she was serving not merely in the capacity as a manager, but as a <i>ba’al ha-bayit</i> [owner], and since without her he could do anything in the businesss, she is permitted to use his property … He cannot object to what she has already done …</small></span>
In our scenario, the <i>Tova’at</i> was viewed by her customers as an owner in the business. Nevertheless, her absence from the business for an extended period of time, as well as her noninvolvement in the major decision-making of the business, approximates the first type of “<i>isha noseit ve-notenet betokh ha-bayit</i>,” who serves as her husband’s agent in managing his business. Adopting this model of “a wife managing a business” would exempt the <i>Tova’at</i> from repaying the outstanding commercial debt<i>.</i> If we indeed adopt this perspective, the husband is solely responsible for debt repayment, even if the wife co-signed the loan.<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Shakh</i>, supra n. 4. In case of a husband who is incapable of satisfying the debt, should a wife possess assets, the creditor may recover from her; see <i>Yam Shel Shlomo</i>, <i>Sma</i>, and <i>Shakh</i>, supra n. 4. Some argue that a creditor’s ability to proceed against the wife is contingent upon the fact that she actually signed the loan agreement; see <i>Netivot ha-Mishpat</i>, HM 77:11; <i>Beit Meir</i>, EH 102:2.</i>
<b>2. Marriage as an Economic Partnership</b>
Another approach to this question focuses on the nature of the marriage relationship. <i>R. Abraham Sharezee</i>, a contemporary <i>dayan</i> serving on the Rabbinical Court Sha’arei Mishpat in Netanya, Israel, observes:
<span class="poetry indentAll"><small>Even though the act of establishing marriage has not changed, and it will never change … nonetheless, in monetary matters there are changes and continue to be major changes, due to a change in mentality and social mores … The unifying force of all these changes is the improvement of the position of women and the transformation of her being a wife to being a full partner, or almost a complete one … in sustaining the home, and the husband being transformed into a partner whose rights and monetary obligations have undergone a change.<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Mehalhei Mishpat</i> (Netanya), p. 95.</i></small></span>
Certain elements of the marriage agreement have not changed, despite changes in society. Thus, the creation of <i>kiddushin</i> remains a consensual agreement that designates a particular woman for a particular man and prohibits her to all others.<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>SA</i>, EH 26, 37, 38, 39, 43 and 44; <i>Kiddushin</i> 2b.</i> Subsequently, the act of <i>nissu’in</i> instates the husband’s monetary obligations, such as spousal support.<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">Should the couple agree to waive this duty, however, the agreement would be valid, provided that it is formalized in accordance with Halakhah.</i> At the same time, financial elements of the marriage relationship have been effected by societal change. Thus, although many of a wife’s assets are halakhically owned by her husband,<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">See infra text accompanying n. 40.</i> the Halakhah allows spouses to determine their own business relationship provided that any agreements between the couple complies with the proper form (for example, entailing a <i>kinyan</i>) and does not violate any prohibitions, such as theft or the interdict against taking interest.
Consequently, if spouses decide to create a business based upon shared ownership of assets, that is their prerogative. Such an agreement is valid and binding not by the virtue of the act of marriage, but rather because the rule in all financial matters is that any individual “may stipulate contrary to what is written in the Torah.”<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Kiddushin</i> 19b; <i>Beit Yosef</i>, HM 305:4; <i>Rema</i>, HM 344:1.</i>
Even in the absence of such an arrangement, <i>R. Sharezee</i> argues that <i>nissu’in</i> is an economic partnership and the following general ruling of the <i>Shulhan Arukh</i> therefore applies to it:
<span class="poetry indentAll"><small>The partners who deposited in a common purse, one placed a manna, the other two, and the other three, and together they engaged in a business and they lost or profited … their profits or losses are shared equally according to their investment share, rather than according to the number of investors.<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote"><i>SA</i>, HM 176:5; see also HM 176:3. Clearly, a family business cannot be equated in every sense to a commercial partnership. Although some decisors argue that disputes between partners may be justification for partnership dissolution, only a mutual agreement between the spouses during marriage, a mutual agreement of the parties to divorce, or a <i>beit din</i> directive to the spouses to execute divorce would lead to dissolution of a family business.</i></small></span>
The position that a marriage may be viewed as a commercial partnership <i>under certain circumstances</i> has been subscribed to by <i>R. Isaac Herzog</i>, <i>R. Yitzhak Graubard</i>, <i>R. Yoezer Ariel</i>, <i>R. Uriel Lavi</i>, <i>R. Daichovsky</i>, and others.<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Maharashdam</i>, HM 206; <i>Pesakim u-Ketavim</i>, vol. 9, HM 33; <i>Teshuvot Havalim ba-Nei’imim</i>, vol. 5, EH 34; <i>Teshuvot Ateret Shlomo</i> 1:48; Shlomo Daichovsky, “Liquidating the Partnership and Dividing the Assets of the Spouses” [Hebrew], <i>Shenaton ha-Mishpat ha-Ivri</i> 16–17 (5750–5751), 501, 508; idem., “The <i>Halakhot</i> of Marital Partnership: The Law of the Kingdom?” [Hebrew], <i>Tehumin</i> 18 (5758),18; <i>PDR</i> 11:116; File No. 9061-21-1, Netanya Regional Rabbinical Court, Ploni v. Plonit, June 26, 2006; File No. 14850-1, Ashdod Regional Rabbinical Court, Plonit v. Ploni, Sept. 19, 2010; File No. 347562-1, Tel Aviv-Yaffo Regional Rabbinical Court, Ploni v. Plonit, Sept. 13, 2011.<br>Some authorities argue that the spouses must sign an agreement that explicitly states that their marriage is based upon an economic partnership. See <i>Teshuvot Maharival</i> 2:23; Ya’akov Ariel, “The Modern Family Unit – Halakhic Implications” [Hebrew], 22 <i>Tehumin</i> (5762), 129, 145. In fact, sharing equally in profits is predicated upon the existence of a family business partnership; see <i>Rema</i>, HM 287:1. In other words, it is the explicit agreement of the spouses to engage in a business partnership, rather than the act of marriage per se, which creates the partnership. Addressing our halakhic matter of allowing a couple to create a partnership based upon a shared ownership of assets, Ben Tzion Schereschewsky, former Israeli Supreme Court Justice states, “The agreement is validly created and binding not by virtue of the act of matrimony, but rather because we are dealing with financial matters, and the principle in financial matters is that a stipulation is valid.” See Civil Appeal 514/76, <i>Demari v. Demari</i>, Israel Supreme Court Decisions 31(2), 505, 514.</i>
Based on such an understanding, should spouses create a family business, we neither seek to identify who infused the most capital into the venture nor who invested more time and effort in developing its growth. Upon dissolution of the family venture, whether during the time of the marriage or upon divorce, any profits and losses will be shared by the spouses in proportion to their respective investment in the venture and the net value of the enterprise will be distributed equally among the parties, unless there is a marital agreement which provides otherwise.<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">Generally speaking, the recovery of profits is to be subsumed under the category of <i>“davar she-lo ba le’olam</i>” (something which is not yet in existence) and therefore one cannot transfer such assets to a partner. Nevertheless, given that there are <i>Poskim</i> who will validate a verbal agreement between partners without undertaking a <i>kinyan</i> or recognize that the formation of a partnership is a <i>“kinyan situmta”</i> (a commercial practice) and commercial practice allows for the recovery of profits, therefore such recovery is valid. See <i>Shakh</i>, HM 176:6; <i>Bi’ur ha-Gra</i>, ad. locum.; <i>Teshuvot ha-Ridbaz</i> 1:380; <i>Teshuvot Sho’el u-Meshiv</i>, vol. 1 (2nd ed.), 2, 39; <i>Teshuvot Hatam Sofer</i>, HM 20, 66, 96, 99; <i>Teshuvot Bris Ya’akov</i> HM 99.<br>Lest one argue that the transfer of profits is “<i>davar shein-bo mamash</i> (something intangible) and therefore ownership cannot be halakhically-legally effective, nevertheless, there are <i>Poskim</i> who argue that if the <i>minhag</i> or secular law <i>(dina de-malkhuta dina</i>) allows such a transfer, the acquisition is valid. See <i>Teshuvot Maharshal</i> 36; <i>Teshuvot Teshurat Shai</i>, 3rd ed., 91, 141; <i>Hazon Ish, Bava Kama</i> 23:2; <i>PDR</i> 2:376, 382; 5:310, 316; 8:47, 52.</i>
<b>3. The <i>Tena’im</i> Contract</b>
There are additional grounds as well to argue that this family enterprise rests upon the foundation of halakhic partnership law. In some communities, it is customary to sign a <i>tena’im</i> document upon the occasion of an engagement (<i>shiddukhin</i>), which enumerates various parental obligations and the anticipated month of the wedding (or the actual wedding date). It stipulates that the parties have fulfilled all of their mutual obligations and should refrain from any fraudulent conduct. This “contract” concludes that “they will control their assets equally” and a monetary penalty will be paid should a party to the agreement default.<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">For differing formulations of this agreement, see <i>Nahalat Shiva</i> 7–8; <i>Tikkun Soferim</i> 11; <i>Zera Avraham</i> 11. For guidelines in drafting this document, see <i>Sma</i>, HM 243:12, 245:2; <i>Teshuvot Hatam Sofer</i>, EH 112 and 135; <i>Teshuvot Beit Yitzchak</i> 1:111; <i>Teshuvot Zera Emet</i>, YD 236.<br>For the halakhic consequences of reneging on such an agreement, see my “Breach of Promise to Marry,” 17 <i>The Jewish Law Annual</i> (2007): 267 and this writer’s “The Theory of Efficient Breach: A Jewish Law Perspective,” in Aaron Levine (ed.), <i>The Oxford Handbook on Judaism and Economics</i> (NY: 2010), 340.</i> This clause has been interpreted to mean that all assets acquired during marriage, as well as all debts incurred during marriage, are to be shared jointly between the spouses.<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">Daichovsky, supra n. 15.</i>
Assuming the spouses originally signed a <i>tena’im</i> document, the agreement is binding.<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Noda be-Yehuda</i>, <i>Mahadura Kama</i>, YD 65; <i>Shulhan ha-Ezer</i> 1:51:4.</i> In fact, based upon such a contract, both <i>Mahari Weil</i> and <i>Maharik</i> ruled against a husband who fraudulently transferred his wife’s assets to a third party during the marriage.<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Mahari Weil</i> 134; <i>Teshuvot Maharik</i>, <i>shoresh</i> 57.</i> Similarly, <i>Maharam Mintz</i>, <i>Noda be-Yehuda</i>, <i>Maharsham</i>, and <i>Rav Pe’alim</i> rule that in certain instances, a wife’s use of her husband’s assets for giving <i>tzedakah</i> may be construed as a misappropriation of another’s property and violation of the <i>tena’im</i>, which stipulates that such behavior constitutes a fraudulent use of her husband’s assets.<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Maharam Mintz</i> 7:3; <i>Teshuvot Noda be-Yehuda</i>, <i>Tanina</i>, YD 158; <i>Teshuvot Maharsham</i> 1:45; <i>Teshuvot Rav Pe’alim</i>, vol. 2, EH 32.</i>
Given that these sources invoke the <i>tena’im</i> regarding the utilization of a husband’s assets for certain domestic and social needs, some have argued that one cannot expand the scope of the application to recognition of a full-fledged partnership of assets between spouses.<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">A. Sherman and S. Daichovsky, “The Law of Marital Partnership – Non-Recognition in Jewish Law” [Hebrew], 19 <i>Tehumin</i> (5759), 205, 209.</i> Others, however, maintain that the <i>tena’im</i> indeed creates an economic partnership. For example, <i>R. Avraham Menachem Steinberg</i>, a twentieth century <i>posek</i>, rules that pursuant to the above provision in the <i>tena’im</i>, if a wife receives a business from her husband and it is registered in her name, upon dissolution, she must give half of its value to her husband, while she may retain the balance for herself or give it to a third party.<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Mahazeh Avraham</i>, vol. 2, HM 19. See also, Daichovsky, supra n.15. Subsequently, R. Daichovsky changed his mind and agreed with R. Sherman; see Daichovsky, supra n. 22, 216.</i>
Because the <i>tena’im</i> document is a bona fide halakhic-legal contract, should one of the spouses claim that he did not understand what he signed, he would remain liable for any of his infractions of the <i>tena’im</i>’s terms.<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote"><i>SA</i>, HM 45:3; <i>Teshuvot Rav Betzalel Ashkenazi</i> 24; <i>Teshuvot Mishpat Tzedek</i> 2:17:2; <i>Teshuvot Rash ha-Levi</i>, EH 14.</i> In modern times, only two witnesses sign the actual <i>tena’im</i>, while the content of the document is conveyed orally to the prospective spouses.<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Noda be-Yehuda</i>, <i>Mahadura Kama</i>, YD 66; <i>Keter Shem Tov</i>, p. 596.</i> If this information was properly transmitted or the parties were familiar with the contents of the <i>tena’im</i>, the document is considered a valid contract and, in that sense, is no different from a <i>ketubah</i>. On the other hand, a claim of ignorance by one of the parties regarding the import of the <i>tena’im</i> would be a defense in such a case,<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Rema</i>, EH 66:13; <i>Teshuvot Rav Betzalel Ashkenazi</i>, supra n. 23; <i>Teshuvot Havalim ba-Nei’imim</i>, supra n. 15.</i> and under such circumstances the provisions of the <i>tena’im</i> would fail to serve as an avenue for the recognition of the concept of matrimonial partnership.
In our case, prior to the signing of the <i>tena’im</i> by two witnesses, the prospective couple was duly informed regarding the import of the document. As such, the <i>tena’im</i> may serve as one of the potential grounds for dividing the marital assets equally between the spouses.
It is important to note, however, that some authorities reject the legal significance of the <i>tena’im</i>, given that the prospective couple does not sign it themselves and it is not drafted as a bona fide <i>shtar</i> mutually obligating the couple to recognize marriage as an economic partnership. These decisors argue that the phrase, “They will share their assets equally,” is simply a “<i>shufra de-shtarah</i>,” an embellished clause added for stylistic purposes that has no halakhic import.<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Tuv Ta’am ve-Da’at</i> (3rd ed.) 181; <i>Teshuvot Beit Yitzchak</i>, EH 1:110.</i> However, in reply to this position, relying upon <i>Rosh</i>’s opinion, <i>R. H. Shlomo Shaanan</i>, a <i>dayan</i> who serves on a Tel Aviv <i>beit din</i> under the aegis of the Chief Rabbinate contends that the <i>tena’im</i> agreement is to be construed as an explicit condition prior to the onset of marriage and as such this agreement <i>trumps</i> the <i>ketubah</i> which becomes a “<i>shufra de-shtarah.</i>”<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot ha-Rosh 33:2</i>, <i>Iyunim be-Mishpat</i> 31.</i>
<b>4. <i>Umdana</i> and <i>Minhag Ha-Medinah</i></b>
Given the problematic nature of the effectiveness of <i>tena’im</i>, are there any other grounds for arguing that marriage can be predicated upon a model of partnership?
Halakhic decisors have debated the extent of the applicability of the principle of “<i>dina de-malkhuta dina</i>,” “the law of the kingdom is the law.” Does this mean that civil law must be adhered to even if the Halakhah makes no such demand, or does it imply that civil law may override Halakhah when the two differ? In the opinion of <i>Rema</i>, civil law may govern all legal matters that are “<i>le-takanat bnei ha-medinah</i>,” for the benefit of the citizenry, understood by <i>Rema</i> as referring to any issues relating to social interaction.<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Darkhei Moshe</i>, HM 369; <i>Rema</i>, HM 73:14, 369:11.</i> Vigorously disagreeing with <i>Rema</i>, <i>Shakh</i> argues, and <i>Hazon Ish</i> concurs, that if the particular secular law contradicts Halakhah, <i>dina de-malkhuta dina</i> does not apply<i>.</i><sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Shakh</i>, HM 73:39; <i>Hazon Ish</i>, <i>Sanhedrin</i>, <i>Likkutim</i> 16:1. Interestingly, although normative Halakhah follows <i>Shakh</i>’s view, some <i>Rishonim</i> and the majority of <i>Aharonim</i> adopt Rema’s position. See <i>Aliyot de-Rabbeinu Yonah</i>, <i>Bava Batra</i> 55a; <i>Teshuvot ha-Rashba ha-Meyuhasot le-Ramban</i>, 22; <i>Levush Ir Shushan</i>, HM 369:11; <i>Teshuvot Divrei Emet</i> 12; <i>Teshuvot Dovev Mesharim</i> 1:76.</i> Nonetheless<i>, Hazon Ish</i> states:
<span class="poetry indentAll"><small>The law of the kingdom [civil government] determinates the expectation of people. Since we customarily abide by the law of the kingdom under certain prescribed conditions, the law influences people, who then decide to rely on civil law … Therefore, when we [the <i>beit din</i>] apply secular law, we are in actuality following our Halakhah rather than their laws.<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Hazon Ish</i>, supra n. 30. Interestingly, Hazon Ish finds precedent for his position in the Ra’avad’s ruling cited by <i>Shakh</i>, HM 73:36. However, a review of that ruling indicates that the invoking of the <i>umdana</i> is limited to instances in which the Halakhah fails to address a situation and the resolution of the situation can be realized by invoking an <i>umdana</i>, while Hazon Ish argues that it is inconceivable that there is any gap in Halakhah. Halakhah as a religious-legal system has the capacity to address all situations based on its own sources and rules of interpretation. As such, by citing Ra’avad’s position, Hazon Ish extends Ra’avad’s affirmation of the workings of an <i>umdana</i> even if the Halakhah provides a contrary solution! Others limit the Ra’avad’s ruling to situations in which the Halakhah regarding a particular matter is subject to diverse opinions; see <i>Teshuvot Maharsham</i> 3:128.</i></small></span>
For example, Israeli civil legislation dictates terms of tenancy (the Law for Tenant Protection). <i>R. Shlomo Karelitz</i> argues in the name of his uncle, <i>Hazon Ish</i>, that absent any agreement to the contrary between a landlord and tenant, there is implicit expectation, an <i>umdana</i>, that the terms of tenancy will be based on civil law.<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Ateret Shlomo</i>, vol. 1, p. 360.</i> However, in the absence of a clear indication that the parties wish to adopt civil law, the assumption is that Halakhah is the governing system.<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Hazon Ish</i>, HM, <i>Likkutim</i> 16:1, 5, 9. The question of how non-observant divorcing couples should be treated is beyond the scope of our presentation.</i>
In effect, the implementation of Halakhah may be predicated upon the expectations of people to follow halakhic tenancy law. The fact that a secular law exists regarding the matter may cause a change in the expectation; although it was originally clear that halakhic tenancy law would be adhered to, there is now a desire to follow secular law. Consequently, the Halakhah changes due to the change in the <i>umdana</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">See Yoezer Ariel, <i>Dinnei Boreruth</i>, 68. For an extensive list of contemporary <i>Poskim</i> who subscribe to this view, see Dov Frimer, “The Influence of Israeli Law upon Jewish Law” [Hebrew], 39 <i>Jewish Studies</i> (1999), 133. R. Sherman challenges this conclusion, arguing that an <i>umdana</i> rooted in non-Jewish practices is ineffective; see Sherman, supra n. 22, 210–1. Cf. my “Varying Approaches Towards the Division of Matrimonial Property upon Divorce” [Hebrew], 71–72 <i>ha-Darom</i> (5761), 129, n. 46, which provides a list of <i>teshuvot</i> that affirm reliance upon non-Jewish practices in monetary matters. Despite his view of the validity of <i>umdana</i> in this context, R. Sherman legitimates adjudication in <i>beit din</i> according to secular law if the spouses agree that their monetary affairs will be resolved in this manner; see infra n. 21. Other Israeli <i>dayanim</i> view the civil legislation dealing with monetary relations between spouses as “<i>minhag ha-medinah</i>” (national custom), and a resident of Israel is obligated to comply with such legislation. See Netanya Regional Rabbinical Court, File no. 764411/1, <i>Ploni</i> v. <i>Plonit</i>, Oct. 3, 2010.</i> Invoking this <i>umdana</i> regarding tenancy law has been endorsed by other authorities as well.<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Minhat Yitzhak</i> 2:86; <i>PDR</i> 16:312. Others focus upon the assumption that the parties wished to follow common practice, which is to adopt secular law, rather than invoking the notion of <i>umdana</i>. See <i>Teshuvot Maharash</i> 6:19; <i>Iggerot Moshe</i>, HM 1:72. Cf. R. Moshe Sofer and R. Z. Goldberg reject imparting validity of secular law based upon <i>minhag</i>; see <i>Teshuvot Hatam Sofer</i>, YD 314; <i>PDR</i> 14:334, 346. See also R. Kleinman, “The Civil Law in the State – <i>Minhag ha-Medinah</i>” [Hebrew], 32 <i>Tehumin</i> (5772), 261, 268–71.<br>If the prevailing custom is that spousal property assets are to be divided equitably based on civil law, need the parties be aware what the content of the secular law states regarding this matter? This question is subject to debate. See <i>Teshuvot ha-Rosh</i> 68:12; <i>Tur</i> and <i>SA</i>, HM 61:5; <i>Shakh</i>, HM 42:36, 61:9, 71:33; <i>Bi’ur ha-Gra</i>, ad loc. 29, EH 66:48; <i>Teshuvot Re’im</i> 16; <i>Pithei Hoshen</i>, <i>Hilkhot Sekhirut</i>, p. 149. If the parties must know of the usage, if one party claims that he was unfamiliar with the expectation of following secular law regarding dividing up marital assets, then it becomes the other party’s responsibility to demonstrate that such an expectation in fact exists. See <i>Shakh</i>, HM 42:36, 61:5, in the name of <i>Rashba</i>; <i>Erekh Shai</i>, EH 50:7; Michael Wygoda, <i>Agency Law: Section 5</i>, Israel Ministry of Justice [Hebrew], n. 390.<br>If everyone follows the <i>nohag</i>, claiming that one does not subscribe to it, will be no defense. However, in monetary matters, should there be a minority who fails to follow the <i>nohag</i>, such a claim will be accepted; see <i>Teshuvot Nahal Yitzhak</i>, vol. 1, HM 61.</i>
The underlying rationale for legitimizing this approach is that the parties essentially agreed to resolve their issues according to their expectations, which in our situation means following secular law.<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">See supra nn. 30–32.</i> In the words of <i>R. Feinstein</i>:
<span class="poetry indentAll"><small>They have operated on the strength of the custom of the region, and that is equivalent to having specifically agreed to this arrangement.<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Iggerot Moshe</i>, supra n. 35. See also, <i>Teshuvot Divrei Yosef</i>, no. 21; <i>Teshuvot Nidiv Lev</i>, no. 13; <i>Teshuvot Mahari ha-Levi</i>, no. 111; <i>Teshuvot Devar Avraham</i>, 1:1.</i></small></span>
While Halakhah itself is immutable, the fact that an <i>umdana</i> can serve as a basis for adopting civil law over the Halakhah in monetary matters is essentially built into the Halakhah itself.<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Kiddushin</i> 19b.</i>
As such, absent any violations of <i>issurim</i>, which can never be overridden by civil law, this rule would equally apply to spousal property arrangements. In a civil system of community property, spouses own equally almost all property acquired during the marriage, regardless of in whose name the property is. In our case, the spouses are residents of such a state and therefore had expectations that its rules would be implemented. In other words, there is an <i>umdana</i> that should divorce occur, their marital assets would be divided up equally.
In bold contrast to <i>Hazon Ish</i> and <i>R. Feinstein</i> who define the <i>umdana</i> as the parties’ expectations emerging from secular law, <i>R. Herschel Schachter</i> contends that there is an <i>umdana</i> that the couple views the marriage as a partnership.<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">R. Schachter – oral communication with this author on Sept. 28, 2012.</i> For example, if the real estate title of a home is in the name of the husband, absent any extenuating circumstances to the contrary, the home belongs to both the wife as well the husband. The differing conceptions of the <i>umdana</i> has practical consequences. For the <i>Poskim</i> who view the parameters of the <i>umdana</i> through the prism of secular law, the result will be that the nature of the economic partnership as perceived by the couple will vary from state to state. Those couples who reside in a state which recognizes community property principles relating to the division of marital assets will expect that both spouses are entitled to an equal share of the marriage. Other couples who reside in a state which have adopted equitable distribution rules for dividing up marital assets will expect that both spouses are entitled to an equitable share of the marriage, each share being equal or a certain percentage of the value of the marital asset. And depending on the individual state law, the assets which are to be divided up may be limited to tangible assets such as a home, a car or a business to the exclusion of career assets. On the other hand, in accordance with <i>R. Schachter</i>’s understanding of the <i>umdana</i>, regardless of where the couple resides the partnership model of marriage may dictate that under all circumstances each spouse is entitled to an equal share of the assets of the marriage.<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">Adopting such a perspective does not mean that all monetary aspects of the marriage reflect an economic partnership. For example, absent any express agreement to the contrary, the husband is obligated to support his wife and provide any necessary medical care for his spouse. Similarly, though a husband will inherit his wife’s estate upon her death, the wife does not inherit his estate upon his demise.</i>
Some contemporary authorities have argued that <i>minhag ha-medinah</i>, customary national practice, should govern the division of marital assets upon divorce, irrespective of <i>umdana</i>. Today, the notion of marriage being an economic partnership is ensconced in the American societal mindset; this concept is part and parcel of the legal fabric of every state which operates a system of equitable distribution or community property.<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">Lenore Weitzman, <i>The Marriage Contract</i>, (NY: 1981), 85–8; Caleb Foote, Robert Levy, and Frank Sander, <i>Case and Materials on Family Law</i> (Boston: 1985), 610–19.</i> In fact, women in observant Jewish families, like their counterparts in the general populace, are in many cases partners with their husbands in maintaining the economic stability of their family. In certain situations, the wife is actually the sole or major breadwinner. As such, the <i>minhag ha-medinah</i> is not only a reflection of or legal reality, but equally a mirror image of our social reality.
Although the halakhic matrimonial property system rejects this doctrine of economic partnership, <i>R. Daichovsky</i> and <i>R. Ariel Yanai</i> argue that a couple may opt to accept <i>minhag ha-medinah</i> in matters involving the division of marital assets.<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">See supra n. 21, 217.</i> <i>R. Yanai</i> contends that <i>minhag</i> has the power to override Halakhah as long as the Halakhah is not completely nullified. Therefore, as long as the division of marital assets based upon the adoption of a community property or equitable distribution system will continue to provide the husband a share in the assets, such a division is acceptable. Only if adoption of one of these systems were to preclude the husband from receiving a share of the marital assets would it be tantamount to a <i>minhag</i> uprooting the halakhic matrimonial property system and it would then be prohibited.<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">Netanya Regional Rabbinical Court, File No. 764411-1, <i>Ploni</i> v. <i>Plonit</i>, Oct. 3, 2010. For precedent to this ruling, R. Yanai cites various <i>teshuvot</i> in the context of <i>yerushah</i> (inheritance law); see <i>Teshuvot Tashbetz</i> 2:292, <i>tikun</i> 3; <i>Teshuvot Maharik</i>, <i>shoresh</i> 8, cited by <i>Rema</i>, HM 281:4; <i>Teshuvot Maharashdam</i>, HM 304; <i>Teshuvot Torat Hayyim</i> 2:19; <i>Teshuvot Ridbaz</i> 1:545. It is difficult, however, to argue that the case of division of marital assets parallels that of <i>yerushah</i>. Regarding inheritance law, there is a biblical prohibition against a testamentary disposition that contradicts the Torah’s laws of inheritance, as the Torah states that it is “<i>hukat mishpat</i>” (<i>Bamidbar</i> 27:11). Thus, this is not entirely a monetary law, and <i>minhag</i> therefore does not have the power to override it entirely, as per <i>Ketuvot</i> 83a-84b. A <i>minhag</i> regarding inheritance is valid only if it does not entirely override the Halakhah. The division of marital assets, however, is entirely a monetary matter, and <i>minhag</i> should therefore have the power to override the halakhic system completely. R. Daichovsky concurs with this conclusion; see supra nn. 15 and 22.</i>
On the basis of recognizing the <i>tena’im</i> as a valid contract and invoking an <i>umdana</i> or <i>minhag ha-medinah</i>, <i>R. Ariel</i>, <i>R. Daichovsky</i>, <i>R. Lavi</i>, <i>R. Avraham Schindler</i>, <i>R. Shlomo Shapiro</i>, <i>R. Sharezee</i>, and <i>R. Yanai</i> have resolved marital disputes based on the notion of the existence of an economic partnership between spouses. Accordingly, Rachel, a resident in a state governed by a community property system, must fulfill the expectations of that system. The debts incurred in a family venture during her marriage are therefore debts of the couple and the responsibility to pay them must be shared jointly.
<b>5. Monetary Award for a Wife’s Contributions to the Domestic Household</b>
Classically, rabbinic decisors dividing a couple’s assets upon divorce have been guided by a three-fold classification system. <i>Nikhsei tzon barzel</i> refer to the wife’s assets held by her husband; he is responsible to provide her with either the actual asset or its value upon divorce, while any appreciation or depreciation belongs to the husband. <i>Nikhsei melug</i> refers to the wife’s estate, of which the fruits belong to him without responsibility for loss or deterioration. These assets must be used by the husband for the benefits of the marriage. Finally, a wife may receive property as a present which the donor stipulates that the husband has no control over.<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">This three part division has been discussed by Ben Tzion Schereschewsky, former Israeli Supreme Court Justice and expert on halachic domestic relations, in Menachem Elon (ed.), <i>The Principles of Jewish Law</i> (Jerusalem: 1975), 390–2.<br>Beyond her property rights, a wife receives certain monetary rights during marriage, including but not limited to support (including food, clothing, the cost of lodging, and reimbursement for medical care) and, upon divorce, the right to the value of her <i>ketubah</i>.</i>
Based upon this matrimonial property regime, unless there is an agreement to the contrary, in our scenario, the wife would not receive any share in the value of the family business.
However, beginning in the late nineteenth century, culminating in a twentieth century <i>teshuvah</i> of <i>R. Ben Tzion Uziel</i> and a series of Israeli rabbinical court decisions spanning a period of over fifty years, <i>batei din</i>, utilizing their discretionary authority, have awarded monetary awards to a wife for her role as a homemaker and\or child caretaker, based on the financial capacity and economic future of her husband as well his current income.<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Simcha la-Ish</i>, EH 5, 33; <i>Teshuvot Mishpetei Uziel</i>, EH 96; Z. Warhaftig (ed.), <i>Ossaf Piskei Din shel ha-Rabbanut ha-Rashit le-Yisrael</i> (hereafter: <i>Ossaf Warhaftig</i>), File 1/6/705, 72; ibid., File 1/30/705, 83; ibid., File 1/12/704, 38; ibid., File 1/16/04, 39; <i>PDR</i> 1:129; 2:289; 7:157; 8:274; 12:124; File 1/22/1510 (Sept. 7, 2004), Supreme Rabbinical Court, <i>Ploni</i> v. <i>Almonit</i> (R. Daichovsky).</i> In fact, in two of the earliest Israeli Rabbinical court rulings regarding such matters, the <i>beit din</i> awarded a monetary share in a husband’s business due to a wife’s involvement in the development of the business and due to her contributions in childrearing and managing the household.<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ossaf Warhaftig</i>, File 1/30/705, 83, 157; <i>PDR</i> 1:129, 138. In contrast, Rabbis Eliezer Goldschmidt, Ovadiah Yosef, Eliezar Waldenburg, and Avraham Sherman refrain from recognizing a spouse’s contribution to the home and/or business in dividing up marital assets. See <i>PDR</i> 1:113; 2:46; 4:131; 5:306; 6:257; 7:111; 8:17; 9:65; <i>Ploni</i> v. <i>Almonit</i>, supra n. 45 (R. Sherman).</i>
Accordingly, due to her contributions to the household and business and her husband’s earning potential, Rachel should receive fifty percent of the value of the family business, regardless of the extent of her husband’s capital investment and contribution to the growth of the business. On the other hand, based upon the cumulative evidence, we view the marriage as an economic partnership rather than an example of an “<i>isha noseit ve-notenet betokh ha-bayit”</i> who is the husband’s agent. The <i>Tova’at</i> is therefore obligated to share in paying off the outstanding commercial debt in proportion to her investment in the venture.
<b>Decision</b>
1. Reuben Cohen shall hereby pay Rachel Cohen fifty percent of the net value of the family business.
2. In accordance with the percentage of her capital investment in the business, Rachel Cohen is hereby responsible for a percentage of the debts incurred by the family business.
<b>A Final Thought</b>
The foregoing presentation, which is inspired by an actual <i>beit din</i> decision, demonstrates that the division of marital assets upon divorce may be resolved by adopting varying and opposing approaches.<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">For an earlier treatment of this topic from other perspectives, such as <i>minhag</i> and <i>dina de-malkhusa dina</i>, see my, “Varying Approaches,” supra n. 34, 129.</i> Invoking a particular perspective will ultimately affect how the marital assets and debts in the family venture will be distributed between the divorcing couple. While adopting one model of “<i>isha noseit ve-notenet betokh ha-bayit</i>” leads one to the conclusion that a husband is solely responsible for any debt incurred even if his wife cosigned the loan, recognizing the notion that marriage is an economic partnership – based upon the validity of the <i>tena’im</i> or an <i>umdana</i> that the parties agreed to abide by secular law – indicates that the wife is responsible for half of the business debts, provided that the capital investment was the same for both spouses, and that she should receive half of the business’s net value. While recognizing the wife’s contribution to the business or as a homemaker leads to the conclusion that she should receive a fair portion of the business, adopting the tripartite marital property classification would entail awarding the entire net value of the business to her husband. <sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">For an identical conclusion reached by a Tel Aviv Rabbinical Court regarding stock shares owned by a husband, see Avraham Sheinfeld, “Special Circumstances in Property Division” [Hebrew], 3 <i>Shurat ha-Din</i> (5755), 136. Regarding joint ownership of financial instruments, including but not limited to bank accounts and stocks, see Rehovot Regional Rabbincal Court, File 057244238-24-1; Supreme Rabbinical Court, File 056130081, in 13 <i>Ha-Din ve-ha-Dayan</i>.</i>
Couples who appear before a <i>beit din</i> may choose to resolve their differences in one or two ways. One option, which was the one chosen by the Levys, is to sign an arbitration agreement empowering the <i>dayanim</i> to resolve this matter according to the applicable portions of <i>Shulhan Arukh</i> and <i>Poskim</i>. Alternatively, a couple may decide to resolve the conflict in accordance with secular law and sign an agreement empowering the <i>beit din</i> to do so (accompanied by a symbolic act confirming the binding nature of the agreement). Some <i>dayanim</i> will resolve division of marital assets according to civil law based on the signing of such an agreement.
Seemingly, the latter approach is fraught with perilous ramifications, as it seems to ignore the prohibition against recourse to <i>arka’ot shel akum</i>, litigating in non-Jewish courts. As <i>R. J. David Bleich</i> notes:
<span class="poetry indentAll"><small>Recourse to a gentile forum is tantamount to a declaration by the litigant that he is amenable to allowing an alien code of law to supersede the law of the Torah … The litigant appears before the gentile courts because he accepts their authority, and if they administer the law of Moses he accepts that law not because he regards it as binding upon him by virtue of having been commanded by God, but because it has been endorsed and adopted by gentiles …<br>The prohibition … is not limited to bringing a suit before a gentile court … The prohibition includes any judicial proceeding that negates the Law of Moses. A judicial body composed entirely of judges who happen to be members of the Jewish community but who administer an alien system of law is undoubtedly to be classified as within the halakhic category of <i>arka’ot shel akum</i> for the simple reason that the laws such a court administers are not those of the Torah.<br>Since … acquiescence by both litigants does not serve to mitigate the prohibition … and since the prohibition applies even when the judges themselves are Jews, it follows that the parties are not entitled to accept the authority of a rabbinic court … but stipulate that the <i>beit din</i> shall apply the law of a secular state. Accordingly, if two parties … enter into a contract and stipulate that any dispute with regard to fulfillment of the terms of the contract is to be resolved by a rabbinic court in accordance with, for example, the laws of the State of Delaware, the stipulation is void by virtue of being inconsistent with biblical law (<i>matneh al mah she-katuv ba-Torah</i>).<sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote">J. David Bleich, “Litigation and Arbitration before Non-Jews,” 34 <i>Tradition</i> (Fall 2000), 58, 65–67. For an earlier treatment of this issue by the same author, see <i>Be-Netivot ha-Halakhah</i> [Hebrew] (NY: 1998), vol. 2, 170–1.</i></small></span>
As noted above, individuals are empowered to make agreements contrary to Halakhah provided that these agreements do not violate ritual law. An agreement to adjudicate in a <i>beit din</i> but according to secular law would thus seem to be invalid.
