Indeed, such arrangements have met with trenchant criticism from the <i>Aharonim</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Teshuvot ha-Rosh</i> 18:2; <i>Taz</i>, HM 26:3; <i>Bi’ur ha-Gra</i>, HM 61:23; <i>Teshuvot Teshurat Shai</i> 529; <i>Hazon Ish</i>, <i>Sanhedrin</i> 15:4; A. Sherman, “Halakhic Principles Regarding the Prohibition of Litigating in Civil Courts in order to Probate Inheritance Directives and Other Claims” [Hebrew], 12 <i>Sha’arei Tzedek</i> (5772), 402, in the name of R. Elyashiv.</i> and a number of contemporary <i>Poskim</i>, such as <i>R. Elyashiv</i>, <i>R.Sherman</i>, <i>R. Spitz</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Minhat Tzvi</i>, <i>Hilkhot Shekhenim</i> 16.</i> and <i>R. Zvi Gartner</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">Jerusalem Supreme Rabbinical Court, File 811368/1, 2010; Zvi Gartner, “Regarding the Matter of <i>Arka’ot”</i> [Hebrew], 11 <i>Yeshurun</i> (2002), 698–701.</i> concur in rejecting such agreements.
Nevertheless, there is a coherent and persuasive halakhic tradition, commencing with <i>Rashba</i>, that supports the acceptance of this type of arrangement.<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot ha-Rashba</i> 6:254. For our understanding of <i>Rashba</i>’s position, see <i>Levush</i>, HM 26:4; Eliav Shochetman, “Halakhic Recognition of the Laws of the State of Israel” [Hebrew], 16–17 <i>Shenaton ha-Mishpat ha-Ivri</i> (5750–5751), 417, 456; Z. Goldberg, “Regarding the Matter of <i>Arka’ot</i>” [Hebrew] 11<i>Yeshurun</i> (2002), 702; Y. Reiss, “Stipulating Regarding Biblical Matters in Monetary Law” [Hebrew] 4 <i>Sha’arei Tzedek</i> (2003), 288; <i>PDR</i> 319, 324; and my “Varying Approaches,” supra n. 34, 129. For others who adopt <i>Rashba</i>’s position, see <i>Teshuvot Tashbetz</i> 3:69; <i>Giddulei Terumah</i>, <i>Sefer ha-Terumot</i>, <i>sha’ar</i> 62, <i>helek</i> 1; <i>Tur</i>, HM 26, in the name of <i>Rif</i> and <i>Sefer ha-Terumot</i>; <i>Tumim</i> 26:4 in the name of <i>Tur</i> 104:10; <i>Sma</i>, HM 26:11, 61:14; <i>Netivot ha-Mishpat</i> 26:11; <i>Teshuvot Divrei Hayyim</i>, HM 2:30; <i>Divrei Ge’onim</i> 25:3, 111:3; <i>Bnei Shmuel</i>, HM 26; <i>Maharitz ha-Hadashot</i> 22; <i>Teshuvot Yosef Ometz</i> 4; <i>Teshuvot Mahara Sasson</i> 64; <i>Kenneset ha-Gedola</i>, HM 22, <i>hagahah</i> 20; <i>Birkei Yosef</i> 26:3, 8; <i>Teshuvot Mayyim Amukim</i> 2:54; <i>Teshuvot Beit Shlomo</i>, HM 130; <i>Ulam ha-Mishpat</i> 26; <i>Teshuvot Shemesh u-Magen</i>, vol. 3, HM 1; <i>Tzedakah u-Mishpat</i>, OH 7; <i>Leket Shikhiha</i> in <i>Karnei Re’em</i>, sec. 4; Haifa Regional Rabbinical Court, File No. 1-24-053917464; E. Batzri, <i>Dinei Mamonot</i>, vol. 3, 197; Z. Goldberg, “Acquisition in the Sale of Kidneys” [Hebrew], <i>Ateret Shlomo</i> (1997), 49, 52; idem. <i>Lev ha-Mishpat</i>, vol. 1, 286; Asher Weiss, 6 <i>Darchei Horo’ah</i> (2007), 111; <i>PDR</i> 18:314, 324; <i>Teshuvot Minhat Yitzhak</i> 9: 112.</i> The <i>Sma</i> and <i>Netivot ha-Mishpat</i> endorsed the idea, as do numerous contemporary authorities, such as <i>R. Daichovsky</i>, <i>R. Z. Goldberg</i>, <i>R. Asher Weiss</i>, and <i>R. Yitzhak Weiss</i> of the Eidah ha-Haredit and other Israeli <i>dayanim</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote">Others include Rabbis Avraham Atlas, Ezra Batzri, Tzvi ben Ya’akov, Dovid Malka, Maimon Nahari, and Shlomo Shaanan who all serve as <i>dayanim</i> on one of the Israeli Rabbinical Courts which are under the aegis of Israel’s Chief Rabbinate.</i> In fact, numerous <i>shtarot</i> (halakhic legal agreements) dating back to the time of <i>Rishonim</i> attest that the parties agreed to have their disagreements resolved according to secular law.<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Mahzor Vitri</i>, 551, 562; <i>Ittur</i>, <i>ot</i> 5, <i>harsha’ah</i>; <i>Sefer Maharil</i>, <i>Minhagim</i> (<i>Likkutim</i>), 17; <i>Teshuvot ha-Rashba</i> 2:54, 126; 7:400; <i>Teshuvot ha-Ritva</i> 156; <i>Teshuvot Maharival</i> 1: 120. Cf. <i>Teshuvot ha-Rosh</i> 18:4 and <i>Teshuvot Tashbetz</i> 2:99, who interpret these <i>shtarot</i> differently.</i> Finally, an agreement by parties to have their differences resolved in accordance with secular law by a <i>beit din</i> ought to be valid; no different than Torah-observant Jews who decide to proceed to an attorney requesting that he draft a contract according to civil law.<sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote">In the context of monetary matters, the operative principle is “an individual may stipulate contrary to what is written in the Torah” including the right to have monetary matters resolved in <i>beit din</i> in accordance with secular law. <i>Kiddushin</i> 19b; File no. 1-35-8935, Jerusalem Regional Rabbinical Court, Nov. 1, 2004, <i>Mosdot Plonim v. K.Y.U.V</i>.</i>
According to these authorities, spouses who enter into an agreement to resolve their financial matters according to civil law are merely withdrawing from the privilege granted by the halakhic legal system in order to avail themselves of some benefit in one particular situation; they are not indicating wholesale affirmation of the secular legal system, and the agreement is therefore binding.<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Rashba</i> and <i>Sma</i>, supra n. 53. Their discussion is implicitely based on the Talmudic discussion in <i>Kiddushin</i> 19b and <i>Makkot</i> 3b regarding the ability of an individual to execute agreements in monetary matters in variance with Halakhah. Alternatively, one may argue that one may not stipulate against what is written in the Torah only when one wishes to nullify what is written there. However, if one stipulates for himself that he does not want to avail himself of a benefit accorded by Halakhah, such a stipulation is understood to mean that the individual waives a privilege accorded to him by Halakhah, and therefore such an agreement is valid. See <i>Rashi</i>, <i>Makkot</i> 3b, s.v. <i>le-dedei</i>; <i>Rashbam</i>, <i>Tosafot</i>, <i>Makkot</i> 3b, s.v. <i>al menat</i>; <i>Teshuvot Tashbetz</i> 1:94; <i>Tosafot Rabbeinu Elhanan</i>, <i>Ketuvot</i> 56a-b; <i>Hiddushei ha-Ritva</i>, <i>Kiddushin</i> 19b; <i>Hiddushei ha-Rashba</i> in <i>Shita Mekubetzet</i>, <i>Ketuvot</i> 56a. See also <i>Sma</i>, HM 97:28; <i>Netivot ha-Mishpat</i> 212:4. Whether Sma would reject an agreement between individuals to accept secular law is subject to debate; see <i>Ulam ha-Mishpat</i> 26; <i>Lev ha-Mishpat</i>, supra n. 53.<br>Accordingly, individuals can make an agreement prior to marriage that their monetary affairs will be governed by civil law. See Tel Aviv Rabbinical Court, File 1-21-5035, <i>Ploni</i> v. <i>Plonit</i>. In fact, various prenuptial agreements relating to the division of marital property based upon secular law have been drafted by rabbinic authorities, such as R. Tzvi ben Ya’akov, a member of the Haifa Rabbinical Court.<br>Whether a community may agree to accept all the laws of a secular system rather than specific ones which benefit themselves in a particular matter is subject to debate and beyond the scope of our discussion. See <i>Teshuvot ha-Rivash</i> 52; <i>Teshuvot Tashbetz</i> 1:61.</i> Such an arrangement is no different than a case in which a woman consents to receive no support from her husband; although she waives her right to one of the privileges of <i>nissu’in</i>, the institution of <i>nissu’in</i> itself remains intact.<sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Beit Shmuel</i>, EH 38:11.</i> Clearly, however, prior to executing such an agreement, the parties should seek the guidance of a rabbinical authority who has expertise in matters of <i>Even Ha-Ezer</i> and <i>Hoshen Mishpat</i>.
Had the couple in this case agreed to have their affairs governed by secular law, the issue of how marital assets and debts would be divided between them would vary from state to state. Since this couple resided in California, which is governed by community-property principles, upon dissolution of the marriage, a <i>beit din</i> would likely have placed the husband and wife on equal footing regarding the sharing of debts and receiving the net value of the family business. If they had been residents of an equitable distribution system state, such as New Jersey and New York, where marital property is divided “fairly” based on a weighing of various factors, the <i>beit din</i> would have arrived at an equitable determination regarding how to address the matter of the outstanding debt and distribution of the net value of the venture between the divorcing couple.

b) Spousal Abuse as Grounds for Obligating a Get

<b><i>B. Spousal Abuse as Grounds for Obligating a</i> Get</b><br><b><i>Rochel Cohen v. Eli Cohen</i></b>
<b>Tova’at<i>’s Claims</i></b>
The <i>Tova’at</i> demands a <i>get</i> because her husband coerces her to perform sexual intercourse or other acts against her will, and she therefore detests him. Because of unnatural cohabitation, she argues her sex life has become a living hell. Her husband demanded that she lie with him during the day and night, leaving her intolerably sleepless and exhausted. Suffering real torment, she was unwilling to comply with such demands. Initially, she refused to have intercourse with her husband out of anger and torment. Now, she refuses to have intercourse because she no longer wants to remain married to him.
In addition to the acts of physical assault, spousal rape, and exaggerated sexual demands, the <i>Tova’at</i> is suing her husband, claiming humiliation due to the bestial manner in which she was treated during intercourse and as the victim of emotional abuse due to his verbal degradation. In addition, over a period of five years, the <i>Nitva</i> conducted extra-marital affairs, and is therefore a <i>ro’eh zonot</i> (adulterer). He has persisted in his activities despite the fact that she has warned him on at least two occasions that such behavior is improper and that she will seek a divorce. Consequently, the <i>Tova’at</i> left the marital home with their children, moved into a separate apartment, and now seeks a <i>get</i>.
Additionally, the <i>Tova’at</i> claims that she is entitled to spousal support until a <i>get</i> is delivered to her. Furthermore, her husband should reimburse her for the outlay of rent for the nine months she had to rent an apartment. Moreover, prior to her marriage, the <i>Tova’at</i> owned an apartment complex. During her marriage, the <i>Nitva</i> managed the buildings and collected the rent from the tenants. The <i>Tova’at</i> has discovered that $150,000 of the rental fees were used by the <i>Nitva</i> to fund his illicit affairs. As such, the <i>Tova’at</i> advances a claim for said monies.
<b><i>Nitva</i>’s Claims</b>
Invoking the Talmudic dictum that “a man may do whatever he wishes with his wife,”<sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Nedarim</i> 20b.</i> the <i>Nitva</i> argues that he has a right to engage in sexual intercourse with his wife in any shape and form, even without her consent. Moreover, he argues that generally speaking he did not physically assault his wife, and there are no perceptible marks on his wife’s body that would corroborate his wife’s claim. The few times that he physically abused her were due to his wife’s incitement, and he should therefore be exempt from responsibility for his behavior. Moreover, he apologized to her on various occasions and desires her forgiveness. Because she voluntarily left the marital home, the husband claims that he is exempt from paying her rent or spousal support from the time she vacated the home. Finally, although he admits to having engaged in illicit affairs, the <i>Nitva</i> wishes to reconcile with his wife and requests that the <i>beit din</i> issue a ruling of <i>shalom bayit</i>, the restoration of domestic harmony.
<b>Discussion</b>
The issue at hand is how the Halakhah addresses physical, sexual, and emotional abuse, as well as adultery. If, in fact, such behavior transpired and the wife warned her husband to desist from this behavior, does there remain grounds for a <i>beit din</i> to foster <i>shalom bayit</i>, or is it the <i>beit din</i>’s duty to compel, obligate, or recommend the husband to deliver a <i>get</i> to his wife?
<b>1. Respecting a Wife’s Dignity</b>
Unlike other secular legal systems, the Talmud emphasizes that a husband is obligated to honor his wife.<sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Bava Metzia</i> 59a; <i>Yevamot</i> 62b. Many of the concepts in, and sources for this decision have been culled from Eliav Shochetman, “Violence against Women as Grounds for Divorce” in Aviad Hacohen (ed.), <i>Jubilee Volume in Honor of Menachem Elon</i> (forthcoming).</i> This position is articulated in a normative fashion by various authorities. <i>Rambam</i> rules:
<span class="poetry indentAll"><small>A man shall honor his wife more than his self and shall adore her as he adores himself … And he shall not intimidate her, and he shall speak gently with her …<sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Mishnah Torah</i>, <i>Hilkhot Ishut</i> 15:19; see also <i>Hilkhot De’ot</i> 5:10.</i></small></span>
Subsequently, this ruling was incorporated by the <i>Shulhan Arukh</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">62</sup><i class="footnote"><i>SA</i>, HM 228:3.</i>
Expressions of the requirement to respect one’s wife are found in the <i>tena’im</i>, the conditions of marital engagement, and in the <i>ketubah</i>, the marriage agreement.<sup class="footnote-marker">63</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Maharam mi-Rotenburg</i> (Cremona edition), 291; <i>Teshuvot ha-Rashba</i> 7:477.</i> Thus, one must work in order to support his wife in accordance with her social status.<sup class="footnote-marker">64</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Binyamin Ze’ev</i>, 50; <i>Helkat Mehokek</i>, EH 70:12.</i>
Moreover, wife-beating is a crime and punitive measures, including but not limited to imprisonment, have been imposed upon violent husbands.<sup class="footnote-marker">65</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot ha-Ridbaz</i> 4:100.</i> In earlier periods of Jewish history, halakhic authorities in various European communities sanctioned different modes of excommunication in such cases, such as expelling the husband from the synagogue.<sup class="footnote-marker">66</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Maharam mi-Rotenburg</i> (Prague edition) 81; <i>Teshuvot Maharshah</i> 2:130; <i>Beit Yosef</i>, HM 154.</i> The ruling that one who injures his friend is obligated to pay damages<sup class="footnote-marker">67</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Bava Kama</i> 8:1.</i> applies equally to wife-beaters.<sup class="footnote-marker">68</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Mishnah Torah</i>, <i>Hilkhot Nizkei Mamon</i> 4:16; <i>Tur</i>, EH 83; <i>SA</i>, EH 83:2, HM 421:2, 424:10–11.</i> Since the duty of honoring one’s wife is greater than the obligation to honor oneself,<sup class="footnote-marker">69</sup><i class="footnote">See supra n. 4.</i> the punitive measures for noncompliance should be more severe.<sup class="footnote-marker">70</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Maharam</i>, supra n. 5.</i> In our case, the <i>Nitva</i> was incarcerated temporarily for his abusive behavior.
Even if the harm directed against the wife occurred during intercourse, a husband is liable for sexual battery.<sup class="footnote-marker">71</sup><i class="footnote"><i>SA</i>, EH 83:1; <i>Mahaneh Ephraim</i>, <i>Hilkhot Nizkei Mamon</i> 5.</i> Furthermore, the <i>Nitva</i>’s representation that the Talmud permits a husband to do whatever he wishes to his wife is incorrect. Although the Talmud<sup class="footnote-marker">72</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Eruvin</i> 100b.</i> and post-Talmudic authorities permit any forms of unnatural intercourse, all forms of intercourse require a wife’s consent. As <i>Rambam</i> states:
<span class="poetry indentAll"><small>[The wife] is not his slave to be compelled to have intercourse with someone she hates.<sup class="footnote-marker">73</sup><i class="footnote"><i>MishnahTorah</i>, <i>Hilkhot Ishut</i> 14:8.</i></small></span>
R. <i>Yosef of Trani</i> (<i>Maharit</i>) notes:
<span class="poetry indentAll"><small>Certainly, she is not subject to him incessantly when she does not desire it.<sup class="footnote-marker">74</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Maharit</i> 5:5.</i></small></span>
Hence, a wife is entitled to object to conjugal relations, such as when she has quarreled with her husband.<sup class="footnote-marker">75</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Tur</i>, EH 154.</i> Coerced cohabitation in any shape or form is prohibited.<sup class="footnote-marker">76</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ba’alei ha-Nefesh</i>, <i>Sha’ar ha-Kedushah</i>, 122–123; <i>Mishnah Torah</i>, <i>Hilkhot Issurei Bi’ah</i> 15:17, 21:12; <i>Tur</i>, EH 25; <i>SA</i>, EH 25:2; <i>Teshuvot Yaskil Avdi</i>, vol. 6, EH 25. Others prohibit unnatural intercourse even with the wife’s consent; see <i>Sefer Haredim</i>, <i>Mitzvah ha-Teshuvah</i>, 2.</i>
<b>2. Marital Separation and the Duty of Continuing Spousal Support</b>
Clearly, if the <i>Nitva</i> refuses to desist from physical and sexual violence, his wife is not only entitled to leave the marital home,<sup class="footnote-marker">77</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Tesuvot Yaskil Avdi</i>, vol. 6, EH 35; <i>PDR</i> 11:327.</i> but <i>beit din</i> is halakhically empowered to issue restraining orders against him, directing him to leave the home.<sup class="footnote-marker">78</sup><i class="footnote"><i>PDR</i> 3:299, 319.</i> The grounds for such separation are the Talmudic guidelines of “a man cannot live with a serpent in the same basket” and “the purpose of our being is for life, not for pain.”<sup class="footnote-marker">79</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ketuvot</i> 61a, 72a.</i>
In the United States, a <i>beit din</i> is not legally empowered to issue such orders. Nevertheless, upon the request of the <i>Tova’at</i> and based upon corroborating information which attests to the <i>Nitva</i>’s behavior, had they been approached, the <i>dayanim</i> would have directed her to seek assistance from the civil authorities.<sup class="footnote-marker">80</sup><i class="footnote">Such an action is not a violation of the prohibition of <i>mesirah</i> (informing). See <i>Nishmat Avraham</i>, vol. 4, 207–11, who cites <i>teshuvot</i> from Rabbis Auerbach, Elyashiv, and Waldenberg regarding reporting cases of child abuse. Their conclusion should equally apply to the reporting of spousal abuse.</i> Given that the <i>Tova’at</i>, on her own initiative, has left the marital home, there is no need to address this matter.
Lest one argue that Halakhah recognizes only the two options of marriage and divorce and rejects the possibility of marital separation,<sup class="footnote-marker">81</sup><i class="footnote">Such a conclusion is forthcoming from a decision recorded in <i>Teshuvot Yaskil Avdi</i>, vol. 6, EH 45.</i> <i>Rema</i> states:
<span class="poetry indentAll"><small>We neither coerce him to divorce her nor her to live with him. <sup class="footnote-marker">82</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Rema</i>, EH 77:3. See also <i>Teshuvot Maharam mi-Rotenburg</i> (Prague edition), 946 (end); <i>Teshuvot ha-Rashba ha-Meyuhasot le-Ramban</i> 102–103; <i>SA</i> and <i>Rema</i>, EH 70:12; and numerous other sources.</i></small></span>
On the contrary, the purpose of separation is to ease the tensions between the spouses and give each spouse time and breathing room to hopefully resolve their differences.<sup class="footnote-marker">83</sup><i class="footnote">See R. Hadaya’s opinion, supra n. 23.</i> Consequently, de facto, the separation was legitimate.<sup class="footnote-marker">84</sup><i class="footnote">For a precedent, see <i>Teshuvot Pnei Moshe</i> 1:55.</i>
Whether a wife who abandons the home is entitled to continuing support is dependent upon the reason for her departure. If she vacated the home due to the fact that her husband was delinquent in his marital duties, such as his duty of spousal support, the wife would not be entitled to such remuneration. On the other hand, if the wife separates because she was abused, she is entitled to ongoing maintenance, as well as reimbursement for lodging expenses.<sup class="footnote-marker">85</sup><i class="footnote"><i>SA</i>, EH 70:12; <i>Rema</i>, EH 154:3; <i>Teshuvot Maharit</i> 1:113; <i>Teshuvot Divrei Yehezkiah</i>, EH 14, 33; <i>PDR</i> 1:7, 238; 15:119; <i>Teshuvot Tzitz Eliezer</i> 4:21; 18:58.</i>
Of course, this entitlement is contingent upon the fact that she can demonstrate that her husband is abusive.<sup class="footnote-marker">86</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Beit Shmuel</i>, EH 70:34; <i>Helkat Mehokek</i> 70:42.</i> In our scenario, the <i>Nitva</i> argues that his wife incited him to act violently toward her and that there therefore was no justification for her to leave the marital home. However, the burden of proof is upon the husband to prove that his behavior was in response to his wife’s incitement.<sup class="footnote-marker">87</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Mishpetei Shmuel</i> 1:26; <i>Teshuvot Yabia Omer</i>, vol. 3, EH 15.</i> There is a presumption that women are faithful, and we therefore do not believe the husband that his wife provoked the assault.<sup class="footnote-marker">88</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Rema</i>, EH 154:3.</i> Moreover, <i>Rabbis O. Yosef</i>, <i>Betzalel Zolty</i>, and <i>S. Yisraeli</i> note that a single act of provocation does not legitimize the right of physical assault. Finally, since he frequently physically abused his wife, we are unwilling to believe that she provoked him on each and every occasion.<sup class="footnote-marker">89</sup><i class="footnote"><i>PDR</i> 11:327, 328; 15:122.</i>
Contrary to the <i>Nitva</i>’s claim, the <i>Tova’at</i> alleges that on numerous occasions, the <i>Nitva</i> abused her without provocation, a claim that the <i>Nitva</i> denies. In light of this denial and the absence of physical indications attesting to such assaults, is there sufficient basis for concluding that the husband is a wife-beater and abuser? This issue is complicated by the fact that the authorities debate whether a woman is believed regarding her allegation that her husband engaged in unnatural intercourse without her consent.<sup class="footnote-marker">90</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sedei Hemed</i>, <i>Gerushin</i> 1:12 and <i>Teshuvot Edut Ya’akov</i>, 36.</i>
Despite the fact that the husband does not admit his guilt, the testimony of third parties – including women, who are usually invalid witnesses – as well as medical records and advisory opinions of health professionals who interviewed and counseled the couple, serve as evidence of the <i>Nitva</i>’s proclivity to violence and antisocial behavior.<sup class="footnote-marker">91</sup><i class="footnote">Regarding the value of third party opinions, including professionals, see <i>Rema</i>, EH 74:10, 154:3; <i>PDR</i> 11:328; Netanya Regional Rabbinical Court, File No. 4564-24-1 (8 Shvat 5766); Tel Aviv Regional Rabbinical Court, File 3426-21-3 (Jan. 28, 2008), <i>Plonit</i> v. <i>Ploni</i>. Regarding the validity of women’s testimony, see <i>Rema</i>, EH 154:3; <i>PDR</i> 20:126, 133 (R. Z. Goldberg).<br>Other circumstantial evidence may be extrapolated from a man’s behavior in <i>beit din</i>. Should he exhibit moments of loss of self-control during a hearing, such conduct may be used to assess his character; see <i>PDR</i> 11:327 and my “Harnessing the Authority of Beit Din to Deal with Cases of Domestic Violence,” <i>Tradition</i> 45 (2012), 37.</i>
Thus, given that the wife’s abandonment of the marital home was justifiable, she is entitled to receive her maintenance and to be reimbursed for her outlay of rent.
<b>3. <i>Shalom Bayit</i> and the Requirement of Forewarning</b>
Prior to addressing whether there are grounds for issuance of a divorce judgment, it is the responsibility of a <i>beit din</i> to inquire whether the restoration of <i>shalom bayit</i> is a possibility. In a case of irreconcilable differences, the <i>beit din</i> would invoke the position of <i>Rabbeinu Yeruham</i> and support the issuance of a <i>get</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">92</sup><i class="footnote">Whether such issuance is to be rendered as an obligation upon the <i>Nitva</i> or as a recommendation to the <i>Nitva</i> is subject to debate. See <i>Sefer Meisharim</i>, <i>netiv</i> 23, <i>helek</i> 8; <i>PDR</i> 6:13; 8:325; 11:95, 255; 13:264, 14:183; File no. 845682, Supreme Rabbinical Court, June 28, 2012, <i>Ploni</i> v. <i>Plonit</i>. For a detailed analysis of R. Yeruham’s position, see my <i>Rabbinic Authority: The Vision and the Reality</i>, vol. 2 (forthcoming).<br>According to R. Tenenbaum, in such circumstances, one cannot compel the issuance of a <i>get</i>; see <i>Teshuvot Divrei Malkiel</i> 3:145.</i> In the present case, however, the husband’s plea for <i>shalom bayit</i> may indicate that this is not a situation of irreconcilable differences, culminating in a “dead” marriage.<sup class="footnote-marker">93</sup><i class="footnote">Irreconcilable differences between spouses and the inability to restore <i>shalom bayit</i> serve as grounds for divorce; see <i>Sefer Meisharim</i>, supra n. 34; <i>Teshuvot</i> ha-<i>Ridbaz</i>, vol. 4, 89; <i>Iggerot Moshe</i>, YD, 4:15; <i>Teshuvot Yabia Omer</i>, vol. 2, EH 10; <i>PDR</i> 6:13; 8:323; 11:95 255; 14:183, 193; 19:57, 65.</i> Although husband and wife have been separated for over nine months, the husband argues that there still remain prospects for reconciliation. We must therefore make an attempt to examine whether, in fact, this marriage could survive and eventually become stable or if it is truly beyond repair.
<i>R. Avraham Sherman</i> states:
<span class="poetry indentAll"><small>The concept of <i>shalom bayit</i> between the couple and among the family is not merely an expression of the individual emotional state between the couple and their children. Marriage between spouses was not designed only to grant privileges to each spouse. The fact of matrimony creates a legal situation which imposes duties upon each spouse vis-à-vis the other, and when children are born, their birth creates a state of parental obligations towards them …<sup class="footnote-marker">94</sup><i class="footnote">Supreme Rabbinical Court, File 5007-64-1 (July 19, 1999), <i>Ploni</i> v. <i>Plonit.</i></i></small></span>
Can we lay the groundwork for promoting tranquility between the spouses and engender a more suitable environment for raising their children? Can we facilitate the sustaining of an environment in which this father will be able to serve as a halakhic-ethical model for his children and inculcate within them Torah values?
It appears that we can extrapolate from a case posed to <i>Rivash</i> and <i>Maharashdam</i>. For ten months, a couple attempted to conceive a child, but it is apparent that the husband is impotent. Is his impotence sufficient grounds for acceding to the wife’s request for a <i>get</i>? According to <i>Rivash</i>, if the wife is insistent on the divorce, the <i>beit din</i> should issue such a judgment.<sup class="footnote-marker">95</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot ha</i>-<i>Rivash</i> 127.</i> <i>Maharashdam</i> demurs, however, arguing that if there is a possibility that the husband may be cured, and for the sake of <i>shalom bayit</i>, we should direct the couple to wait and see if the medical matter resolves itself.<sup class="footnote-marker">96</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Maharashdam</i>, EH 103. For others who endorse this approach, see <i>Ritzba</i>, <i>Teshuvot Maimoniyot</i>, <i>Hilkhot Ishut</i> 6; <i>Teshuvot Noda be-Yehuda</i>, EH, <i>Tanina</i>, 89.</i> If no cure is forthcoming, the <i>beit din</i> may then issue a divorce ruling. Pursuant to both views, if there is no hope for a cure and the wife is insistent on a divorce, the <i>beit din</i> must render a divorce judgment.
In our case, the <i>Nitva</i>’s frequent acts of wife-beating reflect the personality of a mentally deranged individual, and there is no hope for rehabilitating him. As a result, the rendering of a divorce judgment is appropriate. In fact, some professional studies have demonstrated that rehabilitation programs for male perpetrators of domestic violence appear to be less effective in reducing recidivism than programs for other offender groups.<sup class="footnote-marker">97</sup><i class="footnote">Andrew Day, et. al., “Programs for Men who Perpetrate Domestic Violence: An Examination of the Issues Underlying the Effectiveness of Intervention Programs,” 24 <i>Journal of Family Violence</i> (2009), 203.</i> Hence, hope for <i>shalom bayit</i> does not appear to be on the horizon.
Had this marriage been marked by consensual sexual relations with isolated instances of physical abuse, we might have arrived at the conclusion that the <i>Tova’at</i> accepted her husband’s improper behavior and that there therefore may be a prospect for <i>shalom bayit</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">98</sup><i class="footnote"><i>PDR</i> 1:139, 142; 10:3, 8.</i> Clearly, a one-time assault followed by a husband’s desire to appease his wife does not serve as grounds for divorce.<sup class="footnote-marker">99</sup><i class="footnote"><i>PDR</i> 10:3.</i> Furthermore, <i>Rema</i> writes that it is necessary that the husband be warned regarding the consequences of his behavior before a divorce may be demanded.<sup class="footnote-marker">100</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Rema</i>, EH 154:3.</i>
In our case, however, the fact that the <i>Tova’at</i> consistently refused to engage in coerced sexual intercourse and warned her husband more than once to desist from his unacceptable conduct undermines this approach. If a husband persists in his behavior even after he has been warned – by his wife, <i>beit din</i>, or even the police<sup class="footnote-marker">101</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Shema Shlomo</i>, vol. 1, EH 15.</i> – clearly divorce is the only remaining option.<sup class="footnote-marker">102</sup><i class="footnote"><i>PDR</i> 8:303, 310.</i> Moreover, if the abuser has been convicted of abuse, if his one assault posed a danger to his wife’s life, or if his behavior is uncontrollable, there is no need to mandate a forewarning at all.<sup class="footnote-marker">103</sup><i class="footnote">Ibid., 15:145, 153; 8:216, 226; 6:221, 222–3; File 027295641-21-1, <i>Ha-Din ve-ha-Dayan</i>, <i>gilyon</i> 3 (Av 5763), 3.</i> Clearly, then, the <i>Tova’at</i> complied with the requirement of forewarning as mandated by <i>Rema</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">104</sup><i class="footnote">See supra n. 44. Some authorities argue that if acts of violence have been perpetrated over an extended period of time, there is no requirement of a forewarning. See <i>Teshuvot Tashbetz</i> 3:8; <i>Teshuvot Yakhin u-Boaz</i> 2:84; <i>Teshuvot Heikhal Yitzhak</i>, EH 1:3; <i>PDR</i> 1:77; 3:221; 15:152.</i> Furthermore, there are <i>Poskim</i> who contend that in contemporary times there is no requirement of forewarning.<sup class="footnote-marker">105</sup><i class="footnote">R. D. Karlin, <i>Hilkhot Yad Dovid</i>, <i>Hilkhot Ishut</i> 14 (41); <i>PDR</i> 6:37–41 (R. B. Zolty).</i>
Finally, the fact that the husband seeks his wife’s forgiveness has no redeeming value in the wake of a marriage marked by acts of physical violence and cruel treatment.<sup class="footnote-marker">106</sup><i class="footnote">File 017938838-64-1, <i>Ha</i>-<i>Din ve-ha-Dayan</i>, <i>gilyon</i> 2 (Iyar 5763), 5.</i>
<b>4. Physical Abuse as Grounds for Divorce</b>
Thus far, we have seen that the <i>Tova’at</i> was justified in leaving the marital home and is entitled to remuneration for maintenance and rental expenses. We have further concluded that the <i>beit din</i> has little reason to assume that <i>shalom bayit</i> is a likely outcome, and the <i>Tova’at</i> may therefore request a <i>get</i>. The question at hand is therefore whether the <i>Nitva</i> can be compelled to give her the <i>get</i>. In other words, does the husband’s physical abuse of his wife violate Halakhah and therefore constitute grounds for divorce? Should the <i>beit din</i> simply recommend that the parties divorce, obligate the husband to grant a divorce, or actually coerce him to grant a divorce (<i>kefi’at ha-get</i>)?<sup class="footnote-marker">107</sup><i class="footnote">Subscribing to the view of Rabbeinu Hananel, most authorities distinguish between two levels of enforcement of a <i>get</i> ruling: (1) <i>Kofin le-garesh</i> – compelling the <i>Nitva</i> to grant a <i>get</i> to his wife, and (2) <i>hiyuv le-garesh</i> – obligating a <i>Nitva</i> to divorce his wife. See <i>Mordekhai</i>, <i>Ketuvot</i> 194, 205. While the issuance of the former judgment may result in certain criminal, monetary, and/or social sanctions, the latter involves verbal persuasion. See Zorach Warhaftig, “Coercion to Grant a Divorce in Theory and Practice” [Hebrew], 3–4 <i>Shenaton ha-Mishpat ha-Ivri</i> (1976–1977), 153. In contemporary times, in cases in which it is halakhically unclear whether the <i>Nitva</i> should be compelled or obligated to grant a divorce, many <i>batei din</i> employ the terminology that a divorce is “a mitzvah” or “recommended,” which has the connotation of a lower level of sanction than the other two types of enforcement.<br>In the United States, one type of coercion warranted is <i>kefiyah be-derekh bereirah</i>, which means directing a <i>Nitva</i> to fulfill his duty of spousal support or payment of the dowry and the <i>ketubah</i> or, by his choice, to relieve himself of this duty by executing a <i>get</i>. See <i>Beit Meir</i>, <i>Pithei Teshuvah</i>, EH 154:8; <i>PDR</i> 1:15, 19, 77, 80.</i>
An abuser is viewed as a <i>poshei’a</i> (one who acts intentionally is negligent).<sup class="footnote-marker">108</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Nimmukei Yosef</i>, <i>Bava Kama</i> 32a; <i>Piskei ha-Rosh</i>, <i>Bava Kama</i> 2:10.</i> Therefore, if a man injures his wife, even while engaged in consensual sexual relations, if he had intent to injure her,<sup class="footnote-marker">109</sup><i class="footnote">Should the injury be unintentional, the husband would be exempt from responsibility; see <i>Teshuvot Maharsham</i> 1:75; <i>Hazon Ish</i>, <i>Bava Kama</i> 11:21.</i> he must compensate her for the damage.<sup class="footnote-marker">110</sup><i class="footnote"><i>SA</i>, EH 83:2; HM 421:12.</i> Moreover, engaging in conjugal relations without the consent of one’s wife (spousal rape)<sup class="footnote-marker">111</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Eruvin</i> 100b; <i>SA</i> EH 25:2.</i> or when angry with her<sup class="footnote-marker">112</sup><i class="footnote"><i>SA</i>, OH 240:3.</i> are equally violations of Halakhah.
If there was forewarning, attempt for <i>shalom bayit</i> has been undertaken, and other quasi-legal sanctions – such as excommunication or social shunning – have been implemented, some authorities, such as <i>R. Yosef ibn Avitur</i> (eleventh century Spain), <i>Rabbeinu Simcha</i> (thirteenth century Ashkenaz), and <i>R. Yitzhak ben Moshe</i> (thirteenth century Vienna), would compel a husband to grant a <i>get</i> to his abused wife.<sup class="footnote-marker">113</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Otzar ha-Ge’onim</i>, <i>Ketuvot</i>, <i>Teshuvot</i>, paragraph 476; <i>Or Zarua</i> 3, <i>Bava Kama</i>, 161; <i>Teshuvot Maharach Or Zarua</i>, 127; <i>Teshuvot Shevut Ya’akov</i>, vol. 1, EH 135. In cases of physical endangerment to her life, R. Waldenberg has compelled the <i>Nitva</i> to grant a divorce; see <i>Teshuvot Tzitz Eliezer</i> 6:41.</i> Other decisors, such as <i>Rashba</i>, <i>Ridbaz</i>, and <i>R. Avraham di Boton</i>, argue that a <i>beit din</i> may only obligate a husband to grant a <i>get</i> in such a case.<sup class="footnote-marker">114</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot ha-Rashba</i> 5:264; 7:477; <i>Teshuvot ha-Ridbaz</i>, 4:1228; <i>Teshuvot Lehem Rav</i> 31.</i>
In formulating his ruling on the matter, <i>R. Yosef Karo</i> states:
<span class="poetry indentAll"><small>One cannot rely on the opinions of the <i>Agudah</i> and Rabbeinu Simcha, and therefore we cannot coerce him to give a <i>get</i> due to the fact that these opinions have not been mentioned by the renowned decisors.<sup class="footnote-marker">115</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Beit Yosef</i>, EH 154.</i></small></span>
Contemporaneously and in response to <i>R. Karo</i>, <i>Rema</i> argues in his commentary to <i>Tur</i>:
<span class="poetry indentAll"><small>I do not understand his position … There are grounds to rely upon these views. <i>Ramban</i> and <i>Maharam</i> agreed in their <i>teshuvot</i> on the matter of wife abuse and they adduced proofs for their words, and logic concurs with them. The reason why these authorities were not mentioned by the decisors is possibly due to the fact that it was so obvious.<sup class="footnote-marker">116</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Darkhei Moshe</i>, EH 154:17.</i></small></span>
Unsurprisingly, in his gloss to <i>Shulhan Arukh</i>, <i>Rema</i> rules:
<span class="poetry indentAll"><small>If a man beats his wife, this is a sin and is akin to someone who assaults his friend. If he is accustomed to such conduct, <i>beit din</i> is empowered to punish him, excommunicate him, and flog him … And if he does not comply with <i>beit din</i>’s directive, some say we compel him to divorce his wife, provided that he has been forewarned once or twice.<sup class="footnote-marker">117</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Rema</i>, EH 154:3.</i></small></span>
According to <i>Rema</i>, should sanctions and an admonishment be ineffective, a husband who regularly beats his wife will be compelled by <i>beit din</i> to deliver a <i>get</i> to his wife, despite the views to the contrary. Furthermore, it appears that had the views of <i>Rabbeinu Simcha</i> and <i>Agudah</i> been cited by the renowned decisors, <i>R. Karo</i> would have concurred with their position as well.<sup class="footnote-marker">118</sup><i class="footnote">The comments of <i>Rema</i>, EH 154:21, require further explanation, which we leave for another occasion.</i>
Given that halakhic divorce is possible only if both parties agree to it, how is it possible to compel the husband, even an abusive one, to grant his wife a get? Ostensibly, such a <i>get</i> should be classified as a <i>get me’useh</i>, a coerced <i>get</i>, which is invalid. On what grounds, then, can the <i>beit din</i> compel, or even obligate, a recalcitrant husband to divorce his wife?
<i>Rambam</i> offers a famous answer to this question, explaining that every Jew essentially wishes to comply with the tenets of Halakhah; if he refuses to do so, it is due to his evil inclination overpowering his true will. The <i>beit din</i>’s coercion is simply a means to reinstate his “true will” – either to fulfill the Halakah or to provide the <i>get</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">119</sup><i class="footnote"><i>MishnahTorah</i>, <i>Hilkhot Gerushin</i> 2:20. For those who ascribe to one of these two rationales, see <i>Tosafot Rid</i>, <i>Or Zarua</i>, 654 and <i>Teshuvot Or Zarua</i>, 69, 127; <i>Netivot ha-Mishpat</i> 205:1; <i>Teshuvot Hatam Sofer</i>, EH 28; <i>Or Same’ah</i>, <i>Hilkhot Gerushin</i> 2:20; <i>Imrei Binah</i>, <i>Hilkhot Dayanim</i> 1; <i>Hazon Ish</i>, EH 99:1-2.</i> For R. Karo and <i>Rema</i>, the abusive husband’s “true will” in this case is to fulfill the Halakhah and grant his wife a <i>get</i>.
Given that there is some dispute regarding whether an abusive husband should be compelled to grant a <i>get</i>, can a <i>beit din</i> compel him to do so? Echoing the words of <i>R. Yitzhak Elhanan Spector</i>, <i>R. Yitzhak Herzog</i> states:
<span class="poetry indentAll"><small>Even though he [the husband] knew that there is some rule that he should not be compelled, he was reconciled because the court ruled that he should be compelled, because it is a mitzvah to obey the scholars of your generation.<sup class="footnote-marker">120</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Heikhal Yitzhak</i>, EH 1:3. See also <i>Teshuvot Ein Yitzhak</i> 2:5, 35; <i>Teshuvot Sha’arei De’ah</i> 1:119. Some contend that in cases in which the majority view is to coerce, a <i>beit din</i> can coerce a husband; see <i>PDR</i> 4:166.</i></small></span>
In other words, the true will of an observant Jew is to comply with the norms of Halakhah, including the granting of a <i>get</i>, as understood by the <i>beit din</i> that the husband has accepted to serve as the arbiter of his situation. Regardless of the fact that other legists may disagree with his arbiter, the husband’s “true will” is to follow his chosen arbiter; should the arbiter compel him to give a <i>get</i>, there is no element of compulsion, for he voluntarily acquiesces to the directive of his arbiter.<sup class="footnote-marker">121</sup><i class="footnote">See supra n. 62.</i> Pursuant to this view, the existence of controversy regarding whether one should coerce a <i>get</i> in the case of abuse would not preempt the possibility that an arbiter could render a ruling compelling the issuance of a <i>get</i>.
According to <i>Hatam Sofer</i> and others, however, a divorce can only be compelled when there is unanimous agreement among the authorities that a compelled divorce is valid under those circumstances. If there is controversy regarding the matter, the <i>beit din</i> cannot postulate regarding what the “true will” of the husband is.<sup class="footnote-marker">122</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Hatam Sofer</i>, EH 116; <i>Mishnah le-Melekh</i>, <i>Gerushin</i> 11:28; <i>Teshuvot Devar Yehoshua</i>, vol. 3, EH 30. Nevertheless, even those decisors who endorse <i>Hatam Sofer</i>’s view would contend that if the majority opinion argues for compelling a divorce, such a ruling would not generate a <i>get me’useh</i>; see <i>PDR</i> 4:164, 167.</i> In practice, this is the view adopted by many <i>batei din.</i> Consequently, given that it is subject to controversy whether wife-beating is grounds for compelling a divorce, the issuance of such a judgment would fail to reflect the husband’s true will; should a judgment be rendered to compel a divorce, the result would be a <i>get me’useh</i>.
Nonetheless, as we have seen, <i>R. Karo</i> concurs with <i>Rema</i> that in a situation of spousal physical abuse, a <i>beit din</i> will obligate the husband to grant a divorce.<sup class="footnote-marker">123</sup><i class="footnote"><i>PDR</i> 12:84, 92–93; Netanya Regional Beit Din, File 9465-21-1, <i>Plonit</i> v. <i>Ploni</i> (26 Shvat, 5767).</i> Therefore, although we cannot compel the <i>Nitva</i> to grant his wife a <i>get</i> in our case, we can obligate him to do so.
<b>5. Emotional Abuse as Grounds for Divorce</b>
We will now turn our attention to the question of whether the <i>Tova’at</i>’s emotional pain is grounds for granting a divorce judgment.
<i>R. Shimon bar Tzemah Duran</i> (<i>Tashbetz</i>) addresses a case in which a husband physically endangered his wife’s life by depriving her of food. In his attempt to determine whether this constitutes grounds for divorce, <i>Tashbetz</i> draws two analogies to psychological abuse:
<span class="poetry indentAll"><small>If a husband declares, “I will neither feed nor provide for my wife,” he is compelled to grant her a divorce … If bad breath would be grounds for such compulsion [as stated in the Talmud Yerushalmi], her very soul is far more so!<sup class="footnote-marker">124</sup><i class="footnote">See Talmud Yerushalmi, <i>Gittin</i> 9:9.</i> … In this case, he should divorce her and pay the amount stipulated in the <i>ketubah</i>, because it is written that “she was to live with him and not to suffer with him” … If even concerning one who prohibits his wife from doing things which are not so painful for her, we rule that he should divorce her and remit the <i>ketubah</i> money … a fortiori when he causes distress on a regular basis that we should say that he should divorce her and pay the amount earmarked in the <i>ketubah</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">125</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Tashbetz</i> 2:8; see also <i>Teshuvot Yakhin u-Boaz</i> 2:43.</i></small></span>
<i>Tashbetz</i>’s use of analogy to cases of psychological abuse in determining the status of physical abuse is not an isolated instance. In obligating a wife-beater to grant a <i>get</i>, <i>Rashba</i> finds support for his conclusion in the Mishnaic ruling obligating a husband to give a <i>get</i> in a case of a husband who attempts to prevent his spouse from associating with her family and/or friends.<sup class="footnote-marker">126</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot ha-Rashba</i> 7:477.</i>
Thus, as counterintuitive as it appears, for both <i>Rashba</i> and <i>Tashbetz</i>, the level of enforcement of a divorce judgment relating to emotional abuse was clear and required no lengthy argumentation to defend. On the contrary, for these legists, the real issue for resolution lies in the realm of spousal battery; they determine the proper ruling in the latter case based on the conclusion in the former. Medieval legists thus clearly recognized the significance of emotionally abusive behavior and its implications for the health of a marriage.<sup class="footnote-marker">127</sup><i class="footnote">For an additional decisor who treats both physical and emotional abuse as two sides of the same coin, see <i>Teshuvot Binyamin Ze’ev</i>, 88.</i>
Addressing the case of a wife who was the victim of verbal and emotional abuse and who then fled her home to live in a shelter, an Israeli rabbinical court rules:
<span class="poetry indentAll"><small>The truth of the woman’s words is clear and the pain she has articulated is sincere … Emotional violence by a husband is sometimes more serious than physical violence … The woman has a right to live and not to be in sorrow …<sup class="footnote-marker">128</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ha-Din ve-ha-Dayan</i> 1 (2003), 6. See also Netanya Regional Court, File 4637-29-1, <i>Ploni</i> v. <i>Plonit</i> (June 27, 2006).</i></small></span>
As a result, the <i>beit din</i> decided to terminate the marriage and obligate the husband to grant a <i>get</i> to his wife.
Similarly, given the emotional scarring caused by the <i>Nitva</i> in our case, we obligate him to grant his wife a <i>get</i>.
<b>6. Adultery as a Grounds for Divorce</b>
Even had the husband acted completely normally in his relations with his wife, there would be grounds for issuing a divorce judgment in this case based upon the fact that he was an adulterer. Addressing such a situation, <i>R. Alexander Zuslin</i> (<i>Agudah</i>) argues along the same lines as <i>Tashbetz</i> that if the Talmud Yerushalmi directs the compulsion of a <i>get</i> in cases of foul breath, a fortiori, such a conclusion should apply to an adulterer.<sup class="footnote-marker">129</sup><i class="footnote">For additional reasons, see <i>Sefer ha-Agudah</i>,<i>Yevamot</i> 65b, no. 77.</i> Assuming that witnesses corroborate the husband’s behavior or the husband admits to being an adulterer and he has been forewarned to desist from this behavior,<sup class="footnote-marker">130</sup><i class="footnote">A husband’s admission of adultery is not uncommon. In the four cases of adultery that appeared before a court on which I sat, the husband did not contest his wife’s allegation. For the evidentiary requirements, as well the need to demonstrate that he is a frequent adulterer and has been forewarned, see <i>Rema</i>, EH 154:1; <i>Teshuvot Hakham Tzvi</i>, 133.</i> a <i>get</i> should be compelled in such an instance.<sup class="footnote-marker">131</sup><i class="footnote">The ability to coerce a <i>get</i> based upon the application of a <i>kal ve-homer</i> (a fortiori) argument has persuasive precedent. Based on such arguments, a number of authorities have expanded the grounds for coercing a <i>get</i> beyond the examples mentioned in the Talmud. See <i>Teshuvot ha-Rosh</i> 42:1; <i>Piskei ha-Rosh</i>, <i>Ketuvot</i> 7:19; <i>Teshuvot Tashbetz</i> 2:8; <i>Teshuvot ha-Rema</i>, 36; <i>Teshuvot Maharit</i> 1:113; <i>Teshuvot Maharam Alshakar</i> 73.</i> This attests to the horrific nature of the act of adultery in destroying the family unit and the acute need to dissolve this marriage due to such conduct, rather than the sinful nature of the act itself.<sup class="footnote-marker">132</sup><i class="footnote"><i>PDR</i> 1:5, 13 (R. Eliezer Goldschmidt).</i>
<i>Agudah</i>’s ruling that adultery is grounds for coercion of a <i>get</i> was reaffirmed by <i>Rema</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">133</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Rema</i>, supra n. 72<i>.</i></i> Depending on the particular facts of the case, various Israeli rabbinical court decisions have either coerced an adulterer to grant a <i>get</i> or have obligated him to do so.<sup class="footnote-marker">134</sup><i class="footnote"><i>PDR</i> 1:5, 15; 8:254, 261; 12:24, 29; 14:183, 193–194.</i>
Given that the <i>Nitva</i> admitted to his frequent improprieties, we obligate him to grant a <i>get</i> to the <i>Tova’at</i>. Although the <i>Nitva</i> was not forewarned regarding this particular matter, in our estimation, given the psychological makeup of the <i>Nitva</i>, the frequency of his illicit behavior, and the emotional stress caused by his behavior, we may forego the requirement of a forewarning.<sup class="footnote-marker">135</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Tashbetz</i> 3:8; <i>Teshuvot Yakhin u-Boaz</i> 2:84; <i>Teshuvot Heikhal Yitzhak</i>, EH 1:3; <i>PDR</i> 1:77–80; 3:221–222; 12:24, 26–27; 15:152–153.</i>
<b>7. A Husband’s Dissipation of Family Assets</b>
Finally, let us address the <i>Tova’at</i> entitlement to recoup $150,000 from her husband due to his dissipation of the family assets to finance his adulterous behavior. In some communities, on the occasion of a <i>shiddukhin</i> (an engagement), it is customary to sign a <i>tena’im</i> enumerating various parental obligations and the anticipated month of the wedding (or the actual wedding date). The document attests that the parties have fulfilled all of their mutual obligations and should refrain from any fraudulent conduct, and stipulates a monetary penalty should a party to the agreement default.<sup class="footnote-marker">136</sup><i class="footnote">For differing formulations of this agreement, see <i>Nahalat Shiva</i>, 7–8; <i>Tikkun Soferim</i>, 11; and <i>Zera Avraham</i>, 11. For guidelines in drafting this document, see <i>Sma</i>, HM 243:12, 245:2; <i>Teshuvot Hatam Sofer</i>, EH 112, 135; <i>Teshuvot Beit Yitzhak</i> 1:111; <i>Teshuvot Zera Emet</i>, YD 236. For the halakhic consequences of reneging on such an agreement, see my “Breach of Promise to Marry,” 17 <i>The Jewish Law Annual</i> (2007), 267.</i>
Because the prospective couple does not sign the <i>tena’im</i> and because the content of the <i>tena’im</i> is not drafted as a bona fide <i>shtar</i> mutually obligating the couple to engage in their financial affairs with transparency and accountability, some decisors reject the halakhic-legal significance of the <i>tanna’im</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">137</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Tuv Ta’am ve-Da’at</i>, <i>Mahadura Tanina</i>, 181; <i>Teshuvot Beit Yitzhak</i>, EH 1:110.</i> Nevertheless, numerous decisors, such as <i>Mahari Weil</i>, <i>Maharik</i>, <i>Maharsham</i>, <i>Noda be-Yehuda</i>, <i>Birkei Yosef</i>, and <i>Rav Pe’alim</i>, invoke this premarital clause as governing the monetary ties between spouses during their marriage.<sup class="footnote-marker">138</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Mahari Weil</i>, 134; <i>Teshuvot Maharik</i>, <i>shoresh</i> 57; <i>Teshuvot Maharsham</i> 1:45; <i>Teshuvot Noda be-Yehuda</i>, <i>Tanina</i>, YD 155; <i>Birkei Yosef</i>, YD 448:2; <i>Teshuvot Rav Pe’alim</i>, vol. 2, EH 32.</i> Consequently, if such a document were signed, the <i>Nitva</i>’s siphoning of family funds for illicit affairs entails fraud and is a violation of this premarital obligation.
Because some may argue that this conclusion is based on a questionable halakhic position and at best should only be construed as a <i>senif</i> (an appendage) to buttress a more persuasive stance, we will present a more feasible explanation for the <i>Nitva</i>’s liability as well.
Classically, rabbinic decisors dividing a couple’s assets upon divorce have been guided by a three-fold classification system of assets. <i>Nikhsei tzon barzel</i> refer to the wife’s assets held by her husband; he is responsible to provide her with either the actual asset or its value upon divorce, while any appreciation or depreciation belongs to the husband. <i>Nikhsei melug</i> refers to the wife’s estate, of which the fruits belong to him without responsibility for loss or deterioration. These assets must be used by the husband for the benefits of the marriage. Finally, a wife may receive property as a present which the donor stipulates that the husband has no control over.<sup class="footnote-marker">139</sup><i class="footnote">This three-part division has been discussed by Benzion Schereschewsky, former Israeli Supreme Court Justice and expert on halachic domestic relations, in Menachem Elon (ed.), <i>The Principles of Jewish Law</i> (Jerusalem,1975), 390–2.<br>Beyond her property rights, a wife receives certain monetary rights during marriage, including but not limited to support (including food, clothing, the cost of lodging, and reimbursement for medical care) and upon divorce the right to the value of her <i>ketubah</i>.</i>
Based upon this matrimonial property regime, the apartment complex owned by the <i>Tova’at</i> is to be subsumed under the category of “<i>nikhsei melog.</i>” As such, any accrual of profits, such as rent, must be used for the benefit of the domestic household.<sup class="footnote-marker">140</sup><i class="footnote">Yerushalmi, <i>Ketuvot</i> 6:3; <i>Ketuvot</i> 80b; <i>Helkat Mehokek</i>, EH 85:17; <i>PDR</i> 2:101–102.</i> Since the <i>Tova’at</i> was able to prove that the rental receipts were diverted to fund her husband’s improper activities, it is clear that the <i>Nitva</i>’s use of the assets actually undermined the family unit, and the <i>Tova’at</i> is therefore entitled to recoup such monies.
In short, the <i>Nitva</i>’s absconding with these assets was both a violation of a premarital agreement recorded in the <i>tanna’im</i> and an undermining of the matrimonial property regime.
<b>Decision</b>
Based on the facts presented, the panel finds as follows:
1. Eli Cohen is <i>hayav</i> (obligated) to give a <i>get</i> to Rochel Cohen.
2. Eli Cohen is obligated to hereby immediately pay $150,000 to Rochel Cohen.

c) A Father's Duty of Child Support towards His Estranged Children

<b><i>C. A Father’s Duty of Child Support towards His Estranged Children</i></b><br><b><i>Ya’akov Levy v. Miriam Levy on behalf of Yerucham and Talya Levy</i></b>
Pursuant to the terms of a divorce agreement, the father Ya’akov Levy (<i>Tove’a</i>) is obligated in child support until his children reach the age of 18. Physical and legal custody of the two children – his 12-year old son, Yerucham, and 10-year old daughter, Talya – was awarded to Miriam Levy (<i>Nitva’at</i>), the mother, and the father was awarded visitation privileges. The <i>Tove’a</i> subsequently remarried and within three years had two children with his second wife.
The relationship between the father and his children was tense due to the divorce and his subsequent remarriage. The parties mutually agreed to see a psychologist. Nevertheless, after a series of sessions spanning a period of nine months, the therapist was unable to lay the groundwork for reconciliation. Eventually, the children became totally estranged from their father and refused to visit him.
<b><i>Tove’a</i>’s Claims</b>
The <i>Tove’a</i> argues that his children are minors and that their wishes regarding participation in a visitation agreement should therefore be irrelevant. Moreover, given that the divorce agreement grants him visitation privileges, the <i>Tove’a</i> contends that since his children do not want to spend time with him, he is no longer duty-bound to support them.
<b><i>Nitva’at</i>’s Claims</b>
Because the majority of the children’s leisure time is spent in their mother’s home and their father has a new family, the <i>Nitva’at</i> contends that the children are estranged from their father and no longer have a relationship with him; they therefore do not want to visit him in his home with his new family. In response to the <i>Tove’a</i>’s argument that the cessation of visitation is a violation of their divorce agreement and that he is therefore exempt from child support, the <i>Nitva’at</i> claims that the agreement does not stipulate that maintenance is contingent upon visitation. Thus, although the children decline to visit him, the support obligation continues to be operative.
<b>Discussion</b>
<b>1. A Minor as a <i>Ba’al Din</i> (Litigant) in Child Placement Proceedings</b>
Prior to reaching majority (for a girl, up until the age of 12 years and a day, and for a boy, up until the age of 13 years and a day),<sup class="footnote-marker">141</sup><i class="footnote">Additionally, a male must have two pubic hairs and a female must have <i>simanim</i>; see Tosefta <i>Niddah</i> 6:2; <i>Niddah</i> 45b–46a. Regarding the absence of <i>da’at</i>, see <i>Hagigah</i> 2b.</i> a child is viewed as a <i>katan</i> (minor) for all matters. Because they lack <i>da’at</i>, the rational capacity for decision-making, <i>ketanim</i> may not be litigants before <i>beit din</i>, even regarding matters relating to them. Consequently, minors are represented by their parents, who serve as their <i>apotropsim</i> (guardians) and are entrusted to manage their children’s interests, including appearing before <i>beit din</i> on their behalf.<sup class="footnote-marker">142</sup><i class="footnote">The father serves as <i>apotropus</i> on the grounds of his legal standing as a natural guardian; the mother serves as <i>apotropus</i> by virtue of her appointment by a <i>beit din</i> or because she lives with her child. See <i>Teshuvot ha-Rosh</i> 82:2, 87:4, 96:2; <i>Teshuvot ha-Rashba</i> 2:49.</i> It is only upon reaching majority that a child can be a <i>ba’al din</i>, at which point no issue relating to him can be resolved before <i>beit din</i> in his absence.
Thus, for example, if a father obligates himself to support his daughter and subsequently becomes divorced, the mother, in her capacity as <i>apotropos</i>, is mandated to advance any and all claims on behalf of her child. In this case, the mother appeared at the hearing as the <i>Nitva’at</i> on behalf of her minor children in an attempt to ensure that the <i>Tove’a</i> would not renege on his commitment, the <i>hov</i> (duty) of support he has to fulfill towards his children.<sup class="footnote-marker">143</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Tur</i>, EH 114; <i>SA</i>, EH 114:1; <i>Helkat Mehokek</i>, ad loc. 4.</i>
Although a minor is halakhically considered bereft of <i>da’at</i>, this only affects the efficacy of his or her legal acts, such as buying and selling,<sup class="footnote-marker">144</sup><i class="footnote">There are some instances in which minors do possess <i>da’at</i> and their transactions are therefore valid; see <i>Gittin</i> 5:7; <i>Gittin</i> 59a, 64b; Yerushalmi <i>Ma’aser Sheni</i> 4:3; Yerushalmi <i>Gittin</i> 5:7.</i> and his or her accountability for sins committed.<sup class="footnote-marker">145</sup><i class="footnote">For example, if a minor girl voluntarily engaged in an illicit relationship, she is considered as having committed the act under duress. Since <i>da’at</i> is a prerequisite for being liable for adultery, she is neither liable for punishment nor prohibited to her husband, as she would have been were she an adult. See <i>Yevamot</i> 61b.</i> This does not mean, however, that the child’s wishes are entirely irrelevant. Child placement is not a legal action predicated upon the notion of <i>da’at</i>, and a child’s wishes relating to custody or visitation placement are therefore pertinent.<sup class="footnote-marker">146</sup><i class="footnote">Addressing an action of a minor, <i>Maharam Padua</i> notes that this is not a matter of <i>kinyan</i>, <i>kiddushin</i>, or punishment, legal actions contingent on the presence of <i>da’at</i>; <i>Teshuvot Maharam Padua</i> 53; Eliav Shochetman, “Taking into Consideration a Minor’s Wishes Regarding the Laws of Child Placement” [Hebrew], <i>Me’oznei Mishpat</i> 4 (5765), 545, 547.</i> On the contrary, in order for the <i>beit din</i> to determine the placement that is in the best interests of the child, it is certainly prudent to hear from the child himself. In fact, since children are often used as pawns in divorce proceedings and are manipulated by one or both of their parents, it is the <i>beit din</i>’s responsibility to discover the child’s true wishes, rather than to blindly accept what a parent wants the child to communicate to the <i>beit din</i> panel.
The late <i>R. Eliezer Goldschmidt</i>, a renowned Israeli <i>dayan</i>, aptly notes:
<span class="poetry indentAll"><small>The child is the litigant regarding his placement during the time of the divorce, and he is a litigant after the divorce, he and nobody else … The child is always a litigant and he is plaintiff, and the mother is the guardian who represents him in his claim and advances a claim in his name.<sup class="footnote-marker">147</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Piskei Din Beit Mishpat Elyon</i> 15, 1/60 <i>Winter</i> v. <i>Be’ari</i>, 1457,1485–6.</i></small></span>
In fact, a child’s needs are not necessarily determined by a divorce agreement executed between his parents. A child may advance any claim, independent of what has been documented in the divorce agreement, and he may challenge any provision of the agreement that is detrimental to his welfare.<sup class="footnote-marker">148</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Mishpetei Shaul</i>, 175; <i>Edut be-Yehosef</i>, 141–3.</i>
Viewing the child as the plaintiff reflects the idea that a child is a person who must live in an environment that will serve his best interests, rather than as an object for the benefit of one of his parents. The minor’s input regarding his placement and visitation, among other things, contribute towards discerning what environment will provide optimally for his growth and personality development.
For example, the majority of <i>Poskim</i> have ruled that in the case of a daughter, regardless of her age, the welfare of the child is best served by placing her with her mother, so that she will be properly instructed in the ways of modesty and halakhic-moral propriety.<sup class="footnote-marker">149</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ketuvot</i> 102b; <i>Teshuvot Maharashdam</i>, HM 308; <i>Teshuvot ha-Ridbaz</i> 1:263.</i> However, should the minor daughter prefer to reside with her father, we respect her wishes, if the <i>beit din</i> determines that this reflects her best interests.<sup class="footnote-marker">150</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Maharam Padua</i> 53.</i> Similarly, the <i>Rashbash</i> discusses a case in which a widow wished to take her minor daughters with her to Israel and some of their relatives opposed the move. The <i>Rashbash</i> rules that if the daughters are intelligent and agree to the move, the mother’s decision is upheld.<sup class="footnote-marker">151</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Rashbash</i> 116.</i> The <i>Mabit</i> rules that even if a mother consented to having her daughters live with their father, if the girls are uncomfortable with the arrangement and wish to return to their mother, they may do so, as we must consider the children’s wishes and personal welfare.<sup class="footnote-marker">152</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot ha</i>-<i>Mabit</i> 2:62.</i>
Taking a child’s wishes into consideration applies equally to a minor son.<sup class="footnote-marker">153</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Mishnah Torah</i>, <i>Hilkhot Ishut</i> 21:17.</i> For example, <i>R. Moshe Alsheich</i> discusses a case of a father who disappeared and was presumed dead. A <i>beit din</i> entrusted his son to an <i>apotropos</i>. Subsequently, the mother wanted to move to Israel with her minor son, and the <i>apotropos</i> objected, arguing that the son should remain with the father’s parents. The child expressed his wishes to remain with his mother. Ruling that the child’s wishes are paramount and that the guardian’s appointment related solely to the child’s monetary matters, <i>R. Alsheich</i> concludes that the child should move to Israel in order to reside with his mother.<sup class="footnote-marker">154</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Maharam Alsheich</i> 38.</i>
Thus, in our case, although the children are minors, their wishes regarding participation in the visitation agreement must be taken into consideration. The children’s feelings communicated to this panel – relating to, among other things, their father’s decision to divorce their mother, his verbally abusive behavior towards their mother, and his desire to begin a new life with a new family – explain their estrangement from their father. Hence, we affirm the right of the children to decline to visit with their father.
<b>2. The Nature of a Father’s Child Support Obligation</b>
A father is primarily liable for <i>mezonot yeladim</i> (child support) by virtue of his paternity,<sup class="footnote-marker">155</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Shita Mekubetzet</i>, <i>Ketuvot</i> 65b; <i>Piskei ha-Rosh</i>, <i>Ketuvot</i> 4; <i>Yam Shel Shlomo</i>, <i>Ketuvot</i> 4. Others maintain that a father’s obligation in child support stems from his spousal duty to maintain his wife. See <i>Ran</i>, <i>Ketuvot</i> 5; <i>Bah</i>, EH 71; <i>Mishnah le-Melekh</i>, <i>Hilkhot Ishut</i> 12:14; <i>Iggerot Moshe</i>, EH 106.</i> irrespective of whether the marriage has been terminated by divorce or death<sup class="footnote-marker">156</sup><i class="footnote"><i>SA</i>, EH 82:7 and commentaries ad loc.</i> or whether the child was born out of wedlock.<sup class="footnote-marker">157</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Yevamot</i> 22a; <i>SA</i>, EH 71:4, HM 276:6; <i>Shakh</i>, HM 87:57; <i>PDR</i> 1:145, 154; 7:136, 144, 146, 152.</i> Until they reach the age of six, by dint of being a father, he is obligated to support his sons and daughters,<sup class="footnote-marker">158</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Piskei ha-Rosh</i>, <i>Ketuvot</i> 4:14; <i>Tur</i>, EH 112; <i>Beit Yosef</i>, EH 112; <i>Bi’ur ha-Gra</i>, EH 112:12.</i> even if the child has his financial means for self-support.<sup class="footnote-marker">159</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Piskei ha-Rosh</i>, ibid., in the name of <i>Maharam mi-Rotenburg</i>; <i>Tur</i>, EH 1; <i>SA</i>, EH 1:1; <i>PDR</i> 3:299, 307; 8:325, 33. Cf. <i>Teshuvot ha-Rashba</i> 2:391.</i> From the age of six until a child reaches majority, a father is obligated as a relative, pursuant to the laws of <i>tzedakah</i> (charity) or <i>minhag ha-medinah</i> (national custom), rather than as a father.<sup class="footnote-marker">160</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Tur</i>, EH 71; <i>SA</i>, EH 71:1.</i> Consequently, only a wealthy father may be compelled to support a child between the age of six and the age of maturity, while a father of limited means is not subject to compulsion.<sup class="footnote-marker">161</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ketuvot</i> 49b. If the child has financial means to support himself, the father is exempt from supporting him; see <i>Teshuvot ha-Rambam</i> (Blau edition) 1:69; <i>Bah</i>, EH 71; <i>PDR</i> 2:65, 92. Cf. <i>Taz</i>, EH 1:2.</i>
As duly noted by a contemporary rabbinical court:
<span class="poetry indentAll"><small>There is no privity between an individual who is duty-bound by dint of the <i>halakhot</i> of <i>tzedakah</i> and the beneficiary [of the tzedakah], since he [the giver] does not owe him [the beneficiary] a debt; he is obligated in <i>hilkhot tzedakah</i>, rather than [in giving] to a specific person. <sup class="footnote-marker">162</sup><i class="footnote"><i>PDR</i> 1:145, 155.</i></small></span>
Thus, although a father can be compelled to provide for his child until the age of six regardless of his means, after that point, only halakhic-moral persuasion is possible if the father is not wealthy. Thus, a child over the age of six must demonstrate that his father has the means to support him and that the child does not have the wherewithal to support himself.<sup class="footnote-marker">163</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Tashbetz</i> 2:292; <i>Mishpetei Uziel</i>, EH 74 and 116.</i>
In the present case, the children are above the age of six, and their father is therefore obligated to support them pursuant to <i>hilkhot ­tzedakah</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">164</sup><i class="footnote">If the divorce agreement mandated child support until the age of 13, had the parties signed an arbitration agreement empowering this panel to resolve this matter in accordance with civil law or if the <i>dayanim</i> at their discretion chose to adhere to customary practice, the obligation to support the child prior to the age of six would extend until the child attains majority.</i>
<b>3. The Rules of Child Placement</b>
The majority of decisors argue that the welfare of a child of tender years is normally best served by placing him with his mother, provided that the father has visitation privileges in order to fulfill his educational duties vis-à-vis his child.<sup class="footnote-marker">165</sup><i class="footnote"><i>SA</i>, EH 82:7; <i>Maggid Mishnah</i>, <i>Hilkhot Ishut</i> 21:17; <i>Derishah</i>, EH 82:2.</i> Above the age of six, a son must reside with his father, since from this age onwards, the child requires intensive educational guidance from his father.<sup class="footnote-marker">166</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Otzar ha-Ge’onim</i>, <i>Ketuvot</i>, <i>Teshuvot</i> 435; <i>Mishnah Torah</i>, <i>Hilkhot Ishut</i> 21:17.<br>Although a father is obligated to teach his son certain skills, including Torah study, he may delegate this responsibility to a third party, such as a teacher or <i>yeshiva.</i> In Ge’onic times, Torah education was imparted by the father, and the <i>Ge’onim</i> therefore ruled that a 6-year old boy should be placed with his widowed father rather than his grandmother because of the paternal duty to educate one’s son. In modern times, however, education is imparted primarily in formal school settings. Should a father choose to delegate his responsibility to teach his son to others, there is no reason that would mandate that his son live with him, barring the mother’s unfitness as a parent. See the <i>teshuvah</i> of R. Yitzhak de Molena, cited in Avraham David, “R. Yitzchak de Molena” [Hebrew], 44 <i>Kiryat Sefer</i> (5729), 553. Today, <i>batei din</i> generally allow a son who attends <i>yeshiva</i> to remain living with his mother even after he has reached the age of six, while allowing his father to have visitation privileges.</i> Regardless of her age, a daughter remains with her mother.<sup class="footnote-marker">167</sup><i class="footnote">See supra, n. 9.</i>
As we saw above, however, these rules are legal presumptions; given evidence that a different arrangement is in the child’s best interest, the <i>beit din</i> may determine that such an arrangement is the better course of action. <i>Rema</i> observes:
<span class="poetry indentAll"><small>It seems to the <i>beit din</i> that it is good for the daughter to live with her mother. However, if it seems to the <i>beit din</i> that it is better for her to be placed in her father’s home, the mother cannot force the father to relinquish custody.<sup class="footnote-marker">168</sup><i class="footnote"><i>SA</i>, EH 82:7.</i></small></span>
<i>R. Mordekhai ben Yehuda ha-Levi</i> (seventeenth century Egypt) similarly states:
<span class="poetry indentAll"><small>Generally speaking, the Sages of the Talmud said that a daughter should be placed with the mother, as should the son in his earlier years, and later [he should] be with his father … All this is for the benefit of the child … But if the <i>beit din</i> determines that the child’s well-being is not served with this arrangement, but rather is detrimental, they should alter their decision in accordance with the child’s well-being, as it appears to the arbiters.<sup class="footnote-marker">169</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Darhei No’am</i> 26.</i></small></span>
Numerous authorities have endorsed this notion, maintaining that the rules of child placement are not set in stone and the child’s best interests should determine whether to apply a particular rule.<sup class="footnote-marker">170</sup><i class="footnote">See my “Child Custody: A Comparative Analysis,” 14 <i>Israel Law Review</i> (1978), 480.</i>
In certain cases, the arbiter operates based on his own discretion and common sense, without applying any rules. For example, the <i>Ge’onim</i> discuss a case in which an infant’s mother died and his father moved away, and both sets of grandparents sought custody of the child:
<span class="poetry indentAll"><small>It seems that one ought to enquire what will be the good of the baby (<i>tivuta de-tinok</i>). If he is accustomed to one set of grandparents, recognizes them, and is happy with them, they ought to take the baby, since his mind will be more comfortable with them.<sup class="footnote-marker">171</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Otzar ha-Ge’onim</i>, <i>Ketuvot</i>, <i>Teshuvot</i> 434.</i></small></span>
<b>4. The Relationship Between Child Support and Child Placement</b>
The Talmud does not address the <i>Tove’a</i>’s claim that a father’s support obligation is contingent upon the child’s willingness to live with or visit his father. <i>Rambam</i>, however, does address the relationship between child maintenance and child placement:
<span class="poetry indentAll"><small>After the child is six years old, the father may say, “If the child stays with me, I will support him, but if he remains with his mother, I will not pay for support.” And the daughter remains always with her mother, even beyond six years.<sup class="footnote-marker">172</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Mishnah Torah</i>, <i>Hilkhot Ishut</i> 21:17.</i></small></span>
Various interpretations of this statement have been suggested. According to one interpretation, a father cannot coerce his six-year old son to leave his mother and to live with him,<sup class="footnote-marker">173</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Helkat Mehokek</i>, EH 82:7. Others argue that the father can, in fact, force his 6-year old son to reside with him; see <i>Maggid Mishnah</i>, <i>Hilkhot Ishut</i> 21:17; <i>Teshuvot ha</i>-<i>Mabit</i> 1:165.</i> whether the son refuses because he is personally uncomfortable living with his father or because his mother has convinced him to remain with her.<sup class="footnote-marker">174</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Mishpetei Shmuel</i> 23.</i> Others construe <i>Rambam</i> as stating that one cannot remove the son from his mother’s home against her will. According to both interpretations, should the boy remain with his mother, the father is exempt from his support duty.<sup class="footnote-marker">175</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Bi’ur ha-Gra</i>, EH 82:11; <i>Teshuvot Maharival</i> 1:12 (74); <i>Teshuvot Maharam Alsheich</i>, supra n. 14.</i>
Because a daughter always remains with her mother, regardless of her age, it is apparent from <i>Rambam</i>’s ruling that a father cannot deny her support if she does not wish to live with him.<sup class="footnote-marker">176</sup><i class="footnote">However, the daughter has a right to insist on residing with her father if she so wishes; <i>Helkat Mehokek</i>, supra n. 33; <i>Beit Shmuel</i>, EH 82:9–10.</i> However, <i>Rambam</i> is silent regarding a case in which a father is denied visitation rights with his daughter. Is the father’s responsibility to support his daughter contingent on his ability to have a relationship with her?
<i>Maharival</i> discusses a case in which a mother desires to move to another city, thereby depriving the father of visitation privileges. He rules that the daughter may nonetheless remain with her mother, even if her best interests seem to indicate otherwise.<sup class="footnote-marker">177</sup><i class="footnote">According to R. Yosef ibn Lev, the child’s welfare is relevant only when she reaches majority age.</i> In contrast, <i>R. Shmuel de Medina</i> (<i>Maharashdam</i>) argues, among other reasons, that if the daughter’s best interests dictate that she remain with her father, she must remain with him;<sup class="footnote-marker">178</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Maharashdam</i>, EH 123.</i> if she remains with her mother, even if this is in the daughter’s best interest, the father is exempt from child support because his daughter is not accessible to him.<sup class="footnote-marker">179</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Divrei Yosef</i>, EH 86; <i>Teshuvot Shufra de-Ya’akov</i> 60; <i>PDR</i> 13:17, 21, 27–28.</i>
In effect, whereas according to <i>Rambam</i>, the paternal obligation of maintenance is contingent upon placement of the child, for <i>Maharashdam</i>, the support duty is dependent upon the ability to exercise visitation privileges. Most authorities – including <i>R. S. Yisraeli</i>, <i>R. E. Waldenburg</i>, <i>R. O. Yosef</i>, and <i>R. S. Daichovsky</i> – concur with <i>Maharashdam</i>’s view and maintain that a father’s preclusion from visitation of his daughter exempts him from maintenance.<sup class="footnote-marker">180</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Noda be-Yehuda</i> 2:89; <i>Teshuvot Tosafot Re’em</i> 114; <i>Teshuvot Mishpat Tzedek</i> 1:23; <i>Teshuvot Perah Mateh Aharon</i> 2:110. Cf. <i>Teshuvot Tzitz Eliezer</i> 16:44; <i>PDR</i> 13:3, 12; <i>Teshuvot Yabia Omer</i>, vol. 8, EH 22; S. Daichovsky, “Support of Children – Special Legislation in the Laws of <i>Tzedakah</i>” [Hebrew] 16 <i>Tehumin</i> (5756), 87, 100.</i>
A number of rationales have been suggested for this position. For example, the obligation of <i>tzedakah</i>, the basis for the maintenance duty, does not apply if the recipient will be spiritually harmed. Since the father is responsible for teaching his children Torah,<sup class="footnote-marker">181</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Shemirat Shabbat ke-Hilkhata</i> (Jerusalem: 5725), 306–8.</i> their denial of access entails a spiritual detriment, and he is therefore entitled to refrain from furnishing material support. Moreover, the father’s obligation to give <i>tzedaka</i> to his son is based on the principle of “<i>karov karov kodem</i>,” that one’s closest relatives should be given priority in receiving <i>tzedaka</i>. If the son is estranged from his father, he cannot be labeled a “<i>karov</i>” and therefore loses his right to support.
Other authorities, however, maintain that the issue of child support should be addressed on its own grounds, independent of whether a child resides or visits with his father. One Israeli rabbinical court addressed a scenario in which this question was relevant. In the case at hand, a woman separated from her husband without the execution of a <i>get</i> and subsequently cohabitated with another man and had a daughter with him.
