Eventually, the woman separated from the father of the child, and the father advanced a claim in <i>beit din</i> demanding that a visitation schedule be established with his daughter. In the midst of proceedings, the <i>beit din</i> discovered that the woman was actually still married to her first husband and that the girl was therefore a <i>mamzeret</i>, a child born from an illicit relationship. The mother argued that since the father had revealed the illicit relationship publically and their daughter refused to visit her father, he should not be entitled to visitation rights.
Writing on behalf of the panel, <i>R. Goldschmidt</i> rules:
<span class="poetry indentAll"><small>The laws regarding child placement are not laws relating to the good of the parents, but rather laws for the benefit of the children; a son or daughter is not an object subject to the rights of a father or mother. The father and mother have no rights, only duties … [as] they are obligated to raise and educate their children. When a <i>beit din</i> is determining the placement of a child, concerning the ties between the child and his parents, there is only one factor … the good of the child, where he will be and under what conditions. However, the rights of the father and mother do not exist at all …<br>When dealing with visitations of the plaintiff with his child, we are not adjudicating an issue of his rights or the rights of the defendant … There is only a discussion of the welfare of the child.<sup class="footnote-marker">182</sup><i class="footnote"><i>PDR</i> 1:145, 157–158.</i></small></span>
Many years later, addressing a child custody case under discussion in the Israeli Supreme Court, <i>R. Yosef Kapah</i> addressed the above decision:
<span class="poetry indentAll"><small>It is difficult for me to agree completely with this ruling [that parental rights do not exist at all]. It is clear that parents are not inanimate objects. Even parents are composed of body, soul and emotions … Parents have a right to seek emotional satisfaction, seeing their children grown and develop properly and succeed in their studies …<sup class="footnote-marker">183</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Piskei Din Beit Mishpat Elyon</i> 39 (1), 1/81 <i>Nagar</i> v. <i>Nagar</i>, 365, 412.</i></small></span>
Clearly, Halakhah grants parental right and privileges vis-à-vis one’s children when it comes to monetary matters. Thus, for example, if a boy finds an object, it belongs to his father as long as the child is still dependent on him, even if he is older than the mandatory age of support.<sup class="footnote-marker">184</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Bava Metzia</i> 12a-b; Yerushalmi, <i>Ketuvot</i> 6:1; <i>SA</i>, HM 270:2. If a daughter finds an object, it belongs to her father even if she is not dependent on him.</i> Some authorities further maintain that a son’s earnings equally belong to his father as long as his father is supporting him.<sup class="footnote-marker">185</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Tosafot</i>, <i>Eruvin</i> 79b; <i>Tosafot</i>, <i>Gittin</i> 64b. See <i>SA</i>, HM 270:2 and commentaries, loc. cit.</i> <i>R. Goldschmidt</i> does not deny that there are certain halakhic-legal parental rights in the realm of <i>Hoshen Mishpat</i> (monetary law); he simply argues that the parents have no rights in the context of child placement. In that context, a child is not related to as an object belonging to the parent. As <i>R. Ben Tzion Uziel</i> writes:
<span class="poetry indentAll"><small>Neither sons nor daughters of a person are his acquisitions, like his possession of his monetary assets or animals … They are the inheritance of <i>Hashem</i> … to raise and educate them in Torah, commandments, and the way of life.<sup class="footnote-marker">186</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Mishpetei Uziel</i>, EH 91.</i></small></span>
Since only parental duties are relevant in this context, <i>R. Goldschmidt</i> contends that in resolving visitation issues, a <i>beit din</i> must establish whether a parent can fulfill those duties by being accessible to his child on a periodic basis. Parental feelings and the need to develop a bond with one’s offspring cannot be ignored, but the child’s welfare is given priority. <i>R. Yitzhak Herzog</i> similarly wrote that the <i>beit din</i> should not be concerned with “compassion for the parents … but rather the welfare of the daughters …”<sup class="footnote-marker">187</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Pesakim u-Ketavim</i>, vol. 7, 521–2.</i>
<i>R. Kapah</i>’s argument that visitation and placement should be contingent upon a father’s rights has been rejected by many earlier legists. <i>Maharashdam</i>, for example, explicitly acknowledges a father’s monetary rights vis-à-vis his children, but concludes that placement is to be resolved based on the child’s best interests.<sup class="footnote-marker">188</sup><i class="footnote">Supra n. 38; <i>Teshuvot Tzitz Eliezer</i>, supra n. 40.</i>
The panel in the case regarding which <i>R. Goldschmidt</i> wrote determined that it was in the daughter’s best interest to deny the father visitation privileges. The child support obligation is completely unrelated to visitation (or, for that matter, placement), and the panel therefore obligated the father to continue supporting his daughter (as that obligation is not affected by whether or not the child is a <i>mamzer</i>). Although the particular case involved a daughter, <i>R. Goldschmidt</i> argues that the same would apply to a son; if the son’s best interests were to be undermined by visitation, he would be exempt from visiting his father, but the father would remain responsible to provide his son with support.<sup class="footnote-marker">189</sup><i class="footnote">Supra n. 42, 158.</i> In short, whereas, the placement of the child is based upon which parent will promote the child’s best interests, the mandating of child support is grounded in the fact that a particular man is the father of a child.
In a recent decision of the Haifa Regional Rabbinical Court, it was noted that the common practice on the rabbinical courts under Israel’s Chief Rabbinate is to follow <i>R. Goldschmidt</i>’s approach.<sup class="footnote-marker">190</sup><i class="footnote">File no. 586034/4 (Jan. 16, 2011), <i>Ploni</i> v. <i>Plonit</i>. See also <i>PDR</i> 1:65, 75; 2:298, 327; 3:359; <i>Teshuvot Yaskil Avdi</i>, vol. 6, EH 31; <i>Teshuvot Minhat Avraham</i> 3:4; supra n. 43.</i>
<b>5. The Validity and Relevance of the Divorce Agreement</b>
Prior to any divorce, the parties negotiate and settle all issue of conflict regarding how to divide up financial assets and address the placement of their children. A secular divorce agreement thus reflects the compromises and vested interests of each party, rather than the <i>halakhot</i> of family law. A number of decisors have noted that a divorce agreement reflects <i>minhag ha-soherim</i> (commercial practice); as such, no symbolic act of undertaking an obligation (<i>kinyan</i>) is necessary.<sup class="footnote-marker">191</sup><i class="footnote"><i>PDR</i> 3:353, 368–369; 4:275, 279; <i>Teshuvot Tzitz Eliezer</i> 16:53; <i>Minhat Avraham</i>, supra n. 50. In <i>minhag ha-soherim</i>, <i>kinyan situmta</i> (an undertaking recognized in commerce) suffices; see <i>Bava Metzia</i> 74a.</i> Once there has been such an exchange of mutual obligations, there is no longer room for invoking an <i>umdana</i> (the expectations of a given party), as the provisions of the agreement govern any further discussion.<sup class="footnote-marker">192</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Mishnah le-Melekh</i>, <i>Hilkhot Zekhiya</i> 6:1. Although the paternal support obligation is for the children and the children have not undertaken any obligation to their father, nonetheless, since the wife consented to comply with the divorce agreement subject to this duty, we construe this obligation as a mutual obligation between the father and the mother of the children.</i>
However, in our case, we are faced with an <i>umdana gedolah</i> or an <i>umdana de-mukhah</i> (circumstantial evidence) that at the time of signing off on the divorce agreement the father never anticipated that he would support his children until the age of eighteen without having visitation privileges. In such a case, despite the fact that the agreement fails to stipulate that support is contingent upon visitation rights, <i>R. Elyashiv</i> and <i>R. Sherman</i> assume that the father’s expectation was that the two matters were interdependent. Hence, should the children decline to visit him, he ought to be exempt from continuing to maintain them.<sup class="footnote-marker">193</sup><i class="footnote">A. Sherman, “The Nullification of a Monetary Obligation in an Agreement” [Hebrew], 2 <i>Divrei Mishpat</i> (5756), 316, 326.</i>
<i>Rema</i>, <i>Taz</i>, and the late Israeli Chief Rabbi <i>R. Avraham Shapiro</i> disagree with this conclusion, insisting that the terms of the divorce agreement are probative regarding the parties’ expectations; one cannot infer any expectation beyond what is recorded in the actual agreement.<sup class="footnote-marker">194</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot ha-Rema</i> 60; <i>Taz</i>, HM 70; <i>Minhat Avraham</i>, supra n. 50.</i> Consequently, the children’s abstention from visitation with their father will not result in exempting their father from maintenance.
Accordingly, the question of whether the father’s child support duty is contingent upon visitation privileges in our case appears to be dependent upon whether we follow the written word of the divorce agreement or the father’s expectation. If the latter is determinant, the <i>Tove’a</i> should be exempt from maintenance; if we adhere to the former, he should be obligated to continue to pay child support.
A third perspective would argue that the <i>beit din</i> is empowered to seek the situation that suits the best interests of the child regardless of the provisions of the divorce agreement. As we saw above, <i>batei din</i> have consistently emphasized that child placement and visitation entail the fulfillment of a parental duty to raise a child in a proper environment, rather than an entitlement to have a child live or visit a parent.<sup class="footnote-marker">195</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Maharashdam</i> terms this the “<i>zekhut ha-ben</i>,” the child’s right to be raised in the most suitable environment; supra n. 38. See <i>PDR</i>, 1:75, 147.</i> Thus, <i>Mabit</i> argues that if a mother initially agreed that her daughter should remain with her father and then changed her mind, the child should be returned to her mother if that is in her best interest. The initial agreement is not construed as relinquishing the mother’s right to custody.<sup class="footnote-marker">196</sup><i class="footnote">Supra n. 12.</i> In the words of one <i>beit din</i>, children are not “merchandise, so that agreements are executed regarding them;”<sup class="footnote-marker">197</sup><i class="footnote"><i>PDR</i> 11:158.</i> placement and visitation arrangements that will undermine their best interests are therefore invalid.<sup class="footnote-marker">198</sup><i class="footnote">Ibid., 7:3, 9; 11:153, 159, 163.</i> Thus:
<span class="poetry indentAll"><small>In ruling regarding child placement … our first and primary concern is the child’s welfare … Parents are prohibited from compromising regarding the body or soul of their child, and any agreements do not obligate us. Any time a concerned party will appear in <i>beit din</i> and will prove that the child’s welfare is best served with him, <i>beit din</i> will not hesitate to rule that the terms of the agreement dealing with child placement are inapplicable and void, and will resolve the matter according to the evidence submitted during the proceedings, without factoring into consideration the agreement executed by the parents.<sup class="footnote-marker">199</sup><i class="footnote">Ibid., 11:163; 3:353, 359.</i></small></span>
Other <i>batei din</i> have also endorsed the idea that the <i>beit din</i> may modify divorce agreements in order to promote the child’s best interests.<sup class="footnote-marker">200</sup><i class="footnote">Ibid., 1:157.</i>
Based on the cumulative evidence submitted to this panel, including speaking to the children privately (with the written permission of their parents) and a review of the therapist’s conclusions, we find that the children are estranged from their father. Even if one would construe the divorce agreement or assess the father’s expectation that child support would not be forthcoming if his children do not visit him, we find that the children’s best interests dictate that they may choose not to spend time with their father. We hereby direct the <i>Tove’a</i> to continue to furnish child support until the children respectively reach the age of eighteen.
If circumstances change in the future, this panel will revisit this ­matter.
<b>Decision</b>
1. Pursuant to the terms of the divorce agreement, the <i>Tove’a</i> is hereby obligated to pay child support to his children until they reach the age of 18.
2. The children may choose not to visit their father.
3. Any request for modification of this award by the arbitration panel shall be submitted to this panel.

Chapter 5; Decisions in Hoshen Mishpat

a) Tenure Rights and Severance Pay

<b><i>A. Tenure Rights and Severance Pay</i></b><br><b><i>Avraham Cohen v. Yeshiva Har Tzvi</i></b>
In 1995, Yeshiva Har Tzvi hired R. Avraham Cohen as a third grade rebbe commencing with the school year of 1995–1996. He continued to serve in that capacity until the non-renewal of his contract at the end of the school year 2008–2009. Prior to his dismissal, the <i>Tove’a</i> stole funds from the <i>yeshiva</i>. Within two months of his non-renewal, R. Cohen summoned the <i>yeshiva</i> to <i>beit din</i>. In September 2009, this panel was convened to hear this case and a <i>psak din</i> was issued in January 2010.
<b><i>Tove’a</i>’s Claims</b>
The <i>Tove’a</i> admits that he stole funds from the <i>yeshiva</i> but argues that the non-renewal of his contract is a violation of both Halakhah and <i>minhag</i> (common practice). Pursuant to the rulings of <i>R. Feinstein</i>, <i>R. Shlomo Z. Auerbach</i>, and <i>R. Y. Weiss</i>, the <i>Tove’a</i> claims that a teacher’s contract for one year must be renewed, and if the <i>yeshiva</i> feels that there are grounds for non-renewal of the contract, the <i>yeshiva</i> must allow a <i>beit din</i> to serve as the final arbiter regarding the matter. Moreover, he argues, cases of dismissal that received rabbinical sanction are limited to employers who were acting in an individual capacity, rather than as communal representatives. Consequently, as <i>R. Feinstein</i> notes,<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Iggerot Moshe,</i> HM 2:34.</i> the <i>Tove’a</i> is entitled to receive his salary from the period of the non-renewal of his contract until the <i>beit din</i>’s ruling that the termination was justified.
The <i>Tove’a</i> argues that even if one were to contend that the common practice is not to renew teachers’ contracts, Halakhah would not validate this <i>minhag</i>, even though it relates to monetary affairs (<i>dinei mamonot</i>), because it is neither endorsed by a rabbinic authority nor based upon an ordinance approved by the leaders of the community. Moreover, whereas the <i>yeshiva</i> argues that a teacher can be requested to leave “at-will,” according to <i>R. Feinstein</i>, the <i>halakhah</i> that one cannot fail to renew a labor contract or terminate without cause cannot be overridden by a <i>minhag</i> that provides otherwise.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Arukh ha-Shulhan,</i> HM 331:5; <i>Iggerot Moshe</i> 1:72, 75. In effect, R. Feinstein construes this <i>halakhah</i> as an “<i>issur.</i>” Since a <i>minhag</i> cannot nullify an <i>issur</i> (as per <i>Rosh ha-Shanah</i> 15b), an employee cannot be terminated without cause.</i>
Third, the <i>Tove’a</i> argues that the appointment of a <i>melamed</i> (teacher) is a sacred appointment (<i>minui shel kedushah</i>), and he therefore cannot be dismissed except in cases of “<i>peshi’a</i>” (gross negligence).
Although the <i>Tove’a</i> admits that his employment agreement explicitly stipulates that he is not entitled to any post-employment benefits, including severance pay,<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">Severance is a monetary allowance paid by the employer to his employees, generally upon permanent termination of employment or non-renewal of a contract. These payments may be either distributed as a lump sum or as a series of periodic payments. The amount of remuneration is dependent upon the length of employment of the worker.</i> he argues that he originally agreed to waive these benefits because he was financially comfortable at the time. Since the <i>Tove’a</i> lost the majority of his assets in the 2008 market meltdown, however, he is now in dire need of these resources. Furthermore, since the <i>yeshiva</i> provided severance pay for six dismissed rebbes in the past, a <i>minhag</i> has been established and ought to be grounds for awarding severance, overriding the provisions of the employment agreement. Finally, the <i>Tove’a</i> argues that the <i>yeshiva</i> should recognize his unstinting dedication and therefore provide him with severance benefits. His act of thievery was unrelated to his educational contributions to the <i>yeshiva</i> and ought not nullify his right to receive severance pay.
<b><i>Nitva</i>’s Counterclaims</b>
The <i>Nitva</i> argues that the <i>Tove’a</i> was an “at-will employee,” and as such was not contracted for life. Towards the end of every school year, the <i>Tove’a</i>, like all other faculty members of the <i>yeshiva</i>, received a contract for the forthcoming school year. In total, the <i>yeshiva</i> and <i>Tove’a</i> signed ten employment contracts. Consequently, at the end of any given year, the <i>Tove’a</i> had the right to decide whether to renew his contract for the upcoming year. When the facts indicated that the <i>Tove’a</i> stole funds from the <i>yeshiva</i> during the 2008–2009 school year, the <i>Nitva</i> notified the <i>Tove’a</i> in a timely fashion that the <i>yeshiva</i> would decline to offer him a contract for the 2009–2010 school year.
Finally, the <i>Nitva</i> argues that since the <i>Tove’a</i> only became aware that other teachers had received severance packages after the <i>yeshiva</i>’s notification of dismissal and given the circumstances surrounding his dismissal, the <i>Nitva</i> should not be entitled to such benefits. Moreover, the employment agreement precludes the awarding of severance, and as such the contract should override the <i>minhag</i> of the few instances in which severance was provided to others. Finally, the <i>Tove’a</i>’s present economic situation should not affect the question of whether he is entitled to a severance package.
<b>Discussion</b>
The <i>beit din</i> must address two questions. First, is the <i>Tove’a</i>’s employment considered a tenured position, in which case termination or non-renewal of a contract requires prior <i>beit din</i> authorization? Second, is the <i>Tove’a</i> entitled to severance benefits?
<b>1. The Tenured Position</b>
We will begin by addressing the question of tenure and the role of <i>beit din</i> in cases of employment termination or non-renewal of a contract. According to <i>R. Feinstein</i>, a teacher – like any other type of employee – retains his position for life, subject only to removal for cause.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Iggerot Moshe</i>, HM 1:75–8; 2:34.</i> This ruling applies even when the <i>yeshiva</i> and teacher agreed to a fixed term of employment in the contract. In effect, a term contract conveys the notion that the employee is not “an indentured servant”; it is not a device empowering an employer to dismiss his worker at the expiration of the term.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Teshuvot Hatam Sofer</i>, OH 205–206.</i>
<i>R. Feinstein</i> suggests that if the agreement provides that a teacher’s contract may not be renewed even in the absence of just cause, the provision may be invalid, as such action would be contrary to the halakhic rule that a teacher may only be dismissed with good reason. Moreover, prior to dismissal of an employee, a <i>beit din</i> must determine that there are grounds for termination or non-renewal of a contract. Until such <i>beit din</i> determination, R. Feinstein contends that the employer is obligated to pay the worker’s salary.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Iggerot Moshe,</i> HM 1:77.</i>
Notwithstanding the view of <i>R. Feinstein</i>, the consensus of most decisors recognizes a <i>yeshiva</i>’s right to refuse to offer a new contract, without any requisite need for a <i>beit din</i>’s approval.<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">Some authorities invoke the need for <i>beit din</i> determination prior to termination in limited circumstances, such as an employee who is suspected of being a thief or a pedophile. See <i>Teshuvot Lehem Rav</i> 4; <i>Teshuvot Teshurat Shai</i> 1:603; <i>Teshuvot Hikekei Lev</i>, vol. 1, OH 47; <i>Dinei Mamonot</i> 3:34; <i>Teshuvot Divrei Malkiel</i> 3:152–153; <i>Pithei Teshuvah</i>, HM 232:5. Others argue that absent <i>beit din</i> determination, the evidence must demonstrate that he is a thief prior to termination. See <i>Ra’avad</i> and <i>Rashba</i>, <i>Shita Mekubetzet</i>, <i>Bava Kama</i> 28a; <i>Meiri</i>, <i>Bava Kama</i> 28a; <i>Teshuvot Shevut Ya’akov</i> 1:174; <i>Erekh Shai</i>, HM 421; <i>Teshuvot Ohel Yitzhak</i>, HM 53.</i> In fact, it is difficult to find statements in support of <i>R. Feinstein</i>’s position in the Talmud, codes, and <i>teshuvot</i> literature.<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Bava Metzia</i> 109a; <i>Mishnah Torah</i>, <i>Hilkhot Sekhirut</i> 10:7; <i>Ra’avad</i>, ad loc.; <i>Tur</i>, YD 245:17–18 and HM 306:8; <i>SA</i>, HM 306:8, 333:2; <i>Rema</i>, HM 421:6; <i>Teshuvot Asher le-Shlomo</i>, no. 63; <i>Milei de-Nezikin</i> (in the name of R. Moshe Stern), 50–51,133; <i>Teshuvot Divrei Malkiel</i> 3:151; <i>Teshuvot Maharsham</i> 2:132.</i> <i>Rema</i> explicitly states:
<span class="poetry indentAll"><small>If someone has a maidservant and he is suspicious that she will steal from him, he may dismiss her prior to the expiration date of her employment. <sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Rema</i>, HM 421:6.</i></small></span>
A fortiori, in our scenario, in which the <i>Tove’a</i> already had stolen from the <i>Nitva</i>, the <i>Nitva</i> was authorized to refuse to renew a contract with the <i>Tove’a</i> without the prior approval of <i>beit din</i>.
There is certainly precedent for rabbinically sanctioned dismissal of teachers by communal institution without recourse to a <i>beit din</i> judgment.<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Torat Moshe</i> 24; <i>Teshuvot Maharashdam</i>, YD 141; <i>Teshuvot Ohel Yosef</i>, <i>Sekhirut</i> 4; <i>PDR</i> 14:101.</i> In legitimating an employer’s right to terminate a teacher for inappropriate behavior, <i>R. Ya’akov Emden</i> invokes the rule of “<i>avid inish dina le-nafshei</i>,” taking the Halakhah into one’s own hands.<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Mishnat Ya’avetz</i> 2:38.</i> Under certain circumstances, to prevent potential financial damage, an individual is empowered with the authority of a <i>beit din</i> to judge his case for himself.<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">Regarding viewing the plaintiff as assuming the role of <i>beit din</i>, see <i>Dibberot Moshe</i>, <i>Bava Kama</i> 18.</i> In <i>R. Emden</i>’s view, this empowerment equally extends to an employer’s right to dismiss a teacher in cases of negligence. A similar conclusion is arrived at by <i>Hazon Ish</i> concerning the right to demand that an employee leave his work upon the expiration date of the contract.<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Hazon Ish</i>, <i>Bava Kama</i> 23:2.</i>
The <i>Tove’a</i> further alludes to the rulings of <i>R. Auerbach</i> and <i>R. Weiss</i>, which mandate recourse to a <i>beit din</i> prior to termination, but those rulings are limited to cases involving a teacher’s right to strike<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Minhat Shlomo</i> 1:87.</i> and conflicts between a principal and teacher regarding differing educational philosophies.<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Minhat Yitzhak</i> 4:75:12.</i> Hence, these rulings are inapplicable to our situation of alleged malfeasance.
Halakhah recognizes the ability of parties to enter into contractual agreements, including labor contracts.<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote"><i>SA</i>, EH 38:5, HM 207:1; 241:9; 291:17; 305:4; <i>Rema</i>, HM 207:1.</i> At the expiration of the term of a contract, both parties have free rein regarding whether to renew.<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Piskei ha-Rosh</i>, <i>Bava Batra</i> 21a; <i>Teshuvot Rashbash</i> 112; <i>Teshuvot Yaskil Avdi</i>, vol. 4, YD 18; <i>PDR</i> 3:91; 8:129, 143–144.</i>
The question of whether employment generates tenure rights for the employee is governed by <i>minhag ha-medinah</i>, the custom of the region.<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote"><i>PDR</i> 18:94; <i>Pithei Hoshen</i>, <i>Sekhirut</i> 10:11, n. 35. Pursuant to <i>Hazon Ish</i>, <i>Bava Kama</i>, no. 23, if the prevailing practice is that employment is a tenured position, then one can only dismiss such an employee for cause; see <i>PDR</i> 3:91, 93. Whether a congregational rabbi who receives a contract with a fixed-term is subject to dismissal without just cause after the expiration of the term is a question that is beyond the scope of this presentation.</i> Thus, absent a provision in the contract to the contrary, if a particular community construes a labor contract as generating employee tenure rights, then the employee cannot be terminated. On the other hand, absent a provision in the contract to the contrary, if another community views him as “an at-will employee,” then the employer can terminate him at-will or after the expiration date of the contract. The customary practice of a particular community thus will determine whether an employee enjoys tenure or not.
To have binding force, the <i>minhag</i> must be clear, widespread in the particular locale, and have been followed at least three times.<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot ha-Rosh</i> 79:4; <i>Teshuvot ha-Rivash</i> 475; <i>Teshuvot Terumat ha-Deshen</i> 342; <i>Rema</i>, HM 331:1.</i> Although there is a long-standing halakhic dispute regarding whether a monetary custom practiced by the community on their own has any authority or whether a custom requires endorsement by rabbinical authority or approval of communal leaders, <i>Shulhan Arukh</i>, <i>Rema</i>, and others have either explicitly or implicitly ruled that a monetary custom has independent status, even if this result in extraction of money from <i>muhzakim</i> (those in present possession of the assets).<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote"><i>SA</i>, HM 176:10, 218:19, 229:2, 230:10, 232:6, 330:5, 331:12; <i>Teshuvot ha-Rema</i> 19-20; <i>Rema</i>, HM 72:5.</i>
The common practice of the <i>Nitva</i> and the other local <i>yeshiva</i>s is to offer one-year renewable contracts to its teachers. This <i>minhag</i> is valid, even if unaccompanied by rabbinic or communal sanction, either because the <i>Tove’a</i> is viewed as an at-will employee or pursuant to the normative position reflected in the rulings of <i>Shulhan Arukh</i> and <i>Rema.</i> In effect, today, a <i>melamed</i> is appointed pursuant to the provisions of <i>minhag ha-medinah</i>, similar to any other employee, rather than being viewed as a “<i>minui shel kedushah</i>” (a sacred appointment).<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Pithei Hoshen</i>, <i>Sekhirut</i>, p. 260, n. 35. Others do view a <i>melamed</i> as a “<i>minui shel kedushah</i>”; see <i>Tosafot</i>, <i>Yoma</i> 12b, s.v. <i>halakhah</i>; <i>Rambam</i>, <i>Perush ha-Mishnah</i>, <i>Menahot</i> 13:10; <i>Teshuvot ha-Rambam</i> (Blau edition) 1:111; <i>Sefer ha-Mitzvot</i>, negative command 316; <i>Teshuvot ha-Mabit</i>, 3:200; <i>Rema</i>, YD 245:22; <i>Teshuvot Shlomo le-Beit ha-Levi</i>, YD 8; <i>Teshuvot Ya’akov le-Beit ha-Levi</i> 5:50; <i>Kerem Shlomo</i>, YD 50; <i>Teshuvot Hikrei Lev</i>, OC 50, YD 3:100; <i>Teshuvot Avnei Nezer</i> 312:34; <i>Orhot ha-Mishpatim</i> 4:10–11. For an example of a halakhic justification for termination, see Yosef Yedid Halevi, <i>Kuntres u-le-Levi Amar</i> (Jerusalem: 5753).<br>Others contend that a rabbinic post is an example of <i>serarah</i> (communal authority). Since a communal position can be inherited by a member of his family, the right of inheritance affirms that a rabbi holds a tenured position. See <i>Mishnah Torah</i>, <i>Hilkhot Kelei ha-Mikdash</i> 4:20, <i>Hilkhot Melakhim</i> 1:7; <i>Teshuvot ha-Rivash</i> 271; <i>PDR</i> 10:38–60.</i>
Upon review of the evidence presented by the parties and the applicable Halakhah, the <i>beit din</i> accepts the <i>Nitva</i>’s position that the <i>Tove’a</i>’s employment was term employment, as recorded in writing. As such, the <i>Nitva</i> was empowered to dismiss the <i>Tove’a</i> without receiving initial approval from a <i>beit din</i>.
<b>2. Entitlement to Severance Pay</b>
We will now turn our attention to the question of whether the <i>Tove’a</i> is entitled to a severance package.
As is the customary practice in contemporary <i>batei din</i>, both parties to this hearing signed an arbitration agreement (<i>shtar borerut</i>) empowering this panel to resolve their dispute.<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Rema</i>, HM 12:7; <i>Sma</i>, HM 12:18.</i> One of the terms of the agreement is that the <i>beit din</i> has the authority to resolve the matter according to <i>din</i> (Halakhah) or through <i>pesharah kerovah le-din</i> (court-ordered settlement in accordance with Halakhah). Our discussion of the awarding of severance will be addressed on these two functionally and conceptually different planes, in addition to the plane of <i>lifnim meshurat ha-din</i>, a sub-category of <i>pesharah</i> that entails going beyond the demands of Halakhah.<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ramban</i>, <i>Commentary on the Torah</i>, <i>Devarim</i> 6:18; <i>Teshuvot Divrei Malkiel</i> 2:132; <i>Teshuvot Giv’at Pinhas</i> 68. For an approach towards understandin this notion, see Lichtenstein, infra n. 38.</i>
The right of an employee to receive compensation upon dismissal is not mentioned in the Torah or Talmud, nor in the overwhelming majority of post-Talmudic sources. <i>Sefer ha-Hinukh</i> finds moral basis for such remuneration based on analogy to the laws of an <i>eved ivri</i>, a Jew bereft of funds and in need of gainful employment who is indentured to work for six years.<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Tosafot</i>, <i>Kiddushin</i> 16a. Cf. <i>Mishnah Torah</i>, <i>Hilkhot Avadim</i> 3:12, who contends that there is no duty to give a gratuitous gift to an <i>eved ivri</i>. As a result, some authorities refuse to invoke the <i>halakhah</i> of <i>ha’anakah</i> as grounds for awarding severance; see <i>Teshuvot Yam ha-Gadol</i> 22.</i> Just as an <i>eved ivri</i> receives a parting, albeit gratuitous gift – “<i>ha’anakah</i>” – from his employer upon the completion of six years,<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Devarim</i> 15:12–14.</i> a dismissed laborer should receive a severance package:
<span class="poetry indentAll"><small>We shall show compassion to someone who has worked for us and give him from assets indicative of our kindness above and beyond his salary.<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sefer ha-Hinukh</i>, mitzvah 450 (Chavel edition). This position was endorsed by <i>Ri</i>, <i>Kiddushin</i> 15a, s.v. <i>ve’idach</i> and <i>Teshuvot Even ha-Shoham</i>, HM 120 and attributed to R. Moshe Feinstein. See the letter dated 15th of Adar 5763 from R. Dovid Feinstein to R. Friedman (a copy of the letter is on file with author).</i></small></span>
Following in the footsteps of <i>Sefer ha-Hinukh</i>, numerous <i>Poskim</i> endorse the view that the giving of severance is a halakhic-moral obligation rather than a halakhic-legal duty,<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">For the interaction between a halakhic-legal obligations and halakhic-moral obligations, see my “The Theory of Efficient Breach: A Jewish Law Perspective,” in A. Levine (ed<i>.</i>), <i>Oxford Handbook on Judaism and Economics</i> (NY: 2010), 340, 344–48.</i> and as such is unenforceable by a <i>beit din</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Shakh,</i> HM 86:3; <i>Sma</i>, HM 86:2; <i>Tumim,</i> HM 86:3. Cf. <i>Giddulei Terumah</i>, <i>sha’ar</i> 51, 1:5; <i>Mishnah le-Melekh</i>, <i>Hilkhot Avadim</i> 3:14; <i>PDR</i> 3:287.</i> The grounds for this award are either understood as compensation for work beyond the call of duty or as a gift given out of kindness.<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Beit ha-Behirah</i>, <i>Kiddushin</i> 15a; <i>Sefer ha-Hinukh</i>, supra n. 26; <i>Shakh</i> and <i>Sma</i>, supra n. 28.</i> According to the view that severance is a halakhic-moral duty, this panel would be unable to coerce the <i>Nitva</i> to pay severance to the <i>Tove’a</i> according to the strict <i>din</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">If the <i>beit din</i> chose to resolve this matter based on <i>pesharah kerovah la-din</i>, a court-ordered settlement in accordance with Halakhah, it could have mandated that the parties comply with their halakhic moral obligations as well; see <i>Teshuvot Mahari Bruna</i> 241; R. Zalman N. Goldberg, <i>Shivhei ha-Pesharah</i>, sec. 5 (letter to Kollel Mishpetei Aretz, Ofra, Israel). Nonetheless, in this situation, we refrained from invoking <i>pesharah kerovah la-din.</i></i>
Israeli rabbinical courts have preferred to base the duty to pay severance upon the existence of <i>minhag</i> to do so. Accordingly, whether an employee would be entitled to severance pay would depend on whether this is the practice in his community, his profession, and/or in his place of employment.<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote"><i>PDR</i> 1:330, 331; 3:92–94, 272, 287; 4:127–129, 8:78–81. See also <i>Teshuvot Tzitz Eliezer</i> 7:48:10.</i> As we explained above, for a custom to be binding upon the employer, it must be clear that the compensation awarded to the worker was severance and that it is common practice. Even though the <i>Tove’a</i> only became aware of the <i>yeshiva</i>’s custom to award severance after the <i>yeshiva</i>’s notification of non-renewal of his contract, the halakhic power of the <i>minhag</i>, independent of the parties’ knowledge of its existence, establishes the employer duty’s to award compensation and the employee’s right to receive it.<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Dinei Mamonot</i>, vol. 2, 315; <i>Pithei Hoshen</i>, <i>Sekhirut</i>, p. 149, n. 18. Some ground the <i>minhag</i> upon the mitzvah of “<i>ha’anakah</i>”; see <i>PDR</i> 3:272, 287; 4:126, 129. Cf. <i>Shakh</i>, HM 42:36.</i>
However, in our scenario, the parties originally agreed that severance would not be awarded upon termination or non-renewal of the contract. In monetary matters, parties are permitted to design their own business relationship, provided that the arrangement complies with a proper form (for example, involving a <i>kinyan</i>) and is not a violation of any prohibitions, such as theft or the prohibition of taking interest.<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Kiddushin</i> 19b; <i>Beit Yosef</i>, HM 305:4; <i>Rema</i>, HM 344:1.</i> Consequently, the parties’ agreement is valid and binding, even if it is in conflict with a <i>minhag</i> that mandates the award.<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote"><i>SA</i> and <i>Rema</i>, HM 331:1; <i>Hiddushei ha-Ga’on Rabbi Akiva Eiger</i>, <i>Bava Metzia</i> 83a; <i>PDR</i> 8:7; Yisrael Yifrach, “A Labor Agreement in Variance with Local Custom” [Hebrew], 3 <i>Shurat ha-Din</i> (5758), 278. For dissenting views, see <i>Radakh</i>, <i>Shitah Mekubezet</i>, <i>Bava Kama</i> 83a, s.v. <i>ha-soher</i> and Avraham Sherman, “An Employee Who Waived His Rights” [Hebrew], 18 <i>Tehumin</i> (5768), 242.</i> Thus, the fact that the <i>yeshiva</i> and other local schools have awarded severance pay in other instances is irrelevant, and according to the <i>din</i>, the <i>Tove’a</i> is not entitled to severance pay.
One of the primary objectives of compromise is to promote <i>shalom</i>, peace between litigious parties.<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sanhedrin</i> 6b (R. Yehoshua ben Korha’<i>s</i> dictum); <i>Teshuvot Shevut Ya’akov</i> 2:145.</i> The need to foster compromise for the sake of peace equally applies in communal matters, such as our case, and in particular regarding labor relations.<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Meishiv Davar</i> 2:10; <i>Teshuvot Ha’amek Davar</i> 3:10.</i> Nevertheless, Halakhah proscribes implementing a compromise for purposes of promoting peace if there is no justification for a <i>Tove’a</i>’s claim. As this is the case in the situation at hand, <i>pesharah kerovah la-din</i> does not mandate that the <i>Nitva</i>’s funds be transferred to the <i>Tove’a</i>, even for the sake of fostering peace. These assets belong to the <i>Nitva</i> and therefore rightfully ought to remain in his possession.<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">Yoezer Ariel, <i>Dinnei Borerut</i> (Jerusalem), 170–1, 223.</i>
Does the <i>Nitva</i> have any obligation to act in this circumstance <i>lifnim me-shurat ha-din</i>, more generously than the <i>din</i> and <i>pesharah</i> would mandate?<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Bava Metzia</i> 30b, 83a. As R. Aharon Lichtenstein observes, this sphere of halakhic decision-making operates within the realm of “contextual morality.” See his article, “Does Jewish Tradition Recognize an Ethic Independent of Halakhah?,” in Martin Fox (ed.), <i>Modern Jewish Ethics: Theory &amp; Practice</i> (Ohio: 1975), 62, 78.</i> According to many decisors, a <i>beit din</i> can compel an individual to act more generously than the <i>din</i> requires,<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Rema</i>, HM 12:2; <i>Teshuvot ha-Rema</i> 22; <i>Pithei Teshuvah</i>, HM 12:6; <i>Bah</i>, HM 12:4; <i>Shakh</i>, HM 259:3, <i>Taz</i>, HM 259:3; <i>Taz</i>, OH 343:2; <i>Teshuvot Shevut Ya’akov</i> 1: 168; <i>Teshuvot Hatam Sofer</i>, YD 239.</i> even if this would entail financial expense on his part.<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Kenneset ha-Gedolah</i>, HM 12, <i>haga’ot Beit Yosef</i>, n. 9; <i>Gra</i>, <i>Mishlei</i> 2:20; <i>Teshuvot Maharsham</i> 7:191; R. Ben Zion Uziel, cited in M. Findling, <i>Tehukat ha-Avodah</i> (Jerusalem: 1945), 133.</i> The obligation upon a <i>beit din</i> to resolve a case based upon the principle of <i>lifnim me-shurat ha-din</i> applies equally when one of the parties is a communal institution. <i>R. Y. Weiss</i> discusses a case in which an Argentinean Jewish butcher owed a tremendous sum of money to the local Jewish community. Was the community authorized to issue injunctive relief in the form of seizing some of the butcher’s machinery, such as a refrigerator, electric meat carver, and scales, for purposes of satisfying the outstanding debt? Although the community was impoverished, <i>R. Weiss</i> concluded that they should act <i>lifnim me-shurat ha-din</i>, invoking the concept of “the community is not poor.”<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Minhat Yitzhak</i> 5:121. See Yerushalmi<i>, Gittin</i> 3:7; R. Shmuel ha-Sefardi, <i>Commentary on Horayot</i> 10b. See also Y. Herzog, <i>Pesakim u-Ketavim</i>, vol. 9; <i>Teshuvot</i>, HM 88. For these and other sources, see R. Kleinman, “The Community is Not Poor” [Hebrew], 20 <i>Sidra</i> (5765), 195.</i> This principle applies even if the communal institution in question will incur a financial loss.<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Binyan Av</i> 1:38, sec. 3, in the name of R. Shmuel Salant.</i>
Other authorities have argued that a public institution must conduct itself <i>lifnim me-shurat ha-din</i> due to its status as an <i>adam hashuv</i>, a distinguished individual. In the words of one rabbinical court:
<span class="poetry indentAll"><small>[A communal institution] has a greater duty to appease complaints and arguments … and it is duty bound to deflect criticism from its institution …<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">Jerusalem Supreme Court of Appeals, Z. Warhaftig (ed.), <i>Osaf Piskei Hadin</i> 1:110, 114. See also <i>Shakh</i>, HM 420:9; <i>Teshuvot Ahiezer</i> 4:68; <i>Teshuvot Mishnah Sakhir</i>, HM 4.</i></small></span>
In effect, invoking considerations of <i>lifnim me-shurat ha-din</i> upon a communal institution, based either on the concept of “the community is not poor” or its status as an <i>adam hashuv</i>, would create a new line of liability, leading to a result different from what would have occurred under the rubric of <i>din</i>.
The question remains, however, if there are grounds for invoking <i>lifnim me-shurat ha-din</i> in the present context of awarding severance pay in general and the economic situation of the <i>Tove’a</i> in particular. The financial situation of either of the parties is entirely irrelevant in terms of <i>din</i> and <i>pesharah</i>; in fact, we are explicitly and repeatedly exhorted to ignore such factors in resolving a dispute.<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Vayikra</i> 19:15; <i>Ketuvot</i> 84a; <i>SA</i>, HM 17:10.</i> On the level of <i>lifnim me-shurat ha-din</i>, however, the <i>Tove’a</i>’s financial situation may be taken into account. For example, <i>R. Hayyim ben Baer Rapaport</i> (nineteenth century Russia) ordered a community to pay compensation to a dismissed <i>shohet</i> (ritual slaughterer) due to the fact that he was destitute with dependents.<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Mayyim Hayyim</i>, OH 6, cited by <i>Pithei Teshuvah</i>, HM 333:3.</i> A thirteenth century <i>teshuvah</i> states:
<span class="poetry indentAll"><small>Since Reuven is economically limited, it is proper for the community to act <i>lifnim me-shurat ha-din</i>. <sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Maharach Or Zaru’a</i> 222, by A. Weiss, <i>Kovetz Darkhei Horo’ah</i>, vol. 5, 97.</i></small></span>
At first glance, it would seem plausible to argue that the <i>Nitva</i> in this case should be viewed as a communal institution, which is halakhically considered to have “deep pockets” and should therefore be compelled to dispense the severance package to the <i>Tove’a</i>, especially given his financial state. However, in considering resolution according to <i>lifnim me-shurat ha-din</i>, the <i>beit din</i> must take all surrounding circumstances into consideration. This panel concludes that the <i>Tove’a</i>’s act of thievery of the <i>Nitva</i>’s assets precludes the issuance of such a mandate.
<b>Decision</b>
1. The <i>Nitva</i>, as a communal educational institution, had the authority to terminate a teacher’s employment at the expiration of the term of the contract without first receiving a definitive ruling of a <i>beit din</i> that there are halakhically acceptable grounds for dismissal. Hence, we may choose to refrain from adjudicating whether the grounds for dismissal were justifiable or not.
2. Nevertheless, we have decided to address the merits of the <i>yeshiva</i>’s failure to renew his contract. Upon review of the evidence presented by the parties, the <i>beit din</i> recognizes the talents of R. Cohen as an educator, which are attested to by the Yeshiva Har Tzvi administration. His educational credentials and pedagogical achievements notwithstanding, however, a teacher must be simultaneously aware of the Torah values espoused by the <i>yeshiva</i> in which he teaches, as well as the values promoted in the homes of his students. The <i>Tove’a</i>’s improper behavior relating to theft of the <i>yeshiva</i>’s assets undermines the <i>yeshiva</i>’s mission and subverts his ability to serve as a halakhic-ethical model for the students to emulate.
Given the facts of the case, we find that the <i>Tove’a</i>’s removal by the <i>Nitva</i> due to his malfeasance was justifiable. Consequently, there are neither grounds for reinstatement, nor for reimbursement for “lost wages” accrued between his termination date and convening this <i>beit din</i> hearing. Furthermore, there is no basis for the awarding of severance.
3. In summary, all of the claims of the <i>Tove’a</i> are hereby dismissed.
The obligations set forth herein shall be enforceable in any court of competent jurisdiction, in accordance with the rules and procedures of the arbitration agreement.
<b>Final Thoughts</b>
Some contemporary <i>dayanim</i> refrain from awarding severance pay to any employee, absent an explicit provision in the labor agreement, as such an entitlement is not mentioned in the Talmud or codes. When severance pay is awarded, contemporary decisors invoke at least two grounds for doing so.<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>PDR</i> 1:330–332; 4:127–129; <i>Teshuvot Minhat Yitzhak</i> 6:167; <i>Teshuvot Tzitz Eliezer</i>, 7:48:10; <i>Teshuvot ve-Hanhagot</i> 3:473.</i> Nevertheless, I am aware of a number of <i>dayanim</i> who have awarded severance pay to teachers even when the <i>minhag</i> of the particular <i>yeshiva</i> or community was not to do so. Such an award is in contravention of Halakhah, which dictates that such an award is forthcoming only as a form of “<i>ha’anakah</i>” based on the common practice or as a form of <i>lifnim me-shurat ha-din.</i>
Should severance be awarded, the amount is based either on the practice of the particular economic sector or up to the <i>beit din</i>’s discretion. The Israeli Rabbinical Courts have adopted a formula of 8.333% of the employee’s salary per year of employment (<i>hodesh le-shanah</i>).<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>PDR</i> 1:332; 3:272, 287; 4:126; 8:162; <i>Teshuvot Be-Tzel ha-Hokhmah</i> 3:100; <i>Teshuvot Minhat Yitzhak</i> 6:167; <i>Yam ha-Gadol</i>, supra n. 24.</i> In the absence of a prevailing practice in a particular economic sector, in the institution wherein the individual is employed, or in the local community, <i>dayanim</i> in the New York metropolitan area who endorse the propriety of rendering such an award will also grant severance based upon the formula of <i>hodesh le-shanah</i>.

b) The Status of a Promissory Note

<b><i>B. The Status of a Promissory Note</i></b><br><b><i>Ya’akov Silverstein v. Shimon Cohen</i></b>
On or about February 22, 2006, Shimon Cohen issued to Ya’akov Silverman (<i>Tove’a</i>) a promissory note dated May 22, 2008 in the principal amount of $1,000,000. The maturity date was May 22, 2009. As of May 19, 2010, the business loan remained unpaid. The note provides for interest at the rate of 10% per annum (prior to default) and 12% per annum and liquidated damages of $75,000 after default.
Finally, for a half year, the <i>Nitva</i> was employed by the <i>Tove’a</i> and to date he is owed $25,000 salary.
Both the <i>Tove’a</i> and the <i>Nitva</i> are observant Jews. There is no clause in the original agreement addressing whether Halakhah or secular law should resolve any differences between the parties or ambiguities in the note’s provisions. The <i>Tove’a</i> has requested that the decision be grounded in secular law, while the <i>Nitva</i> has requested that Halakhah serve as the basis for our decision.
<b><i>Tove’a</i>’s Claims</b>
The <i>Tove’a</i> claims that he is entitled to the principal amount of the note, as well as the accrued interest of 10% per annum (prior to default) and 12% per annum after default. Since the note was not repaid on its maturity date, the note has been in default since its maturity date. The <i>Tove’a</i> argues that he was entitled to demand interest (<i>ribbit</i>) on the principal because the funds were originally advanced as investment money, rather than a loan.
In an e-mail sent by the <i>Nitva</i> to the <i>Tove’a</i>, dated December 28, 2010, the <i>Nitva</i> writes that he hopes soon to “honor my commitment” to the <i>Tove’a</i> and reiterates that “I am sorry I have not been unable to pay you back yet.” On the basis of this communication and the fact that he signed the written promissory note, the <i>Tove’a</i> argues that the <i>Nitva</i> acknowledges the above commitments, and that to date he has been derelict in his duty of repayment.
Based upon the terms of the written promissory note and applicable civil law governing the oral promise, the <i>Tove’a</i> argues that he is entitled to payment of the principal and accrued interest. Moreover, given the default on the loan, the <i>Tove’a</i> is advancing a claim for liquidated damages (compensation for breach of the agreement) due to the loss of interest income.
<b><i>Nitva</i>’s Counterclaims</b>
The <i>Nitva</i> argues that a promissory note is only a promise, and according to Halakhah, only obligations are enforceable. Therefore, a <i>beit din</i> cannot obligate him to repay his loan. Moreover, the interest accruals are a violation of the interdict against taking <i>ribbit</i> (interest). Although the <i>Tove’a</i> admits that he voluntarily agreed to pay the interest, he argues that the <i>Tove’a</i> should be penalized for lending money accompanied by interest payments.
Furthermore, the <i>Nitva</i> argues that a default interest provision in the form of liquidated damages should be unenforceable under the Halakhah’s rules of obligation. For an obligation to be halakhically binding, there must be firm resolve to finalize an agreement (<i>gemirat da’at</i>). For example, if Reuven agrees to rent a field for the price of a portion of the crop he cultivates and he obligates himself to pay 1,000 <i>zuz</i>, an unreasonable amount, should the land remain fallow, no liability for this amount would ensue. Because he never expected to pay this amount, the agreement would be labeled an <i>asmakhta</i>, the absence of firm resolve, and his liability would be limited to the field’s potential productivity.<sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Bava Metzia</i> 104b; see Aaron Levine, <i>Economics and Jewish Law: Halakhic Perspectives</i> (NY: 1987), 195.</i> The <i>Nitva</i> argues that liquidated damages are uncertain and cannot be determined by any known rule, and there therefore cannot be <i>gemirat da’at</i> in any such agreement. Accordingly, the <i>Nitva</i> should be exempt from paying the liquidated damages.
Regarding the outstanding salary owed to him by the <i>Tove’a</i>, the <i>Nitva</i> is requesting his outstanding salary as well as interest due to the economic loss of the use of the funds.
<b>Discussion</b>
<b>1. The Application of Secular Law in a <i>Beit Din</i> Proceeding</b>
The first issue to be resolved is whether this matter will be adjudicated based upon Halakhah or secular law.
Seemingly, the proposal to resolve this matter pursuant to secular law is fraught with perilous ramifications. To suggest this posture seems to ignore the prohibition against recourse to <i>arka’ot shel akum</i>, litigating in non-Jewish courts. As <i>R. J. David Bleich</i> notes:
<span class="poetry indentAll"><small>Recourse to a gentile forum is tantamount to a declaration by the litigant that he is amenable to allowing an alien code of law to supersede the law of the Torah … The litigant appears before the gentile courts because he accepts their authority, and if they administer the law of Moses he accepts that law not because he regards it as binding upon him by virtue of having been commanded by God, but because it has been endorsed and adopted by gentiles …<br>The prohibition … is not limited to bringing a suit before a gentile court … The prohibition includes any judicial proceeding that negates the Law of Moses. A judicial body composed entirely of judges who happen to be members of the Jewish community but who administer an alien system of law is undoubtedly to be classified as within the halakhic category of <i>arka’ot shel akum</i> for the simple reason that the laws such a court administers are not those of the Torah.<br>Since … acquiescence by both litigants does not serve to mitigate the prohibition … and since the prohibition applies even when the judges themselves are Jews, it follows that the parties are not entitled to accept the authority of a rabbinic court … but stipulate that the <i>beit din</i> shall apply the law of a secular state. Accordingly, if two parties … enter into a contract and stipulate that any dispute with regard to fulfillment of the terms of the contract is to be resolved by a rabbinic court in accordance with, for example, the laws of the State of Delaware, the stipulation is void by virtue of being inconsistent with biblical law (<i>matneh al mah she-katuv ba-Torah</i>).<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote">J. David Bleich, “Litigation and Arbitration before Non-Jews,” 34 <i>Tradition</i> (2000), 58, 65–67. For an earlier treatment of this issue by the same author, see <i>Be-Netivot ha-Halakhah</i> [Hebrew] (NY: 1998), vol. 2, 170–1.</i></small></span>
As noted above, individuals are empowered to make agreements contrary to Halakhah provided that these agreements do not violate ritual law. An agreement to adjudicate in a <i>beit din</i> but according to secular law would thus seem to be invalid. Indeed, such arrangements have met with trenchant criticism from the <i>Aharonim</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Taz</i>, HM 26:3; <i>Bi’ur ha-Gra</i>, HM 61:23; <i>Teshuvot Teshurat Shai</i> 529.</i> and a number of contemporary <i>dayanim</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">See supra chap. 4 – Division of Marital Assets upon Divorce, nn. 48–50.</i>
Nevertheless, there is a coherent and persuasive halakhic tradition, commencing with <i>Rashba</i>, that supports the acceptance of this type of arrangement. According to these authorities, two individuals who enter into an agreement to resolve their financial matters according to civil law are merely waiving the privilege granted by the halakhic legal system in order to avail themselves of some benefit from the secular system in one particular situation. Since they are not indicating wholesale affirmation of the secular legal system, the agreement is binding.<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote">See ibid., nn. 51–54.</i>
Thus, there are grounds for the <i>Tove’a</i> to request that he benefit from the norms of secular law that relate to his claims provided that course of action is agreed to by the opposing party (accompanied by the execution of a <i>kinyan</i>). However, since that the <i>Nitva</i> insists that this matter be resolved according to the <i>halakhot</i> of <i>Hoshen Mishpat</i>, this matter will be adjudicated accordingly.
<b>2. The Promissory Note</b>
<b><i>a. The Binding Nature of the Note</i></b>
A promissory note, sometimes referred to as a “note payable,” is a contract in which one party (the “maker” or “issuer”) makes an unconditional promise in writing to pay a sum of money to the other party (the “payee”), either at a fixed or determinable future time or on demand of the payee, under specific terms.
The terms of a note usually include the principal amount, the interest rate if any, the parties, the date, the terms of repayment (which may include interest), and the maturity date. The provisions of the submitted promissory notes in this case include all of these elements.
As the <i>Nitva</i> notes, a promise is not enforceable in <i>beit din</i>, as it lacks the two critical components of a halakhic obligation – <i>kinyan</i> (a symbolic act of acquisition) and <i>gemirat da’at</i> (concrete articulation of the parties’ resolve to undertake the obligation). According to the <i>halakhot</i> of obligations (<i>hithayvut</i>), in order for a loan agreement (or any agreement) to be legally effective, the payee must undertake the obligation to repay the loan by declaring, for example, “I obligate to pay Reuven such and such,” “I undertake to pay Reuven such and such,” or “I admit that I am obligated to Reuven such and such.” Such language is <i>meshabed nafshei</i> – it “enslaves” the payee in the present by transforming him into a debtor.<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote"><i>SA</i>, HM 60:6; <i>Pithei Teshuvah</i>, HM 60:13; <i>Rema</i>, HM 60:3; <i>Teshuvot Maharshach</i> 3:173; <i>Teshuvot Maharashdam</i>, HM 29.</i> Since a promise entails executing an obligation in the future (for example, a promise to sell goods) or to transfer title of something that is not yet in existence (<i>davar she-lo ba la-olam</i>), it is legally unenforceable.<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Bava Batra</i> 3a; <i>Rashi</i>, ad loc., s.v. <i>kinyan devarim hu</i>; <i>Piskei ha-Rosh</i>, <i>Bava Batra</i> 1:3; <i>SA</i>, HM 245:1. Although a promise is not enforceable on the level of <i>din</i>, according to the strict dictates of the law, it constitutes a halakhic-moral obligation that can be enforced based upon the norms of <i>pesharah</i>. See <i>Maharsha</i>, <i>Sanhedrin</i> 6b; <i>Teshuvot Mahari Bruna</i> 241; Z. Goldberg, “<i>Shivhei ha-Pesharah</i>,” [Hebrew] 1 <i>Mishpetei Eretz</i> (5762), 78, 82.</i>
Despite the lack of a legal obligation to fulfill one’s promise, however, there remains a moral obligation to do so. Accordingly, <i>Shulhan Arukh</i> rules:
<span class="poetry indentAll"><small>When one conducts and concludes commercial transactions using words only [the negotiation and agreement not being completed by a formal act of acquisition], that person should stand by his word, even though none of the purchase price has been taken, nor a buyer’s mark made on the goods … Whoever withdraws from this type of transaction, whether buyer or seller, is deemed a faithless person.<sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote"><i>SA</i>, HM 204:7.</i></small></span>
<i>Rema</i> adds:
<span class="poetry indentAll"><small>A person should stand by his word, even though no act of acquisition has been performed, only mere words have passed between the parties …<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Rema</i>, HM 204:11.</i></small></span>
If a seller articulates an oral commitment to sell realty or other property to a prospective buyer, either party may technically withdraw from consummating the sale, as no <i>kinyan</i> has been executed between them; since enforceability depends upon a <i>kinyan</i>, a promise cannot be enforced by legal action. Nevertheless, should either party renege on the agreement, he is stigmatized as a <i>mehusar amana</i> (lacking faith). A promise is binding because of the halakhic need to keep one’s word, even if the <i>beit din</i> cannot enforce it. As I have articulated elsewhere:
<span class="poetry indentAll"><small>Halakhah views promise-keeping as aligning oneself with the fulfillment of a religious norm rather than compliance with a norm of natural law, institutional moral norm, or moral norm established by social convention.<sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote">See my “The Theory of Efficient Breach: A Jewish Law Perspective,” in A. Levine (ed.), <i>The Oxford Handbook on Judaism and Economics</i> (NY: 2010), 340, 344–6.</i></small></span>
In our case, the promissory note states, “I promise to pay.” Whereas American law would construe such a note as binding,<sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote">E. Allan Farnsworth, <i>Contracts</i> (Boston: 1982), 756, n. 12.</i> Halakhah would seem to disregard the possibility of enforcing such a promise, as it creates no obligation in the present but only one in the future. However, the fact that the money was transferred to the payee at the time that the promise was made transforms the payee into a debtor at the time the note was executed. By dint of the exchange of money, the promise to pay is transformed into an obligation to pay.<sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote"><i>PDR</i> 10:363, 377.</i>
Moreover, although this agreement did not involve a halakhic <i>kinyan</i>, a number of authorities have validated contracts (such as real estate, surety, and divorce agreements) based upon <i>kinyan situmta</i>, transferring an object according to commercial practice.<sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Hatam Sofer</i>, HM 66; <i>Teshuvot Ahiezer</i> 3:79, sec. 7; <i>Teshuvot Maharsham</i> 5:45; <i>Teshuvot Tzemach Tzedek</i> (Lubavitch), YD 233; <i>PDR</i> 6:202, 216; 9:16, 40; 16:133, 138.</i> Accordingly, a promissory note should be valid provided that it was drafted properly and enforceable according to secular law.<sup class="footnote-marker">62</sup><i class="footnote"><i>PDR</i> 12:279, 291; 18:108, 112.</i>
As such, the written promissory note in our case creates an obligation upon the payee to repay the principal to the issuer either due to the money transfer at the time of making the promise or due to the provisions of secular law.
<b><i>b. Payment of Interest during the Term of the Loan</i></b>
The prohibition against taking interest, <i>ribbit</i>, applies even when it was a provision of the original agreement (<i>ribbit ketzutzah</i>). Any interest provision that is structured to increase with time and payable prior to the maturity date of a note is a form of <i>ribbit</i>, and therefore cannot be collected,<sup class="footnote-marker">63</sup><i class="footnote"><i>SA</i>, YD 167:16.</i> regardless of whether the borrower is wealthy or not or if he is willing to pay the <i>ribbit</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">64</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Rema</i>, YD 160:1.</i>
Although the <i>Tove’a</i> claims that he is entitled to collect interest on the principal due to the fact that this sum was originally advanced as investment money rather than a loan, Halakhah mandates that once these monies were transformed from an investment to a loan, the parties are considered a lender and a borrower, and the halakhot of <i>ribbit</i> are therefore applicable.<sup class="footnote-marker">65</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Berit Yehuda</i> 35:6.</i> Accordingly, the <i>Tove’a</i> is not entitled to receive interest on his investment.
<b><i>c. The Validity of a Promissory Note with Ribbit Provisions</i></b>
Does the incorporation of <i>ribbit</i> provisions in the original agreement nullify the note entirely, thus denying the lender his right to collect the principal? In other words, does the halakhic illegality of one provision nullify the other provisions of the agreement, which are acceptable on their own?
The Talmud addresses this question directly:
<span class="poetry indentAll"><small>If a <i>shtar</i> (legal document) provides for <i>ribbit</i>, we penalize him and he cannot collect either principal or interest; this is the position of R. Meir. And the Sages state: He collects the principal, but not the interest.<sup class="footnote-marker">66</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Bava Metzia</i> 72a.</i></small></span>
According to the <i>Sages</i>, the stipulation of <i>ribbit</i> does not nullify the agreement, and the principal is therefore collectible. While <i>R. Meir</i> essentially concurs, he maintains that we penalize the lender for executing a document with interest provisions, and therefore deny him even the principal.<sup class="footnote-marker">67</sup><i class="footnote">According to most commentators, in the view of R. Meir, the loan is treated as an oral agreement and the lender may only recover his debt from the borrower’s unencumbered assets. See <i>Tosafot</i>, <i>Bava Metzia</i> 72a, s.v. <i>shtar</i>; <i>Ramban</i>, <i>Hiddushim</i>, <i>Bava Metzia</i> 72a, s.v. <i>shtar</i>. Cf. <i>Tosafot Ri</i>, <i>Bava Kama</i> 30b, s.v. <i>shtar</i>, who argues that no recovery is possible.</i> According to the rabbinic consensus, the view of the <i>Sages</i> is adopted by Halakhah.<sup class="footnote-marker">68</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Tur</i> and <i>SA</i>, HM 52:1, YD 161:11.</i> Thus, as long as the document clearly differentiates between the principal and the interest, the lender can collect the principal. Therefore, in our case, the incorporation of <i>ribbit</i> provisions in the note does not nullify the validity of the note, and the principal is collectible.
If the distinction between the principal and interest in the note is arguably unclear, the lender could collect the principal based on the borrower’s acknowledgment of the debt.<sup class="footnote-marker">69</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Rema</i>, HM 52:1; <i>Sma</i>, ad loc., sec. 2.</i> This method would be effective even according to <i>R. Meir</i>, who would ordinarily penalize the lender for the <i>ribbit</i> provision. Accordingly, in our case, the <i>Tove’a</i> may collect based upon the <i>Nitva</i>’s admission that he owes the money; there is no need to rely upon the note itself.
<b><i>d. Liquidated Damages Clause</i></b>
As noted above, Halakhah dictates that agreements are binding as long as the parties voluntarily consent to their terms and there is a firm resolve (<i>gemirat da’at</i>) to execute and comply with the terms of the agreement. Should a party have no expectation to pay the monies upon default, that part of the agreement is termed an “<i>asmakhta</i>,” the absence of firm resolve to comply with the clause.<sup class="footnote-marker">70</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Maharshal</i> 36; <i>Teshuvot Maharashdam</i>, HM 380; <i>Teshuvot ha-Ridbaz</i> 1:278, 452; <i>Teshuvot Tuv Tam va-Da’at</i>, <i>Mahadura Kama</i> 269. See also Berachyahu Lifshitz, <i>Asmakhta</i> [Hebrew], (Jerusalem: 5748); Itamar Warhaftig, <i>Ha-Hithayivut</i> [Hebrew], (Jerusalem, 5761).</i> For example, if the promisor obligates himself to pay more than the actual loss caused by him, the amount is considered a <i>guzma</i>, an exorbitant sum. According to many authorities, the promisor is undertaking to pay a penalty that he has no real intention to ever remit, and as such, the <i>asmakhta</i> is unenforceable in <i>beit din</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">71</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot ha-Rashba</i> 3:212; <i>Rashba</i> cited by <i>Shitah Mekubezet</i>, <i>Bava Batra</i> 168a; <i>Teshuvot ha-Rosh</i> 72:7; <i>Beit Yosef</i>, HM 207; <i>Rema</i>, HM 207:13; <i>Minhat Petim</i>, HM 32. <i>Beit Shmuel</i>, EH 50:14, notes that payment for <i>boshet</i> (embarrassment) that results as a breach of a <i>shiddukhin</i> agreement (a promise to halakhic engagement and marriage) may exceed the actual damage caused. Nevertheless, the amount cannot be exhorbitant; if it is, it is an <i>asmakhta</i> and unenforceable. See <i>SA</i>, HM 207:16; <i>Sema</i>, HM 207:47; <i>Beit Meir</i>, EH 50:6.</i>
In our case, is the provision for liquidated damages in the sum of $75,000 an example of <i>asmakhta</i>? One might argue that the default on the loan caused only indirect damages (<i>gerama</i>) to the lender, such as loss of interest income and profits from other business opportunities. Perhaps, then, the demand for such a sum is not justified and should be deemed an <i>asmakhta</i>. It may be further argued that since the agreement to pay for liquidated damages was not accompanied by a classical <i>kinyan</i> and was not written in the proper “language of obligation,”<sup class="footnote-marker">72</sup><i class="footnote"><i>SA</i>, HM 207:14; <i>SA</i> and <i>Rema</i>, HM 207:18; <i>Sma</i>, HM 243:12; and text accompanying notes 12–13 above.</i> nor was it executed in the presence of a <i>beit din</i> or any witnesses, the <i>Nitva</i>’s claim that he never intended to pay the liquidated damages is well-founded.<sup class="footnote-marker">73</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Mishnah Torah</i>, <i>Hilkhot Mekhirah</i> 11:7; <i>Tur</i>, HM 131:4; <i>SA</i>, HM 207:14; <i>Rema</i>, HM 207:14–15; <i>Sema</i>, HM 207:42.</i> Nevertheless, the <i>Nitva</i> is liable to pay it for a number of reasons. First, as long as the amount of liquidated damages is reasonable, as in our case, an agreement to pay for indirect damages is binding.<sup class="footnote-marker">74</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Rif</i>, <i>Bava Metzia</i> 104b; <i>Mishnah Torah</i>, <i>Sekhirut</i> 8:13; <i>Tosafot</i>, <i>Nedarim</i> 27b, s.v. <i>hilkhata</i>; <i>Pithei Hoshen</i>, <i>Sekhirut</i> 3:29, 42. Furthermore, even in the absence of a provision for liquidated damages, if the commercial practice is to incorporate such a provision for liquidated damages in such agreements, we construe the agreement as if such a clause exists. In other words, we assume that everyone knows and expects that such damages will be remunerated, and Halakhah assumes that the party has obligated himself to pay such damages should they materialize. See <i>Piskei ha-Rosh</i>, <i>Bava Metzia</i> 9:3; <i>Teshuvot Havot Ya’ir</i> 168.</i> Second, Halakhah recognizes the execution of a promissory note by virtue of <i>situmta</i> which imparts validity to contractual agreements recognized as <i>minhag ha-soherim</i> (commercial practice).<sup class="footnote-marker">75</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Maharshach</i> 3:8; <i>Teshuvot Hatam Sofer</i>, HM 66; <i>Teshuvot Maharsham</i> 5:45; <i>Mishpat Shalom</i>, HM 194:2; <i>Teshuvot Ahiezer</i> 3:79. The basis of imparting validity to <i>kinyan situmta</i> is the implied <i>gemirat da’at</i> (firm resolve of the parties) to undertake an obligation in accordance with customary practice. See <i>Teshuvot ha-Rosh</i> 55:10; <i>Teshuvot ha-Rashba</i> 2:268; <i>Teshuvot Maharasham</i>, HM 380; <i>Teshuvot Hatam Sofer</i>, YD 314, HM 66; <i>Teshuvot Sho’el u-Meshiv</i>, <i>Mahadurah Kama</i> 2:39.</i> By dint <i>of situmta</i>, the problem of <i>asmakhta</i> is avoided and the <i>Nitva</i> cannot claim that he never intended to pay the liquidated damages.<sup class="footnote-marker">76</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Tosafot</i>, <i>Bava Metzia</i> 66a; <i>Teshuvot Hatam Sofer</i>, supra n. 31; <i>Teshuvot Maharash Engel</i> 1:12. Cf. <i>Mishnah Torah</i>, <i>Hilkhot Mekhirah</i> 11:18.</i> Moreover, even if the amount of damages in this case were to be deemed unreasonable or even exorbitant, the provision is binding due to the fact that commercial practice would uphold such a clause and mandate payment.<sup class="footnote-marker">77</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Tosafot</i>, <i>Bava Metzia</i> 66a, s.v. <i>u-menume</i>; <i>Teshuvot Maharam mi-Rotenburg</i> (Berlin edition), 354; <i>Rema</i>, EH 61:5, 50:6, HM 207:16; <i>Teshuvot Hatam Sofer</i>, HM 66; <i>Arukh ha-Shulhan</i>, HM 201:3; <i>Hazon Ish</i>, HM, <i>Likkutim</i> 16:11; <i>PDR</i> 14:334, 343–344 (R. Z. N. Goldberg). Most decisors, however, argue that secular law cannot serve as grounds towards validating <i>asmakhta</i> clauses. See <i>Hazon Ish</i>, op. cit.; <i>Teshuvot Heishiv Moshe</i>, HM 90; <i>Teshuvot Teshurat Shai</i> 413, 457, 530. Cf. R. Yitzhak Herzog, <i>Teshuvot Pesakim u-Ketavim</i>, HM 59; <i>PDR</i> 5:74.</i>
Finally, in the view of <i>R. Aharon ben Yosef ha-Levi</i> (<i>Ra’ah</i>), the agreement need not explicitly stipulate that consequential damages are recoverable, as the lender relied on the promisor’s commitment to repay the loan and pay him for damages. Because of the benefit to the borrower generated by the induced reliance that established a surety relationship between the two parties, he undertakes an obligation, and payment is therefore enforceable in case of breach. In this sense, the promisor is similar to an <i>arev</i> (guarantor), who assumes liability because the creditor parted with monies on the strength of his assurance. The promisor is similarly liable due to the fact that the promisee relied upon him to remit payment for damages.<sup class="footnote-marker">78</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Hiddushei ha-Ritva</i>, <i>Bava Metzia</i> 73b.</i>
<b>3. The Recovery of Economic Loss of Funds</b>
The <i>Nitva</i> claims that in addition to reimbursement for unpaid salary, he is entitled to payment for economic loss of funds. This issue will be addressed on two planes – that of <i>din</i> (strict law) and <i>pesharah kerovah la-din</i> (court ordered settlement in accordance with Halakhah) – as stipulated in the arbitration agreement.
According to some authorities, in a non-loan transaction – such as cases of unpaid salary, unpaid bailment, or unpaid rent – there are grounds for mandating pre- and post-judgment interest relating to loss of the economic use of funds (in <i>Beit Hillel</i>’s words, “<i>peseida de-shuka</i>”). Accordingly, the demand that the <i>Tove’a</i> pay interest on the unpaid salary to the <i>Nitva</i> would not constitute a violation of <i>hilkhot ribbit</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">79</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Beit Yosef</i>, YD 160 in the name of R. Eliezer of Toille; <i>Beit Hillel</i>, YD 170:1; <i>Teshuvot Hatam Sofer</i> 5:144, 6:26; <i>Teshuvot Imrei Yosher</i> 1:149(2), 2:33; <i>Darkhei Teshuvah</i>, YD 161:12 in the name of <i>Marbeh Torah</i>; <i>Teshuvot Havazelet ha-Sharon</i>, HM 2:29; <i>Teshuvot Avnei Kodesh</i> 2; <i>PDR</i> 15:240–253.</i>
However, the majority of authorities rejects this position and argue that the payment of interest for the economic loss of funds in the context of debt grounded in non-loan transactions is not a legal obligation. In other words, pursuant to the dictates of <i>din</i>, a <i>beit din</i> cannot mandate such an award. While an individual may choose to repay economic loss of funds in such a case, such repayment is a halakhic-moral obligation (“<i>din Shamayim</i>,” the laws of heaven), and is unenforceable by a <i>beit din</i> adjudicating the matter according to <i>din</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">80</sup><i class="footnote">As understood by <i>Brit Yehuda</i>, 26–36, the following decisors view repayment as a halakhic-moral duty: <i>Sefer Ravyah</i> 916; <i>Bah,</i> YD 161; <i>Teshuvot ha-Ridbaz</i> 1:84; <i>Teshuvot Maharashdam</i>, YD 222; <i>Teshuvot Massat Binyamin</i> 37; <i>Teshuvot Sho’el u-Meishiv</i>, <i>Mahadurah Tanina</i> 4:123; <i>Teshuvot Imrei Yosher</i> 2:33. A review of these positions indicates otherwise – that is, that there is a halakhic-legal duty to repay such unpaid monies. Some argue that even on a halakhic-moral level, there exists no obligation to reimburse for the economic loss of such funds. See <i>Ohr Zaru’a</i>, <i>Bava Metzia</i> 181; <i>Beit Yosef,</i> supra n. 31<i>; Shakh</i>, YD 173:8; <i>Teshuvot Havot Ya’ir</i> 190; <i>Teshuvot Imrei Binah</i> 1; <i>Teshuvot be-Tzel ha-Hokhmah</i> 3:51.</i> Accordingly, this <i>beit din</i> is not able to mandate payment for the economic loss of funds on the basis of <i>din</i>.
Since, however, this <i>beit din</i> is empowered to resolve the dispute at hand in accordance with <i>pesharah kerovah la-din</i>, we may mandate that the parties comply with their halakhic-moral obligations.<sup class="footnote-marker">81</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Mahari Bruna</i> 241; Z. Goldberg, <i>Shivhei ha-Pesharah</i>, sec. 5 (letter sent to Kollel Mishpetei Aretz, Ofra, Israel).</i> Such a resolution has the effect of advancing one of the primary objectives of mandated compromise – the promotion of peace between litigious parties.<sup class="footnote-marker">82</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sanhedrin</i> 6b (R. Yehoshua ben Korcha’s dictum); <i>Teshuvot Shevut Ya’akov</i> 2:145.</i> In fact, R. Engel concludes that in a case of compensation for economic loss of funds, there is a need to effect a <i>pesharah</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">83</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Maharash</i> 6:112(5).</i>
A <i>beit din</i> implementing <i>pesharah</i> is mandated to render a decision that it deems fair and equitable under the circumstances.<sup class="footnote-marker">84</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Divrei Malkiel</i> 2:133.</i> Applying a rate of 6% simple interest per six-month rate, we find that the <i>Tove’a</i> suffered an economic loss of funds in the amount of $1,500.
<b>Decision</b>
1. The <i>Nitva</i> is hereby obligated to immediately remit $1,000,000 to the <i>Tove’a</i>.
2. The <i>Tove’a</i> is hereby exempt from paying the accrued interest on that amount.
3. The <i>Nitva</i> is obligated to immediately remit $75,000 to the <i>Tove’a</i> for liquidated damages.
4. The <i>Tove’a</i> is hereby obligated to immediately remit $25,000 to the <i>Nitva</i>, representing the outstanding salary owed.
5. The <i>Tove’a</i> is hereby obligated to immediately remit $1,500, which represents the economic loss of the outstanding salary.
Offsetting Credits and Debits:
6. Based upon provisions 1–5 of this Decision, the <i>Nitva</i> is obligated to immediately remit $1,045,000 to the <i>Tove’a</i>.

c) Rabbinic Contracts

<b><i>C. Rabbinic Contracts</i></b><br><b><i>R. Yisrael Rabin v. Congregation Beth Ya’akov</i></b>
The synagogue’s initial contract with R. Yisrael Rabin (<i>Tove’a</i>) for his services as its spiritual leader was for three years, from July 1, 2000 through June 30, 2003. Prior to the expiration of the third year, Congregation Beth Ya’akov (<i>Nitva</i>), a Modern Orthodox congregation, entered into negotiations for a second contract. Subsequently, a second contract was executed for three years, from July 1, 2003 through June 30, 2006. A similar contract was then negotiated and approved, with an expiration date of June 30, 2009.
Pursuant to its constitution and by-laws, all three contracts were negotiated and approved by the <i>Nitva</i>’s Board of Trustees. However, the decision to employ and renew the rabbi’s contract was made at congregational meetings expressly called in order to vote on this matter. During the general membership meeting’s discussion of the prospective third contract, the <i>Nitva</i> promised to remit a bonus of $25,000 should the <i>Tove’a</i> increase the synagogue membership by 10% by June 30, 2009. Although the membership increased by 10% by the agreed-upon date, the <i>Nitva</i> refused to remit the bonus to the <i>Tove’a</i>.
On April 1, 2009, the <i>Nitva</i> notified the <i>Tove’a</i> that the Congregation would not continue to employ him after June 30, 2009.
<b><i>Tove’a</i>’s Claims</b>
The <i>Tove’a</i> argues that as a rabbi engaged in spiritual matters, his position has <i>hazakah</i> (tenure) and he therefore cannot be removed from office without just cause.
