Thus, once attaining his position, the High Priest in the Holy Temple could not revert to his former role as an ordinary priest, as “we ascend in matters of sanctity, but we do not descend.”<sup class="footnote-marker">85</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Yoma</i> 12b. This concept was equally applied to rabbinical positions; see <i>Berakhot</i> 27b–28a.</i> A rabbi similarly cannot be removed from his position.
The <i>Tove’a</i> argues that this halakhic principle overrides the contract executed between the parties. Thus, once the <i>Tove’a</i> accepted and undertook the position as rabbi, the Congregation could only terminate him for just cause. In the wake of the <i>Nitva</i>’s admission that there were no grounds for his termination, the <i>Tove’a</i> demands that he be reinstated to his position.
Additionally, the <i>Tove’a</i> claims that $25,000 is due to him as a result of the increased membership of the congregation. Due to his present economic situation, he entreats this panel to mandate that the <i>Nitva</i> remit these funds.
Finally, should this panel fail to reinstate him to his former office, the <i>Tove’a</i> is suing for $100,000 for psychological trauma due to the <i>Nitva</i>’s termination without just cause.
<b><i>Nitva</i>’s Claims</b>
The <i>Nitva</i> concedes that had the <i>Tove’a</i> been given a life contract, he would be tenured and it would be impossible to terminate his employment without just cause. In this case, however, the <i>Tove’a</i>’s employment was based upon three contracts, and their provisions do not make any reference to employment beyond the date of expiration recorded in each. In fact, each contract explicitly states that there is an expiration date for his services, “unless extended by mutual agreement of the parties.” As such, the <i>Nitva</i> was empowered to terminate his employment even though the <i>Tove’a</i> was not found to be negligent in the performance of his duties.
The Congregation admits that it pledged to pay the <i>Tove’a</i> a bonus, but since that promise was never recorded in their written agreement, the Congregation is not obligated to remit those funds, as prevailing practice is to comply only with the provisions written in the contract.
The <i>Nitva</i> asserts that the <i>Tove’a</i>’s monetary claim for emotional stress is unfounded, as he is no different from any other employee who is subject to discharge, even if his work entails spiritual matters.
<b>Discussion</b>
<b>1. Is Rabbinic Office a Tenured Position?</b>
Halakhic authorities have consistently argued that a rabbinic position is a <i>minui shel kedushah</i> (a sacred appointment); since, as the <i>Tove’a</i> argues, “we ascend in matters of sanctity, but we do not descend,” a rabbi essentially has tenure for life.<sup class="footnote-marker">86</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Tosafot</i>, <i>Yoma</i> 12b, s.v. <i>halakhah</i>; <i>Rambam</i>, <i>Perush ha-Mishnah</i>, <i>Menahot</i> 13:10; <i>Teshuvot ha-Rambam</i> (Blau edition) 1:111; <i>Sefer ha-Mitzvot</i>, negative mitzvah 316; <i>Teshuvot ha</i>-<i>Mabit</i> 3:200; <i>Rema</i>, YD 245:22; <i>Teshuvot Shlomo le-Beit ha-Levi</i>, YD 8; <i>Teshuvot Ya’akov le-Beit ha-Levi</i> 5:50; <i>Kerem Shlomo</i>, YD 50; <i>Teshuvot Hikrei Lev</i>, OH 50, YD 3:100; <i>Teshuvot Avnei Nezer</i> 312:34; <i>Orhot ha-Mishpatim</i> 4:10–11. See also Yosef Yedid Halevi, <i>Kuntres u-le-Levi Amar</i> (Jerusalem: 5753).<br>Others contend that a rabbinic post is an example of <i>serarah</i> (communal authority). Since a communal position can be inherited by a member of his family, the right of inheritance affirms that a rabbi holds a tenured position. See <i>Mishnah Torah</i>, <i>Hilkhot Kelei ha-Mikdash</i> 4:20, <i>Hilkhot Melakhim</i> 1:7; <i>Teshuvot ha-Rivash</i> 271; <i>PDR</i> 10:38–60.</i> Nevertheless, following in the footsteps of <i>Rivash</i>, authorities traditionally mandated that the rabbi sign a <i>ketav minui</i> (a rabbinical appointment), or what is termed today a rabbinic contract, to ensure that he would not abandon his congregation prior to the expiration date of the agreement.<sup class="footnote-marker">87</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot ha-Rivash</i> 271; <i>Teshuovot Divrei Rivot</i> 224; <i>Teshuvot Mishpat Tzedek</i> 1:78; <i>Teshuvot Pnei Moshe</i> 3:31; <i>Birkei Yosef</i>, OH 53; <i>Teshuvot Hikrei Lev</i>, YD 3:99, HM 2:24.</i> Just as a rabbi cannot choose to forfeit his position,<sup class="footnote-marker">88</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Hikrei Lev</i>, OH 18; <i>Teshuvot Kapei Aharon</i>, YD 2:6–7. Cf. <i>Teshuvot Tzur Ya’akov</i> 74.</i> his congregation cannot terminate his services. So that the strictures of the contract would not turn the rabbi into a sort of indentured slave, fixed-term rabbinic contracts were executed with a term service that did not exceed three to six years.<sup class="footnote-marker">89</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Rema</i>, HM 333:3; <i>Teshuvot Hatam Sofer</i>, OH 205–206.</i>
In the nineteenth century, halakhic authorities consistently viewed rabbinical office as a <i>minui shel kedushah</i>. Accordingly<i>, R. Aharon Azriel</i> observes, “We never heard in the cities of Turkey … that a rabbi was discharged.”<sup class="footnote-marker">90</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Kapei Aharon</i> 2:7.</i> In the same period in Hungary, <i>Hatam Sofer</i> notes that although the nature of the rabbinate has changed since the time of the Sages,<sup class="footnote-marker">91</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Teshuvot Hatam Sofer</i>, OH 12: “Certainly, a <i>nasi</i> of Torah during the time of our Sages was akin to a sacred appointment … However, nowadays he is under the tutelage of the community, receiving compensation for his services.”</i> a rabbi still has a right to tenure.<sup class="footnote-marker">92</sup><i class="footnote">Ibid<i>.</i>, 206. Elsewhere, however, <i>Hatam Sofer</i> rules that if there is a stipulation that the rabbi can be terminated at the expiration of the term of the contract, such a provision is valid; see ibid., 205.</i> <i>R. Malkiel Tenenbaum</i>, a Polish decisor of the same period, ruled that the notion of “<i>minui shel kedushah</i>” is not limited to a rabbi who is formally appointed by a community, but rather applies equally to a rabbi who the people perceive as their authority and whom they consult regularly.<sup class="footnote-marker">93</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Divrei Malkiel</i> 4:82. In the case under discussion there, both an Ashkenazic and Sephardic rabbi served in one town. Upon the death of the Sephardic rabbi, his son wished to succeed his father, but R. Tenenbaum ruled that since the majority of the community solicited rabbinic counsel from the Ashkenazic rabbi, he was de facto rabbi of the Sephardic community as well, and the post therefore belonged to him.</i>
Nevertheless, in the United States today, it is accepted that a congregation may execute an agreement with their rabbi stipulating the conditions of his employment, including the termination of his service on a particular date. Should the congregation deem it appropriate to continue his employment, a new contract will be drafted thereafter. If there is a custom to offer fixed-term contracts with the understanding that the congregation may discharge the rabbi at the expiration of the term, the congregation may terminate his employment at the end of that term.<sup class="footnote-marker">94</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Hikrei Lev</i>, YD 99; <i>Rema</i>, YD 245:25 (end); <i>Teshuvot ha-Rashba</i> 5:283 (as understood by <i>Rema</i>, ibid.).</i> Even in the absence of a prevailing custom to offer fixed-term contracts, many Modern Orthodox congregations rely on various precedents<sup class="footnote-marker">95</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Hiddushei ha-Ritva</i>, <i>Makkot</i> 13a; <i>Beit Yosef</i>, OH 53 (end); <i>SA</i>, OH 53:26; <i>Rema</i>, YD 245:22; <i>Kenneset ha</i>-<i>Gedolah</i>, OH 53:10; <i>Teshuvot ha-Elef Lekha Shlomo</i> 38; <i>Teshuvot Teshurat Shai</i> 1:436. <i>Pnei Moshe</i>, Yerushalmi <i>Ta’anit</i> 4:2; <i>Teshuvot Hemdat Shlomo</i>, OH 7.</i> that perceive a rabbi as no different than a cantor, who occupies public office, or any other employee who can be terminated upon the expiration date of a contract.<sup class="footnote-marker">96</sup><i class="footnote">In contrast, many Hasidic, Yeshiva, and Sephardic communities continue to construe a rabbinical appointment as a “<i>minui shel kedushah.</i>” Nonetheless, the nature of the rabbinic contract and the prevailing custom in each community may vary from one congregation to another. Hence, prior to a <i>beit din</i> determination, each situation must be scrutinized individually.<br>Similarly, the <i>beit din</i> is obligated to scrutinize the facts in every case, even regarding a Modern Orthodox congregation, to ascertain whether, in fact, the congregation is treating the rabbi like any employee regarding termination.</i>
In the case at hand, the explicit provision, “unless extended by mutual agreement between the parties,” is found in all three contracts, clearly attesting to the fact that neither the <i>Tove’a</i> nor the <i>Nitva</i> construed the rabbinic office as a tenured position.<sup class="footnote-marker">97</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Tzitz Eliezer</i> 2:26.</i> As such, the <i>Nitva</i> had the right to terminate the <i>Tove’a</i>’s employment after June 30, 2009.
<b>2. The Validity of a Communal Promise</b>
The question of whether the <i>Nitva</i> is obligated to remit a bonus to the <i>Tove’a</i> in recognition of his efforts to increase the synagogue’s membership will be addressed on two functionally and conceptually different planes, as mandated by the arbitration agreement – the level of <i>din</i> (strict law) and that of <i>pesharah kerovah la-din</i> (court ordered settlement in accordance with Halakhah).
A promise is not enforceable in <i>beit din</i>, as it lacks the two critical components of a halakhic obligation – <i>kinyan</i> (a symbolic act of acquisition) and <i>gemirat da’at</i> (concrete articulation of the parties’ resolve to undertake the obligation). Since a promise entails executing an obligation in the future (for example, a promise to sell goods) or to transfer title of something that is not yet in existence (<i>davar she-lo ba la-olam</i>), it is legally (halakhically) unenforceable.<sup class="footnote-marker">98</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Bava Batra</i> 3a; <i>Rashi</i>, ad loc., s.v. <i>kinyan devarim hu</i>; <i>Piskei ha-Rosh</i>, <i>Bava Batra</i> 1:3; <i>SA</i>, HM 245:1. Although a promise is not enforeceable on the level of <i>din</i>, according to the strict dictates of the law, it constitutes a halakhic-moral obligation that can be enforced based upon the norms of <i>peshara</i>. See <i>Maharsha</i>, <i>Sanhedrin</i> 6b; <i>Teshuvot Mahari Bruna</i> 241; Z. Goldberg, “<i>Shivhei ha-Pesharah</i>,” [Hebrew] 1 <i>Mishpetei Eretz</i> (5762), 78, 82.</i>
Despite the lack of a halakhic-legal obligation to fulfill one’s promise, however, there remains a halakhic-moral duty to do so. Accordingly, <i>Shulhan Arukh</i> rules:
<span class="poetry indentAll"><small>When one conducts and concludes commercial transactions using words only [the negotiation and agreement not being completed by a formal act of acquisition], that person should stand by his word, even though none of the purchase price has been taken, nor a buyer’s mark made on the goods … Whoever withdraws from this type of transaction, whether buyer or seller, is deemed a faithless person.<sup class="footnote-marker">99</sup><i class="footnote"><i>SA</i>, HM 204:7.</i></small></span>
<i>Rema</i> adds:
<span class="poetry indentAll"><small>A person should stand by his word, even though no act of acquisition has been performed, only mere words have passed between the parties …<sup class="footnote-marker">100</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Rema</i>, HM 204:11.</i></small></span>
As I have noted elsewhere:
<span class="poetry indentAll"><small>Halakhah views promise-keeping as aligning oneself with the fulfillment of a religious norm rather than compliance with a norm of natural law, institutional moral norm, or moral norm established by social convention.<sup class="footnote-marker">101</sup><i class="footnote">See my “The Theory of Efficient Breach: A Jewish Law Perspective,” in A. Levine (ed.), <i>The Oxford Handbook on Judaism and Economics</i> (NY: 2010), 340, 344–6.</i></small></span>
Although promises are generally legally unenforceable, <i>Maharam mi-Rotenburg</i> suggests that a promise made by a group or community differs. In the case under discussion, a group of people hired a teacher and enquired of <i>Maharam</i> if they were permitted to retract their offer prior to the commencement of his employment. <i>Maharam</i> rules:
<span class="poetry indentAll"><small>The law is clear … Any matter resolved before the seven elders of the town … one cannot retract given that the behavior of many is unlike the conduct of one individual … and the customary practice is that any matter resolved among many does not require the <i>kinyan</i> which is usually required when dealing with an individual.<sup class="footnote-marker">102</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Maharam mi-Rotenburg</i>, quoted in <i>Mordekhai</i>, <i>Bava Metzia</i>, 457–458.</i></small></span>
<i>Rema</i> indeed rules that communal matters do not require a <i>kinyan</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">103</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Rema</i>, HM 163:6. See also <i>Sema</i>, HM 22:12.</i> Thus, although a <i>kinyan</i> is necessary in order to legally validate an obligation between individuals, no <i>kinyan</i> is necessary when a community obligates itself towards an individual. In the latter case, the community is legally – not only morally – obligated to comply with its promise, even in the absence of a <i>kinyan</i>. It would certainly seem that this ruling would extend to a synagogue body and its governance.<sup class="footnote-marker">104</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Bi’ur Halakhah</i>, OH 153:7; <i>Mishpetei ha-Torah</i> (Spitz), 34.</i> Accordingly, this <i>beit din</i> could obligate the congregation to fulfill its word to the rabbi.
Other authorities disagree with this conclusion, however. <i>Shulhan Arukh</i> rules:
<span class="poetry indentAll"><small>Some say that if a group said to an individual that he would receive a gift, they cannot retract, even if it is a large gift.<sup class="footnote-marker">105</sup><i class="footnote"><i>SA</i>, HM 204:9.</i></small></span>
According to some commentators, <i>Shulhan Arukh</i> implies that there is a halakhic-moral obligation for the public to give the gift, but the public cannot be halakhically-legally coerced to remit it.<sup class="footnote-marker">106</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Maharashdam</i>, HM 94; <i>Mishpat Shalom</i>, HM 204:9.</i>
Others argue that the irrevocability of a community’s promises is contingent upon the fact that there is a prevailing <i>minhag</i> in the community to fulfill promises without an accompanying <i>kinyan</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">107</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot ha-Rosh</i> 6:5. Others contend there is an implicit understanding that there is a <i>minhag</i> to implement public undertakings without a <i>kinyan</i>. See <i>Teshuvot Mayyim Amukim</i> 2:63.</i> Consequently, in locales where no such practice persists, the absence of <i>kinyan</i> allows the public to revoke their offer.<sup class="footnote-marker">108</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Perah Matteh Aharon</i> 123.</i>
Thus, the question of whether a community is halakhically-legally obligated to fulfill its promises is subject to debate, and even if such a duty exists, it may be contingent upon the presence of a <i>minhag</i> to act in such a fashion. Given the difference of opinion regarding this issue and the congregation’s <i>minhag</i> of only complying with what is recorded in the contract, we are unable to obligate the <i>Nitva</i> to remit the promised bonus to the <i>Tove’a</i> according to the dictates of <i>din</i>.
One of the primary objectives of <i>pesharah kerovah la-din</i> is to promote <i>shalom</i>, peace between litigious parties,<sup class="footnote-marker">109</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sanhedrin</i> 6b (R. Yehoshua ben Korcha’s dictum); <i>Teshuvot Shevut Ya’akov</i> 2:145.</i> and the need to foster compromise for the sake of peace equally applies in communal matters, such as our case.<sup class="footnote-marker">110</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Meishiv Davar</i> 2:10; <i>Teshuvot Ha’amek Davar</i> 3:10.</i> However, Halakhah proscribes us from implementing a compromise if this means remitting funds due to the economic situation of a litigant; the injunction “do not be compassionate in <i>din</i>”<sup class="footnote-marker">111</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ketuvot</i> 84a.</i> applies equally to the realm of <i>pesharah</i>. Thus, in our case, the <i>Tove’a</i>’s financial status is irrelevant to the question of whether the <i>Nitva</i>’s funds should be transferred to him. It is possible that these funds are rightfully the <i>Nitva</i>’s and therefore ought to remain in the congregation’s possession.
Nonetheless, pursuant to <i>pesharah kerovah la-din</i>, this panel finds a basis for mandating that the <i>Nitva</i> remit the funds to the <i>Tove’a</i>. At the very minimum, there exists a halakhic-moral obligation for the <i>Nitva</i> to fulfill its promise, even if that promise is unenforceable according to the conventional norms of <i>Hoshen Mishpat</i> (<i>dinei adam</i>). The <i>Nitva</i> is responsible according to <i>dinei Shamayim</i> (the laws of heaven) to comply with its halakhic-moral duties.
Addressing an agreement between three political parties regarding an upcoming municipal agreement which was invalid because it concerned a <i>davar she-lo ba le-olam</i> (a future event), a contemporary rabbinical court notes:
<span class="poetry indentAll"><small>We must add and say to the litigants that as public servants and when acting in this capacity, they should not argue that the obligations which they undertook are not binding because their validity can be disputed under law. Promises and obligations, especially in communal affairs, are holy and must be fulfilled to capacity, in accordance with their original intent, wording, and spirit. Public servants shall not go back on their word and bring ruin on the public by insisting on the strict letter of the law.<sup class="footnote-marker">112</sup><i class="footnote"><i>PDR</i> 6:173, 181; translation culled from Menachem Elon, “Power and Authority,” in Daniel Elazar (ed.), <i>Kinship and Consent</i> (Washington, D.C.: 1983), 204.</i></small></span>
In accordance with the <i>halakhot</i> that govern <i>pesharah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">113</sup><i class="footnote">Regarding the enforcement of halakhic-promissory duties via the means of <i>pesharah</i>, see <i>Maharsha</i>, <i>Sanhedrin</i> 6b; <i>Teshuvot Mahari Bruna</i> 241; Goldberg, supra n. 14, 82.</i> and in light of the fact that <i>pesharah kerovah la-din</i> should be fair and equitable,<sup class="footnote-marker">114</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Divrei Malkiel</i> 2:133.</i> it is this panel’s opinion that it is incumbent upon the <i>Nitva</i>, as a communal organization, to keep its word. The congregation is therefore obligated to pay the promised bonus to the <i>Tove’a</i>.
<b>3. Grounds for Reimbursement for Infliction of Emotional Stress</b>
Finally, we must address whether there are grounds for the <i>Tove’a</i> to file a claim for emotional stress against the <i>Nitva</i> after the congregation terminated his employment without just cause after nine years in the position.<sup class="footnote-marker">115</sup><i class="footnote">This portion of the decision is based upon my, “Recovery for Infliction of Emotional Distress: Toward Relief for the <i>Agunah</i>,” 18 <i>The Jewish Law Annual</i> (2009), 213, reprinted in an expanded form in chap. 3 of this volume.</i> The Talmud teaches us:
<span class="poetry indentAll"><small>If one frightens another, he is not liable by the law of man, but he is liable by the law of heaven. If he blew [with a trumpet or the like] into his ear and made him deaf, he is not liable. But if he held him and blew into his ear and made him deaf, he is liable.<sup class="footnote-marker">116</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Bava Batra</i> 91a. Cf. Yerushalmi <i>Kilayim</i> 8:2.</i></small></span>
Absent physical contact and physical damage, one who frightens another does not thereby incur liability. <i>Maharshal</i> and <i>Ra’avad</i> argue that fright and shock could have not caused injury, since the generally accepted assessment (<i>omed</i>) is that fright and shock do not suffice to cause emotional scarring.<sup class="footnote-marker">117</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Yam Shel Shlomo</i>, <i>Bava Kama</i> 8:38; <i>Hiddushei ha-Ra’avad</i>, <i>Bava Kama</i> (Atlas edition), 223.</i> Others, such as <i>Rosh</i>, <i>Mordekhai</i>, <i>Maharam mi-Rotenburg</i>, and <i>Tur</i>, contend that injury caused by fright is an instance of <i>gerama</i> (indirect damages), and “<i>gerama be-nezikin patur</i>” – there is no liability for an injury that is caused indirectly.<sup class="footnote-marker">118</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Bava Kama</i> 60a. See <i>Piskei ha-Rosh</i>, <i>Bava Kama</i> 2:17; <i>Mordekhai</i>, <i>Bava Kama</i> 91; <i>Teshuvot Maharam mi-Rotenburg</i> (Cremona edition), 300; <i>Tur</i>, HM 420:25.</i> According to all opinions, however, emotional stress in the absence of physical contact generates liability according to <i>dinei Shamayim</i>, the laws of heaven.
Decades ago, <i>R. Avraham I. Kook</i> applied this principle to the case of a rabbi who was discharged from his position,<sup class="footnote-marker">119</sup><i class="footnote">A. Kook, “<i>Hasagat Gevul be-Rabbanut</i>” [Hebrew], 5 <i>Tehumin</i> (5744), 285.</i> and such psychological stress is certainly compounded in our case, in which the removal was without just cause. Nevertheless, <i>Ran</i>, <i>Rashi</i>, <i>Meiri</i>, <i>Tashbetz</i>, and <i>R. Feinstein</i> conclude that every individual who ventures out into the world assumes the risk of emotional injury, exposing himself to unexpected trauma. One should try to be more thick-skinned rather than burden others with responsibility for his emotional weaknesses.<sup class="footnote-marker">120</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ran</i> on <i>Rif</i>, <i>Kiddushin</i> 9a, s.v. <i>keivan debar data</i>; <i>Rashi</i>, <i>Kiddushin</i> 24b, s.v. <i>shani adam</i>; <i>Beit ha-Behira</i>, <i>Bava Kama</i> 56a; <i>Teshuvot Tashbetz</i> 2:114; <i>Iggerot Moshe</i>, HM 1:98.</i> Failure to inure oneself to the routine stresses of existence bars recovery for any ensuing psychological injury.
Thus, on the level of strict <i>din</i>, Halakhah does not mandate recovery for emotional stress, which constitutes indirectly-caused damages that an individual should be able to confront and address without compromising his psychological integrity.<sup class="footnote-marker">121</sup><i class="footnote">The question remains as to why indirectly-caused damages create liability under the laws of heaven (<i>dinei Shamayim</i>).</i>
However, there is a second plane of the halakhic law of damages that is predicated upon the fact that under certain prescribed conditions, an arbiter may exercise his judicial discretion regarding whether or not to award damages. After the lapse of the ordination process in the fourth century C.E., non-ordained arbiters – communal leaders and <i>beit din shel hedyotot</i> (lay arbiters) – were granted authority to resolve cases involving common injuries that caused the injured party a financial loss. The compensation that these arbiters could impose, however, was limited to reimbursement for loss of income and medical expenses;<sup class="footnote-marker">122</sup><i class="footnote"><i>SA</i>, HM 1:1–2; <i>Bah</i>, HM 1; <i>Shakh</i>, HM 1:7.</i> claims of liability due to <i>boshet</i> (shame and embarrassment) although arising from bodily damage ensuing from a physical act, could not be submitted to a non-ordained arbiter for adjudication, since there was no loss of income.<sup class="footnote-marker">123</sup><i class="footnote"><i>SA</i>, EH 83:1.</i> Nevertheless, communal leaders and laymen are empowered to exercise exigency jurisdiction in matters of civil damages such as <i>boshet</i> under the rubric of <i>le-migdar milta</i> (protective measures) and <i>ha-sha’ah tzerikhah lekakh</i> (the times require it).<sup class="footnote-marker">124</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Yevamot</i> 90b. See <i>Teshuvot Yakhin u-Boaz</i> 1:126; <i>Beit Yosef</i>, HM 2; <i>SA</i>, HM 2:1; <i>Rema</i>, HM 2:1; <i>Levush Ir Shushan</i>, HM 2; <i>Sma</i>, HM 2:9; <i>Netivot ha-Mishpat</i>, HM 2:5; <i>Arukh ha-Shulhan</i>, HM 2:2.</i> To promote broader policy goals, arbiters are authorized, under certain circumstances, to deviate from the black-letter Halakhah of damages and impose actual and/or punitive damages.
The question at hand, then, is whether the case in question constitutes an exigency situation in which the <i>beit din</i> is empowered to mandate damages even though they are not demanded by the <i>din</i>. There are three approaches to defining what constitutes an exigency situation that would empower an arbiter to mandate such damages.<sup class="footnote-marker">125</sup><i class="footnote">See Emanuel Quint and Neil Hecht, <i>Jewish Jurisprudence: Its Sources and Modern Applications</i> (Switzerland: 1980), 174–5.</i> According to <i>Maharam Lublin</i>, any individual can be punished in order to deter others from emulating his behavior.<sup class="footnote-marker">126</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Maharam Lublin</i> 138.</i> The <i>Sma</i> maintains that this is true only if the wrongdoer is habitual in his misconduct,<sup class="footnote-marker">127</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sma</i>, HM 2:2.</i> while the <i>Sha’ar Ephraim</i> holds that the arbiters have this authority only if other people in the community are engaging in the same type of conduct and deterrence is necessary.<sup class="footnote-marker">128</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Sha’ar Ephraim</i> 2.</i>
It seems clear that our case is not an exigency situation that empowers the <i>beit din</i> to impose damages upon the <i>Nitva</i>. As noted above, the practice of offering renewable contracts that can be extended by mutual agreement of the congregation and rabbi is a practice in many synagogues, including that of the <i>Nitva</i>; discharging the rabbi is a widespread practice among Modern Orthodox synagogues and is grounded in halakhic precedent. As such, the <i>Nitva</i> did not act improperly and there are no grounds for awarding damages for the infliction of emotional stress. The <i>Tove’a</i>, like all people who venture out into the world and endure difficult situations, must learn to cope with his experience.
<b>Decision</b>
1. The <i>Nitva</i> did not act improperly in dismissing the <i>Tove’a</i> from his position as rabbi.
2. The <i>Nitva</i> shall immediately pay $25,000 to the <i>Tove’a</i>.
3. The <i>Tove’a</i>’s claim for damages based upon the <i>Nitva</i>’s infliction of emotional stress is hereby denied.

d) Self Dealing in the Not for Profit Boardroom

<b><i>D. Self-Dealing in the Not-for-Profit Boardroom</i></b><br><b><i>Yeshiva Har Tzion v. Yossi Stein</i></b>
Due to severe overcrowding conditions in their facility, Yeshiva Har Tzion sought land to build an additional building. Yossi Stein, a trustee on the <i>yeshiva</i> board, is the sole owner of Century Realty Corporation, which owned a plot of land adjacent to the <i>yeshiva</i> that was suitable for the <i>yeshiva</i>’s needs. Mr. Stein persuaded his real estate broker to offer the plot to the <i>yeshiva</i>, but neither Mr. Stein nor the broker disclosed Stein’s ownership of Century. The <i>yeshiva</i> ultimately bought the plot for one million dollars.
Realizing that the <i>yeshiva</i> required additional capital to finance the property purchase, Mr. Stein persuaded the board to sell a vacant lot owned by the <i>yeshiva</i> to his nephew. The nephew agreed that should the sale materialize, he would use his political connections to arrange for his uncle’s appointment as a councilman on the municipal board. Again, neither Mr. Stein nor his nephew disclosed their familial ties to the <i>yeshiva</i>, nor the nephew’s offer to his uncle.
The <i>yeshiva</i>’s board voted to approve both the purchase of the property and the sale of the <i>yeshiva</i> land. After the finalization of the sale, the <i>yeshiva</i> became aware of Mr. Stein’s ownership of the real estate company, as well as the identity of the buyer of their property. In light of the circumstances, Mr. Stein was removed from his board position.
<b><i>Tove’a</i>’s Claims</b>
The <i>Tove’a</i> argues that this situation can be best described as “a self-dealing transaction” in which the <i>Nitva</i> had a financial interest in the <i>yeshiva</i>’s purchase. The <i>Nitva</i> was therefore obligated to exhibit transparency by disclosing his interest in the transaction. Moreover, the <i>Nitva</i> utilized his power of persuasion as a board member to purchase this land at a cost of one million dollars. Upon discovering the <i>Nitva</i>’s ties to the transaction, the board became aware that the fair market value of the property was $900,000. Consequently, the <i>Tove’a</i> is suing for $100,000 from the <i>Nitva</i>.
Similarly, because the board was unaware of the fair market value of their property, the <i>Nitva</i>’s nephew was able to purchase the property at $150,000 below its market value.<sup class="footnote-marker">129</sup><i class="footnote">While the apparent complete ignorance of the board appears surprising in this case, it is not surprising for members of a group to adopt a view or views simply because another member or members endorse that position. This is known as the phenomenon of “groupthink,” which entails a desire to identify with the dominant position and label it as “fair” even when it is not without engaging in open group discussion and analysis of the matter. In effect, this leads to irrational decision-making, failure to seek information from outside sources, voting on matters without due diligence, and arriving at decisions which may undermine the institution’s best interests. Hence, groupthink may lead to the emergence of the validation of self-dealing. See Cass Sunstein, “Deliberative Trouble? Why Groups Go to Extremes,” 110 <i>Yale Law Journal</i>, (2000), 71. Consequently, it is unsurprising to find that the <i>Nitva</i> was able to pursue his agenda and receive group support, buttressed by the board’s failure to perform due diligence regarding his vested interest in two transactions.</i> The <i>Tove’a</i> is therefore suing for an additional $150,000.
<b><i>Nitva</i>’s Counterclaims</b>
If the purchase price of the property did not reflect the fair market value, it was the board who shirked its responsibility by failing to perform due diligence and leg-work that might have demonstrated that both transactions were not the good deals they seemed to be. Moreover, while other boards have the practice of requesting that its members read a conflict-of-interest policy, list any affiliations in which they have a financial interest, and recluse themselves from deliberation should a conflict arise, there is no such practice on the <i>Tove’a</i>’s board. As such, the process of purchasing the land was not tainted, and the <i>Nitva</i> is exempt from any responsibility regarding this matter. Finally, <i>Nitva</i> argues that his removal from the board was unjustified and he should be instated to his former position.
<b>Discussion</b>
<b>1. The Requirement of Transparency</b>
The <i>Nitva</i>’s argument is that a duty to refrain from self-dealing is predicated upon the signing of a conflict-of-interest questionnaire. If a board member signed such a document, he has an obligation to avoid self-dealing transactions, but if he did not do so, no such duty exists and there is no requirement to inform the board of his vested interest in any matter up for discussion or vote.
This argument is halakhically untenable. The board in question serves the interests of a <i>yeshiva</i> whose ultimate goal is the inculcation and internalization of halakhic norms and Jewish education. Those same norms apply to the <i>yeshiva</i>’s board irrespective of the existence or nonexistence of any contractual agreement executed by its members.
Halakhah instructs us to not only be innocent, but also to appear in the eyes of our fellowmen as innocent. The Mishnah thus instructs that the person who enters the Temple office for the purpose of collecting donated half-shekels must act in a particular manner:
<span class="poetry indentAll"><small>The collector may not enter dressed in a loosely-hanging garment [with sleeves in which money can be hidden], nor wearing boots or sandals or <i>tefillin</i> or an amulet [in which money can be concealed] … For a person must be guiltless before his fellowman as before God, as the Torah states: “You shall be guiltless before God and before Israel.”<sup class="footnote-marker">130</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Shekalim</i> 3:2.</i></small></span>
In short, one must exhibit transparency in one’s relations with others. This imperative is not limited to an individual engaging in social interaction, but rather equally applies to a person who occupies public office. The identical halakhic requirement applies to a member of a board when he is serving his institution and when he is interacting with others in a social context.
Since the professional halakhic-ethics of a board is synonymous with the halakhic code of social ethics, we must analyze a trustee’s professional responsibility through the same categories that govern interaction with others in other contexts. We will therefore consider whether a trustee is to be viewed as a <i>dayan</i> (judge), <i>shomer</i> (bailee), <i>apotropos</i> (guardian), or <i>shaliah</i> (agent). The trustee’s halakhic identity will determine how his conflict of interest will ultimately be viewed.
<b>2. The Trustee as a <i>Dayan</i></b>
Numerous decisors have advanced the idea that the authority of the community and its public servants are comparable to and function like a <i>beit din</i>, with each and every municipal officer serving as a <i>dayan</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">131</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot ha-Rashba</i> 3:411; <i>Teshuvot ha-Rivash</i> 214, 249; <i>Teshuvot Rashbash</i> 566; <i>Teshuvot Maharik</i>, <i>shoresh</i> 17; <i>Teshuvot Re’em</i> 53, 57; <i>Hazon Ish</i>, <i>Bava Kama</i> 4:8.</i> This conclusion extends by analogy to any communal, synagogue, or <i>yeshiva</i> board.<sup class="footnote-marker">132</sup><i class="footnote">Yerushalmi <i>Pe’ah</i> 8:6 and <i>Rash Sirilio</i> ad loc.; <i>PDR</i> 7:225, 250, 261.</i>
A <i>dayan</i> may not judge any case that may benefit him in some way.<sup class="footnote-marker">133</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot ha-Rashba</i> 1:642; <i>Teshuvot Terumat ha-Deshen</i> 354; <i>SA</i>, HM 7:12, 37:19; <i>Shakh</i>, HM 7:10.</i> By extension, all self-dealing transaction involving individuals who are empowered to render communal decisions should be prohibited. Invoking the community leader<i>-dayan</i> paradigm, <i>R. Yehezkel Landau</i> argues:
<span class="poetry indentAll"><small>Regarding taxation matters, they are interested parties, as explicitly stated by <i>Rema</i> 163, that for all communal needs … one must enlist all the taxpayers to preside on these matters.<sup class="footnote-marker">134</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Noda be-Yehuda</i>, <i>Mahadurah Kama</i>, HM 20.</i></small></span>
In other words, all communal matters, including tax concerns, are to be construed as vested interests of each and every board member, and therefore only the local citizenry may resolve these matters. Others have also subscribed to the view that all acts of self-dealing are prohibited for communal leaders.<sup class="footnote-marker">135</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Rashbash</i> 568.</i>
Public office holders should act at all times in a manner that demonstrates integrity, avoiding even the appearance of a conflict-of-interest, and thus serving as examples to fellow officers and the community. Given that the scenario described regarding Mr. Stein and the board may be characterized as a self-dealing transaction, such a potential sale should neither have been reviewed nor deliberated upon by the <i>Nitva</i>.
Was the <i>Nitva</i> precluded as well from involvement in deliberations regarding the sale benefiting his nephew, a sale from which he would derive no financial gain? A <i>dayan</i>, and by extension a trustee, must equally distance himself from self-dealing that involves a non-financial interest. Regarding a witness or <i>dayan</i> with a vested interest in a non-financial matter, <i>Rambam</i> rules:
<span class="poetry indentAll"><small>If the witness finds that he has an interest in the testimony … he should not testify regarding it … Just as he shall not testify in this matter due to the possibility that he is an interested party, similarly he shall not judge this matter …<sup class="footnote-marker">136</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Mishnah Torah</i>, <i>Hilkhot Edut</i> 16:4–5. See also <i>SA</i>, HM 37:21; <i>Shakh</i>, HM 37:10.</i></small></span>
The exclusion of a “<i>nogei’ah be-davar</i>” (an interested party) from involvement in deliberations has been extended to public servants, who are compared to <i>dayanim</i>. R. Yosef ibn Lev (<i>Maharival</i>), for example, discussed a case in which a <i>hazzan</i> obligated himself to serve a particular community but then accepted a position in another community, claiming that the first community had breached the terms of the agreement. The members of the community claimed, on the other hand, that the <i>hazzan</i> remained obligated to serve them. <i>Maharbil</i> ruled that the testimony of the head of a <i>kehillah</i> was not admissible; they were <i>noge’im be-davar</i>, as they wished to continue to benefit from the <i>hazzan</i>’s skill.<sup class="footnote-marker">137</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Maharival</i> 1:36. See also <i>Teshuvot ha</i>-<i>Mabit</i> 1:81.</i> If the citizenry’s testimony is invalid in such a case, the leadership’s input regarding such a matter should certainly be discounted.
Consequently, our case, in which the <i>Nitva</i> persuaded the board to sell the <i>yeshiva</i>’s vacant lot to his nephew in the hopes that his nephew would facilitate his appointment to the municipal board, is an example of self-dealing. Given that such a civil appointment would benefit the <i>Nitva</i>, the transaction is tainted.<sup class="footnote-marker">138</sup><i class="footnote">Had it been unclear that such an appointment would materialize, the <i>Nitva</i>’s input regarding the transaction would not be tainted. See <i>Teshuvot Terumat ha-Deshen</i> 354; <i>SA</i>, HM 37:10. Moreover, if the nephew were to explicitly state that the civil appointment was a gift rather than remuneration for facilitating the transaction, the trustee may accept the civil office. See <i>Rashi</i>, <i>Ketuvot</i> 98b, s.v. <i>she-yesh</i>; <i>Teshuvot ha-Rashba ha-Meyuhasot le-Ramban</i> 60.</i>
The question of the propriety of selling the <i>Tove’a</i>’s land to the <i>Nitva</i>’s nephew is actually addressed in a <i>teshuvah</i> of <i>R. Yosef Trani</i>, who discusses whether a trustee of a charitable foundation may sell some of its assets to a relative.<sup class="footnote-marker">139</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Maharit</i>, HM 2:1.</i> <i>R. Trani</i> contends that this would be a self-dealing transaction and is therefore prohibited. First, the factor of <i>hashad</i> (suspicion) ought to preclude such an act.<sup class="footnote-marker">140</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Shabbat</i> 23a–23b; <i>Sefer Yerei’im</i>, commandment 340; <i>Sefer ha-Hinukh</i>, mitzvah 349; <i>Teshuvot Hesed Avraham</i>, OH, 21; <i>Iggerot Moshe</i>, OH 2:40, 4:82.</i> Furthermore, it is unlikely that there will be serious negotiations and use of bargaining tactics in discussion with a relative of a board member. Finally, potential buyers with more attractive offers than the relative will abstain from the bidding process due to their assumption that the trustee’s relative has the sale “locked up.”
<b>3. The Trustee as a <i>Shomer</i></b>
Halakhah recognizes that obligations may be created by parties undertaking duties based upon oral or written agreements, whereby each party establishes a claim against the other that the latter is obligated to honor. Thus, a <i>shomer</i> (bailee) is obligated by dint of agreement with the <i>mafkid</i> (bailor) to safeguard the asset entrusted to his safe-keeping. In effect, a communal officer who is entrusted with dispensing, investing, and dealing with public funds has the status of a <i>shomer</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">141</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Maharam Schick</i>, HM 12; <i>Teshuvot Ba’ei Hayyei</i>, HM 1:217; <i>Mahane Ephraim</i>, <i>Hilkhot Shomerim</i> 17; <i>Pithei Teshuvah</i>, HM 301:9.</i>
As Talmud notes, a <i>shomer</i> is precluded from involvement in self-dealing transactions:
<span class="poetry indentAll"><small>If one deposits fruit with his neighbor and it spoils … he [the <i>shomer</i>] may not touch it – this is R. Meir’s ruling. But the Sages maintain: He provides a solution for them by selling them pursuant to the <i>beit din</i>’s direction, and he must sell it to strangers, not to himself.<sup class="footnote-marker">142</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Bava Metzia</i> 38a; see <i>SA</i>, HM 292:19.</i></small></span>
Even though the transaction has been approved by a <i>beit din</i> of experts (<i>mumhin</i>),<sup class="footnote-marker">143</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>beit din</i> is required either to appraise the bailment or to sanction the <i>shomer</i>’s right to execute a sale, which is beyond the mandate of his bailment.</i> the asset may only be sold to a third party. If we were to allow the <i>shomer</i> to buy it himself, others would be suspicious that he lowered its value prior to acquiring it for himself. Moreover, even though the purchase received <i>beit din</i> approval, people would be unaware that there was a <i>beit din</i> appraisal or that a <i>beit din</i> may execute such an appraisal, and they would thus assume that the <i>shomer</i> deceived the <i>beit din</i> regarding the asset’s market value.<sup class="footnote-marker">144</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Pesahim</i> 13a; <i>Rashi</i>, <i>Bava Metzia</i> 38a; <i>Tosafot</i>, <i>Ketuvot</i> 98a, s.v. <i>de-amrei</i>; <i>Mordekhai</i>, <i>Bava Metzia</i> 428; <i>Perishah</i>, HM 290:15.</i> Should the bailment be sold to the <i>shomer</i>, the sale, although executed under the auspices of <i>beit din</i>, is null and void.<sup class="footnote-marker">145</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Mishnah Torah</i>, <i>Hilkhot She’eilah ve-Pikadon</i> 7:5; <i>SA</i>, HM 292:19.</i> In short, a <i>shomer</i> has no right to engage in a self-dealing transaction, and even <i>beit din</i> review cannot validate a <i>shomer</i>’s purchase.
In our case, the <i>Nitva</i>, a public servant, is considered a <i>shomer</i> and was therefore proscribed from purchasing the property for himself under the guise of an offer advanced by a real estate broker.
<b>4. The Trustee as an <i>Apotropos</i></b>
Public servants are empowered either by election or appointment by the constituency of their respective communities or voluntary associations to be entrusted as guardians over their assets.<sup class="footnote-marker">146</sup><i class="footnote">In general, <i>hilkhot apotropus</i> deal with persons who are incapable of taking care of their own affairs, such as minors or adults who are mentally impaired, but the institution of guardianship applies equally to a public servant. See <i>Rashba</i> and <i>Ramban</i> cited by <i>Beit Yosef</i>, YD 169 (end); <i>Rashba</i> cited by <i>Beit Yosef</i>, HM 128, 163; <i>Teshuvot Maharit</i> 1:117.</i> The creation of these bodies and the responsibilities of the trustees vis-à-vis these constituencies are shaped and molded by <i>hilkhot apotropsut</i>, the laws of guardianship.
An <i>apotropos</i> charged with guardianship of an orphan must avoid any <i>hashad</i> (suspicion) that he is mismanaging his ward’s funds. To be above suspicion, any profit derived by an <i>apotropos</i> from a personal transaction with a third party concerning the orphan’s assets requires the prior scrutiny of a <i>beit din</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">147</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ra’avad</i> and <i>Nimmukei Yosef</i>, <i>Bava Batra</i> 67a; <i>Beit Yosef</i>, HM 290:15; <i>Rema</i>, HM 290:8; <i>Perishah</i>, HM 290:15. The <i>beit din</i> must evaluate the transaction to ensure that the <i>apotropos</i>’s self-dealing does not endanger the orphan’s assets. See <i>Shakh</i>, HM 290:10; <i>Sma</i>, HM 290:26; <i>Teshuvot Maharsham</i>, HM 46, 349, 434.</i> which may determine that the sale is to the orphan’s financial benefit as well and therefore may validate the sale.<sup class="footnote-marker">148</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Helkat Mehokek</i>, EH 93:45.</i>
The same conclusion would be applicable to a trustee, who has the status of an <i>apotropos</i>. <i>R. Adas</i>, <i>R. Ya’avetz</i>, and <i>R. Elyashiv</i> invoke this parallel, emphasizing that an overarching concern of an <i>apotropos</i> managing a communal organization is to avoid suspicion when disbursing funds to needy individuals.<sup class="footnote-marker">149</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Bikkurei Asher</i> 2:53.</i> In other words, <i>hilkhot apotropos</i> mandates that trustees comply with full disclosure of their vested interests and that a <i>beit din</i> or administrative body review any and all interested transactions by implementing “a fairness standard.”
In our case, to avoid the issue of <i>hashad</i> in the public eye, the <i>Nitva</i> was required to disclose his ties to Century Realty Corporation and his familial relationship to the purchaser of the <i>yeshiva</i>’s property. Validation of the two transactions would require a determination by the <i>beit din</i> or any administrative body, including but not limited to the board,<sup class="footnote-marker">150</sup><i class="footnote">For a possible precedent allowing a layman to appraise a widow’s self-interested action relating to an orphan’s assets, see <i>Mishnah Torah</i>, <i>Hilkhot Ishut</i> 17:14; <i>Maggid Mishnah</i> and <i>Lehem Mishnah</i> ad loc.</i> that the transactions and its terms have been arrived at through a rational and fair process, that the terms of the sale are fair and reasonable, and that these transactions are in the organization’s best interests.
If an <i>apotropos</i> was appointed by a father for his child and there is corroborating evidence, such as the existence of witnesses, that testifies to the <i>apotropos’</i>s self-dealing activities, he may be relieved of his duties.<sup class="footnote-marker">151</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Mishnah Torah</i>, <i>Hilkhot Nahalot</i> 10:7.</i> According to some decisors, a <i>beit din</i> appointed <i>apotropos</i> may be removed from his position even if he is only suspected of engaging in self-dealing,<sup class="footnote-marker">152</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Shakh</i>, YD 257:3; <i>Arukh ha-Shulhan</i>, HM 290:10.</i> while others argue that witnesses to his activities are required prior to removal.<sup class="footnote-marker">153</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Rema</i>, HM 290:5.</i> Given the facts in our case, the <i>Tove’a</i> certainly had well-substantiated grounds to remove the <i>Nitva</i> from his status as an <i>apotropos</i>. Indeed, contemporary decisors have applied <i>hilkhot apotropos</i> in mandating the removal of trustees from the board of a religious charitable foundation due to their improper behavior.<sup class="footnote-marker">154</sup><i class="footnote">See supra n. 21.</i>
<b>5. The Trustee as <i>Shaliah</i></b>
It is also possible to view the role of a board through the prism of <i>hilkhot shelihut</i>, the laws of agency, as the board members must serve the best interests of their constituency. A <i>shaliah</i> (agent) is proscribed from being in a conflict-of-interest with the person who appointed him. As <i>Rambam</i> states:
<span class="poetry indentAll"><small>If someone appoints an agent to betroth a woman on his behalf, and he [the agent] proceeded to betroth her for himself, she is betrothed to the agent. But it is prohibited to act in such a fashion, and whoever acts in this manner and the like in other matters of commerce is called wicked.<sup class="footnote-marker">155</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Mishnah Torah</i>, <i>Hilkhot Ishut</i> 9:17.</i></small></span>
Although his act is not nullified due to his deviation from his mandate, the <i>shaliah</i>’s self-dealing is viewed as inappropriate behavior.
At first glance, it would seem that Halakhah frowns upon the recalcitrant <i>shaliah</i> because he fails to fulfill the mandate of the one who sent him. In truth, however, Halakhah is also concerned with the character and integrity of the agent himself. Thus, the <i>Shulhan Arukh</i> rules that if a person appoints a <i>shaliah</i> to sell his field, the <i>shaliah</i> may not buy it himself, even if his property neighbors the field and he therefore should, by right, be offered the land first (<i>mezran</i>).<sup class="footnote-marker">156</sup><i class="footnote"><i>SA</i>, HM 175:16.</i> One of the reasons for this ruling is that we are concerned that there will be a <i>hashad</i> that the agent acquired the field at a price below its fair market value.<sup class="footnote-marker">157</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sema</i>, HM 175:26. Cf. others who argue that as an agent, he is the “<i>yad</i>,” the arm of the principal. Consequently, purchasing the field for himself is a halakhic impossibility; a person cannot sell something to himself. See <i>Ketuvot</i> 98a; <i>Teshuvot ha-Rashba ha-Meyuhasot le-Ramban</i> 44; <i>Teshuvot ha-Rosh</i> 105:2.</i>
This same applies, by extension, to public servants.<sup class="footnote-marker">158</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot ha-Rashba</i> 7:109; <i>Teshuvot ha-Ritva</i> 114; <i>Teshuvot ha-Rashbash</i> 573; <i>Teshuvot Maharam Schick</i>, HM 19; <i>Teshuvot Mayyim Amukim</i> 2:63.</i> The purpose of a board is to promote and foster the best interests of the organization; a board’s agenda epitomizes an agent’s mandate: “I appointed you for my benefit rather than detriment.”<sup class="footnote-marker">159</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Mishnah Torah</i>, <i>Hilkhot Sheluhin ve-Shutafim</i> 1:2.</i> Self-serving behavior undermines the institution’s goals, as well as its ideological and financial integrity, and it should thus be deemed unacceptable. As such, transparency and accountability for its activities is paramount.
Emerging from this discussion is that the board of the <i>yeshiva</i> ought to have obligated all its members to read the organization’s conflict-of-interest policy and fill out an annual disclosure form enumerating the affiliations in which they have an ownership interest and, to the extent known, those affiliations of family members covered by the conflict-of-interest policy. The board could have passed a simple prohibition against all self-dealing, thereby reinforcing the fiduciary concept of loyalty and accountability. Alternatively, the board could have chosen to govern its members’ activities under <i>hilkhot apotropos</i>, in which case a <i>beit din</i>, administrative panel, or the board itself would review any conflicts of interest that might arise and determine if the potential transaction complies with the fairness standard. Accordingly, we would have to consider whether the interested trustee complied with the full disclosure policy; whether the board environment was unbiased at the time the decision was made to engage in the transaction; whether there was due diligence in assessing the fairness of the transaction; and whether the transaction promoted the organization’s ideological, economic, and financial interests.
In this sense, the <i>Nitva</i> is correct in stating that the board did not fulfill its duties in serving the community’s best interests, as it failed to issue or enforce conflict-of-interest guidelines and was certainly negligent in the realm of due diligence. However, the <i>Nitva</i> cannot claim that the board’s failure to address interested transactions gives him license to do whatever he wishes. As a member of a covenant-faith community, his actions must be transparent and above any and all suspicion. Consequently, the <i>Nitva</i>’s failure to disclose his relationship to Century Realty Corporation, his familial ties to the purchaser of the <i>yeshiva</i>’s property, and their arrangement for the <i>Nitva</i> to receive a municipal office tainted the process, as well as the two transactions.<sup class="footnote-marker">160</sup><i class="footnote">On the other hand, if the <i>Nitva</i> used the organization’s funds to give a gift to or fund a trip for his nephew in order to close the deal, such behavior would not be construed as deriving benefit from communal assets. See <i>Piskei ha-Rosh</i>, <i>Bava Batra</i> 8:55; <i>Beit Yosef</i>, HM 286:3; <i>SA</i>, HM 286:2; 290:4. Clearly, prior board authorization for such actions would be advisable.</i> As such, the <i>Nitva</i> is monetarily liable for his actions.
<b>Decision</b>
The <i>Nitva</i> is hereby obligated to immediately pay the <i>Tove’a</i> $250,000.

e) Real Estate Brokerage Fee Commission

<b><i>E. Real Estate Brokerage Fee Commission</i></b><br><b><i>Simcha Levy vs. Bayla Srulowitz</i></b>
In June 2005, Simcha Levy, a New York licensed real estate broker (<i>Tove’a</i>) entered into a written brokerage agreement with his client, Bayla Srulowitz (<i>Nitva’at</i>), whereby he would show commercial properties for sale to the <i>Nitva’at</i> and would receive a 7% commission, to be shared equally by the buyer and seller, on any consummated sale. Pursuant to this agreement, the <i>Nitva’at</i> was shown several properties. In May 2006, the <i>Nitva’at</i> purchased one of those properties for three million dollars. The <i>Nitva’at</i> refused to pay the 7% commission on the transaction to the <i>Tove’a</i>.
In August 2005, the <i>Nitva’at</i> placed an ad in the local newspaper that she was interested in selling a piece of residential property. Upon reading the ad, the <i>Tove’a</i> secured a buyer for the <i>Nitva’at</i>’s property and the <i>Nitva’at</i> sold it. Inadvertently, the <i>Tove’a</i> forgot about the transaction, and only recalled it eighteen months later. At that time, the <i>Tove’a</i> billed the <i>Nitva’at</i>, but she refused to pay the broker commission for this transaction.
<b><i>Tove’a</i>’s Claims</b>
Based upon the written agreement executed between the parties, the <i>Tove’a</i> argues that he is entitled to a brokerage fee for his services rendered in the commercial property transaction. Although there was no express brokerage agreement between the parties in the residential property transaction, the <i>Tove’a</i> claims that based upon commercial practice, he is entitled to a broker’s commission fee of 6% for services rendered. In both transactions, the <i>Nitva’at</i> accepted the benefits of the <i>Tove’a</i>’s services, and he is therefore entitled to a commission.
<b><i>Nitva’at</i>’s Counterclaims</b>
Since there was no express written brokerage agreement between the <i>Tove’a</i> and <i>Nitva’at</i> regarding the purchase of the residential property, according to commercial practice, the <i>Tove’a</i> is not entitled to a commission for services rendered. Moreover, even if the <i>Tove’a</i> is entitled to payment, New York State’s statute of limitations for filing a lawsuit regarding unpaid real estate commission is six years; since the time limit for pursuing a remedy has expired, commercial practice dictates that the <i>Tove’a</i> is not entitled to recover his commission.
Regarding the purchase of the commercial property, the <i>Nitva’at</i> admits that the <i>Tove’a</i> provided the services as promised, but she argues that the rate of commission recorded in the brokerage agreement is above the market rate for property sold for three million dollars, and he therefore is entitled to a maximum of 6% brokerage commission.
<b>Discussion</b>
Halakhah dictates that a <i>metavekh</i> or <i>sarsur</i> (broker) is duty-bound to comply with the <i>halakhot</i> of <i>hiyuvim</i> (obligations). Remuneration of a broker for his services is thus guided by the principles of <i>zeh neheneh ve-zeh haser</i> and <i>minhag ha-soherim</i>.
<b>1. <i>Zeh Neheneh Ve-Zeh Haser</i></b>
If one individual benefits from another (<i>zeh neheneh</i>) and the latter sustains a loss due to the benefit conferred (<i>zeh haser</i>), the beneficiary is liable to pay, either because he is viewed as a <i>gazlan</i> (a thief) who is benefiting without remitting payment or a <i>mazik</i> (tortfeasor).<sup class="footnote-marker">161</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Tur</i>, HM 371:10; <i>Or Samea’ach</i>, <i>Hilkhot Nizkei Mamon</i> 1:2.</i> In secular law, this is referred to as “unjust enrichment.” Prior to imposing monetary liability in such a case, the loss caused to the benefactor must first be defined.
Real estate brokers invest their time to identify a property that best fits their clients’ needs and budgets. They obtain listings and execute agreements with owners to place properties for sale with the real estate company, and they compare the listed property with similar properties that recently sold in order to determine the market value of the property. A broker may meet frequently with prospective buyers to discuss and visit available properties. When buying property, brokers arrange for title searches to verify ownership and for meetings between a seller and potential buyer, during which they agree to the details of the transaction. A broker also acts as an intermediary in price negotiations between a buyer and seller, and in some instances may assist in arranging financing from a lender for the prospective buyer, which may be the catalyst “to close the deal.” The broker’s outlay of expenses for advertising, licensing, and transportation, as well as his profit, is covered by his market commission. Accordingly, consummation of a sale without paying the broker his commission constitutes a loss for the broker. As this is an example of <i>zeh neheneh ve-zeh haser</i>, the broker is entitled to reimbursement.
<i>Rema</i> rules that this is true even in the absence of a written or verbal agreement to provide services:
<span class="poetry indentAll"><small>If an individual performs an action or benefit for his friend, he [the beneficiary] cannot say that it was done gratis, as he did not tell him to do it, and he must compensate him.<sup class="footnote-marker">162</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Rema</i>, HM 264:4. See also <i>Rema</i>, HM 246:17 and 363:10. There are exceptions to this rule. <i>Rema</i> contends that certain actions, such as expressing a willingness to serve as an <i>arev</i> (guarantor on a loan) without performing a <i>kinyan</i> (symbolic act of undertaking an obligation), are not actions for which one customarily receives compensation; hence, an <i>arev</i> would not be able to recover a fee for his services from the borrower. See <i>Rema</i>, HM 129:22. For the rationale of <i>Rema</i>’s ruling, see an earlier discussion found in <i>Teshuvot ha-Rosh</i> 64:3.</i></small></span>
According to some authorities, the basis for <i>Rema</i>’s position is a case brought in the Talmud, in which a man planted trees in a field without the owner’s permission and then requested remuneration. The owner, however, repeatedly asserted that he was not interested in the improvements to his property. Subsequently, the owner fenced in the trees, and Rav then instructed him to pay the man who had planted them; by taking efforts to protect the trees, the owner revealed that he was, in fact, interested in them.<sup class="footnote-marker">163</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Bava Metzia</i> 101a. See <i>Bi’ur ha-Gra</i>, HM 185:13.</i>
Numerous legists endorse <i>Rema</i>’s view,<sup class="footnote-marker">164</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Ketzot ha-Hoshen</i>, HM 246:1; <i>Netivot ha-Mishpat</i>, HM 246:5; <i>Teshuvot Maharam Alsheich</i> 70; <i>Teshuvot Maharitz</i>, vol. 2, EH 21; <i>Teshuvot Maharashdam</i>, HM 345; <i>Teshuvot Havot Yair</i> 134; <i>Beit Shmuel</i>, EH 70:28; <i>PDR</i> 11:278, 282–283. Given the apparently contradictory rulings in <i>Rema</i> (see supra n. 2), some <i>Aharonim</i> view this matter as a legal doubt and rule that one cannot extract money from the beneficiary. See <i>Bah</i>, HM 363; <i>Shakh</i>, HM 246:9, 363:13; <i>Teshuvot Noda be-Yehuda</i>, <i>Mahadurah Tanina</i>, HM 34; <i>Teshuvot Hatam Sofer</i>, HM 119; <i>Teshuvot Dovev Meisharim</i> 1:42.</i> arguing that an individual is entitled to a fee for services rendered regardless of whether the individual was duly empowered by another to perform those actions or he performed the services on his own initiative, provided that the beneficiary of the work did not direct him to stop working or communicated to him that he would not receive remuneration for his services.<sup class="footnote-marker">165</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Bava Metzia</i> 101b; <i>Tur</i>, HM 375:1 (in the name of <i>Rosh</i>); <i>Beit Yosef</i>, HM 375:1; <i>Teshuvot Mahari ha-Levi</i> 2:151. Cf. <i>Hiddushei ha-Rashba</i>, <i>Bava Metzia</i> 101b; <i>Hiddushei ha-Ramban</i>, <i>Bava Metzia</i> 101b; <i>Teshuvot Peri Tevu’ah</i> 58; <i>Teshuvot Maharash Engel</i> 3:15.</i>
Alternatively, failure to remit compensation for work rendered is subsumed in the category of “encroaching upon another’s property without permission.”<sup class="footnote-marker">166</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Bi’ur ha-Gra</i>, HM 87:7, 185:3.</i> As such, even in the absence of an agreement between the parties, a broker should receive compensation for his services.<sup class="footnote-marker">167</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Iggerot Moshe</i>, HM 2:49; <i>PDR</i> 13:34; <i>Teshuvot Tzitz Eliezer</i> 15:67; <i>Teshuvot Halikhot Yisrael</i> 1.</i>
In effect, a broker’s performance is a quasi-contract based upon unjust enrichment, <i>zeh neheneh ve-zeh haser</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">168</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Sho’el u-Meishiv</i>, <i>Mahadurah Tilita’ah</i> 3:7; <i>Teshuvot Halikhot Yisrael</i> 14.</i> Hence, if a broker furnished his services and the buyer changed his mind and withdrew from the deal, the buyer is obligated to pay a full commission.<sup class="footnote-marker">169</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Sha’ar Ephraim</i>, HM 150.This position is endorsed by numerous <i>Aharonim</i>, including <i>Bnei Hayyei</i>, HM 185; <i>Misgeret ha-Shulchan</i> 185:21; <i>Mishkenot ha-Ro’im</i>, <i>ot</i> 60, <i>sirsur</i> 120; <i>Teshuvot Mayyim Rabbim</i>, HM 27.</i>
<b>2. <i>Minhag Ha-Soherim</i> (Commercial Practice)</b>
One of the issues at bar is whether New York commercial practice (<i>minhag ha-soherim</i>) allows for the recovery of a brokerage commission in connection with a broker’s efforts to procure a buyer for a residential property. Significantly, the <i>Tove’a</i> does not allege that he had an express agreement with the <i>Nitva’at</i> to perform brokerage services for this property, but he claims that New York commercial practice entitles him to such compensation. The <i>Nitva’at</i>, however, claims that this is not the prevalent commercial practice.
New York case law clearly supports the requirement to pay a broker’s commission, even in the absence of a written agreement. In one case, the court ruled:
<span class="poetry indentAll"><small>[T]he contract of employment may be established either by proof of an express and original agreement that the services should be rendered, or by facts showing, in the absence of such express agreement, a conscious appropriation of the labors of the broker. Indeed, “the contract may be established in some cases by the mere acceptance of the labors of a broker.”<sup class="footnote-marker">170</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Joseph P. Day Realty Corp. v. Chera</i>, 308 A. D. 2d 148,152, 762 N.Y.S. 373, N.Y. AD, 2003. 308 AD 2d 148, 153 [2003].</i></small></span>
This holding is reflective of a series of earlier New York rulings, dating back to 1881, that a broker is entitled to commission based upon facts indicating conscious appropriation of the labors of the broker by the client – that is, an implied contract of employment.<sup class="footnote-marker">171</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sibbald v. Bethlehem Iron Co.</i>, 83 NY 378, 380 (1881); <i>Colvin v. Post Mortgage &amp; Land Co.</i>, 225 NY 510 (1919); <i>Greene v. Hellman</i>, 51 NY 2d 197,205–206 (1980); <i>Gronich &amp; Co. v. 649 Broadway Equities Co.,</i> 169 AD 2d 600 (1991). For other grounds for recovery, see <i>Ficor Inc. v. National Kinney Corp.</i>, 67 AD 2d 659, 659–660 (1979); <i>Helmsley-Spear Inc. v. NY Blood Center</i>, 257 AD 2d 64, 67 (1999).</i>
Although neither party is knowledgeable in New York law, they both expected to resolve their differences based on <i>minhag ha-soherim</i>, which is reflected by the law. The Talmud instructs that “an individual may stipulate contrary to what is written in the Torah” with regard to monetary matters, whereas Jews are not entitled to accept secular law that violates Halakhah.<sup class="footnote-marker">172</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Kiddushin 19b</i>.</i> As such, should parties expect to resolve their matters entailing violations of theft and <i>ribbit</i> (prohibited interest), their arrangement would be halakhically unenforceable. Provided that no <i>issurim</i> (prohibitions) are violated, however, parties’ expectations (<i>umdana</i>) that broker-client relations should be governed by commercial practice are <i>halakhically</i> enforceable.
As <i>Hazon Ish</i> states:
<span class="poetry indentAll"><small>The law of the kingdom [civil government] determinates the expectation of people. Since customarily we abide by the law of the kingdom under certain prescribed conditions, the law influences people who then decide to rely on civil law … Therefore, when we [<i>beit din</i>] apply secular law, we are in actuality following our Halakhah rather than their laws.<sup class="footnote-marker">173</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Hazon Ish</i>, HM, <i>Likkutim</i> 16:1. Interestingly, <i>Hazon Ish</i> finds precedent for his position in the Ra’avad’s ruling cited by <i>Shakh</i>, HM 73:36. However, a review of that ruling indicates that the invoking of the <i>umdana</i> is limited to instances in which the Halakhah fails to address a situation and the resolution of the situation can be realized by invoking an <i>umdana</i>, while Hazon Ish argues that it is inconceivable that there is any gap in Halakhah. Halakhah as a religious-legal system has the capacity to address all situations based on its own sources and rules of interpretation. As such, by citing Ra’avad’s position, Hazon Ish extends Ra’avad’s affirmation of the workings of an <i>umdana</i> even if the Halakhah provides a contrary solution! Others limit the <i>Ra’avad</i>’s ruling to situations in which the Halakhah regarding a particular matter is subject to diverse opinions; see <i>Teshuvot Maharsham</i> 3:128.</i></small></span>
For example, Israeli civil legislation dictates terms of tenancy (the Law for Tenant Protection). <i>R. Shlomo Karelitz</i> argues in the name of his uncle, <i>Hazon Ish</i>, that absent any agreement to the contrary between a landlord and tenant, there is implicit expectation, an <i>umdana</i>, that the terms of tenancy will be based on civil law.<sup class="footnote-marker">174</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Ateret Shlomo</i>, vol. 1, 360.</i> However, in the absence of a clear indication that the parties wish to adopt civil law, the assumption is that Halakhah is the governing system.<sup class="footnote-marker">175</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Hazon Ish</i>, HM, <i>Likkutim</i> 16:1, 5, 9. The question of how non-observant divorcing couples should be treated is beyond the scope of our presentation here.</i>
In effect, the implementation of Halakhah may be predicated upon the expectations of people to follow halakhic tenancy law. The fact that a secular law exists regarding the matter may cause a change in the expectation; although it was originally clear that halakhic tenancy law would be adhered to, there is now a desire to follow secular law. Consequently, the Halakhah changes due to the change in the <i>umdana</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">176</sup><i class="footnote">See Yoezer Ariel, <i>Dinei Borerut</i> [Hebrew] (Jerusalem: 5765), 68. For an extensive list of contemporary <i>Poskim</i> who subscribe to this view, see Dov Frimer, “The Influence of Israeli Law upon Jewish Law” [Hebrew], 39 <i>Jewish Studies</i> (1999), 133.<br>R. Sherman challenges this conclusion, arguing that an <i>umdana</i> rooted in non-Jewish practices is ineffective; see A. Sherman and S. Daichovsky, “The Law of Marital Partnership – Non-Recognition in Jewish Law” [Hebrew], 19 <i>Tehumin</i> (5759), 210–11. Cf. my “Varying Approaches Towards the Division of Matrimonial Property upon Divorce” [Hebrew], 71–72 <i>ha-Darom</i> (5761), n. 46, which provides a list of <i>teshuvot</i> that affirm reliance upon non-Jewish practices in monetary matters. Despite his view of the validity of <i>umdana</i> in this context, R. Sherman legitimates adjudication in <i>beit din</i> according to secular law if the spouses agree that their monetary affairs will be resolved in this manner. R. Sherman and other Israeli <i>dayanim</i> view the civil legislation dealing with monetary relations between spouses as “<i>minhag ha-medinah</i>” (national custom), and a resident of Israel is obligated to comply with such legislation. See Netanya Regional Rabbinical Court, File no. 764411/1, <i>Ploni</i> v. <i>Plonit</i>, Oct. 3, 2010.</i> Invoking this <i>umdana</i> regarding tenancy law has been endorsed by other authorities as well.<sup class="footnote-marker">177</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Minhat Yitzhak</i> 2:86; <i>PDR</i> 16:312. Others focus upon the assumption that the parties wished to follow common practice, which is to adopt secular law, rather than invoking the notion of <i>umdana</i>. See <i>Teshuvot Maharash</i> 6:19; <i>Iggerot Moshe</i>, HM 1:72. R. Moshe Sofer and R. Z. Goldberg reject imparting validity of secular law based upon <i>minhag</i>; see <i>Teshuvot Hatam Sofer</i>, YD 314; <i>PDR</i> 14:334, 346.<br>If the prevailing custom is that spousal property assets are to be divided equitably based on civil law, need the parties be aware what the content of the secular law states regarding this matter? This question is subject to debate. See <i>Teshuvot ha-Rosh</i> 68:12; <i>Tur</i> and <i>SA</i>, HM 61:5; <i>Shakh</i>, HM 42:36, 61:9, 71:33; <i>Bi’ur ha-Gra</i>, ad loc. 29, EH 66:48; <i>Teshuvot Re’im</i> 16; <i>Pithei Hoshen</i>, <i>Hilkhot Sekhirut</i>, p. 149. If the parties must know of the usage, if one party claims that he was unfamiliar with the expectation of following secular law regarding dividing up marital assets, then it becomes the other party’s responsibility to demonstrate that such an expectation in fact exists. See <i>Shakh</i>, HM 42:36, 61:5, in the name of <i>Rashba</i>; <i>Erekh Shai</i>, EH 50:7; Michoel Wygoda, <i>Agency Law: Section 5</i>, Israel Ministry of Justice [Hebrew], n. 390. If everyone follows the <i>nohag</i>, claiming that one does not subscribe to it will be no defense. However, in monetary matters, should there be a minority who fails to follow the <i>nohag</i>, such a claim will be accepted; see <i>Teshuvot Nahal Yitzchak</i>, vol. 1, HM 61.</i>
The underlying rationale for legitimizing this approach is that the parties essentially agreed to resolve their issues according to their expectations, which in our situation means following secular law. In the words of <i>R. Feinstein</i>:
<span class="poetry indentAll"><small>They have operated on the strength of the custom of the region, and that is equivalent to having specifically agreed to this arrangement.<sup class="footnote-marker">178</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Iggerot Moshe</i>, HM 1:72.</i></small></span>
While Halakhah itself is immutable, the fact that an <i>umdana</i> can serve as a basis for adopting civil law over the Halakhah in monetary matters is essentially built into the Halakhah itself.<sup class="footnote-marker">179</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Kiddushin</i> 19b.</i>
Given that the custom of the locale reflects civil law, the parties’ expectations to follow that law is analogous to having accepted such an arrangement between themselves. In other words, the assessed expectation of the parties is no different than parties executing a contract and providing for a choice of law provision stating that their differences will be resolved by applying secular law.
With regard to the sale of the residential property in our case, there is ample evidence in the record that the <i>Nitva’at</i> accepted the benefit of the <i>Tove’a</i>’s services and that the <i>Tove’a</i>’s efforts were instrumental in consummating the sale. As <i>R. Yisrael Grossman</i> argues, the negotiations between the broker and the seller constitute <i>“hoda’at ba’al din</i>” (admission) that the <i>Nitva’at</i> was interested in using a broker and gave her implicit consent to his appointment, and she is therefore obligated to pay.<sup class="footnote-marker">180</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Halikhot Yisrael</i> 39.</i> Alternatively, as <i>R.Ya’akov Blau</i> contends, we invoke <i>umdana</i>, the expectations of the parties, which entail following <i>minhag ha-soherim</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">181</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Pithei Hoshen</i>, <i>Sekhirut</i> 336.</i> Since the commercial practice in New York, as reflected in the law, is to require brokerage fees even in the absence of a written agreement, the <i>Tove’a</i> is entitled to a commission for his efforts.
<b>3. The Amount of a Brokerage Commission</b>
When the parties’ agreement does not specify the specific amount of the commission or, as in our case, when no agreement was executed regarding brokerage services, how can the rate be determined? <i>R. Yaakov ha-Levi Ettinger</i> (seventeenth century Salonika) writes that the answer depends on whether real estate matters fall under the purview of the Talmudic rule of <i>dina de-malkhuta dina</i> (the law of the kingdom is the law).<sup class="footnote-marker">182</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Nedarim</i> 28a; <i>Gittin</i> 10b; <i>Bava Kama</i> 113a–b; <i>Bava Batra</i> 44b–45a.</i> In the opinion of <i>Rema</i>, all matters that are “<i>le-takanat bnei ha-medinah</i>,” for the benefit of the citizenry – understood by <i>Rema</i> as referring to any issues relating to social interaction – may be governed by civil law.<sup class="footnote-marker">183</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Darkhei Moshe</i>, HM 369; <i>Rema</i>, HM 73:14, 369:11.</i> Vigorously disagreeing with <i>Rema</i>, <i>Shakh</i> argues that if the particular secular law contradicts Halakhah, <i>dina de-malkhuta dina</i> does not apply<i>.</i><sup class="footnote-marker">184</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Shakh</i>, HM 73:39. Although normative Halakhah today is to follow <i>Shakh</i>’s view, some Rishonim and the majority of Aharonim adopt the <i>Rema</i>’s position. See <i>Aliyot de-Rabbeinu Yonah</i>, <i>Bava Batra</i> 55a; <i>Teshuvot ha-Rashba ha-Meyuhasot le-Ramban 22</i>; <i>Levush Ir Shushan</i>, HM 369:11; <i>Teshuvot Divrei Emet 12</i>; <i>Teshuvot Dovev Meisharim</i> 1:76.</i> In our instance<i>,</i> there is no conflict between Halakhah and civil law regarding this matter, and <i>Shakh</i> would therefore concur that the rule is applicable. Hence, in our case, the <i>Tove’a</i> is entitled to a commission. <i>R. Ettinger</i> notes that even if the civil law were to contradict Halakhah, given that there is halakhic-legal doubt regarding whether we should follow the view of <i>Rema</i> or <i>Shakh</i>, we may resolve the rate of a brokerage commission based upon <i>minhag</i> (commercial custom).<sup class="footnote-marker">185</sup><i class="footnote">Other <i>Aharonim</i> have the same hesitancy; see <i>Teshuvot Hatam Sofer</i>, HM 65; <i>Erekh Shai</i>, HM 73:14; <i>Teshuvot Havatzelet ha-Saharon</i>, HM 8.</i> Many decisors have endorsed this opinion regarding a real estate broker and <i>shadkhan</i> (marriage broker) alike.<sup class="footnote-marker">186</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Maharshach</i> 1:79; <i>Teshuvot Ginat Veradim</i>, HM 1:1; <i>Teshuvot va-Yeshev Moshe</i> 38; <i>Mishpat Shalom</i>, HM 185; <i>Teshuvot Beit Yitzhak</i>, EH 1:115; <i>Teshuvot be-Tzel ha-Hokhmah</i> 3:28;<i>Teshuvot Panim Meirot</i> 2:63; <i>Teshuvot Rabaz</i>, EH 62; <i>PDR</i> 11:278, 284; <i>Iggerot Moshe</i>, HM 2:57.</i>
Based upon the foregoing, the <i>Tove’a</i> is entitled to receive the customary commission in New York at the time of the sale of the residential property – 6%.
<b>4. Status of a Real Estate Agreement</b>
Before proceeding to discuss whether the <i>Nitva’at</i> is obligated to pay the agreed upon 7% rate of commission on the commercial property, we must first establish the halakhic status of the real estate agreement.
Two components are required in order to undertake an obligation – <i>kinyan</i> (a symbolic act) and <i>gemirat da’at</i> (firm resolve of the parties).<sup class="footnote-marker">187</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Hazon Ish</i>, HM 22; Yehezkel Abramsky, <i>Dinei Mamonot</i> (Bnei Brak: 5731).</i> In this case, a <i>kinyan sudar</i> – handing a handkerchief or another article by one party undertaking the obligation to the other party – would not have been effective in validating the agreement, as a <i>kinyan</i> cannot obligate a party in a <i>davar she-lo ba la-olam</i>. Since the commission was not yet in existence at the time of the agreement, a <i>kinyan</i> cannot obligate the <i>Nitva’at</i> to pay the brokerage fee.<sup class="footnote-marker">188</sup><i class="footnote"><i>SA</i>, HM 60:6, 209:4–7. Cf. <i>Ketzot ha-Hoshen</i> 332:6.</i>
Furthermore, the terms of the agreement in question are as follows:
<span class="poetry indentAll"><small>Owner agrees to pay a commission of 7% of the sale price if the broker procures a buyer who is ready, willing, and able to purchase the premises … If the property is sold … the commission provided for herein shall be due and payable …</small></span>
Numerous authorities view this sort of language as a promise to execute a future action. This constitutes a <i>kinyan devarim</i>, which fails to create an enforceable duty.<sup class="footnote-marker">189</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Bava Batra</i> 3b, 148b; <i>SA</i>, HM 245:1, 253:3; <i>Teshuvot Mahairval</i> 1:59; <i>Teshuvot Lehem Rav</i> 147; <i>PDR</i> 11:131.
