See my “Breach of a Promise to Marry,” 17 <i>Jewish Law Annual</i> (2007), 267.</i> Only an agreement that incorporates language of <i>hithayvut</i> (obligation), such as “I obligate myself to pay” or “I obligate myself now to pay,” creates a monetary obligation, essentially transforming the parties into debtors.<sup class="footnote-marker">190</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sma</i>, HM 243:12.</i> Hence, it would seem that a clause providing for payment of a commission in the future, even if it reflects the market rate, is unenforceable.
However, Halakhah recognizes the validity of a contract by virtue of <i>kinyan situmta</i>, the presence of a “seal.”<sup class="footnote-marker">191</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Maharshach</i> 3:8; <i>Teshuvot Hatam Sofer</i>, HM 66; <i>Teshuvot Maharsham</i> 5:45; <i>Mishpat Shalom</i>, HM 194:2; <i>Teshuvot Ahiezer</i> 3:79.</i> If an agreement is acceptable according to commercial practice, there is implied <i>gemirat da’at</i> to undertake the obligation in accordance with the <i>minhag</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">192</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot ha-Rosh</i> 55:10; <i>Teshuvot ha-Rashba</i> 2:268; <i>Teshuvot Maharsham</i>, HM 380; <i>Teshuvot Hatam Sofer</i>, YD 314, HM 66; <i>Teshuvot Sho’el u-Meishiv</i>, <i>Mahadurah Kama</i> 2:39.</i> While agreements implementing a <i>kinyan sudar</i> are valid <i>min ha-din</i> (based upon the formal laws of obligations), contracts characterized by language considered <i>kinyan devarim</i> – such as surety agreements, preliminary agreements, contracts to sell a house, arbitration agreements, and divorce agreements – are binding by virtue of <i>minhag ha-soherim</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">193</sup><i class="footnote"><i>SA</i>, HM 176:10, 218:19, 229:2, 230:10, 232:6, 330:5, 331:12; <i>Teshuvot ha-Rema</i> 19–20; <i>Rema</i>, HM 72:5. According to <i>Shulhan Arukh</i> and <i>Rema</i>, a <i>minhag</i> regarding monetary matters that is unaccompanied by rabbinic or communal sanction has an independent status, even if the result will be to extract money from <i>muhzakim</i> (those in present possession). See <i>PDR</i> 16:40, 16:133, 18:354.</i> A real estate brokerage agreement is no different than these agreements. Consequently, a contractual provision providing for a <i>davar she-lo ba la-olam</i>, such as a payment of a commission in the future, is valid and binding.<sup class="footnote-marker">194</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Rema</i>, HM 129:6; <i>Sma</i>, HM 129:15; <i>Shakh</i>, HM 129:13.</i>
In addition to the halakhic requirements for solemnizing an agreement between parties discussed above<b>, </b>Halakhah requires that the terms of an agreement incorporate certain stipulations, referred to as the “<i>mishpetei ha-tenai’im</i>” (laws of conditions), in order to be binding.<sup class="footnote-marker">195</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Tur</i>, HM 207:3; <i>Netivot ha-Mishpat</i>, HM 207:7; <i>SA</i>, EH 38:2.</i> In our case, the brokerage agreement should have been drafted in the following fashion:
<span class="poetry indentAll"><small>If the broker renders his services properly, he is entitled to a commission; if the broker fails to render his services properly, he is not entitled to a commission.</small></span>
Nevertheless, <i>Shulhan Arukh</i> concludes that the parties’ intent to establish terms of a commercial contract overrides any failure to comply with the <i>mishpetei ha-tenai’im</i>. Accordingly, notwithstanding the agreement’s failure to properly employ the formal language of stipulation mandated under Halakhah, the present agreement is binding upon the parties.<sup class="footnote-marker">196</sup><i class="footnote"><i>SA</i>, HM 61:16; <i>Bi’ur ha-Gra</i>, HM 61:39; <i>Sma</i>, HM 61:26.</i>
<b>5. The Validity of a Mutually Agreed upon Commission Above the Market Rate</b>
In the contract between the <i>Tove’a</i> and <i>Nitva’at</i> regarding the commercial property, the parties agreed that the brokerage commission would be 7% of the purchase price. Given that the rate of compensation is linked to local custom standards, the <i>Nitva’at</i> alleges that the mutually agreed upon rate is above the market standard, which is between four to six percent. Consequently, she should be required to pay less than the rate stipulated in the contract.
Relying upon the <i>Maharam</i>’s ruling, <i>Rema</i>, <i>Shakh</i>, and others claim that a broker is limited to receiving a commission that reflects the market rate because the client can always allege that he was jesting (“<i>hashata</i>”) and never seriously intended to pay the higher rate stipulated.<sup class="footnote-marker">197</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Mordekhai</i>, <i>Bava Kama</i> 172 (in the name of <i>Maharam</i>); <i>Teshuvot Maharam mi-Rotenburg</i> (Berlin edition), 498, 499, 952; <i>Rema</i>, HM 264:7; <i>Shakh</i>, HM 264:15; <i>Teshuvot Maharil Hadashot</i> 158; <i>Teshuvot Beit Yitzhak</i>, HM 76. Many sources in this section have been culled from Y. Goldberg, “A Broker’s Claim for Wages Exceeding the Market Rate” [Hebrew], 8 <i>Shurat ha-Din</i> (5763), 183.<br>This position is corroborated by the <i>halakhah</i> that if a buyer provides his agent with an advance prior to the agent’s completion of his services and afterwards refuses to pay the balance because he argues that the balance exceeds the market rate, he is exempt from remitting the balance. See <i>SA</i>, HM 81:26; <i>Sma</i>, HM 81:53; <i>Sha’ar ha-Mishpat</i> 81:16.</i> In fact, even if it is clear that the client was serious in offering the higher rate, he is still only obligated to pay the market rate.<sup class="footnote-marker">198</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot ha-Rosh</i> 64:3. Cf. <i>Ketzot ha-Hoshen</i> 81:4; <i>Mahaneh Ephraim</i>, <i>Hilkhot Sechirut</i> 15.</i> If, however, the client voluntarily paid the promised rate, he has no grounds for advancing a claim of “jesting” and the entire sum belongs to the broker.<sup class="footnote-marker">199</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot ha-Rashba</i> 1240; <i>SA</i>, HM 264:8.</i>
The majority of authorities, however, do not accept this view and maintain that a broker is entitled to receive the agreed upon amount even if the rate exceeds the market standard.<sup class="footnote-marker">200</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Maharach Or Zaru’a</i> 3; <i>Teshuvot Maharival</i> 1:99; <i>Teshuvot Divrei Ribbot</i> 396; <i>Teshuvot Mishpat Tzedek</i> 3:71; <i>Teshuvot Hikrei Lev</i>, HM 2:135; <i>Imrei Yosher</i>, <i>Hilkhot Dayanim</i> 18.</i> Indeed, even the authorities who contend that a broker’s remuneration cannot exceed the customary rate agree that once the parties sign a contract detailing the agreed upon commission, the client is bound by those terms. The execution of a <i>kinyan</i> demonstrates the <i>gemirat da’at</i> of the parties, precluding the use of the “jesting” argument.<sup class="footnote-marker">201</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Rosh</i>, supra n. 32; <i>Shakh</i>, HM 81:6; <i>Netivot ha-Mishpat</i>, HM 264:8; <i>Ketzot ha-Hoshen</i> 129:8, 264:4; <i>Teshuvot Shevut Ya’akov</i> 2:157; <i>Mishpat Shalom</i>, HM 185.</i> Furthermore, if local practice is that the client must remit whatever amount he promised, he must do so even in the absence of a halakhic agreement; the existence of a <i>minhag</i> entails that there is automatically <i>gemirat da’at</i> to comply with one’s duty to remit payment.<sup class="footnote-marker">202</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Edut be-Yehosef</i> 2:35; <i>Teshuvot Maharshach</i>, supra n. 31.</i>
As we saw above, although the written agreement in this case cannot be validated by way of <i>kinyan sudar</i> or the <i>kinyan devarim</i> within it, it is valid as per the <i>minhag ha-soherim</i>. Since there was <i>gemirat da’at</i> at the time of its writing, all views would agree to the validity of the parties’ written agreement to pay a brokerage commission rate that exceeds the customary rate.
<b>6. Statute of Limitations Regarding Outstanding Debt</b>
Even if the <i>Tove’a</i> is entitled to a commission for the sale of the residential property, the <i>Nitva’at</i> argues that the statute of limitations in the state of New York precludes his collection of those funds. In New York, after the expiration of six years, the injured party loses the right to file a lawsuit seeking monetary damages or other relief. The <i>Nitva’at</i> argues that since the <i>Tove’a</i> filed his suit after that time had elapsed, commercial practice dictates that he is precluded from receiving any relief.
Lapse of time may, indeed, be sufficient basis to impugn the credibility of evidence in support of a claim, leading a <i>beit din</i> to believe that fraud is at play and therefore to find on behalf of the defendant.<sup class="footnote-marker">203</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot ha-Rosh</i> 68:20, 85:10; <i>Tur</i>, HM 61:16–17; <i>SA</i>, HM 61:9, 98:2; <i>Teshuvot Maharik</i>, <i>shoresh</i> 190; <i>Teshuvot Divrei Rivot</i> 109; <i>Teshuvot ha-Mabit</i> 2:142; <i>Teshuvot Maharashdam</i>, HM 73; <i>Teshuvot Torat Hayyim</i>, HM 1:44.</i> However, in this case, the <i>Nitva’at</i> does not deny the existence of the debt and there is no reason to be suspicious of the reliability of the <i>Tove’a</i>’s claim. With few exceptions,<sup class="footnote-marker">204</sup><i class="footnote">Exceptions include the statute of limitations regarding a widow who failed to advance a claim for payment of her <i>ketuba</i> or support for an extended period of time or a daughter who failed to claim one tenth of her father’s estate for her wedding; see <i>Ketuvot</i> 68b; 96a,104a. In monetary affairs, if a <i>dayan</i> accepted a bribe and the giver does not claim its return, some construe his silence as waiving his right of recovery; see <i>Bah</i>, HM 9:2; <i>Levush</i>, HM 9:1.</i> a lapse of time in the absence of fraud does not curtail the right to advance a claim for an outstanding debt. Thus:
<span class="poetry indentAll"><small>A creditor may recover a debt at any time, even if it has not been mentioned.<sup class="footnote-marker">205</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Tosefta Ketuvot</i> 12:3.</i></small></span>
<i>Shulhan Arukh</i> rules:
<span class="poetry indentAll"><small>When the creditor submits a note of indebtedness which is authenticated, the <i>beit din</i> says to the borrower, “Pay!” Even if there was a lapse of a few years and he did not advance a claim, we do not say that he waived it.<sup class="footnote-marker">206</sup><i class="footnote"><i>SA</i>, HM 98:1. See also <i>SA</i>, EH 101:3; <i>Rema</i>, HM 163:2.</i></small></span>
Thus, unless a creditor has explicitly waived his right of collection,<sup class="footnote-marker">207</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Shakh</i>, HM 98:2; <i>Sma</i>, HM 98: 2; <i>Yam Shel Shlomo</i>, <i>Bava Kama</i> 2:17; <i>Teshuvot Helkat Ya’akov</i> 3:134.</i> the consensus of authorities is to reject a statute of limitations regarding unpaid debts.<sup class="footnote-marker">208</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot ha-Rashba</i> 2:26; <i>Teshuvot ha-Rivash</i> 404; <i>Teshuvot Tashbetz</i> 3:85; <i>Teshuvot Maharam Alsheich</i> 17; <i>Teshuvot Devar Shmuel</i> 41; <i>Ketzot ha-Hoshen</i> 104:2; <i>Teshuvot Avodat ha-Gershuni</i> 7; <i>Teshuvot Maharsham</i> 2:15; <i>Teshuvot Imrei Yosher</i> 1:107; <i>Teshuvot Sho’el u-Meishiv</i>, <i>Mahadurah Kama</i>, 1:50.<br>However, if initially the creditor’s intent is to waive the outstanding debt and subsequently he changes his mind, the debt is considered waived. See <i>Netivot ha-Mishpat</i> 12:5; <i>Teshuvot Havazelet ha-Sharon</i>, vol. 2, HM 30.</i>
In a locale in which there is no existent <i>minhag</i> to pay brokerage fees, there may be doubt as to whether a time lapse should be viewed as <i>mehilah</i>, a waiver of the debt.<sup class="footnote-marker">209</sup><i class="footnote">R. Avraham Alkali (nineteenth century Hungary) accordingly raises such a doubt; see <i>Zekhor le-Avraham</i>, HM, vol. 3, <i>erekh sakhar</i>.</i> However, today, when it is common practice to pay for such services, <i>R. Ovadiah Yosef</i> argues that in the absence of an explicit waiver, the right of collection is not curtailed due to lapse of time.<sup class="footnote-marker">210</sup><i class="footnote"><i>PDR</i> 13:34, 43.</i>
Although Halakhah itself does not recognize a statute of limitations in the case of outstanding debt, both <i>Rishonim</i> and <i>Aharonim</i> argue that if the common commercial practice is to recognize a statue of limitations, the custom is valid.<sup class="footnote-marker">211</sup><i class="footnote">For an extensive review of this issue, see M. Elon, “The Statute of Limitations in Jewish Law” [Hebrew], 14 <i>Ha-Praklit</i> (5718),179, 243.</i> For example, <i>Rosh</i> states:
<span class="poetry indentAll"><small>If an announcement appears, that anyone who has a claim and lien regarding land should now inform us and advance his claim, if he remains silent without protest, all his rights will be null, since this is the practice and this is the law of the land.<sup class="footnote-marker">212</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot ha-Rosh</i> 79:13.</i></small></span>
<i>Shulhan Arukh</i> endorses this view as well.<sup class="footnote-marker">213</sup><i class="footnote"><i>SA</i>, HM 104:2.</i> Such practices continued through the nineteenth century and were supported by some decisors.<sup class="footnote-marker">214</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Mizgeret ha-Shulhan</i>, HM 61:16; <i>Ateret Tzvi</i>, HM 61:16; <i>Teshuvot Shevut Ya’akov</i> 3:182; <i>Netivot ha-Mishpat</i> 61:18; <i>Kesef ha-Kedoshim</i>, HM 61:9.</i> For these decisors, the statute of limitations is validated based upon common practice in the locale (<i>minhag</i>), a communal ordinance (<i>takanat ha-kahal</i>), or civil law (<i>dina de-malkhuta</i>).
This view is subject to serious challenge. <i>Minhag</i> only has the ability to override Halakhah in monetary matters, not those involving <i>issurim</i> (ritual prohibitions);<sup class="footnote-marker">215</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Bava Metzia</i> 83a–b; Yerushalmi, <i>Bava Metzia</i> 7:1.</i> we are powerless to permit what Halakhah prohibited. For example, a <i>minhag</i> not to cancel debts in the sabbatical year has no validity, as there is an <i>issur</i> to collect such debts.<sup class="footnote-marker">216</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot ha-Rosh</i> 64:4.</i> Similarly, the Jewish community is only empowered to legislate matters in the realm of <i>mammon</i>, not <i>issur</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">217</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Tashbetz</i> 2:132; <i>Teshuvot ha-Rivash</i> 178, 185.</i> Thus, since it is prohibited to imprison a Jew due to his delinquency in repaying a debt, any communal legislation sanctioning this form of punishment is invalid.<sup class="footnote-marker">218</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot ha-Rosh</i> 68:10; <i>Teshuvot ha-Rivash</i> 484; <i>SA</i>, HM 97:15. Although a community is empowered to legislate contrary to Halakhah in monetary matters, it may not pass legislation that entails appropriating a citizen’s assets, resulting in profit for one citizen and financial loss for another. That limitation, however, applies only if the legislation is directed against a particular individual(s). If the legislation is passed for the benefit of the community and an individual subsequently incurs a loss due to its passage, the ordinance is binding. See <i>Nahalat Shiva</i> 27:13; <i>Teshuvot She’erit Yosef</i> 9; <i>Rema</i>, HM 2; <i>Teshuvot Maharit</i> 1:237. Accordingly, were it not for the element of <i>issur</i> involved, the communal passage of a statute of limitations addressing the community at large would be valid.</i> Finally, although Halakhah recognizes civil legal provisions in many monetary matters, should a civil law entail an element of <i>issur,</i> Halakhah will decline to impart recognition to the particular secular law.<sup class="footnote-marker">219</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Tashbetz</i> 1:158.</i> For example, if there were to be a civil law allowing for the collection of <i>ribbit</i> (interest), one would be proscribed from following it.<sup class="footnote-marker">220</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Avnei Nezer</i>, YD 133.</i>
A broker is considered a <i>kablan</i> (a contractor for certain services)<sup class="footnote-marker">221</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Maggid Mishnah</i>, <i>Sekhirut</i> 9:4; <i>Rema</i>, HM 333:5.</i> for the real estate services rendered.<sup class="footnote-marker">222</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Halikhot Yisrael</i> 1–2.</i> By revoking his legal right to collect an outstanding debt, we would be sanctioning the act of <i>gezelah</i>, misappropriating funds from a creditor. Although the matter at hand involves money, it is not a monetary matter; it involves the <i>issur</i> of thievery and therefore cannot be overridden by <i>minhag</i>, <i>takanat ha-kahal</i>, or <i>dina de-malkhuta</i>.
Accordingly, <i>R. Ben Tzion Uziel</i> levels criticism against those arbiters who invoke these factors in validating a statute of limitations:
<span class="poetry indentAll"><small>Our matter is different from a custom adopted wherein we accept modes of <i>kinyanim</i>, such as <i>situmta</i> or <i>minhag ha-sokherim</i>, which are biding pursuant to custom. There is no sanction to steal due to customary practice … This involves the law of theft, and theft is not permitted due to custom.<sup class="footnote-marker">223</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Mishpetei Uziel</i>, HM 28.</i></small></span>
Based upon the foregoing, we find that the <i>Tove’a</i>’s right to recover his compensation remains until this very day and was never extinguished due to the lapse of time.
<b>Decision</b>
1. The <i>Nitva’at</i> is hereby obligated to pay a 7% commission as a brokerage fee for the sale of the commercial property.
2. The <i>Nitva’at</i> is hereby obligated to pay a 6% brokerage commission for the sale of the residential property.

f) Drafting a Halakhic Will

<b><i>F. Drafting a Halakhic Will</i></b><br><b><i>Reuben Levy v. Rachel Singer and Leah Shlanger</i></b>
The parties signed an arbitration agreement dated October 29, 2007 before the <i>beit din</i> to resolve their differences and disputes in reference to the estate and inheritance of the late R. Simeon Levy. At the hearing, the following document – henceforth referred to as the alleged <i>shtar matanah</i> (gift document) – was submitted as evidence. Prepared in 1985 by the testator, with an addendum signed in 2007, it purports to be an allocation by the late R. Levy of his assets among his three children, his son and two daughters.
<b>The Will</b>
חתימת ידי על שטר מתנה זו תעיד עלי כמאה עדים איך שהיום יום ראשון לס’ כי תשא, שהוא עשרה ימים לחודש אדר תשמ״ה, אני מתחיל לכתוב כתב צוואה זו בדעת צלולה שעזה״י והיא צוואה לבני ובנותי שליט״א ומהיום על אחר אריכת ימי השנותי על כל הירושה שתשאר אחרי בין נכסי דניידי ובין נכסי דלא ניידי, בין אותם נכסים שיש כבר תחת ידי ובין אותם נכסים שיבואו תחת ידי עד עת קצי על הכל אני מצוה בשטר זה שיחולו כנזכר לעיל בשורה הראשונה.
א.	השני בתים שיש לי בסטעטן איילענד יתחלקו בשוה כדי שווים בין בני ושתי בנותי.
ב.	כל הכסף המונח בבנק וכן כל הסטקס ובונדס יתחלקו שוב בשוה בין הבן והבנות או בדרך החלוקה של הנ״ל או ע״י מכירה, ודמיהם יחלקו ביניהם כנ״ל.
ג.	הבית החיים ישאר ביד בני (ובכתב יד למעלה מהמלה המודפס ״ובנותי״).
ד.	כל הספרים יחלקו שוה בשוה בין הבן והבנות, כפי ערכם.
ה.	העמודים של מורה נבוכים שנכתבו והודפסו בברצלונה ששילמתי בעבורם אלף דולר לכל עלה יתחלקו בין הבן והבנות חוץ מאיזה עלים לכל אחד מחתני יחיו (לא ברור) שלשה לחתניי.
ו.	בשנת תש״מ קניתי עשרה מקומות בביה״ח בבית שמש. מקום אחד מכרתי לאחין, יענקל. מט’ הנשארים יש לי ״קניני שטרות״ מונחים בכספת של הבנק ואני מבקש מבני שאחרי מוע״ש להוליך אותי שמה באחד המקומות הנ״ל. ואם אפשר שבני יחיה ילוה אותי עד שמה, מה טוב. שאר המקומות יניחו רק לבני משפחתנו הי״ו.
ז.	כל כלי הכסף יתחלקו שוה בשוה בין הבן והבנות כפי שויים וערכם.
ח.	אם יהיו ח״ו איזה חילוקי דעות בין הבנים והבנות באיזה ענין הנוגע להירושה אזי אני מרשה בכל כחי ודעתי את בני הרב ר’ ראובן להכריע בכל דבר גדול וקטן ואליו ולהכרעתו ישמעון. ואני מבקש בכל לשון של בקשה שלא יהיו שום חילוקי דעות בדבר מן הדברים רק הכל יהיה בשלום ובדעה אחת שזהו כבודי וכבוד המשפחה וכבוד אמכם תחי’. וכבר שנתברר שאצל משפחות כבודות היו מתעקשים ולא חסו על כבוד הנפטר איך היו לחרפה ולבזות של כל המשפחה.
ט.	אני מצוה לבנותי תחי’ להזהר בצניעות יתרה בלבושי בגדים וביחוד בשערות ראש בלי שום פשרות וזה עניי מכל עמלי להשאיר בן ובנות בעוה״ז הולך והולכות בדרכי אבוה״ק . . . שעל זה נתתי את נפשי בעזהש״י לחנכם ולגדלם על דרכי התורה והיראה במסורת אבותינו ורבותינו.
חתימה: הרב שמעון בן יוסף לוי, י’ אדר תשמ״ה
כל הנ״ל הוא רצוני האמיתי ושיגיע המתנה מהיום ושעה אחת קודם מיתתי לבני ולבנותי בלי שום יוצא מן הכלל ובתיקון חז״ל שו״ע והפוסקים. והריני מוסר מודעה שהיות שנאבד הצוואה המקורי ע״כ הנני מאשר וחותם על העתק זה ומבטל כל שאר צוואות שנכתבו כבר בנוגע לגבי הירושה והנכסים. וא״א לבטל צוואה זו רק בפני בני ובנותי, וחתימת ידי תעיד עלי על כל הנ״ל בהודאה גמורה בפני שתי עדים החתומים מטה ונגמר בקאג״ס ובשאר קנינים המועילים חתימת שמי היום בדעה צלולה, יום ה’, פרשת בוא, ו’ שבט תשס״ז, 25 ינואר 2007.
חתימה: הרב שמעון בן יוסף לוי, פרשת בוא, ו’ שבט תשס״ז, 25 ינואר 2007 חתימה: יוסף כהן, עד לפי הכתובת חתימה: אריה רבינוביץ, עד לפי הכתובת
My signature on this gift award shall serve as proof upon myself as one hundred witnesses, whereas that today, Sunday of the Torah reading <i>Ki Tissa</i>, 10 Adar, 5745 (March 3, 1985), I begin the writing of this will, with a clear state of mind with the assistance of the Almighty, which is a will for my son and daughters, may they live and be well, and [is to be effective] from today and after demise on the entire estate that will be left after me, both moveable property and real property, both such assets that I already have in my possession and such assets as may come into my possession until my demise. I am hereby offering my desire in this document, which shall become effective as mentioned above in the first line.
<b>1. </b>The two houses I have here in Staten Island shall be equally distributed according to their value between my son and daughters.
<b>2. </b>All of the funds in the bank, as well as all of the stocks and bonds, shall be equally distributed between my son and daughters, either by distributing the above or by selling [them], and the proceeds shall be distributed amongst them as mentioned above.
<b>3. </b>The cemetery shall remain the possession of my son [handwritten word on top of typewritten text reads: <i>u-venotai</i>, “and my daughters”].
<b>4. </b>All my religious books shall be equally distributed amongst my son and daughters, in accordance with their value.
<b>5. </b>The pages of an illustrated copy of the <i>Moreh Nevukhim</i>, written and published in Barcelona, for which I paid one thousand dollars for each flip page, shall be distributed amongst my son and daughters, except for a few flip pages to my sons-in-law [illegible] three to my sons-in-law.
<b>6. </b>In 1980, I acquired ten plots at the Beth Shemesh Cemetery in Israel. I sold one plot to my nephew, Yankel. For the remaining nine, I have “contracts of sale” in my bank safe, and I request of my son that after my demise I shall be taken there, to be buried in one of the aforementioned plots. If at all possible, my son should escort me until there; that is my preference. The rest of the plots shall be left for family members.
<b>7. </b>All the silverware shall be distributed equally among my son and daughters according to their worth and value.
<b>8. </b>In the event there will be any differences of opinion amongst my son and daughters on any matter that relates to the estate, I authorize, with all of my powers and my mind, my son R. Reuben to decide on every significant or insignificant matter; he and his determinations should be followed. And by utilizing every possible expression of appeal, I request that there shall be no differences of opinion on any matter of the matters, but that everything shall be peaceful and unanimous, for this is my dignity and the dignity of the family. It has already turned out that in respectful families people were stubborn and they did not care about the deceased’s dignity, and the entire family became disgraceful and shameful.
<b>9. </b>I direct my daughters to be careful in [exhibiting] an abundance of modesty in their attire, and especially regarding the hair of the head, without any compromises; this is my compensation for all my effort, to leave behind my son and daughters in this world, who proceed in the path of my holy forefathers of blessed memory, for which I have devoted myself to the Grace of the Almighty, to educate them and to raise them on the path of the Torah and fear of God.
<b>Affixed Signature:</b> R. Simeon ben Yosef Levy, 10 Adar 5745.
<b><i>The Addendum</i></b>
All the above is my true will, and the gift shall arrive today and a moment before my death to all my sons and daughters without any exception according to the usage of our Torah scholars, <i>Shulhan Arukh</i> and decisors. I hereby declare (<i>mesirat moda’ah</i>) that since the original will is lost, therefore I confirm and sign off on this copy and nullify retroactively all wills that were written regarding my inheritance and assets, and one can nullify this will only in the presence of my son and daughters. My signature shall attest to all the above as a full admission (<i>hoda’ah gemurah</i>) in the presence of the two signed witnesses below, and it is executed with a <i>kinyan sudar</i> and other effective <i>kinyanim</i>. Today, I have signed with sound mind, Thursday, <i>Parashat Bo</i>, 6 Shevat, 5767, January 25, 2007.
<b>Affixed signature</b>: R. Simeon ben Yosef Levy, Thursday, <i>Parashat Bo</i>, 6 Shevat, 5767, January 25, 2007.
<b>Affixed signature</b>: Joseph Cohen, witness with an address at …
<b>Affixed signature</b>: Aryeh Rabinowitz, witness with an address at …
<b><i>Tove’a</i>’s Claims</b>
Mr. Reuben Levy maintains that his father’s entire estate and inheritance should be given to him, in accordance with the Torah law of inheritance. Since his sister, Rachel Levy, is the firstborn of the family, no claim of <i>bekhorah</i> (right of the firstborn male to a double portion) is advanced by Reuben.
The plaintiff claims that the alleged <i>shtar matanah</i> is a forgery. Moreover, even if it is authentic, given that this alleged <i>shtar matanah</i> was neither attested by witnesses nor validated by a <i>kinyan</i>, a symbolic act to effect the transfer of assets, the alleged <i>shtar matanah</i> is halakhically inconsequential.
Furthermore, given that their father was diagnosed with dementia in 2005 and was under Aricept treatment, the <i>Tove’a</i> argues that he lacked the halakhic-legal capacity to execute a <i>shtar matanah</i>. In fact, Dr. Springlass confirmed (in writing and via the telephone during a <i>beit din</i> hearing) that R. Levy, his patient, was bereft of the mental capacity to create a halakhic-legal document during that time period. Consequently, the reconfirmation of the alleged <i>shtar matanah</i> in the presence of witnesses, which took place in 2007, had no validity. Hence, in the absence of an authenticated <i>shtar matanah</i> to the contrary, their father’s assets ought to be divided according to the Torah laws of inheritance. As such, the <i>Tove’a</i> is the <i>yoresh me-de-oraita</i>, the sole heir to his father’s assets. Should the <i>beit din</i> affirm the testamentary disposition, they will be engaging in <i>avurei ahsanta</i>, disinheriting the heir who is the only one recognized as such by the Torah.<sup class="footnote-marker">224</sup><i class="footnote">Given that in this will, the testator desires to transfer all of his assets, immovable and movable property, the assumption of Mr. Reuben Levy is that <i>avurei ahsanta</i> applies regarding both types of property. Although <i>Hilkhot Ketanot</i> 2:267 supports such a conclusion, others argue that disinheriting a Torah heir applies only to immovable property, <i>karka</i>. See <i>Teshuvot Maharit</i>, HM 2:6; <i>Levush Orah</i>, <i>Bereshit</i> 24:10.</i>
Moreover, the <i>Tove’a</i> claims that the defendants’ initiation of probate proceedings in Brooklyn Surrogate Court and in Brooklyn Supreme Court regarding other inheritance matters not covered by this arbitration agreement involved recourse to <i>arka’ot shel akum</i>, gentile courts, which is prohibited by Torah law. Hence, the <i>Tove’a</i> argues that he is entitled to receive $14,093.75 reimbursement from the defendants for court and legal costs.
<b><i>Nitva’ot</i>s’ Counterclaims</b>
Rachel Singer and Leah Shlanger, the <i>Nitva’ot</i>, argue that on March 3, 1985, instructions for dividing up the assets of their late father equally between his children were recorded in a one-page document that was prepared, typed, and signed by the deceased himself. At the time, although R. Levy signed the alleged <i>shtar matanah</i>, there were neither witnesses present to affirm his wishes, nor did their father execute a <i>kinyan</i> to validate the document and transfer his estate “one moment before my demise.” This document was placed by their father in an envelope (with outside markings of “<i>tzava’ah</i>” [will]), which was then placed in a dresser in his home.
Years later, although the original alleged <i>shtar matanah</i> was lost, copies of it were circulating among various family members. Concerned that the <i>Tove’a</i>, their brother, would contest the alleged <i>shtar matanah</i>, at the behest of the defendants, on 6 Shevat, 5767 (January 25, 2007), the sisters allege that their father desired to add an addendum to the alleged <i>shtar matanah</i> and consented to its validation by two witnesses, accompanied by the execution of a <i>kinyan</i>. The addendum was prepared by a local rabbi.
The addendum provides that R. Simeon Levy, being of clear mind, arranges that his gift will transpire a moment before his demise, acknowledges the loss of the alleged <i>shtar matanah</i>, and asserts that his signature attests to all that was stated as a firm admission in the presence of the two witnesses and accompanied by a symbolic transaction, <i>kinyan sudar</i>. Accompanying this addendum is the testator’s signature, as well as the signatures of the two witnesses.
Simeon Levy passed away on Rosh Hodesh Av, 5767 (July 16, 2007). Upon his demise, the <i>Nitva’ot</i> argue that the disposition of their father’s assets should be executed pursuant to the alleged <i>shtar matanah</i>, which allegedly was halakhically validated on January 25, 2007. The <i>Nitva’ot</i> argue, pursuant to the alleged <i>shtar matanah</i>, that all items belonging to their father, including but not limited to manuscripts and books, are to be divided equally among all three children, except if specified otherwise in the alleged <i>shtar matanah</i>.
The <i>Nitva’ot</i> further claim that their initiation of probate proceedings in Surrogate Court does not fall under the category of arranging proceedings in <i>arka’ot shel akum</i>. Moreover, they claim that pursuant to New York case law, the court will not confirm an award of any arbitration panel concerning the distribution of the assets of a deceased’s estate. Since the <i>Tove’a</i> would be left with no means to enforce the <i>beit din</i>’s award, he would be forced to file in Brooklyn Supreme Court in any event. Second, the <i>Nitva’ot</i> filed in secular court out of fear that the <i>Tove’a</i> would abscond with items from their father’s assets. The same concern motivated the <i>Nitva’ot</i>s’ decision to initiate a restraining order in Brooklyn Supreme Court that effectively blocked the sale of the property by the <i>Tove’a</i> without the authorization of the <i>Nitva’ot</i>. Hence, they argue, they are liable neither for remuneration of the <i>Tove’a</i>’s court and legal fees relating to the Brooklyn Supreme Court action, nor for the fees for the probate proceeding.
<b>Summary of Submitted Testimony</b>
<b>R. Israel</b>: R. Israel described the procedure that took place on January 25, 2007 at the testator’s home. R. Israel read the entire alleged <i>shtar matanah</i> to R. Levy in the presence of two kosher witnesses, Mr. Joseph Cohen and Mr. Aryeh Rabinowitz. At the conclusion of each clause, R. Israel asked the testator if the instructions conformed to his wishes. Although the typewritten alleged <i>shtar matanah</i> was reconfirmed, at the testator’s request, R. Israel added the following in his own handwriting:
1. In section three, illegible words that possibly read “and my daughters” were added.
2. In section five, the words “three to my sons-in-law” were added.
Upon completing the reading of the alleged <i>shtar matanah</i> and the addendum, the testator dated the document, accompanied by his personal signature, the two witnesses affixed their signatures, accompanied with their addresses, and a <i>kinyan sudar</i> was performed.
<b>Mr. Joseph Cohen and Mr. Aryeh Rabinowitz</b>: Both individuals profess and are known in their respective communities to be Orthodox Jews. Both attested to the aforementioned procedure as outlined by R. Israel. They understood the purpose of the procedure and testified that Simeon Levy was cognitively aware of what was transpiring at the time.
<b>Dr. Springlass</b>: Dr. Springlass was the deceased’s internist since 1982. Having observed Simeon Levy in October 2006, three months prior to his reading and signing of the addendum, Dr. Springlass attested that Simeon Levy had difficulties with speech and expression, was incontinent and physically weak, and required total care. He was taking Aricept, a medication intended to help dementia patients that generally does not impair their cognitive abilities. Although he was unable to create a legal document, he was able to follow simple commands and express his approval concerning a specific question posed to him, and he may have understood what he read.
In his testimony, Dr. Springlass writes: “A diagnosis of dementia indicates that the patient has impaired judgment, but even a demented patient could make a decision (yes or no) to a clear directive while retaining a certain level of comprehension.” In other words, a patient with mild, moderate, and even severe dementia is able to demonstrate capacity to make some decisions, including the execution of an advance directive whether to accept or deny certain medical treatments.
<b>Mr. Miller, Mr. Simon, and Mr. Levine</b>: All three individuals knew R. Simeon Levy, the <i>gabbai</i> (sexton) of their synagogue, for over two decades. All three testified that the deceased was an organized individual who handled the record keeping of the contributions to the synagogue, and they surmised that he was a successful businessman. Attesting to the deceased’s business acumen, Mr. Levine acknowledged that he would not have hesitated in asking him to be a partner in his personal business. Although the consensus among the three men is that R. Levy was frail and exhibited slurred speech in 2006, Mr. Miller and Mr. Levine pointed out that he was aware of his surroundings when in the synagogue. Furthermore, both Mr. Miller and Mr. Simon remarked that one could clearly see from his expression and demeanor whether he was content with a particular person honored by being called up to the Torah.
<b>Mikhail Szold</b>: In April 2006, prior to R. Levy’s admission to the hospital as well as during his hospital stay, Mr. Szold was an attendant for R. Levy during the night hours. Mr. Szold testified that R. Levy would fall asleep at the dinner table and was incontinent, unable to converse, and incapable of signing a legal document.
<b>Discussion</b>
<b>1. The Authenticity of the Will</b>
Based upon the cumulative evidence, it is our opinion that this typed document (the alleged <i>shtar matanah</i>) was prepared by R. Simeon Levy. His meticulous concern for detail is clearly demonstrated, for example, in sections 5 and 6 of his testamentary disposition. In addition to the will’s directives being reflective of the testator’s character and personality, the signature on the document is unassailably R. Simeon Levy’s signature.<sup class="footnote-marker">225</sup><i class="footnote">Our conclusion was based upon comparing the signature recorded on the will with other documents signed by the testator. For the grounds of verifying a contested signature based upon a review of other documents signed by the same individual, see <i>Teshuvot ha-Rashba ha-Meyuhosot le-Ramban</i> 22; <i>Sefer ha-Terumot</i>, <i>sha’ar</i> 13, <i>helek</i> 1, <i>halakha</i> 3; <i>Tur</i>, HM 69; <i>Tumim</i>, HM 69:7; <i>Netivot ha-Mishpat</i> 69:8 in the name of <i>SA</i>, HM 69:2. Cf. <i>Shakh</i>, HM 69:12; <i>Ketzot ha-Hoshen</i>, HM 46:5; <i>PDR</i> 1:108, 112.</i> There is no evidence on record that Simeon Levy was coerced into writing this document. Quite to the contrary, he crafted this document (which he intended to be a <i>shtar matanah</i>) in total privacy from his family. By his own admission, the <i>Tove’a</i> knew of its existence many years before the execution of the addendum and lodged no protest of fraud. Hence, we reject the <i>Tove’a</i>’s contention that the alleged gift document is a fraud.
<b>2. The Prohibition of <i>Avurei Ahsanta</i></b>
Assuming the authenticity of this testamentary disposition, is there any basis for the <i>Tove’a</i>’s contention that a <i>beit din</i> affirming this document will be engaging in <i>avurei ahsanta</i>, disinheriting the halakhic heirs? The Mishnah notes:
<span class="poetry indentAll"><small>If a man assigns his estate to others and leaves nothing for his sons to inherit, what he has done is done [i.e., it is valid], but the spirit of the Sages shows no pleasure in him.<sup class="footnote-marker">226</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Bava Batra</i> 8:5, codified by <i>Mishnah Torah, Hilkhot Nahalot</i> 6:11; <i>SA</i>, HM 282:1. Whether this ruling entails a prohibition against disinheritance or is a halakhic-moral imperative that inheritance assets be given to Torah heirs is subject to debate. See Yerushalmi <i>Bava Batra</i> 8:6; <i>Rif</i>, <i>Bava Batra</i> 133b; <i>Teshuvot ha-Rosh</i>, <i>kelal</i> 85, <i>siman</i> 3; <i>Rashbam</i>, <i>Bava Batra</i> 133b, s.v. <i>assur</i>; <i>Mishnah Torah</i>, <i>Hilkhot Nahalot</i> 6:11; <i>Kehillot Ya’akov</i> 46. Regarding whether it is a biblical or rabbinic prohibition, see <i>Sedei Hemed, Kelalim</i>, <i>kelal</i> 3. <i>Teshuvot Ranah</i> 118 notes that one should refrain from retracting a verbal commitment to disinherit a Torah heir lest one be regarded as a “<i>mehusrei emunah</i>,” lacking in trustworthiness.<br>The <i>Poskim</i> emphasize that either both the testator and <i>beit din</i> or the testator alone must refrain from engaging in <i>avurei ahsanta</i>. See <i>Sefer ha-Hinukh</i>, mitzvah 400; <i>Teshuvot Ranah</i> 118; <i>Netziv</i>, <i>Ha’amek She’eilah</i>, <i>She’ilta</i> 135; <i>Ramban</i>, <i>Devarim</i> 21:16–17; <i>Maharam Schick</i>, <i>Sefer ha-Mitzvot,</i> mitzvah 401.</i></small></span>
Although our Sages frowned upon and even warned against any disinheritance or diminution of inheritance assets from Torah heirs, numerous decisors have argued that assets may be diverted to others as long as a significant share – and according to some, even a nominal amount – is set aside for the Torah heirs.<sup class="footnote-marker">227</sup><i class="footnote">As <i>Pithei Hoshen</i>, vol. 8, ch. 4, n. 9 observes, there is no clear resolution of the question of how much must be given to the Torah heirs in order to avoid the strictures of disinheritance. See <i>Sefer ha-ittur</i>, <i>Matnat Shekhiv Mera</i> 59b (p. 118); <i>Teshuvot Tashbetz</i> 3:147; <i>Teshuvot Avkat Rokhel</i> 92; <i>Taz</i>, EH 113:1 in the name of <i>Teshuvot ha-Rema</i> 92; <i>Sho’el u-Meishiv</i>, <i>Mahadurah Tinyana</i> 4:1 in the name of <i>Teshuvot ha-Rema</i> 92; <i>Nahalat Shiva</i> 21:4, 6; <i>Ketzot ha-Hoshen</i> 282:2; <i>Iggerot Moshe</i>, EH 1:110, HM 2:49–50; <i>Teshuvot Minhat Yitzhak</i> 3:135. Cf. others who argue that any amount of diminution of inheritance is proscribed; see <i>Rosh</i>, supra n. 3; <i>Teshuvot Hatam Sofer</i>, HM 151; <i>Teshuvot Maharsham</i> 7:12. <i>Teshuvot Zera Avraham</i> 2:110 argues that half of the estate should be left to the Torah heir(s), provided that the testator is wealthy. According to R. Z. Goldberg, the amount being given to the Torah heirs who will not be inheriting assets should be mentioned in the will; see <i>Shurat ha-Din</i>, vol. 2, 360, n. 11<i>.</i></i> Although we are unaware of the deceased’s intent in choosing to divide his assets equally between his son and daughters, Halakhah sanctions such a distribution for the purpose of financially benefiting all of one’s children, rather than for egalitarian considerations.<sup class="footnote-marker">228</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Hatam Sofer,</i> HM 153.</i>
<b>3. Gifting During One’s Lifetime</b>
Given that death divests the testator of title and automatically transfers title to the Torah heirs,<sup class="footnote-marker">229</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Bava Batra</i> 135b.</i> how can one disinherit or diminish the assets of the Torah heirs during one’s lifetime? By making a gift in a halakhically effective manner during one’s lifetime – <i>matnat bari</i>, the gift of a healthy person – one can divest himself of his assets so that upon death, the title to the property will not automatically vest with the Torah heirs.<sup class="footnote-marker">230</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Rosh</i>, supra n. 3; <i>Kenesset Gedolah</i>, HM 282:10; <i>Ha’amek She’eilah</i>, <i>Parashat Vayetzei</i>, <i>She’ilta</i> 21; <i>Teshuvot Minhat Yitzhak</i> 7:132; <i>Dinnei Mamonot</i>, vol. 3, <i>sha’ar</i> 4; <i>Pithei Hoshen</i>, vol. 8, ch. 4 (end). For one of the earliest post-talmudic uses of this technique, see the will attributed to R. Sa’adia Gaon found in the Cairo Geniza; see Yosef Rivlin, <i>Inheritance and Will in Jewish Law</i> [Hebrew], ch. 9 and accompanying addendum. For examples of authorities who reject this technique, see <i>Teshuvot Maharam Mintz</i> 31; <i>Teshuvot Ranah</i> 118; <i>Teshuvot Maharashdam</i>, HM 311; <i>Teshuvot Maharsham</i> 7:12; <i>Teshuvot Tzemach Tzedek</i> (Lubavitch), HM 42; <i>Teshuvot Hatam Sofer</i>, HM 151; <i>Teshuvot Zera Avraham</i>, supra n. 4.</i>
In order for a gift during one’s lifetime (<i>donatio inter vivos</i>) to be effective in the transfer of assets, certain terminology must be used; otherwise, as far as Halakhah is concerned, the document is invalid. Proper language indicating a gift transfer would be the following:
<span class="poetry indentAll"><small>I hereby acknowledge by a perfect acknowledgment (<i>hoda’ah gemurah</i>) that I transferred a perfect gift, a gift of a healthy person, that is publicly known from today, and retain the right to revoke this gift during my lifetime until one moment before my demise the following assets …<sup class="footnote-marker">231</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Pithei Hoshen</i>, vol. 9, 174. For similar terminology, see Dayan Yitzhak Grunfeld<i>, The Jewish Law of Inheritance</i> (Jerusalem: 1987), 108–9; R. Zvi Y. ben Ya’akov, <i>Mishpatekha le-Ya’akov</i>, vol. 2, 296.</i></small></span>
In effect, the gift recipient receives title to the property, while the testator, similar to any donor, retains the right of the income derived from the property during his lifetime and has the authority to revoke or modify his gift until his demise.<sup class="footnote-marker">232</sup><i class="footnote"><i>SA,</i> HM 257:1, 7.</i> In this manner, the donor continues to retain possession of his assets during his lifetime and avoids becoming dependent upon others.
To formally transfer title, there is also a requirement to execute a <i>kinyan sudar</i>, a symbolic act of transfer by handing over a scarf or any other object by the beneficiary to the donor or the witnesses to the agreement, as a symbol that the object has been transferred.
The disadvantage in utilizing this <i>kinyan</i> is that it is effective in transferring property and chattel only. Currency, bank accounts, promissory notes, and mortgages cannot be transferred in this manner.<sup class="footnote-marker">233</sup><i class="footnote"><i>SA,</i> HM 190:1, 195:1, 203:1; <i>Pithei Hoshen,</i> vol. 8, 170, n. 2.</i> Furthermore, <i>kinyan sudar</i> is ineffective in transferring assets that are neither in the donor’s possession nor in existence at the time the <i>kinyan</i> is made.<sup class="footnote-marker">234</sup><i class="footnote"><i>SA</i>, HM 209:4–7.</i> Moreover, according to some decisors, this <i>kinyan</i> is ineffective if the language of transfer is “the person will take,” “will possess,” “will be distributed” and the like, as such language refers to a future time, not the present.<sup class="footnote-marker">235</sup><i class="footnote">Many <i>Poskim</i> view this language as a promise to execute a future action and therefore a form of <i>kinyan devarim</i>, which fails to create a halakhically enforceable obligation. See <i>Bava Batra</i> 3b, 148b; <i>SA</i>, HM 245:1, 253:3; <i>Tur</i>, HM 253; <i>Sma</i>, HM 253:9; and my “Breach of a Promise to Marry,” <i>Jewish Law Annual</i> 17 (2007), 267. A promise for future action is invalid because death relinquishes the testator’s title and vests title with the Torah heirs, and there is “no <i>kinyan</i> after death.”<br>If the prevailing <i>minhag</i> is that the word “shall” or “will” is employed in contracts to obligate oneself, such language would be effective in asset transfer. See <i>Teshuvot ha-Rivash</i> 105; <i>Hagahot Imrei Baruch</i>, HM 201; <i>Teshuvot Divrei Hayyim</i>, HM 2:26.</i> Finally, a gift must be given openly, in a public fashion, lest the donor be parsimonious in his donation or give the same gift to another individual in public.<sup class="footnote-marker">236</sup><i class="footnote">SA, HM 242:3–5. For extenuating circumstances allowing for the drawing up of a secret gift document, see <i>Pithei Hoshen</i>, vol. 8, 352, n. 91. Some argue that nowadays one need not be concerned with the possibility of a secret gift; nonetheless, <i>le-khathilah</i>, one should mention this fact in the document. See <i>SA</i>, HM 242:5; <i>Rema</i>, ibid.</i>
To overcome some of the limitations of a <i>shtar matanah</i>, many wills incorporate a <i>hoda’ah</i>, an acknowledgement utilizing the language of “I gave this object to … ,” ”this object belongs to …” or “I am obligated to … this amount of …” alongside the gift formula.<sup class="footnote-marker">237</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Rema</i>, HM 60:6, 257:7; <i>SA</i>, HM 40:1, 250:3. The acknowledgment of the debt utilizing this language constitutes a <i>kinyan</i>. See <i>SA</i>, EH 108:3; <i>SA</i> and <i>Rema</i>, HM 281:7.</i> The mere acknowledgment of the debt creates a legal title. Sometimes called “<i>odita</i>,”<sup class="footnote-marker">238</sup><i class="footnote">Although <i>Rishonim</i> and <i>Aharonim</i> utilize the concepts interchangeably, R. Saadia Gaon distinguishes between them. See Berachyahu Lifshitz, <i>Asmakhta</i>, [Hebrew] (Jerusalem: 1988), 265, n. 322.</i> for this admission to be effective in transferring the assets, it must be executed either in writing, before witnesses, or before a <i>beit din</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">239</sup><i class="footnote">This matter is subject to much debate. See <i>Mishnah</i> Torah, <i>Hilkhot Mekhirah</i> 11:15; <i>Ketzot ha-Hoshen</i> 40:1, 194:3; <i>Hazon Ish</i>, <i>Bava Kama</i> 18:6; <i>Netivot ha-Mishpat</i> 40:1, 60:17.</i> If he complies with these formal requirements, a testator may transfer property that is not yet in existence or not in his present possession.
Because he wished to leave to his children bank accounts, stocks and bonds, land, buildings, manuscripts, books, silver items, and assets that would come into his possession in the future, R. Levy prepared a will in the form of a <i>shtar matanah</i> and <i>hoda’ah</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">240</sup><i class="footnote">The combination of both formulations of gift-giving and admission is advanced by many contemporary <i>Poskim</i>. See Grunfeld, supra n. 8, 106–11; <i>Pithei Hoshen</i>, vol. 9, 174; <i>Mishpatekha le-Ya’akov</i>, vol. 2, 296; vol. 3, 370; R. Sha’anan, “A Will According to Halakhah” [Hebrew], 13 <i>Tehumin</i> (5752–5753), 317; R. M. Bleicher, “A Will: Its Drafting and Formulation” [Hebrew], 2 <i>Shurat ha-Din</i> (5754), 353. Cf. R. Feivel Cohen, <i>Kuntres mi-Dor le-Dor</i>, 9–17. For advocates of implementing <i>odita</i> as a vehicle to transfers assets that are not yet in existence, see <i>Yad Rama</i>, <i>Bava Batra</i> 149a; <i>Ri Megash</i>, <i>Bava Batra</i> 149a; <i>Mishnah Torah</i>, <i>Hilkhot Zekhiyah</i> 9:9.</i> Did his will meet the requirements of a properly written <i>shtar matanah</i> and <i>hoda’ah</i>? If the will was defective, was the addendum prepared years later properly crafted, addressing the shortcomings of the original will?
A review of the original will indicates that the basic building blocks of a gift donation are absent. Instead of incorporating the conventional gift language, “I retain the right to revoke this gift during my lifetime until one moment before my demise,” the will reads, “to be effective from today and after my demise.” Moreover, the will was prepared in the privacy of R. Levy’s home, not in the presence of his family, and is bereft of language indicating that these gifts were being given in an open and public fashion. Finally, the continuous use of the language “shall be distributed” throughout the various sections of the will denotes a promise of future action, which is ineffective terminology for transferring assets.<sup class="footnote-marker">241</sup><i class="footnote">See supra n. 12.</i>
Similarly, the implementation of “<i>hoda’ah</i>” in the opening words of the will – “my signature on this gift award shall serve as proof upon myself as one hundred witnesses, whereas that today … I begin the process of writing this will” – is faulty. In effect, the testator is utilizing a <i>hoda’ah</i> for the purpose of attesting to the timing of the disposition, not as a form of <i>hithayvut</i>, undertaking an obligation. Proper <i>hoda’ah</i> in this case would have been language to the effect of, “I acknowledge that I gave this object to … ,” which would then enable transfer of property.<sup class="footnote-marker">242</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Rema</i>, HM 60:6, 245:1; <i>SA</i> and <i>Rema</i>, HM 250:3; <i>Ketzot ha-Hoshen</i> 40:1.</i> Finally, even if the proper <i>hoda’ah</i> language had been used by R. Levy, the absence of the performance of a <i>kinyan</i> or the absence of a <i>kinyan</i> executed in the presence of specified witnesses may have made this will invalid.<sup class="footnote-marker">243</sup><i class="footnote">See supra n. 13.</i>
Thus, the original will drafted by R. Levy is problematic in terms of both gift-giving and obligating through admission to the transfer of certain assets. The outstanding question is whether the addendum that was prepared years later properly addressed the halakhic shortcomings of the original will.
Normative Halakhah is that improper formulation of a <i>shtar</i> can be remedied by performing a <i>kinyan</i> and inserting at the end of the document that an effective <i>kinyan</i> was made. We can then assume that the individual who was obligating himself in the original <i>shtar</i> wished to effect a transfer of assets.<sup class="footnote-marker">244</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot ha-Rashba</i> 2:31; <i>Beit Yosef</i>, HM 195:20, 22; <i>Rema</i>, HM 60:6, 212:1. See Y. Goldberg, “An Improperly Drafted Legal Document Finalized by a <i>Kinyan</i>” [Hebrew], 1 <i>Shurat ha-din</i> (5754), 301. According to <i>Ketzot ha-Hoshen,</i> HM 245:1-2, a <i>kinyan suddar</i> must be implemented.</i> Accordingly, the incorporation of the language “from today and one moment prior to my death” in the addendum, linking the <i>hoda’ah</i> to the enumerated assets, as well as the performance of a <i>kinyan</i> and the signing of proper witnesses, ought to validate the original will, despite its flaws, and serve to properly transfer the assets to the sons and daughter.<sup class="footnote-marker">245</sup><i class="footnote">In effect, the burden of proof to authenticate a will is equivalent of that required in secular criminal law – proof beyond a reasonable doubt, rather than negating any uncertainty as to the will’s veracity.</i> The will’s defects do not, per se, create doubts as to its veracity regarding the testator’s subjective wishes to execute the asset transfer.<sup class="footnote-marker">246</sup><i class="footnote">Consequently, any further claims against the veracity of the will, unrelated to the defects in question, should be treated as claims against a valid will.</i>
<b>4. Required Mental Capacity to Confirm an Existing Will</b>
If a properly worded addendum and performance of a <i>kinyan</i> can validate a previously invalid will, we must address the question of whether the addendum in our case is valid, given R. Levy’s mental state at the time of its execution.
Although R. Levy may have been cognitively impaired to the extent that he could not execute a new halakhic document in January 2007 and is therefore considered a <i>shoteh</i> in that context,<sup class="footnote-marker">247</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Hakhmei Provencia</i> 57; <i>Yavin She’mua le-ha-Rashbatz</i>, <i>Tikkun Soferim</i>, <i>ha’ar</i> 16.</i> one who is deemed mentally incompetent in certain realms is not necessarily deemed globally impaired.<sup class="footnote-marker">248</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sma</i>, HM 35:21; <i>Tevuot Shor</i>, YD 1:11; <i>Teshuvot Tzemah Tzedek</i>, EH 153; <i>Iggerot Moshe</i>, EH 1:120.</i> People with dementia are often incorrectly assumed to be globally decisionally incapacitated, but certain halakhic views,<sup class="footnote-marker">249</sup><i class="footnote">See supra n. 24. Cf. <i>Teshuvot Oneg Yom Tov</i> 153; <i>Teshuvot Yehuda Ya’aleh</i> 93; <i>Teshuvot Mishnat Rabbi Aharon</i> 56.</i> as well as contemporary medical research,<sup class="footnote-marker">250</sup><i class="footnote">See Zev Schostak, “Alzheimer’s and Dementia in the Elderly: Halachic Perspectives,” 52 <i>Journal of Halacha and Contemporary Society</i> (Fall 2006), 83, 86–88.</i> indicate otherwise.
It is our view that R. Levy, although suffering from occasional memory loss and mild disorientation, was sufficiently lucid and oriented (<i>bar da’at</i>) to understand that the personally prepared document enumerating his wishes of 1985 was being read to him in January 2007 and, upon the conclusion of its reading, to decide to sign it. This level of <i>da’at</i> was sufficient to reaffirm the original will.
To buttress this position, we invoke <i>R. Yehezkel Landau</i>’s ruling that “<i>gadol omeid al gabav</i>” – if a legally responsible individual supervises a <i>shoteh</i>’s execution of a divorce document, the <i>get</i> is valid.<sup class="footnote-marker">251</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Noda be-Yehuda</i>, cited in <i>Or ha-Yashar</i> (<i>Teshuvot</i> Regarding the <i>Get</i> of Cleves) 30.</i> Our case involves a level of <i>da’at</i> more basic than that necessary to execute a <i>get</i>, and R. Levy’s reaffirmation should therefore be valid by way of a <i>kal va-homer</i> (a fortiori).
<b>5. Rejecting the Validity of the Will</b>
Our conclusion thus far may be challenged. If we accept the view that prior to the distribution of the inheritance assets, the “<i>muhzakim</i>,” the possessors of the estate, are the heirs as indicated by the Torah,<sup class="footnote-marker">252</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Pnei Moshe</i> 15; <i>Teshuvot Maharyah ha-Levi</i> 2:86; <i>Teshuvot Hatam Sofer</i>, HM 142; <i>Mahairval</i> 3:35. Cf. <i>Teshuvot Minhat Shai</i> 75, 79.</i> then this sole Torah heir – R. Levy’s son – who will receive a smaller share in the inheritance due to his father’s will, can argue that he follows those opinions that would invalidate such a will, despite the later addendum.<sup class="footnote-marker">253</sup><i class="footnote">For a list of <i>Poskim</i> who invalidate a <i>matnat bari</i> as a technique for estate planning, see n. 7 above.<br>This type of argument is known as “<i>kim li</i>,” “I hold the opinion.” The rationale of the argument is that we do not extract money on the basis of uncertainty. See <i>Get Pashut</i>, <i>kelal</i> 1; <i>Teshuvot Hikrei Lev</i>, vol. 1, HM 38. Even if the <i>muhzak</i> does not advance this argument, <i>beit din</i> has the right to raise the plea; see <i>Dinei Mamonot</i>, vol. 4, 144. To avoid the possibility of this argument, R. Levy could have inserted a provision in his will that precluded the advancement of such a plea. In fact, a contemporary proposed will incorporates such a disclaimer; e.g. <i>Dinei Mamonot</i>, vol. 3, 185.</i> According to this argument, since the will did conform to <i>hilkhot shtarot</i>, the laws of legal documents, it cannot deprive him, the Torah heir and estate holder, of his rights, regardless of the addendum attesting to its validity. If the <i>shtar matanah</i> is not a valid legal document, we must find other avenues of accepting its content as obligatory.
<b>6. “<i>Mitzvah le-Kayem Divrei ha-Met</i>” as Means of Validating the Will</b>
Although R. Levy’s employment of terminology of gift-giving and admission in his original will falls short of creating a halakhically acceptable disposition of his assets, it may be argued that the will should be validated based upon the rule of “<i>mitzvah le-kayem divrei ha-met</i>,” the religious duty to carry out the wishes of the deceased.<sup class="footnote-marker">254</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Gittin</i> 14b–15a; <i>Ketuvot</i> 69b–70a; <i>Bava Batra</i> 149a; <i>SA</i>, HM 252:2. Whether this rule is a type of <i>kinyan</i> is subject to debate; see <i>Teshuvot Maharit</i> 2:95; <i>Teshuvot ha-Rivash</i> 207; <i>Mahane Ephraim</i>, <i>Hilkhot Zekhiyah u-Matanah</i> 29; <i>Ketzot ha-Hoshen</i> 248:5; <i>Rema</i>, HM 252:2; <i>Teshuvot Ahiezer</i> 3:35; <i>Teshuvot Lev Aryeh</i> 2:57; <i>Iggerot Moshe,</i> HM 2:53; <i>Teshuvot Heshev ha-Efod</i> 2:135.</i> A testamentary disposition of assets, even if it does not fulfill the standards of <i>hilkhot shtarot</i>, certainly reflects “the wishes of the deceased.”<sup class="footnote-marker">255</sup><i class="footnote">The assumption is that this rule is effective regarding a <i>shtar matanah</i>; see <i>Tosafot</i>, <i>Ketuvot</i> 70a, s.v. <i>ho kibeil</i>; <i>SA,</i> HM 252:2. Cf. <i>Teshuvot ha-Rivash</i> 207; <i>Teshuvot Maharsham</i> 2:224.</i>
Although this rule has none of the attendant formalities of <i>hilkhot shtarot</i>, its scope is subject to debate and its applicability has been limited by a number of authorities. According to <i>Rabbeinu Tam</i> and those who subscribe to his position, in order for the rule to be applicable, the property or funds must be deposited by the donor with a third party (“<i>hashlashah</i>”) for the purpose of implementing the testator’s wishes, which was not the case here.<sup class="footnote-marker">256</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Pithei Hoshen</i>, vol. 9, 143, nn. 84–85.</i> <i>Tosafot Ri ha-Zaken</i> and <i>Ramban</i> argue that the rule is binding only if the testator clearly designates to the heirs a third party who will comply with the testator’s wishes, which was also not the case here. Finally, according to <i>Rosh</i> and others, the deposit with a third party must have been executed prior to the verbal directive in order for the rule to be applicable, which again was not the case here.<sup class="footnote-marker">257</sup><i class="footnote">For a summary of the different positions, see <i>Teshuvot Maharival</i> 2:39.</i> Thus, “<i>mitzvah le-kayem divrei ha-met</i>” may not serve as the basis for affirming R. Levy’s will.<sup class="footnote-marker">258</sup><i class="footnote">It is possible that if R. Levy possessed retirement assets, pursuant to R. Tam’s position (codified by <i>SA,</i> HM 252:2), those assets that he transferred to a trustee during his lifetime to a trustee would have a status of <i>hashlashah.</i> Accordingly, transfer to the beneficiaries would be effective based on “<i>mitzvah le-kayem divrei ha-met.</i>”</i>
<b>7. The Mitzvah of <i>Kibbud Av</i> as Means of Validating the Will</b>
Despite the shortcomings in this testamentary disposition based upon <i>hilkhot shtarot</i> and its failure to meet the criteria for “<i>mitzvah le-kayem divrei ha-met</i>” due to the absence of <i>hashlashah</i>, it is our opinion that the children’s compliance with the instructions of this document is required based on the <i>mitzvot</i> of <i>kibbud av</i>, honoring one’s father,<sup class="footnote-marker">259</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Tashbetz</i> 2:53; <i>Teshuvot Maharashdam,</i> YD 203; <i>Teshuvot Mahari Levy</i> 2:86; <i>Teshuvot Havot Ya’ir</i> 214; <i>Teshuvot Minhat Shai</i> 79; <i>Teshuvot Maharsham</i> 2:224 (14); <i>Pithei Hoshen</i>, vol. 9, 146–8; and M. Schwartz<i>, Mishpat ha-Tzava’ah</i>, 467–8.</i> and <i>mora av</i>, filial reverence.<sup class="footnote-marker">260</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Hazon Ish,</i> YD 148:8. R. Akiva Eiger is unsure whether to validate a will based upon these grounds; see <i>Teshuvot Rabbi Akiva Eiger</i> 1:68.</i>
<i>Mora av</i> dictates that a son may neither stand nor sit in his father’s place nor contradict his words.<sup class="footnote-marker">261</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Tosefta</i>, <i>Kiddushin</i> 1:11; <i>Kiddushin</i> 30b–31a.</i> Fundamentally, a son must refrain from diminishing the dignity, identity, or self-worth of his father. In fact, the centrality of <i>mora</i> resonates in the words of R. Levy, who wrote in his will:
<span class="poetry indentAll"><small>And by utilizing every possible expression of appeal, I request that there shall be no differences of opinion on any matter of the matters, but that everything shall be peaceful and unanimous, for this is my dignity, the dignity of the family, and the dignity of your mother.</small></span>
Thus, although the gift disposition and accompanying addendum may not constitute a valid gift according to the laws of <i>shtarot</i> or by way of <i>mitzvah le-kayem divrei ha-met</i>, the deceased’s concern for his own dignity and the paramount significance of avoiding “<i>kalon avihem</i>,” embarrassment to the parties’ father in the distribution of his assets,<sup class="footnote-marker">262</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Bava Metzia</i> 62a: “If a father leaves a cow or a garment or anything that is stolen, the heirs are obligated to return it in order to uphold the dignity of their father.” See <i>Rashi,</i> ad loc., s.v. <i>ha-mesuyam; Tosafot, Ketuvot</i> 86a., s.v. <i>perias ba’al hov</i>; M. Schwartz, <i>Mishpat ha-Tzava’ah</i>, 469. The implicit assumption of our opinion is that the mitzvah of <i>mora</i> is applicable even after a father’s demise; see <i>Birkhei Yosef</i>, YD 240:24 (subsection 17).</i> propels this panel to affirm this document, the wishes of R. Levy, and to transfer the assets as established in the will<i>.</i><sup class="footnote-marker">263</sup><i class="footnote">Based upon the <i>shtar borerut</i>, the signed arbitration agreement between the parties, it is within our authority to coerce the parties to comply with the <i>mitvzot</i> of <i>kibbud av</i> and <i>mora.</i> Thus, we shall refrain from addressing whether <i>beit din</i> is empowered to do so in the absence of such an agreement. See <i>Sefer ha-Hinukh</i> 33; <i>SA</i>, HM 107:1; <i>Shakh</i>, HM 107:1; <i>Sma,</i> HM 107:2; <i>Rema</i>, YD 240:1. For the grounds of our authority, see <i>SA</i> and <i>Rema</i>, HM 12:7; <i>Sma</i>, HM 12:18; <i>SA</i>, HM 13:2.</i>
As such, the validity of the addendum executed on January 25, 2007 is not relevant, and we need only address the status of the handwritten clarifications (in provisions 7 and 10) as incorporated in the <i>shtar matanah</i> at that juncture in time.
<b>8. Deciphering the Provisions of the Will</b>
Section three of the will reads: “The cemetery shall remain the possession of my son,” and a handwritten clarification possibly reads “and my daughters.” We find that this addition, which seems to indicate that R. Levy changed his mind regarding the distribution of this asset, is unacceptable. According to Halakhah, any incorporation of a handwritten change must be followed by either the depositor’s signature or initials or another <i>kiyum</i> as certification of the emendation.<sup class="footnote-marker">264</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Pithei Hoshen</i>, vol. 9, 171.</i> Neither R. Levy’s signature nor his initials are annexed to this handwritten change, and in the case of doubt (<i>safek</i>) regarding the intent of the deceased, we do not remove an asset from the Torah heirs, as they are the presumptive heirs.<sup class="footnote-marker">265</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Pnei Moshe</i> 15; <i>Teshuvot Mahari ha-Levi</i> 2:86; <i>Teshuvot Maharashdam</i>, EH 144; <i>Teshuvot Hatam Sofer</i>, HM 142.</i>
Regarding the validity of the handwritten clarification in provision five, which now reads “three to my sons-in-law,” R. Levy’s presumed intent was to distribute three pages of the <i>Moreh Nevukhim</i> to each of his sons-in-law. We find this addition to be acceptable based upon common usage (<i>lashon bnei adam</i>).<sup class="footnote-marker">266</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot ha-Rivash</i> 207; <i>Sma</i>, HM 42:28; <i>Pithei Hoshen</i>, vol. 9, 4:35.</i>
<b>9. The Prohibition of <i>Lifneihem ve-Lo Lifnei Arka’ot shel Akum</i></b>
The prohibition of <i>lifneihem ve-lo lifnei arka’ot shel akum</i> proscribes Jews from litigating their disputes in an adversarial proceeding in civil court.<sup class="footnote-marker">267</sup><i class="footnote"><i>SA</i>, HM 26:1.</i> Matters that are administrative in nature, such as confirming an award of a <i>beit din</i> or probating an uncontested testamentary disposition, do not fall within the parameters of this prohibition.<sup class="footnote-marker">268</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Dovev Meisharim</i> 1:76; <i>Teshuvot Maharshach</i> 1:192; <i>Teshuvot Emunat Shemuel</i> 17.</i> However, in a contested testamentary disposition, there must be a determination of how to divide up the assets by a <i>beit din</i> prior to recourse to a probate proceeding; initiating a probate proceeding regarding a contested testamentary disposition prior to a <i>beit din</i> ruling entails a violation of the prohibition of recourse to <i>arka’ot shel akum</i>.
Should one party submit a claim in civil court in violation of the prohibition of litigating in <i>arka’ot shel akum</i>, resulting in the incurrence of expenses, such as legal and court fees, to the other party, upon proving his outlay of expenses, the party who submitted the claim is obligated to pay for all these expenses.<sup class="footnote-marker">269</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Tur</i>, HM 26:7; <i>Teshuvot Divrei Hayyim</i>, vol. 2, HM 1; <i>Teshuvot ha-Ridbaz</i> 1:172.</i>
However, if a party institutes proceedings in the form of injunctive relief (<i>ikul</i>) in civil court for the purpose of rescuing funds that otherwise may be lost to him, while some authorities require prior permission of <i>beit din</i>, others argue that if “time is of the essence,” there is no violation of <i>lifneihem ve-lo lifnei arka’ot shel akum</i> (either <i>le-khathila</i> or only <i>be-dieved</i>).<sup class="footnote-marker">270</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Kesef ha-Kodshim</i>, HM 26:1; <i>Teshuvot Havot Ya’ir</i> 45; <i>Minhat Petim</i> 26.</i> Under such circumstances, the other party would not be remunerated for his court and legal fees.
A “<i>heter arka’ot</i>,” a <i>beit din</i>’s permission to litigate a matter in civil court, does not mean that one is allowed to accept every award issued in such a court. If a Jew receives a monetary award in civil court, he may accept it only if Torah law would have sanctioned such an award; should he accept such an award when Torah law would not permit it, he is considered a thief.<sup class="footnote-marker">271</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Tashbetz</i> 2:290, cited by <i>Teshuvot Rabbi Akiva Eiger</i>, HM 26; <i>Teshuvot Hut ha-Meshulash</i> 1:19.</i> Hence, pursuant to the matters within its jurisdiction as expressed in the signed arbitration agreement, this <i>beit din</i> is the final arbiter regarding the provisions of this document and the deceased’s distribution of his assets, and the son and daughters of the late R. Levy cannot accept an award in Surrogate’s Court that is in excess of the award mandated by this decision.
<b>Decision</b>
We hereby order, pursuant to our authority under the signed arbitration agreement, that R. Levy’s assets be divided up between his son and daughters in accordance with his wishes as expressed in his gift document dated Adar 10, 5745 (March 3, 1985) and the handwritten clarification of provision number five of January 25, 2007.
Any assets that the gift document does not refer to shall be allocated to the son, the <i>yoresh mi-de-oraita</i> (inheritor according to Torah law). Should the three children agree to divide any asset or assets differently from the directive of this decision, the children’s wishes will be determinative and override the ruling of this <i>beit din</i>, as well as the wishes expressed in their father’s gift document.
<b>Pursuant to the above</b>
1. All <i>seforim</i> that belonged to R. Simeon Levy are to be divided up equally according to their value among his son and daughters.
2. All <i>seforim</i> belonging to R. Levy that will appear in the future are to be divided up equally according to their value among his son and daughters.
3. All of the silverware items from R Levy’s home shall be distributed equally according to their value among his son and daughters.
4. If any <i>seforim</i> or silver items are missing, and upon submission of evidence it is demonstrated that the possessor is either the plaintiff or one of the defendants, the item will be returned and allocated as per the foregoing. If the item has been sold to a third party, the seller of the item to the third party shall pay the value of the item to the other siblings pursuant to the foregoing.
5. Each son-in-law will receive three pages of the manuscript of <i>Moreh Nevukhim</i>.
6. The cemetery plot shall remain in the possession of Reuven Levy.
7. All the funds in the banks, stocks, and bonds shall be distributed equally, according to their value, between his son and daughters.
8. The proceeds from the sale of the two houses in Staten Island shall be distributed among his son and daughters.
9. As of the date of the issuance of this decision, Mr. Reuven Levy has failed to submit the name(s) and address(es) of the person(s) who posses estate items given by him without the consent of his siblings or this <i>beit din</i>. Accordingly, for <i>bizayon beit ha-din</i> (denigrating a <i>beit din</i>), Mr. Reuven Levy is fined $10,000, with each sibling to receive $5,000 from him.<sup class="footnote-marker">272</sup><i class="footnote">Regarding imposing monetary penalties for contempt of <i>beit din</i>, see <i>Teshuvot Yaskil Avdi</i> 6:96. Regarding authority to impose penalties for withholding information, see S. Rafael, 10 <i>Seridim</i> (Shevat 5749), 18, 27.</i>
With the handing down of this <i>psak din</i>, may <i>menuhat ha-nefesh</i> be restored to your personal lives and may <i>shalom</i> reign between you and your families.
The obligations set forth herein shall be enforceable in any court of competent jurisdiction, in accordance with the rules and procedures of the <i>beit din</i> and the arbitration agreement. Any request for modification of this award by the arbitration panel shall be in accordance with the rules and procedures of the <i>beit din</i> and the arbitration agreement of the parties.
All of the provisions of this order shall take effect immediately.

g) A Revocable Living Trust Agreement

<b>G. A Revocable Living Trust Agreement</b><br><b><i>Motel Rabinowitz v. Rochel Mayer</i></b>
On May 13, 2007, Yosef Rabinowitz (“the Settlor”) created a living trust, a legal document intended to ensure that one’s assets are distributed to his beneficiaries according to his wishes upon death. The Settlor chose his son Motel (<i>Tove’a</i>) to manage the trust and designated as the beneficiaries of the trust his children, Rochel Mayer (<i>Nitva’at</i>) and the <i>Tove’a</i>.
On June 11, 2009, the Settlor passed away. Upon his demise, pursuant to the trust agreement, his estate was to be transferred to the beneficiaries, namely the <i>Tove’a</i> and <i>Nitva’at</i>. Attempting to prevent the transfer of assets to the <i>Nitva’at</i>, <i>Tove’a</i> summoned the <i>Nitva’at</i> to <i>beit din</i>.
<b><i>Tove’a</i>’s Claims</b>
The <i>Tove’a</i> argues that the provision of the trust agreement which provides that his father’s estate be divided up equally between his children is in violation of the halakhic rules of <i>yerushah</i> (inheritance), according to which such assets belong to the son. The <i>Tove’a</i> is the “<i>yoresh me-de-oraita</i>,” the sole Torah heir to his father’s assets, and should the <i>beit din</i> affirm the trust agreement, it will be engaging in “<i>avurei ahsanta</i>,” disinheriting the only heir recognized as such by the Torah. Consequently, the <i>Tove’a</i> should inherit the entire estate.
Since the <i>Nitva’at</i> was firstborn of the family, no claim of <i>bekhorah</i> (right of the firstborn male to a double portion) is advanced by the <i>Tove’a</i>.
<b><i>Nitva’at</i>’s Claims</b>
Given that her father divided up the estate equally between his children, we should respect the deceased wishes as a testator and a father (<i>kibbud av</i>, honoring one’s father), and she is therefore entitled to half of the estate.
Moreover, during her father’s lifetime, the <i>Nitva’at</i> claims that her brother, as co-trustee of the estate, diverted $200,000 from the estate and deposited said monies in his personal bank accounts. Consequently, in addition to her share of 50% of the estate currently in the trust, she is seeking recovery of $100,000 of the diverted funds.
<b>Discussion</b>
<b>1. The Torah’s Order of Inheritance</b>
A person can only dispose of his property during his lifetime; upon his demise, human ownership ceases and halakhic succession law determines who will inherit his estate. This process of inheritance is automatic, as <i>Rabbeinu Gershom</i> notes:
<span class="poetry indentAll"><small>No one benefits man, but rather he automatically receives his ancestor’s inheritance.<sup class="footnote-marker">273</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Bava Batra</i> 141b. Many of the sources for this decision have been culled from Mattisyahu Schwartz, <i>Mishpat ha-Tzava’ah,</i> vol. 1<i>.</i></i></small></span>
Thus, there is no transfer of assets between the testator and his heirs through the implementation of a <i>kinyan</i>, a symbolic act of transfer. As the Talmud states:
<span class="poetry indentAll"><small>There is no <i>shtar</i> (halakhic-legal document) after death.<sup class="footnote-marker">274</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ketuvot</i> 55b; <i>Bava Batra</i> 152a.</i></small></span>
Thus, the late <i>Dayan Yitzhak Grunfeld</i> of London, England writes:
<span class="poetry indentAll"><small>The logical consequence of this is that any money in the hands of a beneficiary of a will under the law of the land which, as far as Jewish religious law is concerned, belongs to a different person, namely, the proper heir in accordance with the Halakhah of inheritance, has to be returned to that heir.<sup class="footnote-marker">275</sup><i class="footnote">Dayan Yitzhak Grunfeld, <i>The Jewish Law of Inheritance</i> (New York: 1987), 53–55. For an extensive discussion regarding the halakhic arguments pro and con regarding the efficacy of a civil will, see my “The Propriety of a Civil Will,” <i>Hakirah</i> (forthcoming) and my <i>Rabbinic Authority: The Vision and the Reality</i>, vol. 2 (forthcoming).</i></small></span>
Pursuant to the Mishnah,<sup class="footnote-marker">276</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Bava Batra</i> 88b.</i> upon demise of the deceased, the order of succession is as follows: (1) the sons (2) their descendants (3) the daughters (4) their descendants (5) the father (6) the brothers (7) their descendants (8) the sisters (9) their descendants (10) the grandfather (11) the brothers of the father (12) their descendants (13) the sisters of the father (14) their descendants, etc.
In the context of its discussion of the <i>halakhot</i> of succession, the Torah concludes by stating that the order is “<i>hukat mishpat</i>” (a statute of judgment). The description of <i>hilkhot yerushah</i> as a “<i>hok</i>” implies, among other things, that these <i>halakhot</i> are immutable, even though they relate to monetary matters.<sup class="footnote-marker">277</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Mishnah Torah</i>, <i>Hilkhot Ishut</i> 12:9; <i>Hilkhot Nahalot</i> 6:1; Yisrael Moshe Hazan, <i>Nahalah le-Yisrael</i>, 49.</i> Thus, although Halakhah permits individuals to determine the parameters of their monetary relationships, provided that the arrangement complies with a proper form (<i>kinyan</i>) and does not violate any prohibitions (such as theft or <i>ribbit</i>),<sup class="footnote-marker">278</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Kiddushin</i> 19b; <i>Beit Yosef</i>, HM 305:4; <i>Rema</i>, HM 344:1.</i> <i>hilkhot yerushah</i> is an exception. As <i>Rambam</i> states:
<span class="poetry indentAll"><small>A man cannot cause his estate to descend to someone who is not potentially his heir; nor can he deprive the heir of the inheritance even though this is a monetary matter. For it says … “And it shall be for the children of Israel a statute of judgment” … This means that this statute cannot be altered and no stipulation can affect it.<sup class="footnote-marker">279</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Mishnah Torah</i>, <i>Hilkhot Nahalot</i> 6:1.</i></small></span>
In other words, stipulating that assets are to be distributed to a non-halakhic heir falls in the category of “<i>matneh al mah she-katuv ba-Torah</i>.”<sup class="footnote-marker">280</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Kiddushin</i> 19b.</i> Such an arrangement is null and void and one is prohibited to execute it.<sup class="footnote-marker">281</sup><i class="footnote">Yerushalmi <i>Bava Batra</i> 8:6; <i>Rashbam</i>, <i>Bava Batra</i> 133b, s.v. <i>mah</i>; <i>Piskei ha-Rosh</i> 8:37; <i>Teshuvot ha-Rosh</i> 85:3; <i>Teshuvot Maharam Padua</i> 60; <i>Teshuvot Maharashdam</i>, HM 336; <i>Teshuvot Ranah</i> 1:118; <i>Teshuvot Hatam Sofer</i>, HM 151; <i>Teshuvot Maharasham</i> 7:12; <i>Teshuvot Zera Avraham</i> 2:110.
