Cf. others who argue that it is improper to engage in disinheritance; <i>SA</i>, HM 282:1; <i>Sma</i>, HM 282:2.</i> Barring any halakhically-sanctioned arrangement allowing assets of an estate to be distributed to non-Torah heirs, numerous authorities invalidate a testamentary gift document prepared by an attorney and recognized by a civil court.<sup class="footnote-marker">282</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Ramban</i>, <i>Rashba</i>, <i>Rosh</i>, and <i>Ran</i>, <i>Gittin</i> 10b; <i>Teshuvot ha-Rashba</i> 7:250; <i>Teshuvot ha-Rivash</i> 203; <i>Teshuvot Tashbetz</i> 1:158; <i>Teshuvot Avkat Rokhel</i> 75; <i>Teshuvot Mishpetei Shemuel</i> 103; <i>Teshuvot Ziknei Yehuda</i> 27:113; <i>Teshuvot Edut be-Ya’akov</i> 71; <i>Teshuvot Mishpetei Tzedek</i> 150.</i>
Based upon the foregoing, it would seem that the <i>Tove’a</i>, the Torah heir, is entitled to the entire estate.
<b>2. The Parameters of “<i>Mitzvah le-Kayem Divrei ha-Met</i>”</b>
In the case at hand, a trust was created by the Settlor, namely the father, who entrusted during his lifetime all of his property to the <i>Tove’a</i>, the trustee. The <i>Tove’a</i>, who holds legal title to the property, is obligated to hold and manage the property for the beneficiaries, the <i>Nitva’at</i> and himself. As a trustee, he owes a fiduciary duty to manage the trust in the best interests of the beneficiaries. As provided by the agreement, the Settlor and the trustee may lease, mortgage, or reduce the ownership rights over all or part of the trust property. After the demise of the Settlor, the <i>Tove’a</i> shall distribute the assets of the trust to the beneficiaries. Pursuant to the terms of the agreement, the trust is revocable; during his lifetime, the Settlor may revoke the trust agreement in whole or in part. In effect, by the creation of this trust, the assets have been transferred out of the Settlor’s probate estate, thereby reducing or eliminating taxation of property later in the hands of the beneficiaries or their estates.
Is the implementation of a revocable living trust permissible, or is it a contravention of “<i>avurei ahsanta</i>,” disinheriting a Torah heir? Since the Settlor had a right of revocation of the trust agreement, do we conclude that he retained title to his assets and that transferring these assets to a trustee would therefore remain subject to the Torah order of succession?
The Talmud writes that there is a “<i>mitzvah le-kayem divrei ha-met</i>,” a duty to comply with the wishes of the deceased.<sup class="footnote-marker">283</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Gittin</i> 14b–15a; <i>Ketuvot</i> 69b–70a.</i> According to <i>Ri ha-Zaken</i> and <i>Ramban</i>, if the testator expressed a clear directive to a <i>shalish</i> (a third-party) that he desires to give an asset to a particular individual upon his death, his wish should be complied with.<sup class="footnote-marker">284</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Tosafot</i>, <i>Bava Batra</i> 149a, s.v. <i>de-ka</i>; <i>Hiddushei ha-Ramban</i>, <i>Gittin</i> 13a; <i>Ran</i> on <i>Rif</i>, <i>Gittin</i> 5b; <i>Teshuvot Tashbetz</i> 2:53; <i>Beit Yosef</i> (in the name of <i>Mordekhai</i>, <i>Sefer Mesharim</i>, <i>Ra’ah</i>, and <i>Ritva</i>), HM 252; <i>Teshuvot Tzitz Eliezer</i> 7:48:11(4). Some argue that the <i>shalish</i>’s awareness and agreement to be entrusted with these assets suffices, and there is no need for a directive from the testator; see <i>Teshuvot Maharsham</i> 2:224(7).<br>These sources as some of the sources found infra in nn. 13–31 are culled from Reuven Ungar, Trusteeship (<i>Ne’amanut</i>), Israel Ministry of Justice [Hebrew] (manuscript on file with author).</i> According to some legists, even in the absence of asset transfer between the testator and the <i>shalish</i> and/or execution of a <i>kinyan</i> (a symbolic act of transfer), the verbal commitment constitutes a <i>neder</i> (vow) or is akin to a <i>neder</i>, which serves as the grounds for being obligated to comply with this mitzvah.<sup class="footnote-marker">285</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Nedarim</i> 8a; <i>Maggid Mishnah</i>, <i>Hilkhot Mekhirah</i> 22:15; <i>SA</i> and <i>Rema</i>, YD 213:2; <i>Ketzot ha-Hoshen</i> 212:3, 252:3; <i>Teshuvot Hatam Sofer</i>, HM 110; <i>Teshuvot Ahiezer</i> 3:34.</i> Others argue that the testator’s instructions must be recorded in a <i>shtar</i> (halakhic document) in order to attest to the seriousness of his desire to transfer his assets.<sup class="footnote-marker">286</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Mateh Yosef</i>, HM 5; <i>Teshuvot Sha’ar Asher</i>, HM 2:26; <i>Zikhronot Eliyahu</i>, <i>Ma’arekhet Mem</i>, 506. Cf. <i>Tosafot</i>, <i>Bava Batra</i> 149a, s.v. <i>de-ka</i>.</i> If the <i>hashlashah</i> (a directive to entrust assets or an actual deposit with a third party) transpired for safekeeping or any other reason unrelated to estate directives or if the funds were deposited after the issuance of a will, the “<i>mitzvah le-kayem divrei ha-met</i>” is not fulfilled.<sup class="footnote-marker">287</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Tosafot</i>, <i>Gittin</i> 13a, s.v. <i>ve-ha</i>; <i>Piskei ha-Rosh</i>, <i>Ketuvot</i> 6:22; <i>Rema</i>, HM 252:2; <i>Teshuvot Maharit</i>, HM 2:95.</i>
However, the communication of such a directive or the execution of a <i>shtar</i> with a <i>shalish</i> suffices only according to certain authorities. According to <i>Rabbeinu Tam</i>, as codified by <i>Shulhan Arukh</i>, the actual assets must be deposited with a <i>shalish</i> who will have control over these assets for the express purpose of complying with the wishes of the departed, with clear instructions regarding how these assets must be allocated upon the testator’s demise.<sup class="footnote-marker">288</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Tosafot</i>, <i>Gittin</i> 13a; <i>SA</i>, HM 252:2.</i> Others contend that absent actual possession of the estate assets, the control of these assets for the purpose of actualizing the testator’s wishes suffices to enable the “<i>mitzvah le-kayem divrei ha-met.</i>”<sup class="footnote-marker">289</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Hiddushei ha-Ritva</i>, <i>Bava Batra</i> 149a; <i>Teshuvot Maharival</i> 2:39; <i>Teshuvot Maharit</i>, vol. 2, HM 95.</i>
These assets are under the <i>shalish</i>’s control; the beneficiaries do not have ownership rights.<sup class="footnote-marker">290</sup><i class="footnote">See supra n. 17. Whether the beneficiaries may refuse to comply with the <i>shalish</i>’s instructions is subject to debate. See <i>Teshuvot Ginat Veradim</i> HM 5:11, 14; <i>Torat Gittin</i> 14; <i>Teshuvat Teshurat Shai</i> 447.</i> Thus, should the property remain in the <i>shalish’</i>s possession and the testator subsequently passes away, the assets will be transferred to his designated beneficiaries and are not subject to the <i>halakhot</i> of the order of Torah succession. Accordingly, there is no violation of the prohibition of “<i>avurei ahsanta.</i>”
Upon establishment of the trust, the scope of this instrument encompasses only assets that were in existence at the time of the trust’s formation. Consequently, assets acquired later or not mentioned in the trust agreement are not governed by the trust. To address these assets, a Settlor may execute a “pour over will,” which will transfer assets into the trust after the Settlor’s demise. However, since Halakhah does recognize any transfer of property after death, such an arrangement is halakhically ineffective. Moreover, Halakhah dictates that in general, assets that are not yet in existence cannot be transferred at the time a disposition is prepared.<sup class="footnote-marker">291</sup><i class="footnote"><i>SA</i>, HM 60:6, 209:4.</i> Accordingly, any assets acquired over the years, before the Settlor’s death, will not be transferred into the trust. However, many authorities invoke the “<i>mitzvah le-kayem divrei ha-met</i>” as grounds for justifying the inclusion of future assets into the trust.<sup class="footnote-marker">292</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Mahaneh Ephraim</i>, <i>Hilkhot Zekhiya u-Matanah</i> 30; <i>Teshuvot Maharivel</i> 3:43; <i>Teshuvot Maharashdam</i>, HM 353; <i>Sha’arei Uziel</i>, <i>sha’ar</i> 21, 4:6.</i>
<b>3. The Trustee as a <i>Shalish</i></b>
As explained above, a revocable living trust is a legal document intended to ensure that a person’s assets are distributed to his beneficiaries according to his wishes upon his death. In creating a living trust during his lifetime, the Settlor actually transfers ownership to the trust or the trustee and then selects a trustee(s) to manage it, but he retains the right to revoke the trust agreement and he may use the assets and derive profit from them.
Without addressing whether Halakhah recognizes the legal fiction of a trust instrument, the function of the trustee is clearly identical to that of a <i>shalish</i>. Both individuals are duty bound, either halakhically<sup class="footnote-marker">293</sup><i class="footnote">See Mordechai, <i>Bava Batra</i> 592; <i>Netivot ha-Mishpat</i> 250:2.</i> or legally, to manage the assets for the beneficiaries until the testator’s death. Upon the testator’s demise, the assets are to be transferred to the designated heirs. In both the instances of the <i>hashlashah</i> and that of a trust, the testator and Settlor are authorized to revoke the appointment of the <i>shalish</i> or trustee at any time.<sup class="footnote-marker">294</sup><i class="footnote">See Z. Goldberg, “<i>Yerushot ve-Tzava’ot</i>” [Hebrew], 5 <i>Ha-Yashar ve-ha-Tov</i> (5768), 3, 8.</i> Thus, despite the fact that both agreements are subject to revocation, the net result of the appointment of a <i>shalish</i> or a trustee to manage assets for the benefit of future heirs is that the property under their control will eventually belong to the beneficiaries, rather than reverting to the Torah heirs upon the testator’s death.
In addressing the trust instrument that provides for a Settlor and third party as co-trustees, <i>R. Feivel Cohen</i> rules:
<span class="poetry indentAll"><small>Although the Torah does not recognize the “<i>gavra</i>” (the legal entity) known as a trust, if there is a co-trustee who can manage the assets for the family, it is equivalent to <i>hashlashah</i> with a third party and enables the fulfillment of the <i>halakhah</i> of “<i>mitzvah le-kayem divrei ha-met</i>.”<sup class="footnote-marker">295</sup><i class="footnote">R. Feivel Cohen, letter to R. Chaim Jachter (on file with author). In his letter, R. Cohen addresses a case of two trustees, but his conclusion would equally apply to our case of one trustee.</i></small></span>
Setting aside the issue of whether Halakhah recognizes the institution of a trust, the arrangement in the present case fulfills the criteria of <i>hashlashah</i> according to all authorities. According to <i>Ri ha-Zaken</i> and <i>Ramban</i>, the very appointment of a trustee to manage the estate in accordance with the Settlor’s wishes and to transfer his assets to the designated beneficiaries after his death is a sufficient form of <i>hashlashah</i>, even in the absence of clear instructions. Moreover, since the trustee is one of the designated beneficiaries and he has control of the assets, even though the actual assets are not in his hands, <i>Rabbeinu Tam</i> and <i>Shulhan Arukh</i> would agree that there are sufficient grounds to implement the duty to fulfill the deceased’s wishes.<sup class="footnote-marker">296</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sma</i>, HM 252:8; <i>Shakh</i>, HM 252:4, 7.</i>
There are far reaching implications of this analysis, as it does not simply offer grounds for viewing a trust arrangement as a mode of <i>hashlashah</i>, but also demonstrates the concept of a split ownership of property. The Mishnah, Talmud, and restatements recognize the possibility that a seller will sell something and retain a portion of it for himself by stipulating a reserve clause (<i>shiur</i>).<sup class="footnote-marker">297</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Bikkurim</i> 1:6; <i>Bava Batra</i> 5:4; <i>Gittin</i> 48a; <i>Bava Kama</i> 78b; <i>Bava Batra</i> 48a–b, 63a–b; <i>SA</i>, HM 214–216.</i> For example, one can sell a tree while retaining the right to any fruits growing on the tree or one can sell an apartment and retain the right to occupy it. Applying the concept of a reserve clause to a testator’s estate distribution, <i>R. Z. Goldberg</i> explains, is an illustration of transferring “the actual asset today and the fruits after death.” Thus, the testator transfers ownership to the <i>shalish</i> today, yet reserves the right to continue to use those very assets until his death, upon which the recipient owns the fruits.<sup class="footnote-marker">298</sup><i class="footnote">Supra n. 20, 8. This understanding of “the actual asset today and the fruits after death” as an expression of <i>shiur</i> is found explicitly in Yerushalmi <i>Bava Batra</i> 8:8.<br>Others argue that this rule is in actuality “a continuing <i>kinyan.</i>” In other words, in the context of inheritance, the <i>kinyan</i> for the fruits commences during the testator’s lifetime and ends after his demise. See <i>Rashbam</i>, <i>Bava Batra</i> 136a, s.v. <i>ha-totev</i>. Although “there is no <i>kinyan</i> after death,” since the <i>kinyan</i> commenced during his lifetime, it will be effective; see <i>Teshuvot Havot Binyamin</i> 72:4.<br>Others contend that the fruits are transferred a moment prior to the testator’s death; see <i>Teshuvot Rabbi Akiva Eiger</i>, <i>Tanina</i> 85, in the name of <i>Beit Meir</i>.</i> In effect, there is a bifurcation of ownership between the testator (and by extension the Settlor), who continues to retain use of the assets, and the <i>shalish</i> (and by extension the trustee), who receives ownership of the assets.
The transfer of ownership to another party – in our case, to the beneficiaries – is contingent upon the fact that the ownership of the seller – in our case the Settlor – manifests itself in his ability to use the asset. As <i>Hazon Ish</i> states:
<span class="poetry indentAll"><small>It is impossible that the giver retain it for himself by only protesting that the recipient refrain from using it.<sup class="footnote-marker">299</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Hazon Ish</i>, EH 73:4. See also, <i>Teshuvot Hessed le-Avraham</i> (Teomim), HM 37; <i>Teshuvot Divrei Malkiel</i> 5:216.</i></small></span>
In other words, the seller’s reserve clause cannot simply direct the buyer to refrain from using the item. Rather, the seller (and by extension the Settlor) must retain ownership, which is limited to his right of usage.
However, whereas the ownership of the testator/Settlor is limited to usage, the ownership of the <i>shalish</i>/trustee is not limited to his right of usage for the benefit of the heirs, but rather to his empowerment to transfer the assets to the heirs upon the demise of the testator/Settlor. Given the right of usage of the testator/Settlor, the <i>shalish</i>/trustee is proscribed from selling the asset(s) to a third party.<sup class="footnote-marker">300</sup><i class="footnote"><i>SA</i>, HM 257:1.</i>
The concept of a bifurcation of ownership was examined in the Talmud regarding a father who decided to disinherit his wayward son.<sup class="footnote-marker">301</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Nedarim</i> 48b.</i> Although he disapproved of his son’s repeated acts of thievery and desired to disinherit him, he did not want to infringe upon the inheritance rights of his grandson. Therefore, the father stipulated, “Let my son acquire the property, and should my grandson become a Torah scholar, let him acquire it.” Whether such “acquiring on the condition to convey” is effective was subject to debate. The Talmud resolves that the son could have limited ownership rights, which expressed itself in his acquisition of the estate<sup class="footnote-marker">302</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Hiddushei ha-Rashba</i>, <i>Nedarim</i> 48b; <i>Ran</i> on <i>Rif</i>, <i>Nedarim</i> 48b.</i> and his empowerment to transfer these assets to the grandson, who would become the owner.<sup class="footnote-marker">303</sup><i class="footnote"><i>SA</i>, YD 223:3.</i> Similarly, the <i>shalish</i>/trustee is duly authorized to transfer assets to the designated heirs upon the death of the testator/Settlor.
The parameters of the ownership of a <i>shalish</i>/trustee were scrutinized in two Israeli Rabbinical Court decisions. In one case, a testator wished to distribute his estate to Torah and philanthropic institutions upon his death. He therefore established a religious trust (<i>hekdesh</i>) and appointed trustees who were authorized to distribute these assets in a discretionary fashion. In the trust agreement, the testator requested that those institutions which had already commenced in building or received a promise from him that he would participate in their construction would receive funding prior to other institutions recorded in his will as potential recipients in the distribution of his estate. After his demise, a <i>yeshiva</i> claimed that the testator had promised to assist them monetarily in the construction of their institution and that it was therefore entitled to receive monies from the estate. The trustees argued that they had a right to benefit from the estate and they were unaware of such a promise; consequently, they were unwilling to distribute funds to the <i>yeshiva</i>.
The parties brought their dispute to the Petach Tikvah Regional Rabbinical Beth Din for resolution. Upon deliberation, the <i>beit din</i> concluded that the trustees were empowered to resolve this matter and they rightfully declined the <i>yeshiva</i>’s request. The <i>yeshiva</i> then appealed to the Supreme Rabbinical Court in Jerusalem. Writing for the majority, <i>R. Elyashiv</i> observes:
<span class="poetry indentAll"><small>From the content of the testamentary disposition, from its language and meaning, it is crystal clear that he never intended and had not even contemplated to transfer his assets to the trustees so that they should have full ownership in his assets … He simply wanted to guarantee that the will would be valid and that Torah and <i>hessed</i> institutions would receive their due portion … The extent of their acquisition did not extend beyond “acquiring on the condition to convey,” which is effective.<sup class="footnote-marker">304</sup><i class="footnote"><i>PDR</i> 8:240, 243, reprinted in <i>Kovetz Teshuvot</i> 1:213.</i></small></span>
In the trust agreement, the Settlor of the religious trust stipulated that the estate assets were to be distributed to Torah/<i>hessed</i> institutions that had already embarked on construction or those to whom the Settlor had promised to give funds. Given that a promise was communicated to this <i>yeshiva</i> by the Settlor, the trustees were bereft of authority to use the assets for their own benefit and deny the earmarked funds to the <i>yeshiva</i>. The trustees’ scope of authority is limited to complying with the Settlor’s guidelines regarding the potential candidates for asset distribution, just as the rights of the wayward son mentioned in the Talmud were characterized as “acquiring, on the condition to convey.”
Thus, despite the testator’s right of revocation, the <i>shalish</i>’s limited ownership rights serve as the vehicle for fulfilling the duty of respecting the wishes of the departed, bypassing in a halakhically legitimate fashion the Torah order of <i>yerushah</i>. The existence of a revocable trust instrument providing for a bifurcation in ownership and use serves as a vehicle for implementing the wishes of the departed and allows the <i>Nitva’at</i> to receive a 50% share in her father’s estate, including assets which were acquired by the Settlor after the trust’s formation.
<b>3. The Validation of a Will Based upon <i>Kibbud Av</i></b>
Additionally, the <i>Nitva’at</i> argues that her entitlement is a concrete expression of the fulfillment of the mitzvah of <i>kibbud av</i>. At first glance, it would seem that this argument lacks foundation. As we know, in situations where compliance with a parental desire violates a divine imperative, we affirm the halakhic norm.<sup class="footnote-marker">305</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sifra</i>, <i>Kedoshim</i> 1:10; <i>Kiddushin</i> 31b; <i>Yevamot</i> 5b.</i> In our situation, it appears that the father’s request to distribute a portion of his estate to the <i>Nitva’at</i> in effect violates the Torah order of succession, which mandates that the entire estate be inherited by the <i>Tove’a</i>.
However, we do not assume that the father intended to bypass the <i>hilkhot yerushah</i>, and compliance with his wishes is therefore a fulfillment of <i>kibbud av</i>. Moreover<i>,</i> as we have noted, as long as the Torah heir receives a portion of the estate, there is no violation of the prohibition of “<i>avurei ahsanta</i>,”<sup class="footnote-marker">306</sup><i class="footnote">See the previous decision, n. 4; <i>Mishpat ha-Tzava’ah</i>, 478.</i> and there is therefore not contradiction in our case between <i>kibbud av</i> and the Torah requirement.
Nevertheless, one might argue that the mitzvah of <i>kibbud av</i> should be inapplicable in our case. While a child is obligated to support his parents,<sup class="footnote-marker">307</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Mekhilta</i>, <i>Yitro</i>, <i>ha-hodesh</i> 8.</i> he may refrain from such support if he incurs a financial loss as a result.<sup class="footnote-marker">308</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Kiddushin</i> 32a; <i>Tosafot</i>, ad loc., s.v. <i>oro</i>; <i>Teshuvot ha-Rashba</i> 4:56; <i>SA</i>, YD 240:5. Cf. Yerushalmi <i>Pe’ah</i> 1:1, <i>Kiddushin</i> 1:7; <i>Sefer ha-Yashar le-Rabbeinu Tam</i> 141; <i>Arukh ha-Shulhan</i>, YD 240:6.</i> In our scenario, <i>kibbud av</i> would entail financial loss for the son, who would have to share his <i>yerushah</i> with his sister, and he should therefore be exempt from the requirement. <i>R. Akiva Eiger</i>, <i>R. Yair Bachrach</i>, and others indeed note this <i>safek</i> (doubt) regarding whether a testamentary disposition that involves a son’s loss should be affirmed based on <i>kibbud av</i>, but they conclude that compliance with the testator’s wishes is based upon <i>kibbud av</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">309</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Havot Ya’ir</i> 214; <i>Teshuvot Mahari ha-Levi</i> 86; <i>Teshuvot Maharsham</i> 1:52, 2:224 (16). And possibly this is R. Akiva Eiger’s position. See <i>Teshuvot Rabbi Akiva Eiger</i> 1:68.</i>
This conclusion would equally apply to a secular will.<sup class="footnote-marker">310</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Heshev ha-Ephod</i> 2:106.</i> Despite the various problems posed by validating a civil will – or, for that matter, a halakhic will that has been drafted in a questionable fashion – there are authorities who will validate these documents based upon <i>kibbud av</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">311</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Tashbetz</i> 2:53; <i>Teshuvot Maharashdam</i>, YD 283; <i>Mahari ha-Levi</i>, supra n. 37.</i>
In sum, based upon the execution of a trust agreement and in compliance with <i>kibbud av</i>, we affirm the <i>Nitva’at</i>’s right to receive a 50% share of her father’s estate.
<b>4. The Trustee as a <i>Shomer</i></b>
The <i>Nitva’at</i> contends that the <i>Tove’a</i>, in his capacity as a trustee, misappropriated some of the trust’s assets, and she is therefore entitled to recover half of the absconded assets as her rightful share in the estate, as provided by the terms of the trust agreement. The trustee’s consent to manage the estate’s assets places him in the category of a <i>shomer</i> (bailor). Implicit in this arrangement is that the <i>Tove’a</i> is obligated to guard the assets and that the <i>Tove’a</i> accepts this responsibility.
Some argue, however, that since a trust is a legal fiction, it should be treated like “<i>hekdesh</i>.”<sup class="footnote-marker">312</sup><i class="footnote">Aaron Kirschenbaum, “Legal Person,” in Menachem Elon (ed.), <i>The Principles of Jewish Law</i> (Jerusalem: 1975), 161.</i> This originally referred to an administrative body that collected animals and money earmarked as donations for the maintenance and the sacrificial service in the Holy Temple. Upon consecration, these assets, which were originally the donor’s, essentially were transferred to God’s ownership. Although the Temple <i>gizbar</i> (treasurer) was charged with managing these assets and representing the Temple’s interests, there was a distinction between ownership and management<sup class="footnote-marker">313</sup><i class="footnote">Ibid., 159–63.</i> (similar to the idea of a modern day corporation).<sup class="footnote-marker">314</sup><i class="footnote">Berle and Means were among the first to note this distinguishing characteristic of a corporation. See Adolf Berle and Gardiner Means, <i>The Modern Corporation and Private Property</i> (NY: 1967), 2–5.</i> The <i>Mekhilta</i> states:
<span class="poetry indentAll"><small>“When a person gives his friend money or objects to guard” – the verse states “his friend” – his friend, and not <i>hekdesh</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">315</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Mekhilta</i>, <i>Shemot</i> 22:6; <i>Bava Metzia</i> 57b.</i></small></span>
Bailment only applies to property given to individuals, and <i>hekdesh</i> is therefore not subject to its rules. If a bailment has been donated to <i>hekdesh</i>, the laws of bailment are inapplicable to it. Analogously, the entrustment of assets to a trustee for the benefit of a trust, an independent legal entity, should not be subject to the laws of bailment.
However, in our issue at bar, we are dealing with a revocable trust, and therefore the right of revocation resides with the Settlor. Consequently, even though a trust has been established, there is an identifiable human being, the Settlor, who is the <i>mafkid</i> (bailee). Consequently, the <i>Tove’a</i>, as trustee, is to be construed as a <i>shomer</i>. Moreover, given the fact the <i>Tove’a</i> receives remuneration for his services, whether a trust is subject to the laws of bailment is irrelevant; he is a <i>po’el</i> (a worker), and is therefore accorded the responsibility of a <i>shomer</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">316</sup><i class="footnote">Baruch Kahane, <i>Shomerim</i> [Hebrew] (Jerusalem: 1998), 1334.</i>
Furthermore, as <i>metzuvim</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">317</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Kiddushin</i> 31a.</i> members of a covenant-faith community, we are instructed not only innocent, but also to appear so in the eyes of our fellowmen.<sup class="footnote-marker">318</sup><i class="footnote">See the Mishnah, <i>Shekalim</i> 3:2, which describes the lengths to which one must go to ensure that there is not suspicion that he has stolen from the Temple treasury. For contemporary applications of this Mishnah, see my “Self-Dealing in the Not-for-Profit Board Room: An Inquiry into a Trustee’s Multi-Faceted Halakhic Identity,” 43 <i>Tradition</i> (2010), 7.</i> If this applies to every individual, it certainly applies to a trustee who manages an estate on behalf of beneficiaries. In our case, in which the <i>Tove’a</i> is a potential heir as well, the need to be above suspicion is of paramount significance.<sup class="footnote-marker">319</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Teshuvot Maharit</i>, HM 2:1, who writes that for this reason, among others, a trustee of a charitable foundation may not sell some of its assets to a relative. Regarding the concern of <i>hashad</i> (suspicion), see also <i>Shabbat</i> 23a–23b; <i>Sefer Yere’im</i>, mitzvah 340; <i>Sefer ha-Hinukh</i>, mitzvah 349; <i>Teshuvot Hessed Avraham</i>, OH 21; <i>Iggerot Moshe</i>, OH 2:40, 4:82.</i>
This relationship of trust is not limited to the requirement of transparency, but equally extends to a proscription against any misappropriation of a beneficiary’s asset. As a <i>shomer</i>, the trustee cannot acquire any of the trust assets for himself; should he acquire them, he is a thief.<sup class="footnote-marker">320</sup><i class="footnote"><i>SA</i>, HM 292:1.</i> As a trustee, his limited ownership rights serve as a means to facilitate the management of an estate ensconced in a trust instrument,<sup class="footnote-marker">321</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Perishah</i>, HM 267:16.</i> rather than a vehicle toward his self-aggrandizement.
Consequently, due to the <i>Tove’a</i>’s misappropriation of trust funds, the <i>Nitva’at</i> is entitled to receive $100,000 from the <i>Tove’a</i>.
<b>Decision</b>
1. The <i>Nitva’at</i> is entitled to receive 50% of the assets of her father’s estate.
2. The <i>Tove’a</i> is hereby obligated to pay the <i>Nitva’at</i> $100,000.
